# N
## N-type sheep
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a mutation of the Romney breed of sheep, used for experimental breeding.

>  He [Dr Dry] was a very good geneticist, working with what were called 'N-type' sheep..they were mutations from the Romney flock. They were hairy, the rams had horns. (Nov 9, 2000 -- ,Massey,21)



## N.Zd
 
 <i>abbr.</i> New Zealand [fairly rare, probably idiosyncratic]

>  <b>BUTLER</b> [consistently] (1820 -- ,Journals and Correspondence,97, 252, 365)

>  <b>HASWELL</b> N.Zd Co's Settlements [isolated use] (1841 -- ,Letters & Journals,1/62)



## N.Zealand
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand [ORIGIN: abbreviation, Also 1792 Vancouver's Expedition in McNab Historical Records 1/144 1820 S.Marsden in McNab op.cit 1/478 ; 1838 Polack New Zealand - A Narrative; 1839 T.M. Partridge in Revans to Chapman Letters 13; 1840-44 Colenso M.S. Memoranda; [Consistently uses N.Zealand (and N.Zealanders); 1846 J. White M.S. of Journal 20 [only occasionally used, with NZ and NZld.]]

>  <b>McCRAE</b> NOTES ON N. ZEALAND BY AN OFFICER. (1820 -- ,Journal,9,1928 ed.)

>  <b>HASWELL</b> In true N.Zealand style (1836 -- ,Letters & Journals,1/13 et pass.) [NOTES: [Consistently used (11 times in vol. I) but also N. Testament: - 1841 uses `N Zd Co.', and `N.Z.'; 1849-55 N.Zealand]]

>  <b>MACKIE</b> The N. Zealand flax - Phormium tenax -was growing abundantly by the road side - excellentrope is made from it ... (Feb 2, 1853 -- ,Traveller under Concern,74,1973 ed.)

>  <b>WEEKES</b> [G.A. N.Zealand was Selwyn's usual signature] (1843 -- ,Journal,42) [NOTES: ALSO 1843 Selwyn Journal 97 (et pass.)]

>  uses (Feb 7, 1/76 and Aug 13, 1/103) N.Zealand 1862-8 Ashwell Letters and Journals 3/412 pass [uses N.Zealand consistently] (1851 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1960 ed.) [NOTES: J.C. Richmond]



## N.Zeald, N.Zld, N.Zd
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand (rare, ?idiosyncratic) [ORIGIN: abbreviation, See 1838 Cunningham in Colenso Correspondence IV/6 N.Zeald [4 times] ;1840 Mar 16 E.J. Wakefield MS Diary NZd ;1850 John White MZ Journal N.Zld [1846 New Zld, New Z'l'ds] ; 1859 E.W. Stafford's usual abbrev. in Richmond-Atk. Papers eg. 17 Aug 1/481-482]



## N.Zeeland
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand [ORIGIN: abbreviation]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> The Land was seen N.E. by E. ...so we probably off West Cape, on the South part of N. Zeeland or Tavai poenamoo[e dieresis]. Feb 25 Anderson Journal in Cook's Journals (1967) 3/818 Not being sufficiently acquainted with the Language of N.Zeeland to converse with the natives ... (Mar 25, 1773 -- ,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/237)



## na te whanau
 
 <i>phr.</i> na (through), te (the), whanau (family) [ORIGIN: Maori, http://translator.kedri.info/index.html]

>  Followed by a church service at Te Paroa UrupaNau mai, haere maiNa te whanau (Jan 17, 2006 -- ,Whakatane Beacon,10)



## naati
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a hardy breed of horse used as a saddle horse or station hack [ spec. to East Coast, North Island.]

>  <b>Salisbury</b> naati: brumby-type hack. (2000 -- ,Glossary,2)

>  <b>CRUMP</b> Here's how we're going to catch ourselves some of those long-haired tussock-eating ngatis. (1961 -- ,Hang on a Minute, Mate,54)

>  naati: a horse. (2001 -- ,Questionnaire Respondents (DB))

>  Waka Huia: A story about a hardy breed of horse, Hoiho Naati, unique to Tologa Bay[sic]/Ruatoria. [ngati; naaiti] (Jan 6, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,D12)



## nab rate
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) catch rate

>  [Hdg] Speed cameras reset for nab rate rise. (Dec 12, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,5)



## nabu
 
 <i>n.</i> a non-adjustable balls-up [WWII] (See [cf. sabu](../S#sabu),[tabu](../T#tabu))

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> There are three words in the Middle East soldier's vocabulary describing the normal maladministration of an army. A Tabu, an acrostic word, is `typical army ball-up', a Sabu is self-adjusting ball-up, while a Nabu is a non-adjustable ball-up: eg be on the receiving end of a Tabu. (1994 -- ,Troop Target,38)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Nabu; sabu; tabu; Non-Adjustable Balls-Up; Self-Adjusting Balls-Up; Typical Army Balls-Up: Army, esp. among New Zealander's and Australians in N. Africa: 1940-3 (J.H. Fullerton, TroopTarget, 1943.) (1949 -- ,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,1116)



## NAC
 
 <i>abbr.</i> (historical) National Airways Corporation [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  NAC has a monopoly of internal scheduled passenger and mail transport by air ... (Oct 1952 -- ,NZ Geographer,158) [NOTES: Bishop]

>  Away For The Long Weekend BOOKINGS HEAVY ... National Airways will be making hundreds of flights in its busiest Labour weekend ever this year. N.A.C.'s bookings were heavy ... (Oct 15, 1954 -- ,Evening Post,6)

>  In this case the tumultuous history of the National Airways Corporation (NAC), so lovingly described as "the wings of the nation", has instead poked behind the political curtain... NAC was.. the first state-owned enterprise... Some will remember NAC for its crackers and cheese. (May 5, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),60)

>  NAC supports in principle the ultimatedevelopment of a diversionary airport inthe Wellington district. (Aug 14, 1968 -- ,Evening Post,20)

>  <b>KEITH</b> By 1948, NAC could advertise that it hadcarried 187,000 passengers on its 12,743kilometres of network air routes. (1991 -- ,Lovely Day Tomorrow, A,52)

>  Her uncle worked with NAC, Air New Zealand's predecessor. (Oct 13, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32)

## NAFTA
 
 <i>abbr.</i> New Zealand, Australia Free Trade Agreement. [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  <b>LELAND</b> N.A.F.T.A.: New Zealand-Australia, Free Trade Agreement. (1980 -- ,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,69)

>  NAFTA n. acronym for New Zealand, Australia Free Trade Agreement. (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,747) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 498; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 323]]

>  It is important to remember that when the Closer Economic Relationship between Australia and New Zealand began to be aired about 10 years ago, the long-term goal was not only to come to terms with the cumbersome provisions of Nafta, but also to provide a basis of strength for an assault on world markets, including Asia. (Dec 12, 1987 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),11)

>  [hdg] Nafta paved the path. Derogatory remarks have been made recently about the benefits to New Zealand of Nafta (the 1966 New Zealand-Australia Free Trade Agreement), comparing it unfavourably with CER (the 1983 Closer Economic Relations agreement). (Jun 1, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,B4)



## nafu
 
 <i>n.</i> non-adjusting fuck-up [ORIGIN: acronym, WW2 (Army)] (See [cf. nabu](../N#nabu),[safu](../S#safu))

>  [`Infanteer' writes] Never was the phrase `She's right' used [in 1940-1944] ... to refer to a job badly done or done with a `couldn't-care-less' attitude. This was never necessary because in the Army we had other phrases to cover such contingencies when things did not go according to plan, such as NAFU and SAFU, the first being a non-adjusting fiasco, the second being a self-adjusting fiasco. (Jan 30, 1963 -- ,Dominion,8)



## nag
 
 <i>v.</i> talk [?=mag]

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> He and Dad will nag away for hours. (1978 -- ,Elver, The,43)

## nail
 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [shearers' rhyming slang]

>  But [shearers] also use a rhyming slang sometimes among themselves; say, `found a nail' for round the tail', `hutch' for `crutch', and so on. I have always meant to collect these terms but never have. (C.) (Nov 25, 1933 -- ,Press, The,15) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BAKER</b> found a nail, [shearers'] rhyming slang for `round the tail'. (1941 -- ,NZ Slang,42) [NOTES: [OEDS2] P: 1096/1 B 1941 NZ.]



## nail in the boot
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) (of an animal) lame

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> nail in the boot: lame (of an animal.) Usually an auctioneer's euphemism for footrot. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,55)



## nailrod
 
 <i>n.</i> A dark tobacco in a tight nail-like roll. [(obs)]

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> Well, the fact is ... I want to enjoy the aroma as well as the smoke [of the cigar], and if it gets mixed with that of the nail-rod you fellows are smoking I shall miss it. (1875 -- ,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,125,1935 ed.)

>  (OED) Nailrod and 1lb bars ... with a full assortment of havana ... Cigars. (Nov 8, 1886 -- ,NZ Herald,7/3)

>  nailrod n. coarse, dark tobacco smoked by bushmen. [from shape of plug] (1929 -- ,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,8)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Nail-rod or Nailrod. 1. orig. (ca 1885) a stick of `Two Seas' tobacco; obsolet. by 1915. from the shape (Morris) 2. Hence (- 1896), any coarse, esp. if dark, stick of tobacco; ob. by 1915, [obsolete] by 1925. Both senses, New Zealand and then Australian. The New Zealand Herald, Nov. 8, 1886 (1937 -- ,Dictionary of Slang,549)

>  .. a `nailrod' was a double finger of tobacco; `digger' was a familiar greeting of the 50's; ... (Apr 2, 1938 -- ,Press, The,18) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]

>  nailrod n. coarse, dark tobacco smoked by bushmen. (O.E.D.); Morris 1896 H. Lawson; AC1890 (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),13)

>  <i>On Sale by the Undersigned</i> .. 90 cases Cavendish and Nailrod Tobacco ... (Sep 27, 1850 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  S. COCHRANE, BROTHER & CO. Will Sell at their Stores .. 1 half-tierce Fine Nailrod Tobacco .. (Jul 6, 1860 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  DUNCAN McLEAN, BOUNDARY STREET, HAS FOR SALE .. TOBACCOS - Best brands, American Navy, Nailrod, and Aromatic ... (May 22, 1891 -- ,Grey River Argus,3)

>  And then for those who smoke a stick of "nailrod" per week is allowed, and every day for quarter of an hour after dinner incense at the shrine of My Lady Nicotine is permitted to be burned. (Oct 12, 1907 -- ,Manawatu Standard,9)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>



## NAIT
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) [acronym for] National Animal Identification and Tracing [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  To enable animals to be traced, people in charge of cattle need to register with NAIT, tag and register their stock, and record when animals move off-farm. (Jul 2, 2012 -- ,NZ Farmers' Weekly,24)

>  Some key features of NAIT: * Properties associated with cattle and deer (farms, saleyards, meat processors, etc) will need to be registered on NAIT. * Farmers, transporters, processors, saleyards and other parties will need to record some new information about animals to meet NAIT purposes. (Jun 19, 2008 -- ,Daily News,A15)

>  Animals now need to be registered with NAIT within one week of being tagged .. to meet your NAIT obligations you need to: get a NAIT number, Tag your animals, Register your animals with NAIT ... (Jul 3, 2012 -- ,Rural News,15)



## Nakahi

>  Heke became Ngapuhi paramount chief and developed a religion, Nakahi, based on the Old Testament. (Mar 1, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,A5)

## Naki ('Naki, the Naki)
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) Taranaki [ORIGIN: abbreviation]

>  [Hdg] Slack Naki out to regain some flash. (Sep 15, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B6)

>  .. (if you're lucky - the mountain does briefly reveal herself on her last day in "the Naki", before retreating behind cloud again). (Apr 29, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,A12)

>  The "plot" has Tom Cruise captured by samurai and held in a picturesque village high in the 'naki. (Jan 21, 2004 -- ,Capital Times,10)

>  <b>Geary</b> Gary had to get out of the Naki before he did something stupid. (2008 -- ,Six Pack 3, The,118)

>  .. poor Tom [Cruise] might find it difficult to truly get to know the Naki. (Jan 25, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),19)

>  The Naki chaps barracked for the Blues when they beat the Crusaders this season. (Jul 6, 2003 -- ,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),17)

>  He's won <i>Pulp Comedy's</i> New Faces award, has his sights set on the Billy T award, and he's from the 'naki - where they close the pubs early for <i>Shortland Street.</i>. (Apr 24, 2002 -- ,Capital Times,8)

>  I left Naki pursuing my law and came to Wellington for that reason, it wasn't really rugby-related. (Sep 10, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post  Sport & RaceForm,3)

>  [Hdg] Mepi magic does trick for the 'Naki... So clearly there are no Eden Park heebie-jeebies for the men of the 'Naki. (Aug 18, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B3)

>  The site offers free videos of the 'Naki Yogis in action, plus pages of them in various poses with descriptions of how to contort. (Apr 30, 2003 -- ,Capital Times,4)

>  I don't recall Taranaki being known as "The Naki" before Devlin came along, trainspotting took on a whole new meaning when he used it (but then, he may have nicked it from someone else); and the Cake Tin might not have been Devlin's original phrase, but if it wasn't, it should have been. (Nov 6, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,D8)

>  It's day three of the inaugural World Masters Surf Titles and the 'Naki is firing. (May 17, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26)

>  According to a Diary spy, [Tom] Cruise will have a flood of bottled water in tow when he arrives here to film <i>The Last Samurai</i> in Taranaki early next year. Perhaps he has heard dark tales of "something in the water" in The 'Naki? (Dec 3, 2002 -- ,Dominion Post,B4)

>  She's just a chick from the 'Naki. (Nov 8, 2001 -- ,TV2) [NOTES: <i>Shortland Street</i>]

>  One barman tells me it's because the kids in the 'Naki like Hard House, and Havoc's tunes might be a bit soft. (May 26, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),21)

>  [Hdg] Six of the best as 'Naki All Black fan gets to milk it. (Jun 6, 2004 -- ,Sunday Times (NZ),B4)

>  [Hdg] 'Naki knackered. (Sep 26, 2004 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B1)



## Nakiwood
 
 <i>n.</i> Taranaki (after Tom Cruise)

>  [Hdg] Nakiwood has fled in Tom Cruise's jet, allowing the province's natural stars back into the limelight... Well, yes, but the Hollywood version was chopped well before Nakiwood Tom jumped back on his Lear jet and headed out into public. (Jul 6, 2003 -- ,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),16)

>  Try suggesting to Nakiwood locals that <i>The Last Samurai</i>'s economic benefit to them might be arguable, and you've lost a vote. (Dec 13, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15)

>  "The inspection of animal flesh seems an inescapable royal duty"... In Taranaki - sorry, Nakiwood - Tom Cruise will be lucky to avoid it. <i>Ibid.</i> 18 New signs on the outskirts of New Plymouth these days say, "Welcome to Nakiwood", much-needed light relief from older billboards that monitor the province's facial-eczema-risk level. <i>Ibid.</i> 21 "NAKIWOOD!" yelled the sign on SH3. "Money!" yelled landlords on other roads. (Jan 25, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),4)

>  But right now he is enjoying a final coffee in Nakiwood. (Jun 14, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33)



## namu
 
 <i>n.</i> The sandfly [Maori.] W.217 Namu ... Austrosimulium spp., sandfly. [nammoo, ngamu; interesting that there are no newspaper citations and none found on checking Papers Past.]

>  I think of the trees as old friends, and myself as a visiting <i>namu</i>, a [40]sandfly. (Jul 1998 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 39/39)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> Simulium ? Namu, or sand-fly. Polack, New Zeal., p.319. ... To some insect, of a genus allied to Simulium, is to be referred the New Zealand sand-fly alluded to in the following passage:- `A sort of little crane-flies (tipula alis incumbentibus) became remarkably troublesome during the bad weather. They were numerous in the skirts of the woods, not half so large as gnats or musketoes, and our sailors called them sand-flies. Their sting was extremely painful. . . . All, however, were not equally affected.' Forster, Voyage, ., pp. 135, 136. `The most mischievous animals (at Dusky Bay) are the small black sand-flies, which are very numerous, and so troublesome, that they exceed everything of the kind I ever met with: wherever they bite they cause a swelling ...' Cook, Voyage in Resolution and Adventure, i., p.99. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,2/290) [NOTES: 17?? Forster quoted in 1843 Dieffenbach Travels in NZ 2/290]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> A sand-fly Nammoo (1817 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/337)

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> N|amu, s. A mosquitoe. (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,184)

>  sandflies - nam|moo [namu] (1835 -- ,Boultbee Journal,109,1986 ed.) [NOTES: c1826-1827 list of Maori words in c1835 Boultbee Journal (1986) 109]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> [We] were most severely beset with <i>namus</i>; ... <i>Ibid.</i> 335 <i>Namus</i> and moschettos trying and vexatious. (Feb 25, 1833 -- ,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,285,1961 ed.)

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> Fine and Moon light Nights I go and sleep in the Bush as the House is full of fleas, Mosquitoes and Sand flys or Nammouies [<i>namu</i>]. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),46,1963 ed.)

>  <b>POLACK</b> ... I was covered with the villainous kutus' [u-acute : sic], who have the <i> license </i>[sic] to crawl where they list among the natives; ... and the <i>namu</i>, or sand-fly, who never [234] leaves his victim until overgorged with blood, ... (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),1/233)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> One of the greatest pests ... is a small black fly called the namu, a sand fly, it is little larger than a flea but not only bites much sharper but so poisons the wound as to create a large lump and intolerable itching, ... (Mar 29, 1839 -- ,Journal,2/78)

>  <b>POLACK</b> fn It is the scourge of sand-flies (<i>namu</i>) and mosquitoes, (<i>waiwai-roa</i>). (1840 -- ,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/126)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> During the day a sandfly (ngamu), a tipula, is very troublesome in New Zealand, especially near the sea-shore; ... (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/145)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> The women's limbs were anointed with <i>kokowai</i>, or red ochre and grease, to protect them from the attacks of the <i>namu</i>, a small species of sand-fly, the bite of which is peculiarly venemous and irritating. <i>Ibid.</i> 1/327 [Kokowai], mixed with oil, is also rubbed over the arms and legs to protect them from the merciless attacks of the <i>namu</i>, or sand-fly. (1847 -- ,Savage Life,1/274)

>  <b>WEKEY</b> Of insects the waeroa, or mosquito, I have seen in my room ... the <i>namu</i>, or black sand-fly, is troublesome in summer ... (1862 -- ,Otago As It Is,67)

>  <b>COLENSO</b> They sometimes rubbed the fresh juice of the Ngaio ... over their skin, to keep of the persecuting Namu (Sandfly)... (1869 -- ,TrNZI,1/III/38) [NOTES: Botany of the North Island]

>  <b>COWAN</b> Their bare legs and arms might be covered with <i>namu</i> (sandflies), but they apparently did not feel their bites. (1922 -- ,NZ Wars, The,2/13,1983 ed.)

>  <b>ANDERSEN</b> <i>namu</i>, a gregarious insect (Simulia australiensis), sandfly, a most pertinacious suicidal little bloodsucker which will suffer death rather than relinquish the feast. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Maori Words in English]

>  <b>ANDERSEN</b> <i>namu</i>, a gregarious insect (Simulia australiensis), sandfly, a most pertinacious suicidal little bloodsucker which will suffer death rather than relinquish the feast. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Maori Words in English]

>  <b>DENNIS</b> <i>Sandflies (namu). </i> (1981 -- ,Paparoas Guide,192)

## nancra
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BIDWILL</b> It appears, however, that the very existence of these lakes is a mere matter of tradition, as none of the present generation have ever ascended to their shores, through fear of the nancras, or imaginary centipedes, or crocodiles (for it does not appear very clearly which is meant by the term), which inhabit the banks of all these inaccessible lakes, and with which even whole valleys are said to be so infested that it is impossible to get a native to visit them. (1841 -- ,Rambles in NZ,27,1952 ed.)

## nanny state
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  It's curious that those who use the phrase 'nanny state' as an expletive are always those rich enough to afford nannies. (Oct 3, 1998 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),29)

>  She was able to explain to New Zealanders that they could not expect "nanny state" to accompany them around the globe. (Mar 5, 2001 -- ,Dominion,2)

>  ... let Maori keep their own tax money and decide for themselves how to get ahead, out of the brollie-shade of the nanny state. (Oct 30, 2004 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23)

>  From July 1, 1997, NZF proposes that the nanny state should extract three percent of the total income of each adult... (Dec 14, 1996 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33)

>  He's [sc. Lindsay Perigo] back, and he's talking about cars. Ain't no one gonna stop him! Least of all the nanny state! (Nov 30, 1996 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),86)

>  The scourge of the nanny state, Act's Muriel Newman, was quick to climb into the debate with her oft-stated view that Maori are being destroyed by welfare. (Mar 22, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),22)

## nannygai
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> NANNYGAI (<i>Beryx affinis</i>). ... It is called in Sydney nannygai which is said by Mr. Hill to be derived from the aboriginal name of mura ngin a gai, whatever that may mean. Among the early colonists it used also to be called `mother nan a di,' probably a corruption of the native name. (1886 -- ,Handbook Fishes of NZ,68)

>  <b>AYLING</b> GOLDEN SNAPPER <i>Centroberyx affinis (Trachichthodes affinis</i>) (Nannygai, red snapper, koarea) ... In spite of its name the golden snapper is not related to the true snapper and is in fact more similar to the roughys. ... Schools of golden snapper often move out over open sand flats ... and are then regularly caught in bottom trawls and sold as red fish. (1982 -- ,Collins Guide,177,1984 ed.)

>  [species name] red snapper .. [scientific name] <i>Centroberyx affinis</i> .. [other name(s)] golden snapper, nannygai, redfish (Jul 23, 1986 -- ,Codes for Species in NZ Waters)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] nannygai 1015 (1987 -- ,Fishes: A Bibliography)

## nanua
 
 <i>n.</i> W.218 <i>Nanua ... Chironemus spectabilis</i> ... = maratea -->[poss. a mishearing of e nanua pounamu] [no newspaper citations found]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> EHOUHOUNAMU (<i>Chilodactylus spectabilis</i>). ... [19] Dr. Hector says it is common near the East Cape, and is called by the Natives ehouhounamu or nanua. The family to which this fish belongs - the <i>Cirrhitidae</i> - contains the hiwi-hiwi, the tarakihi, the moki, the trumpeter, and the porae, most of which are well-known fish, and stand, as it were, sponsors of its belonging to a good parentage. The same fish is found in Tasmania, where it is call the `carp,' (1886 -- ,Handbook Fishes of NZ,18)

>  CHIRONEMUS SPECTABILIS (Hutton). <i>Maratea; Nanua</i>. A fish common in the Auckland market. ... Distribution: North Island coasts (rare south of Bay of Plenty). (Jun 1921 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/.116) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Chironemus spectabilis</i> Hutton. RED MOKI; NANUA. (1927 -- ,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,38,1971 ed.)

>  It is also famous for nanua, which are at their best in March, but-after that month lose condition. The nanua (<i>Chironemus spectabilis</i>) may also be caught. (1926 -- ,TrNZI,58/618) [NOTES: Buck]

>  <b>BEST</b> The following fish were caught with hook and line: araara, maomao, moki, mango, ngoiro, pakirikiri, kahawai, tamure, tarakihi, and warehou. The kanae, kehe, kopipiro, moho, nanu and other species were taken by net... The kehe, nanua, and kopipiro, are believed by the Maori to live on vegetable matter,... (1929 -- ,Fishing Methods,43)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> RED MOKI (NANUA) <i>Chironemus spectabilis</i> Hutton (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,253,2nd ed.)

>  <b>PARROTT</b> THE RED MOKI <i>Chironemus spectabilis</i> (Hutton) ... Maori name: NANUA ... This is a northern species and is common around the Northern coasts of the North Island, and occurs rarely south of the Bay of Plenty. (1957 -- ,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,118)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAQND</b> RED MOKI ... <i>Other names: Chironemus spectabilis</i>; banded morwong (Australia); nanua (Maori). (1966 -- ,Sea Anglers' Guide,251)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> RED MOKI [<i>Chironemus spectabilis</i>] ... known as banded morwong in Australia and by the Maori name nanua in some of New Zealand's northern areas. (1967 -- ,Marine Fishes,40)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> The red moki or nanua, <i>C. spectabilis</i>, red above, orange below, increases north of Cook strait. (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,222)

>  <b>AYLING</b> RED MOKI (Banded morwong, nanua) <i>Cheilodactylus spectabilis ... Coloration of the red moki is particularly distinctive with eight red-brown vertical stripes on the body and fins ... </i> (1982 -- ,Collins Guide,243,1984 ed.)

It is acurious construction from the French "Iln'y en a plus" which means "There is notany more," and this is also how it isused - as for destroyed Ypres or Bapaumethey are "napoo," Arras, "presque napoo"- a subtle language, and if they stealsomeone's girl, it is "Napoo all right." (Jan 30, 1918 -- ,Chronicles NZEF,III/36 : 286)

## nark
 
 <i>n.</i> an annoying or unpleasant person or thing

>  <b>MARSH</b> It's a blooming nark, dinkum it is. (1937 -- ,Vintage Murder,114)

>  Nark - an unpleasant person. (1944 -- ,US War Dept. Short Guide to NZ,39) [NOTES: Glossary of Slang]

>  <b>SOLJAK</b> The following New Zealand expressions derive from British dialects, ... nark: to spoil; a fair nark: unpleasant person (1946 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),116)

>  ... when the narks had gone. (1948 -- ,Landfall,6/109) [NOTES: Ballantyne]

>  <b>SLATTER</b> `He'll be back next week to see how we're doing. Not to be a nark like, he's not that sorta bloke.' <i>Ibid.</i> 167 `And here ya stickin up for Canterbury. What a bloody nark you turned out to be. A joker oughta bash ya.' (1959 -- ,Gun in My Hand, A,51)

>  <b>255</b> You're bloody lucky I don't do you over just for being such a nark. (1960 -- ,Word for Word)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> nark: spoilsport. (1964 -- ,Collected Stories,301) [NOTES: 1964 Pearson Glossary to 1964 Sargeson Collected Stories 1935-1963]

>  n.(slang). spiteful, unpleasant person: one who takes pleasure in spoliing that of other people. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  nark n. person who consistently deprecates the work of others; envious critic, soured obstructionist. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  <b>BENNETT</b> `There you go, lad, picking on a fellow again, the moment you see him. Bloody little nark you are. Can you lend me a quid?' (1980 -- ,Canterbury Tale, A,83)

>  nark Brit. & N.Z. sl. ~ n. 1. an informer or spy: copper's nark. 2. someone who complains in an irritating or whining manner. vb. 3. (tr.) to annoy, upset, or irritate. 4. (intr.) to inform or spy, esp. for the police. 5. (intr.) to complain irritatingly. [C19: prob. < Romany nak nose] (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,749) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 499; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 323]]



 
 <i>v.</i> [criminals']

>  He believed someone had "narked" on his drug activities to the police after his mother's house was searched and went to a Porirua house to"have it out with them". (Feb 17, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,9)

>  <b>Looser</b> A person who breaks [solidarity] by narking (informing to the authorities) risks serious reprisals. Once labelled a nark or a finger the person is branded and cannot escape this reputation even if he or she relocates to another prison. (Oct 1999 -- ,NZWords,2 (2): 2)

>  <b>NEWBOLD</b> NARK (v) Betray, inform upon. Possibly from French narquois (sly, cunning). (1982 -- ,Big Huey, The,251)

>  He declined to join the witness protection programme even though he was clearly in the extremely vulnerable position of not only giving evidence against gang members, but of hoping to be one himself - an unlikely progression now that he had marked himself out as someone who would nark to the police. (Jul 20, 2002 -- ,Weekend Herald,B5)

## nashi
 
 <i>n.</i> an Asian pear-like fruit, alsonashi pear [Although a pear, nashi are apple-shaped - perhaps this should go into the definition somewhere.]

>  Yet Turners has also been a vigorouschampion of free enterprise, leading thesuccessful battle against the behemoth ofthe Apple and Pear Marketing Board, whenit looked as though the new glamour crop,the nashi, might be regulated as a pearand go under the exclusive export controlof the board. >  <b>BOLLARD</b> The nashi, or oriental pear (<i>Pyrus serotina</i>) is a pip fruit which essentially has not been tried in New Zealand. (1981 -- ,Prospects for Horticulture,111)

>  <b>YEREX</b> Asian pear (nashi) is not really a subtropical at all as it will, in the main, grow in most areas where apples and ordinary pears grow. (1988 -- ,Best of Both Worlds,108)

>  The word 'nashi' is Japanese for pear and, in New Zealand, is now used to describe pear cultivars that have been introduced from Japan. (1990 -- ,Nashi,2)

>  Nashi cultivars have been selected from the wild pear, <i>Pyrus pyrifolia</i> Nakai (<i>P. Serotina</i> Rehder), which is endemic to Japan and the southern parts of China and Korea. <i>Ibid.</i> 76 The NZ Nashi Council has formed a Grade Standards Committee. (1990 -- ,Nashi,2)

'This is an eptionally callous act, even by National Party standards. (Nov 10, 2011 -- ,Scoop)

## National Park
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  We are doing our very best to stop the introduction of heather and gorse into the National Park. (Dec 9, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,44)

>  It may be remarked that National Park is American in origin, dating from 1872, about the time that Yosemite was set aside for recreational purposes. In England, park, like forest, may have a definite legal meaning when it applies to a tract of land held by royal grant for keeping beasts of the chase. Unlike a forest, a park is enclosed, and [31] it does not have special laws or offices. In all the uses to which park has been put in England, until the recent adoption of National Park, there has been the idea of enclosure, as there is in the cognate paddock. The term forest park, as in the Tararua Forest Park, would once have seemed a contradiction in terms. (Sep 1958 -- ,Tararua,30) [NOTES: Ruth Mason `Track and Trail']

>  The concept of national parks - places where the natural environment would be preserved - developed quickly in New Zealand. Our first national park - Tongariro National Park - was created in 1877, only five years after Yellowstone in the United States, the first in the world. (Nov 19, 2001 -- ,Evening Post,19)

>  The Main Trunk railway runs within a few miles of this proposed National Park. (May 14, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)

>  Judging from the question put down by Mr.W.F. Buckland as to native disputes over the block at the Tongariro National Park, that gentleman is of the opinion that the title to the lands is not finally settled.<i>Ibid.</i> 14 May I called today on Mr. McKerrow, Surveyor-General,.. with a view to the inspection of the maps in relation to the national park, Tongariro, which it is proposed to set aside by statute. (May 7, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,14)

>  The Tongariro National Park proved more popular than ever this season as a tourist resort. (Jan 5, 1949 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,19)

## Native
 
 <i>n.</i> Applied to Maori people and their concerns. a. The synonym Aborigine(q.v.) was used very early as a term familiar to the Colonial Office (and perhaps to missionary organisations as the Church Missionary Society) and had some currency from the offical name and office `Protector of Aborigines'. See also Cook's (and early explorer's) indian. b. native preceded Maori q.v. in common use in early journals and accounts. (eg Markham, Polack etc) the latter being first used (as perhaps native was) as an adjective, and its frequency was (until the 1850's or 1860's) matched only by the use of New Zealand and New Zealander (especially the latter as a noun) for `Maori'. -->[Discuss usage in eg. Markham, Williams, McLean etc.] In Richmond - Atkinson papers Maori seems to be the main personal usage of the correspondents except for H. Atkinson who uses negro, nigger in private correspondence. Abusive early synonyms: see black, nigger, later hori.

>  <b>PARKINSON</b> ... discovered many of the natives (who seemed to be of very dark hue) and several of their canoes hauled upon the beach. (Oct 9, 1769 -- ,Journal,87) [NOTES: [used for Maori from the first sighting]]

>  Towards noon we were visited for the first time by some of the Natives, they stayed and dined with us and it was not a little they devoure ... . (May 23, 1773 -- ,Cook's Journals,2/167) [NOTES: [not Indian]]

>  ... every man, woman, and child in the colony shall be taxed to pay it [i.e. the debt of the New Zealand Company], not only English, but Native. (Sep 27, 1851 -- ,Lyttelton Times,5)

>  Nelson ... deficient in natives. (Feb 7, 1851 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/77)

>  <b>MOSER</b> [cap] It merely represented what my early impressions were on the moral condition of the Natives, and ... closer intimacy with the Maori race has considerably shaken the opinions I thenformed. (1863 -- ,Mahoe Leaves,58)

>  <b>BUDDLE</b> The hon. gentleman said, `Hitherto there has been no attempt to meet the special and local wants of the Aborigines ... Between the provincial Councils and the General Assembly the wants of the Natives in [17] these respects have ... been wholly neglected.' (1860 -- ,Maori King Movement,16)

>  <b>CARBERRY</b> It seems that a native from Taranaki was enduced to join the fighting Maoris here from promises of getting land and money belonging to the Pakeha - ... (Nov 18, 1863 -- ,Journal)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> [?derog] As our countrymen increased and occupied the country, the necessity of keeping on terms of friendship with the natives became less felt, and by the fresh comers, not at all. <i>Ibid.</i> 113 The native is naturally as shrewd and sensible as the European, and far more so than many. (1868 -- ,Past and Present of NZ,111)

>  <b>DOMETT</b> [Maori : distinct from SETTLER (cf Maori v. colonist)] Having a little discussion about the New Zealand war, and to whose fault, that of the colonists or the Maories, it was attributable; Dr. H. [Hodgson, Professor Political Economy, Edinburgh] apparently getting the worst of the argument, came off triumphantly and turned it into a laugh by asking the riddle, `Do you know the difference between a good oyster and a bad one?' - `No!' `Do you give it up?' - `Yes!' `Well, a good oyster's a Native - and a bad one's a Settler!' - which settled the argument. (Aug 28, 1873 -- ,Diary,102,1853 ed.)

>  <b>PAYTON</b> It is always the custom in New Zealand, especially in the North Island, to use Maori words frequently in referring to the natives and their doings; a colonist would no more dream of calling a Maori whare `a house,' or a tangi `a ceremony of weeping' than he ... (1888 -- ,Round about NZ,viii)

>  <b>COLENSO</b> [cap consistently used `If Pikopo and his priest go in, we [Colenso et al.], for the sake of our position among the Natives, should go in also.' [fn] The common Maori name by which the Roman Catholic bishop and the priests were known. <i>Ibid.</i> 15 Nearly in the midst stood Hakitara, a tall Native of the Rarawa Tribe, dressed in a very large and handsome silky white kaitaka mat (finest and best kind of garment, only worn by superior chiefs), ... the whole of Native (I might truly say of national) design and manufacture. (1890 -- ,Treaty of Waitangi,13)

>  [cap] The Natives still own about 20,000 acres, but not much of it is fit for settlement. (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,6)

>  `Waimate Witness' records that an old Native, Tiki, ... still smacks his lips in recollection of ... long pig. (Aug 17, 1905 -- ,NZ Truth,9-5-8)

>  <b>WHITE</b> Beginning with the shrubs at the back, first comes Pomaderris elliptica, or Kumarahou, as it is called by the natives. (1905 -- ,My NZ Garden,10)

>  A Colac Bay native narrated that the late Rawiri te Awha had been one of a party of Maoris living on the shores of Te Anau some eighty or ninety years ago. Under the custom of ohu the men were assembles assisting a man to make a garden at some distance from the whare (houses). (Apr 1921 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/88) [NOTES: Beattie]

>  <b>MULGAN</b> `Pillar of the Church and sweats his work girls.' `Made money in the war selling stores to the troops and ammunition to the natives.' <i>Ibid.</i> 44 There were always a few natives about. ... Some of them were in town on land court business. (1934 -- ,Spur of Morning,42)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Pakeha and natives alike, now that it was possible to do so, were eager to come to terms. (1926 -- ,Tutira)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> [The] following [was] employed by those who served in the [Great] War ... ti-tree oneself, to take shelter from artillery, the ti-tree being very common in N.Z., where during the Maori War,the natives had an exasperating habit of retreating to `the bush', wooded country; (1935 -- ,Slang Today,286) [NOTES: 1935 Mitchell and Strong in Partridge Slang Today]

>  <b>BEAGLEHOLE</b> [cp Maori] Many Maoris resent the fact that they are called `natives' by the pakehas. Joking among themselves, they may call each other natives, but the use of the word in this connection becomes a joke just because of the resentment at its use by the pakeha. One informant expressed the matter thus: `We're not natives, we're Maori. A native is an aborigine, a savage, while we are three or more generations away from that. We don't like having a Native Department, or Native schools, or a Native Land Court, or a Native Minister. We like to be called Maori. We're proud of being Maori, not natives.' (1946 -- ,Some Modern Maoris,300)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Aute, n. 1. Broussonetia papyrifera, paper mulberry, formerly cultivated by the natives in New Zealand. (1975 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,1971 ed.)

>  Temple rightly notes Dieffenbach's enlightened perception of the Maori as the first inhabitants with fundamental rights, and not inferior, intellectually and morally, to the invading European. But he labours the point in seeing evidence of this in Dieffenbach's reference to them as `New Zealanders, by implication the true inhabitants of the country, when the terms Maori and Native were become synonymous and implied that they were inferior to the intellectual and moral superiority of white, especially British, races'. At the time, `New Zealanders' was commonly used (eg The New Zealanders Illustrated by G F Angas, 1847) while the term `native', meaning `native to the place' without the depressed sense it was later to acquire, was also used freely by Dieffenbach. (Mar 8, 1986 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),36) [NOTES: Nancy M.Taylor review of Temple's New Zealand Explorers]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> At four we assembled for evening service in the chapel, when all the natives attended: we commenced with a native hymn, and after the church service we sang an english [sic] hymn, native prayer, after which I then addressed the natives from John 1.29. (Aug 24, 1828 -- ,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,142,1961 ed.)

>  <b>EARLE</b> some few among them ... had deserted from their ships, and were then leading a mean and miserable life amongst the natives. (1832 -- ,Narrative of a Nine Months Residence in NZ,82,1962 ed.)

>  Narcobatus fairchildi (Hutton). SOUTHERN NUMBFISH, OR WHAI REPO. ... The species is also called `torpedo' or `electric ray.' Hutton states that the Natives (presumably of Napier) call it whai-ngenge. (1930 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/98) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  <b>LAMBERT</b> When Dr. F.F. Ormond purchased the Orangitirohia block on the north side of the river, not allthe Natives had been satisfactorily dealt with, and a `Native difficulty' of the first grade soon arose. (1936 -- ,Pioneering Reminiscences of Old Wairoa,20)

>  <b>CRAIK</b> Among those which the natives principally make use of, are the henow, from which they extract a black dye, and the vow, a species of cork tree. (1830 -- ,New Zealanders, The,175)

>  The greatest reception the Governor has had from natives, the most loyal reverence for the Queen's name and `mana', the most intelligent acquaintance with the English tikanga[fn. Policy.], ... are to be found in the Ngapuhi ... (Mar 21, 1858 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/367,1960 ed.) [NOTES: F D Bell]

>  Native Land Act, 1865. "Native" shall mean an aboriginal Native of the Colony of New Zealand and shall include all half-castes and their descendants by natives. (1865 -- ,NZ Statutes,29 Vict. 71/264)

>  Native Land Act, 1873. "Native" shall mean an aboriginal Native of the Colony of New Zealand and shall include all half-castes and theirdescendants by natives. (1873 -- ,NZ Statutes,37 Vict. 56/239)

>  Native Land Act, 1931. "Native" means a person belonging to theaboriginal race of New Zealand; andincludes a half-caste and a personintermediate in blood between half-castesand persons of pure descent from thatrace. (1931 -- ,NZ Statutes,22 GV 31/165)

>  Native Housing Act, 1935. "Native" means a person belonging to the aboriginal race of New Zealand, and includes a person descended from aNative. (1935 -- ,NZ Statutes,GV 34/212)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> we found the voices came from some Natives, one of which stood on the top of a projecting rock. (Apr 7, 1773 -- ,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/248)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> we heard a loud hallooing on ... an island, which on this occasion obtained the name of Indian Island; and standing on the shore we perceived one of the natives ... He stood with a club or battle-axe in his hand, ... and behind him ... two women, each of them having a long spear. (1777 -- ,Voyage Round the World,1/137)

>  <b>SAVAGE</b> It is advisable not to suffer any natives to come on board until the ship is brought to an anchor, as the novelty of their appearance is liable to take off the attention of the seamen from their duty, ... (1807 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),6)

>  <b>MARSDEN</b> In a few weeks some natives of New Zealand came to Port Jackson again; three of these men besides Duaterra (Ruatara) I took into my own house. (Nov 28, 1813 -- ,Marsden's Lieutenants,38) [NOTES: 1813 Nov 28 Marsden in Elder Marsden's Lieutenants (1934)]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Government House, Sydney, New South Wales ... and his Excellency being equally solicitous to protect the natives of New Zealand and the Bay of Islands, in all their just rights and privileges, [32] as those of every other dependency of the territory of New South Wales, ... (Nov 9, 1814 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/31) [NOTES: 1814 Nov 9 Sydney Gazette in 1817 Nicholas NZ]

>  <b>MARSDEN</b> When our arrival was known we were soon surrounded by numbers of the natives, who vied with one another in their attentions. None of us were under the smallest apprehensions of danger, any more than if amongst Europeans. (1815 -- ,Letters & Journals of Samuel Marsden,96,1932 ed.)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Like the natives [of a North Cape tribe], he [a Tahitian who had been `civilised' in Port Jackson] wore the mat thrown over his shoulders, but the one he had on was bordered with a deep vandyke of different colours, and fairly bedizened with the feathers of parrots and other birds, ... (1817 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/95)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> [initial cap] I afterwards visited three settlements towards Waitangi. The first a few Natives from inland; the second a company of Ship girls; ... <i>Ibid.</i> 24 Aug. 142 At four we assembled for evening service in the chapel, when all the natives attended: we commenced with a native hymn, and after the church service we sang an english [sic] hymn, native prayer, after which I then addressed the natives from John 1.29. (Apr 22, 1827 -- ,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,53,1961 ed.)

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> Crow made all hands of the Natives (or Tangata Mouries) give us some of their Dances, ... <i>Ibid.</i> 53 The Sawyers tell the Natives when in Anger, They will exterminate the `Tangata Mouries' as the `Rah Parkieah' has the `Rah Mourie'.  <i>n.</i> a Moriori

>  The Natives who had quitted their station as soon as they saw us land now advanced hastily, ... (Nov 29, 1791 -- ,Journal of the 'Chatham') [NOTES: in 1908 McNab Historical Records 2/504]

>  - we had proceeded but a little way when we first observed the [Chatham Island] Natives forming rather hastily into a Body by the edge of the wood [63] abreast of us, - (Nov 1791 -- ,Journal of the 'Chatham',62) [NOTES: 1791 Nov E.Bell Journal of Voyage in H.M.S. `Chatham' (Vancouver expedition) (MS ATB)]



 
 <i>adj.</i> Used within New Zealand, in competition with the more specific New Zealand, Maori to denote especially fauna (including human fauna) and flora considered indigenous. It is now (1984) anobsolescent or non-preferred use, chiefly because of the felt derogatory application to people and things Maori (cf the change in 1947 from Department of `Native Affairs' to `Maori Affairs', and such shifts as native earth oven amended to earth oven between the sixth (1957) and seventh (1971) editions of Williams Dictionary (Umu 1 p467); and also of greater feeling of identity among born New Zealanders expressing itself in a more specific descriptive term `New Zealand'. (Shifts such as native bush to indigenous forest are an example of vernacular or common as against technical or `professional' forestry usage: the latter being the converse of exotic forest.) The New Zealand non-specificity of native can be seen in its Australian applications (see Morris), and is illustrated by OED 13c. `In the names of Australian animals and birds in the names of Australian plants, fruits, etc.' [J.A. Andersen's comments (`Popular Names of New Zealand Plants': 1926 Tr NZI 662) still hold: `The word `native' has been prefixed to almost as many names as the words `New Zealand .... This word may perhaps be descriptive if used within New Zealand and between New Zealanders; but if used outside - say in Australia - its meaning will be quite changed; for should the New Zealander speak of `native teak' the Australian would think not of a New Zealand but of an Australian tree. The confusion might be avoided were the word `Maori' used instead of the word `native', and indeed, instead of the long double `New Zealand'. See also 1933 Acland's note on indigenous. Usage is separated below into 1. general and 2. specific applications. 1.]

>  There is a change-over in manyDepartments from "Native" to "Maori" asoccasion demands. (1947 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CCLXVI/491)

>  Maori Purposes Bill... It is the usualyearly Bill with this difference that itprovides for the alteration of the word"Native" to "Maori" in all Statutes. (1947 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CCLXXIX/952)

>  Wherever the term "Native" appears in anyAct, regulation, rule.. that term shall..be hereafter read as the term "Maori." (1947 -- ,NZ Statutes,59/653 Sect. 2(2)) [NOTES: Maori Purposes Act, 1947]

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> [Hdg] The future of native avifauna. (1926 -- ,Tutira,203)

>  <b>POLACK</b> `Native Tomahawk', `Native spear' (1840 -- ,Manners & Customs of the NZers,2/31/2)

>  <b>ASHWELL</b> Native minister .... the native question. (1861 -- ,Letters & Journals,3/386)

>  (Acland Gloss.) Native.- Indigenous. Not as a rule used here in the Australian (and Kipling's) sense of `the native born'. N. used in combinations to make names of various plants and birds, e.g. n. broom, n.pigeon. ([1898 Morris Austral-English]) (Nov 11, 1933 -- ,Press, The,15)

 <i>n.</i> a Maori [Native n and aj. remained in official use until 1947. (Cf R. Mason's note, 1953.) The term `Native' in `Native Minister', `Native Department' etc. was dropped during the war in favour of `Maori'. I understand in deference to opinion of B.B.C. who found native `a term much disliked in India etc. & wished to avoid it in broadcasts. Native schools now Maori schools')]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Native, n. This word, originally applied, as elsewhere, to the aboriginal inhabitants of Australia, is now used exclusively to designate white people born in Australia. The members of the `Australian Natives' Association' (A.N.A.), founded April 27, 1871, pride themselves on being Australian-born and not immigrants. Mr. Rudyard Kipling, in the `Times' of Nov. 1895, Published a poem called `The Native-Born,' sc. born in the British Empire, but outside Great Britain. As applied to Plants, Animals, Names, etc., the word Nativebears its original sense, as in `Native Cabbage,' `Native Bear,' `Native name for,' etc., though in the last case it is now considered more correct to say in Australia `aboriginal name for,' and in New Zealand `Maori name for.' (1898 -- ,Austral English,315)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> ... Tutira was leased by forty native owners to T.K.Newton ... The block was held in common by these natives, ... (1926 -- ,Tutira,117)



## native
 
 <i>n.</i> native plant

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Another reason assigned for the disappearance of the natives is ability to compete with alien breeds in regard to food supply. (1926 -- ,Tutira,204)

>  But when you've got trees there's always something happening - at the moment the natives are flowering and the tuis are everywhere. (Dec 16, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,C9)



 
 <i>adj.</i> In combinations merging into compounds in the popular names of local fauna and flora, especially where the local variety was named after some real or fancied resemblance to a European or overseas model (eg native bluebell). This use can be (and is being) replaced by New Zealand aj, and Maori aj. In the dictionary native is considered as a separable prefix for reasons of convenience when used in names of fauna and flora. See anise, aniseed, ash, bitter cress, bleu-bell, bramble, burr, carrot, clematis, currant, cypress, daisy, dock, fig, flax, fuchsia, heath, hemp, holly, honeysuckle, ivy, laburnum, laurel, lilac, mimosa, mistletoe, mulberry, myrtle, passion fruit, pepper, sarsaparilla, teak, thorn, tobacco; honey; quail, salmon.

>  <b>SWAINSON</b> .. for a period of ten days or a fortnight, little or no gold was to be seen. The sight of even a single ounce ... would now have satisfied the most impatient, ...; still, not an ounce of native gold could be purchased in Auckland. (1835 -- ,Auckland,105)



 
 <i>adj.</i> (of country) in its aboriginal state, virgin.

>  On native country Flax is always a sign of good land. (Oct 14, 1933 -- ,Press, The,15) [NOTES: Acland Gloss]

>  <b>POLACK</b> My native property has been purchased in a manner agreeably to the custom of the missionaries, ... (1840 -- ,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/xxxii)



 
 <i>n.</i> item of native fauna

>  Although many of the creatures are exotic, they are now concentrating on New Zealand natives, including the extinct Canterbury mudfish, which looks like thriving. (Jul 28, 1986 -- ,NZ Woman's Weekly) [NOTES: quoted in 1986 Oct 4 Listener 5]



 
 <i>n.</i> native tree

## Native Affairs
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  The Hon. Mr. Mason (Minister of NativeAffairs) replied. (1946 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CCLXXIII/786)

>  When I first entered this House I saidthat the word "Native" should beabolished and the word "Maori" be usedinstead whenever it applied to the Maoripeople. Since then the fruits arebeginning to be seen because there usedto be a.. Native Affairs Committee..which is now called the Maori AffairsCommittee and I understand that the PrimeMinister, who is our Native Minister...Why not Maori Minister?... I think hewill eventually be called the Minister incharge of Maori Affairs. (1947 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CCLXXVI/269)

>  All references to the Native Minister inany Act, regulation.. shall be hereafterread as references to the Minister ofMaori Affairs. (1947 -- ,NZ Statutes,59/653 Sect. 2(3)) [NOTES: Maori Purposes Act 1947]

>  I gather that the administration ofNative affairs is not to be intrusted toMinisters, but to the Governor alone. (May 2, 1856 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,1856-1858 / 49)

>  I want to know, since you gave it up who is at the head of the Native Reserve Department - ... Native Affairs. (May 23, 1859 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/459,1960 ed.) [NOTES: Domett]



## Native Affairs Department
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  The substance of that arrangement[founded on the departmental memorandumof May, 1856] was this: Ministers were toconsult the Native Affairs Department inthe ordinary way - that is, the adviseHis Excellency on all questions withoutexception, while His Excellency reservedto himself the absolute right of actingor not as he pleased. (Aug 10, 1860 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,1858-1860 / 281)

>  The Native Affairs Department became the Maori Affairs Department, the Native Land Court the Maori Land Court, etc. (Dec 28, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),8)

## native bird
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The advantage to our native birds of compulsory protection has been amply demonstrated by results. (1899 -- ,TrNZI,31/10)

>  It is the opinion of the curator that native birds have considerably increased since he arrived on the [Little Barrier] island. (Nov 24, 1900 -- ,Egmont Star,19)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> The real cause of diminution of native birds is easy to give. (1926 -- ,Tutira,205)

>  Surely it would be as easy to preserve what native birds we have, by their capture and removal, as to catch wild birds in far away England, and transport them fourteen thousand miles. (Nov 24, 1900 -- ,Egmont Star,19)

>  The Acts of 1880 protection of native birds and imported game is in force in Chathams. (Mar 1, 1906 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,431,1950 ed.)

>  <b>SANSOM</b> As for those, who despite our laws continue to kill native birds ..., as an old Orkney woman said `I dinna like them.' (1982 -- ,In the Grip of an Island,100)

>  <b>NEAVE</b> We were delighted with the background of native bush, and glad to ... listen to the lovely bird calls, which were strange to our ears, accustomed to ... absence of native birds in our district. (1980 -- ,Land of Munros, The,47)

>  <b>MARSHALL</b> (Kenneth's Friend) I could hear the native birds in the bush, and the waves on the beach. (1984 -- ,Day Hemingway Died, The,7)

## native block
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a grazed area of land in indigenous tussock or other ground cover.

>  M H-B P Fine Wool 37 There are 33 blocks on the oversown country, ranging from 40 ha to 190 ha, three Maku blocks and five native blocks. (1982 -- ,Fine Wool)



## native blowfly
 
 <i>n.</i> [1850s in 1971 Hall-Jones Mr Surveyor Thomson 56 (Griffiths Collection)] (See [also Maori blowfly](../A#also-maori-blowfly))

>  Native blow-flies <i>(Calliphora quadrimaculata)</i> were very much more abundant in the country districts than they are now. Mr. Mieville, who settled in the Mataura district in 1854, in his contribution to <i>Pioneer Recollections</i>, collected by Mr. H. Beattie (second series), says, `The trouble of pioneers is the bluebottle-fly, which is to be met with only in new country, where its ravages are beyond belief.' (1922 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/275) [NOTES: (Bathgate, Otago)]

>  There's old timers we knew, and some who shot through A hard case, drag the chain, done a bunk, As busy as a blow fly, and bugalugs - from the boo-ai The rouseabout, as drunk as a skunk. (Dec 2004 -- ,NZ Memories,p18)

## native broom
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>LEVY</b> ... shrubs common in the tussock zone: matagouri, native brooms, dwarf koromikos, monoao, sarmentose tutu, the grasslike spiky-leaved Spaniard, the harsh patotara, golden cottonwood, cushion-like pimeleas, and the low, matted, carpet-like growth of the scabweeds are characteristic of the low tussock country, ... <i>Ibid.</i> 72 The tussock is interspersed with shrubs, and of these the matagouri and native brooms are common. <i>Ibid.</i> 356 Native brooms - <i>Carmichaelia spp </i> . (1951 -- ,Grasslands of NZ,35,1970 ed.)

>  Many native broom species are on the decline because of land development, fire, animal browsing, ... (1987 -- ,Triennial Report Botany Division,60)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Broom, native. <i>Carmichaelia </i> , various sp. (1867 -- ,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),764)

>  <b>1/iii/40</b> Native Brooms. Carmichaellia crassicaulis, Hook. f. (1869 -- ,TrNZI) [NOTES: (Buchanan Botany Otago)]

>  <b>FEATON</b> CARMICHAELIA (<i>Brown.</i>) The Carmichaelias, or native brooms. <i>Ibid.</i> [110?] CARMICHAELIA FLAGELLIFORMIS (<i>Col</i>) ... The `NATIVE BROOM'.- This species is well known by its long whip-like branches, ... (1889 -- ,Art Album NZ Flora,107)

>  Carmichaelia is perhaps the most characteristic of the many remarkable endemic genera ... represented from the North Cape to Foveaux Strait by numerous species ... but in all localities recognised by the settler as native broom and by the Maori as <i>makaka.</i> (1897 -- ,TrNZI,29/501)

>  The `wild Irishman' ... may be associated with the native brooms [<i>fn Carmichaelia </i> ] and several other almost leafless shrubs. (1900 -- ,Canterbury Old & New,179)

>  ... although species of <i>Carmichaelia </i> (native broom) are unpalatable shrubs, they are often chewed ... (1938 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,20/233A)

>  <b>EVANS</b> The whole [Waikaka] valley and the ... ridges were covered [in 1876] with luxurious white tussocks (Poa caespitosa) diversified with a few snow grass tussocks (Danthonia raoulii), trees and shrubs comprising Maukoko, or native broom (Carmichaelia australia[?lis]), Koromiko (Veronica salicifo), daisy tree (Aleria rirgata [?virgata]), cottonwood (Cassinia fulvida), ... native thorns or matagouri and mingimingi (Coprosma propinqua). [check] (1962 -- ,Waikaka Saga,239)

>  <i>Carmichaelia flagelliformis </i> (1916 -- ,Dunedin Field Club Plant Catalogues) [NOTES: (And)]

>  <i>Carmichaelia subulata </i> (1914 -- ,TrNZI,46/) [NOTES: (And)]



## native bush
 
 <i>n.</i> indigenous forest [ca1950 still a popular term, though in technical and semitechnical Forest Service publications it has been replaced by the unpromising indigenous bush or forest (cf indigenous - rather than native timber, tree etc distinct from exotic).]

>  A tastefully-arranged lawn.. and a famous garden.. backed up by the grand old native bush complete the ideal of a rural home. (May 22, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,00) [NOTES: Check page]

>  The farm has three main enterprises - a 500 cow dairy, a 30ha kiwifruit orchard and 65ha of productive forestry plantations, with another 100ha of native bush in steep gorges being fenced off. (May 27, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,C7)

>  <b>PYKE</b> On the left side arose a succession of grassy bluffs surmounted by native `bush'. (1873 -- ,Wild Will Enderby,IV/iii/122,1889,1974 ed.)

>  Clearing piri-piri from edge of the native bush and planting tomatoes. The piri-piri is 7 ft. high round many trees with an average of three feet all about and thick as felted wool. (Nov 17, 1882 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,314,1950 ed.)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> Clearing piri-piri from edge of the native bush and planting tomatoes. The piri-piri is 7 ft. high round many trees with an average of three feet all about and thick as felted wool. (1888 -- ,Antipodean Notes,150)

>  <b>DAVIDSON</b> The native bush which covers a large portion of the [Dunedin] `town belt' is in itself a heritage to be highly prized, ... (1889 -- ,Stories of NZ Life,61)

>  ... a wide bleak prospect opened out ... with random patches of native bush showing here and there ... (1889 -- ,Zealandia,1/6) [NOTES: (Graham)]

>  <b>NEIL</b> KORAMIKA (NATIVE VERONICA). ... It is found growing in almost every native bush. (1898 -- ,Family Herb Doctor,28)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> Two or three hundred yards beyond begins an irregular fringe of native bush, broadleaf, veronica, fuchsia, pittosporum, and tangled supplejack; beyond these rise the lofty pines and other forest giants. (1898 -- ,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,79)

>  <b>BELL</b> To the artistic eye, the bush clearings ... may appear as a sore blemish on the face of the landscape. Thickly strewn with blackened logs and branches, ... these clearings stand out in ugly contrast with the virgin native bush. (1899 -- ,In the Shadow of the Bush,2)

>  <b>KAYE</b> The fragrance of the native bush (1900 -- ,Haromi,36)

>  <b>WAITE</b> The clean blue sky, the waving toi toi on the fringe of native bush, ... made an ineffaceable impression. (1919 -- ,New Zealanders at Gallipoli,9)

>  <b>REISCHEK</b> Perhaps, also, he will help to remind them how good, how strenuous life was `in the good old days,' when their Dominion was still half-savage, still unsettled after the toughest of colonial wars, and before the fire and axe of succeeding generations of peaceful settlers had robbed her of so much of her incomparable possession, the native bush. (1930 -- ,Yesterdays in Maoriland,7) [NOTES: H.E.L.Priday Preface]

>  <b>HYDE</b> Gradually he weaned them to trees and shrubs, and gave them long lectures about the duty of preserving their heritage of native bush - which they never saw, as it lay miles away over the hills. His favourite day in the year [32] was Arbor Day, ... (1938 -- ,Godwits Fly, The,31,1970 ed.)

>  <b>CHERRILL</b> ...a timber mill, right up in the native bush ... [53] Oh, that's just Native Bush. (1950 -- ,Story of a NZ Sheepfarm,43)

>  <b>DEWAR</b> Fern and moki and fuchsia, laced through with lawyer bramble, grew dense and hopeless at their feet. But beyond was the native bush [in Southland]. (1953 -- ,Chaslands,12)

>  <b>HINTZ</b> It is scrub-clad, with some lovely remnants of native bush. (1964 -- ,Trout at Taupo,36)

>  <b>FRANCE</b> Now the mountains marched beside them, and native bush swelled in the low valleys. (1961 -- ,Ice Cold River,43)

>  <b>TURNER</b> Now the mountains marched beside them, and native bush swelled in the low valleys. (1966 -- ,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,52)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> It was held ... at Hood's Bush, a curious patch of native bush ... on the south side of the Whitecliffs bridge. (1972 -- ,Letter from James,143)

>  <b>BELL</b> If you follow the Waikato river, .../ There're waterfalls and native bush, breathtaking scenery;/ (1974 -- ,Ballads of a Racegoer,13)

>  <b>NEAVE</b> We were delighted with the background of native bush, and glad to ... listen to the lovely bird calls, which were strange to our ears, accustomed to ... absence of native birds in our district. (1980 -- ,Land of Munros, The,47)

>  <b>MANTELL</b> Native bush grew above them on the right, dark green, ... (1980 -- ,Murder and Chips,18)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> Thus plants which are the bane of farmers can be beneficial guardians of regenerating native bush. (1981 -- ,Paparoas Guide,23)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> George drove us up into the foothills, which open out into unexpectedly wide rolling tussocklands, with good stands of native bush in the gullies. (1982 -- ,Historic Sheep Stations NI,5)

>  <b>KIDMAN</b> There were many trees and bush around about and here and there a glimpse of woody-looking houses, stylish modern set-pieces for good placement amongst native bush. (1982 -- ,Mrs Dixon and Friend,131)

>  <b>SHERWOOD</b> She ... saw now why the early settlers had disliked the native bush, calling it gloomy ... (1985 -- ,Botanist at Bay,69)

>  <b>McGILL</b> The rail wound ... round the river, and presented a comprehensive picture of native bush. (1985 -- ,G'day Country,33)

>  <b>FRAME</b> At last we entered the Hauraki Gulf sailing slowly past the Bays with their unexpectedly colourful houses ... set against ... the darker green where stands of native bush remained. (1985 -- ,Envoy from Mirror City,160)

>  Chatham Island Warbler Gerygone albofrontata GRAY, 1844 ... The Chatham Island warbler has not adapted to human settlement and lives only in native bush. (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,279)

>  native bush NZ woods or forests composed of indigenous trees and shrubs. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,500) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 324]

>  Whoever burnt off the original native bush must have left them there, ... (Aug 29, 1987 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),45) [NOTES: Davin]

>  The regenerating native bush ... has greatly diminished. (1988 -- ,Through the Looking Glass,154) [NOTES: Kirk]

>  <b>SEWELL</b> After surveying our Estates rode home with Mr Raven through the Native Bush - not so picturesque by half as the Wellington Woods, but striking. (1853 -- ,Journal,1/429,1980 ed.)

>  <b>FRAME</b> Yes, New Zealand trees. And there's native bush along the east of Gillespie Street, towards the mountains. (1988 -- ,Carpathians, The,23)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) forest composed entirely of native vegetation, with mixed species specific to locality [ not just manuka, ponga and ferns.]

>  <b>LANDS & SURVEY</b> The last name 'Bush-sickness' persisted long after it was known that the native bush had really nothing to do with the disease. (1975 -- ,Prosperity from Pumice Land,7)

>  Fresh water stream flows through entire property. Areas of native bush retained. (Feb 1993 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,53)

>  The contour ranges from rolling to hilly with some steep scrub reverted sidlings merging into native bush. (Jun 15, 1994 -- ,NZ Farmer,44)

>  10ha land plus native bush, pony paddock. (Jul 9, 2000 -- ,First Choice for Real Estate,2 37)

>  Approx 170 ha in grass, 80 ha native bush, 50 ha scrub and pongas. (Feb 9, 2002 -- ,Taranaki Daily News,36)

>  .. near new sheep and cattle yards and loading race. Nice patch of native bush, good track access. (Mar 30, 2002 -- ,Taranaki Daily News,44)



## native cabbage
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> My party made an oven in which they place a large quantity of native cabbage which grows there [near Mangawhero] very plentifully. What a blessing did Cook confer on the country by thus sowing a little seed. (Nov 3, 1852 -- ,Journal,VIII/50)

>  <b>WEKEY</b> There is the native cabbage, a succulent plant, freely growing, and used by the Maories as a substitute for the last-named vegetable. (1862 -- ,Otago As It Is,45)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The natives ... caught a rat, cut some blubber from a stranded whale, and added a few pieces of a cuttlefish which had been left on the beach ...; these well mixed with a few potatoes, some wild native cabbage, and above all the optimum condimentum added, made a most sumptuous feast. (1898 -- ,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,54)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Native Cabbage </i>... The <i>Nasturtium palustre</i>, De C., <i>N.O. Cruciferae</i>, is so called, but in spite of its name it is not endemic in Australia. In New Zealand, the name is sometimes applied to the <i>Maori Cabbage. </i> (1898 -- ,Austral English,315)

>  <i>cabbage n.</i>-native cabbage collective n. cabbage plants that have become wild in New Zealand; the Maori cabbage. (1929 -- ,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,3)



## native celery
 
 <i>n.</i> aka Maori or wild celery, Apium prostratum

>  Here in New Zealand we also have a native celery, tutaekoau or Apium prostatum, a low creeping plant growing on beaches in gravel, rocks or mud. (Dec 24, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,C9)

>  [Cook] put the native celery and scurvy grass on the menu for officers only. (Jun 2004 -- ,North & South,32)

>  I'll be looking out for native celery when I next go walking along the coast. This creeping plant, tutae koau (<i>Apium prostratum</i>) is found throughout the country, accoding to Andrew Crowe, the author of <i>A Field Guide to the Native Edible Plants of New Zealand</i>... (May 29, 2004 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),41)

## native country
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land that has native grass, shrubs or bush growing on it.

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> A large area of native country taken over by the Crown was released for ballot. (1962 -- ,Life's Roundabout, A,39)

>  We aim .. stock on the native hill country .. requiring less time to spot and snowrake. (May 19, 1993 -- ,NZ Farmer,6 15)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> native or native country: pasture which has never been under the plough. Even if it has been oversown with exotics amongst the indigenous grasses it is till termed native. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,55)

>  Hilly native country is an ideal zone for production of fine and super fine merino wools. ..the bulk of the hilly native country is not stocked all summer. (Sep 1985 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,32)

>  Glenaray farms approximately 4,000 ha of oversown and top-dressed hill country and 60,000 ha of semi-developed native high country. (Jul 14, 2001 -- ,Dominion,52)

## Native Department
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  The mode in which the Native Departmenthas been administered in the past hasnot been such as to inculcate habits ofindustry, and care, and exactitude in itsofficers. (1909 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CXLVIII/1334)

>  Hence the need of a permanent NativeDepartment, with the Governor at itshead, not responsible to the Assembly,but bound to supply it with everyinformation sought for. (Jul 5, 1858 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,1856-1858 / 581)

>  Changes in the personnel of the NativeDepartment have been much more rapid thanMinisterial changes have hotherto provedin New Zealand. (1858 -- ,AJHR,E-No. 1/11)

>  He [the Governor] has no knowledge oftheir language, and hence is entirely inthe hands of the officials of the NativeDepartment. (Aug 3, 1860 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,1858-1860 / 190)

>  The activities of the Native Department have reached the stage when the pakeha, who had been strenuous in his advocacy of the proper utilisation of native lands, was due to look for weaknesses ... (Sep 17, 1932 -- ,Na To Hoa Aroha,3/20,1988 ed.) [NOTES: 1932 Sep 17 in 1988 Na To Hoa Aroha (Ngata-Buck Correspondence) 3/20 (Ngata)]

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> We talk of `taihoa' in connection with our Native Department. The real home of unadulterated`taihoa' is Italy ... (1912 -- ,Impressions of a NZ Pastoralist on Tour,162)

>  The activities of the Native Department have reached the stage when the pakeha, who had been strenuous in his advocacy of the proper utilisation of native lands, was due to look forweaknesses ... <i>Ibid.</i> 3/204 (Buck) I am pleased to hear that a change for the better has taken in the head office of the Native Department. The bureaucratic rule by civil servants who have no sympathy or knowledge of the native people that the department is supposed to administer ... (Sep 17, 1932 -- ,Na To Hoa Aroha,3/20,1988 ed.) [NOTES: Ngata]

>  This [Ruatoki] valley is Native land and is being farmed by the Maori owners with the assistance and supervision of the Native Department. It is only one of the many Native-land-development schemes scattered throughout New Zealand, ... (1948 -- ,Our Own Country,49)



## Native difficulty
 
 <i>n.</i> = Maori problem

>  <b>MANING</b> Talk to me of the `native difficulty' - pooh! I think it was in 1822 that an old friend of mine bought, at Kawhia, a woman who was just [212] going to be baked. (1863 -- ,Old New Zealand,(xiv) 211)

>  <b>WILLIAM MORGAN</b> Were this native difficulty settled, the country would go ahead with amazing stride, .... (May 27, 1863 -- ,Journal,41,1963 ed.)

>  <b>VOGEL (ed.)</b> native difficulty, native question (1875 -- ,Official Handbook of NZ,65)

>  My own impression with regard to the Native difficulties in the past is that there has scarcely been a difficulty - there has scarcely been a single war in this colony - in which the Europeans have not been more to blame than the Natives. (Aug 16, 1881 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,39/550) [NOTES: John Ballance]

>  <b>LAMBERT</b> When Dr. F.F. Ormond purchased the Orangitirohia block on the north side of the river, not allthe Natives had been satisfactorily dealt with, and a `Native difficulty' of the first grade soon arose. (1936 -- ,Pioneering Reminiscences of Old Wairoa,20)

## native dog
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McNAB</b> Q. Are their any quadrupeds in New Zealand, wild or tame? A. None but the native dog that resembles that of New Holland, tho' in New Zealand it is capable of being domesticated and they are trained to catch pigs. (1908 -- ,Historical Records,1/536) [NOTES: 1821 May Evidence of Ensign McCrae before Commissioner Bigge in 1908 McNab Historical Records 1/536]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> ... the native dogs are great obstacles to their [sc. poultry] increase for as their owners only feed them on vegetables it is natural that they will ... make a banquet on a fowl. (May 10, 1839 -- ,Journal,2/131)

>  <b>HAAST</b> The old native dog (kuri) seems to be extinct. (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,135)

>  <b>HAY</b> Pigeons, nestors, parrots, rail, kiwi, swamp-fowl, water-fowl, owls, parson-birds, all these and more were eaten; while the native dogs and rats were held to be great dainties. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/151)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Crozet described native dogs as looking like domesticated foxes, ... (1904 -- ,Maori Race, The,167)

>  <b>BEST</b> The native dog, now extinct, is said to have been introduced by old-time Polynesian voyagers from the isles of Polynesia. It was sometimes slain for food. The native rat was quite an important article of food. (1925 -- ,Tuhoe: The Children of the Mist,11)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> ... I placed it [sc. soft whalebone] on the roof of a native house; but on the following morning I had the mortification to find that the rats and native dogs ... had eaten all the softer parts ... <i>Ibid.</i> 1/362 [The] native rat still lingers here and there [in the central North Island], although it has disappeared on the coast; the native dog has become a mongrel. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/50)

>  And yet, strange to say, the numerous half-starved native dogs never think of destroying them. (1841 -- ,Establishment of New Plymouth Settlement) [NOTES: 1841 Establishment of New Plymouth Colony in 1940 Establishment of New Plymouth 76]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> What are called native dogs by the Middle Island shepherds are English animals, free, not wild. That the true native dog was introduced into New Zealand is proved by traditions, by the Maori term for dog being a true Polynesian word. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,1/22)

>  <b>S. PERCY SMITH</b> At 2 p.m. we got into a pretty little clearing, where our dogs were driven in by some native dogs. It being such an unusual thing for native dogs to show so much pluck, we imagined there might be some Hauhaus about, so we left the camp. (Feb 11, 1867 -- ,Taranaki Journal) [NOTES: >1867 Feb 11 S. Percy Smith Taranaki Journal [=?Maori dog, i.e. a dog belonging to a Maori] in 1922 Cowan NZ Wars 2/533]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> It [Central Otago] was a silent land, voiceless save for ... the long-drawn, eerie howl of a wild native dog. (1939 -- ,First White Boy Born in Otago,118)



## native duck
 
 <i>n.</i> -

>  There are eleven species of Native Ducks belonging to nine genera, all found elsewhere, except two--the little Flightless Duck of the Auckland Islands (genus <i>Nesonetta</i>) and the Blue Mountain Duck (<i>Hymenolaemus</i>). Among the most interesting of the non-endemic forms, are the Paradise Duck or Sheldrake (<i>Casarca variegata</i>), the Brown Duck (<i>Anas chlorotis</i>), the Shoveller or Spoonbill duck (<i>Rhynchaspis variegata</i>), and the Scaup or Black Teal (<i>Fuligula Novae-Zealandiae</i>). (1889 -- ,Catalogue NZ Exhibition,117) [NOTES: [Morris]]

## native flax
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Native flax. (1785 -- ,Cook's Third Voyage)

>  <b>CAPT. RAVEN</b> The flax grows here [in Dusky Sound] in great abundance, from which our people made fishing-lines and kellick-ropes. (Nov 19, 1793 -- ,Letter) [NOTES: 1793 Nov. 19 Capt. Raven to Lieut-Govenor King in 1908 McNab Historical Records 1/179]

>  <b>SAVAGE</b> The dress of the natives consists in a mat - finely woven of the native flax, and its glossy appearance, with a fringe all round, renders it by no means an inelegant sort of mantle. <i>Ibid.</i> 56 Small baskets made of the green native flax. (1807 -- ,Some Account of NZ,50)

>  New Zealand must be always considered as the great emporium of the South Seas, from its local situation, its safe harbours, its navigable rivers, its fine timber for ship building, its rosin, native flax, etc., etc., specimens of which I intend sending to the Society by this conveyance. (Sep 22, 1814 -- ,Letters & Journals of Samuel Marsden,133,1932 ed.)

>  (And) (Jan 1856 -- ,Edinburgh New Philosophical Journal) [NOTES: Tancred Notes on the Natural History of ... Canterbury]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Flax, Native, n. The European flax is <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>, N.O. <i>Linea</i>. There is a species in Australia, <i>Linum marginale</i>, Cunn., N.O. <i>Linacea</i>, called Native Flax, In New Zealand, the Phormium is called <i>Native Flax</i>. See next word [<i>Flax, New Zealand</i>]. (1898 -- ,Austral English,147)

>  <b>BOYD</b> (And) <i>Phormium tenax</i> (1900 -- ,Our Stolen Summer)

>  (And) <i>Phormium tenax</i> (1921 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology)

>  (And) <i>Phormium tenax</i> (1923 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology)

>  <b>A.BATHGATE</b> ... we passed through an area where grew the most luxuriant native flax I have ever seen. (1925 -- ,Random Recollections,19)

>  <i>native flax n 2</i> : NEW ZEALAND FLAX (1961 -- ,Merriam-Webster Third International,1506)

>  <b>GRUNDY</b> Our back yard rose steeply from the house to the fence line, where native flax had been planted as a wind break. (1968 -- ,Who'd Marry a Doctor?,30)

>  In all, New Zealand has over 15 800 ha of self-sown native flax, ... (1974 -- ,NZ Agriculture,153)

>  <b>ASKEW</b> The kits are made of native flax. They have two handles or loops to lift them by. They are the same in shape as those used by the carpenters, and some are as well made. (1857 -- ,Voyage to Australia & NZ,347)

>  <b>NEAVE</b> The [Otematata homestead] building [in 1858] consisted of the usual cob and clay, ... and thatched with cutty grass tied on with native flax which had been boiled to make it tough. (1980 -- ,Land of Munros, The,65) [NOTES: 1917 James Strachan Experiences (Hocken L.) in 1980 Neave The Land of Munros 65]

>  native flax n. plant yielding fibre used by aborigines for making fishing nets. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  native flax n. plant yielding fibre used by abos. for making fishing nets. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)



## native forest
 
 <i>n.</i> [[NB: never native woods]]

>  <b>POLACK</b> (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),1/347)

>  The southern portion of the county was originally covered with native forest, ... (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,274)

>  The Island is much as it was but Wharekauri [his station] is returning to its native forest. (Jan 24, 1915 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,414,1950 ed.)

>  <b>COWAN</b> Here and there along the rim of the valley were patches of native forest. (1922 -- ,NZ Wars, The,1/270)

>  <b>SOLJAK</b> These exotic forest resources [insignis pine, Douglas fir, etc.] are being expanded to ... facilitate preservation of the native forests, which the early settlers wastefully reduced ... (1946 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),11)

>  <b>HAWES</b> ...suddenly.. he could see forest. Native, uncut. You can tell real native forest, it sort of sticks out like pop-up books, as though it's in 3D - or 4D - because it has another dimension from the flatness of other bush. (2000 -- ,Dream of Nikau Jam, The,168)
