## native frog
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Leiopelma Archeyi</i>

>  According to information supplied ... the native frog is confined to the upper slopes of Mount Moehau, near the north end of the Hauraki Peninsula, and lives in the moss there so abundant.... A second species of New Zealand native frog was recently discovered on Stephen Island (see ...[TrNZI 1919 51/447-449]). (Jan 1921 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,3/308) [NOTES: (Morgan)]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Native Frog - peketua, pepeke, <i>Leiopelma </i> (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12)

>  <b>MCALLISTER</b> Halfway up, [we] found our first native frog, <i>Leiopelma Archeyi</i> - a timid brown and green creature less than 2 inches long. (1976 -- ,Old Taranaki,183) [NOTES: SIR GILBERT ARCHEY [1890-1974] was Director Auckland Institute & Museum to 1964]



## native grass
 
 <i>n.</i> [often in plural] (See [cf. English grass(es)](../E#english-grass(es)))

>  <b>YATE</b> The native grasses flourish throughout the year ... All English grasses flourish well; but the white clover never seeds; ... (1835 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),75)

>  <b>POWER</b> The only plan will be to lease sheep and cattle runs, at a cheap rate for a long term of years, on much the same system as in New South Wales with the squatters; with this difference, that the rate ought to be even cheaper in this country, as there are scarcely any native grasses, and the whole face of the country is fern and swamp, which is improved each year by the introduction of stock, - ... (1849 -- ,Sketches in NZ,113)

>  <b>EARP</b> `A few words on the native grasses will not be out of place. `The grasses of the Wairarapa ... are very numerous, and if cultivated, would be fully equal to English grasses: ... There is abundance of native rye-grass, and the sheep are very fond of a species of catch-grass ... ` (1853 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),125) [NOTES: 1853 Allom letter in 1853 Earp NZ]

>  <b>MACKIE</b> In his paddocks he has several of the english grasses, they are found to be very advantageous here, being hardier than the native grasses, they produce more herbage through the winter. (May 2, 1853 -- ,Traveller under Concern,104,1973 ed.)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> I may as well here correct an error, which I had been under ... - native grass cannot be mown. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,iv/47) [NOTES: 1860 Feb 13 in 1863 Butler First Year]

>  Native grass is a good as oates and the open air in this climate keeps them [sc. horses] as harde as nails. (Nov 1, 1865 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,203,1950 ed.)

>  Mr. Holmes' sheep are running on native grass and the above increase has been obtained at one lambing. (1914 -- ,History of North Otago,177,1978 ed.) [NOTES: 1865 Dec 1 in 1914 History of North Otago (1978)]

>  <b>BARKER</b> Nothing seems [25] so wonderful to me as the utter treelessness of the vast Canterbury plains; occasionally you pass a few Ti-ti palms (ordinarily called cabbage-trees), or a large prickly bush which goes by the name of `wild Irishman,' but for miles and miles you see nothing but ... undulating downs of yellow tussocks, the tall native grass. (1865 -- ,Station Life in NZ,24)

>  To facilitate the collection of such information, they prepared a printed list of such native grasses as appeared to them most valuable (33 in number), ... <i>Ibid.</i> 298 Several of the native-grasses, as Microloena stipoides, Danthonia semi-annularis, Agrostis oemula, etc., maintain their ground against, and unite with, several of the introduced kinds in the formation of natural pasture in many places in this province. (1872 -- ,TrNZI,4/292) [NOTES: Report on Grasses]

>  <b>BLAIR</b> In the olden times it was an accepted theory in Otago, that ... the milk of cows fed on native grass would not make butter; ... (1884 -- ,Industries of NZ,59)

>  In the Middle Island a large area is covered with native grasses. (1892 -- ,NZ Official Handbook,1)

>  The extent of land in sown grasses was, in November, 1899, no less than 2,320,505 acres, while 2,042,789 acres were in tussock or native grass. (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,11)

>  Then on to see J.J.Fougere where I got a sod of buffalo grass and another sod of native grass ... (Apr 17, 1909 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,447,1950 ed.)

>  <b>MACDONALD</b> In some districts the seeds of native grasses, such as danthonias, would be more suitable for sowing; ... (1920 -- ,Australian & NZ Sheep Farming,122)

>  <b>PERRY</b> Nearly all the ordinary commercial flocks in New Zealand started from the Merino, but change of pasture from Native to English grasses ... has driven the Merino back to the high country. (1922 -- ,Sheep Farming in NZ,50)

>  <b>LEE</b> In those days the rolling hill-tops were still native grass and virgin plume tussock, good natural pasturage long since ploughed under and replaced with imported grasses. (1934 -- ,Children of the Poor,28,1949 ed.)

>  In the light of these conditions, and bearing in mind that native grasses are the dominant crop on uncultivated areas, the dependence of New Zealand on a pasture economy can be readily understood. (1936 -- ,Agricultural Organization in NZ,20)

>  <b>AYSON</b> All the country was very rough, with scrub, fern and tutu mixed with native grass, especially long green almond grass, which made splendid cattle feed. (1937 -- ,Thomas,101)

>  <b>HYDE</b> But the native grass stands back,/ Uncouth and alone. (1939 -- ,Selected Poems,85,1984 ed.) [NOTES: c1938-1939]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> From this onward [in Central Otago c1860] two native grasses, known to the early stockmen as barley grass and oat grass respectively from a chance resemblance to those two cereals, were predominant ... (1939 -- ,First White Boy Born in Otago,119)

>  The half-bred, three-quarter bred, and Romney sheep are more likely to overgraze areas of native grasses ... (1941 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,22/22A)

>  <b>LEVY</b> The early settlers of a century ago introduced to New Zealand various `English' grasses and clovers to supplement and later to replace the native grasses on the average-to-superior soil types. (1951 -- ,Grasslands of NZ,166,1970 ed.)

>  <b>41</b> The floor and gently sloping sides were verdant with English and native grasses from the broad strath at our feet right up to where the cleared country narrowed and ran out in the higher ground of the bush range at the head. (1953 -- ,Golden Bush, The,SUTHERLAND,1963 ed.)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> Unless you walk over this country [Central Otago] you'll miss the native grasses, like danthonia and the blue tussock. To a Merino, one mouthful of this hard feed [13] is worth 10 of soft greenfeed. (1982 -- ,Historic Sheep Stations NI,12)

>  <b>BOYD</b> The Maori ran pigs in the swamp and grazed horses, sheep and cattle on the few rough patches of native grass on the side of the old track from Te Awa O Te Atua to Paki Paki and on the margins of the two creeks. (1984 -- ,City of the Plains,4)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> [A.=Aust.; T.=Tasmania] -->[Only species marked N.Z. selected from the Morris entry and given below: A. = Aust.; T. = Tasmania.] -->Mr John Buchanan ... has prepared ... a `Manual of the Indigenous Grasses of New Zealand', which enumerates eighty species, many of them unnamed in English, and many of them common also to Australia and Tasmania. ...3 (1898 -- ,Austral English,167) [NOTES: Morris's list is incorporated into grass]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) indigenous grass species, principally danthonia and tussocks.

>  <b>BARKER</b> Tussocks, the tall native grass, has the colour and appearance of hay. (1869 -- ,Station Life in NZ,25) [NOTES: Station Life in NZ (1874)]

>  The pakihi seems to need further capital fertiliser after the fourth year, otherwise it starts reverting to yorkshire fog, lotus, native grass and fine-stemmed pakihi rushes. (Mar 1987 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,26)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> And the carrying capacity of all the native grass ... (1889 -- ,Colonial Couplets,2)

>  In general hill country carrying native grasses is safe, but soem hill paddocks do produce the disease and these should be avoided. (Mar 15, 1943 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,146)

>  The programme of development is for around 100 to 150 acres of native grass and more predominantly manuka scrub ... (Nov 1963 -- ,NZ Farmer,16)

## Native land
 
 <i>n.</i> [often in plural]

>  Of the Native lands in this district, a very considerable portion has been leased to Europeans, but there still remains in the hands of the Maoris a valuable estate, ... (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,11)

>  This [Ruatoki] valley is Native land and is being farmed by the Maori owners with the assistance and supervision of the Native Department. It is only one of the many Native-land-development schemes scattered throughout New Zealand, ... (1948 -- ,Our Own Country,49) [NOTES: from Korero, AEWS magazine]

>  The Government has it in its power to perpetrate the grossest favouritism by allowing one man to acquire native lands, while it prevents another. (Apr 19, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,12)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> When he left school he got a job in the Native Lands Department - a swanky white- collar job like the Pakehas admire. (1938 -- ,Brown Man's Burden,18)

>  Take the case of the New Zealand Native Land Settlement Company. (Aug 27, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)

>  For the purpose of rendering any Nativeland or any land owned by Natives fit forsettlement, the Native Land SettlementBoard (formerly the Native Minister) maydecide to apply the provisions of section522 to that land. (1934 -- ,AJHR,G-11/32)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) land owned by Maori.

>  <b>WILSON</b> There were two classes of offenders - those who leased land from the natives and paid them rent, and those who allowed their sheep and cattle to graze on native land and refused to pay rent for such grazing. (1976 -- ,History of Hawke's Bay,212)

>  [union platform] That native lands which benefit from the expenditure of special and general rates shall be placed upon the same footing as European lands so situated. (Mar 9, 1907 -- ,Farmers' Union Advocate,4)

>  With the idea of giving information to those requiring land, there will be published in this column full particulars of all Crown, public, and native lands thrown open for settlement. (Aug 13, 1908 -- ,Weekly News,47)

>  He exhibited a map showing the large tracts of native lands that were locked up in the Kawhia County. (Jun 3, 1909 -- ,Weekly News,21)

>  .. signs of a modest prosperity are forthcoming in the much-improved rate collection on European and native lands alike. (May 3, 1945 -- ,NZ Farmer,6)



## Native Land Court
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  ... an able an popular man, well known in the Native Land Courts ... (1895 -- ,TrNZI,27/598)

>  The Native Land Courts, in the eyes of the present generation of colonists, are chiefly interesting as the means of investigating Maori titles to land, and as the agency for peacefully transferring, by consent of the Maori proprietors, these lands to European owners. (1876 -- ,TrNZI,8/422) [NOTES: J.C. Firth]

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> A debt of this nature contracted some twelve generations ago was recently recognized by the Native land court. (1941 -- ,Numismatic History of NZ,14)

>  The Native Land Court of New Zealand (hereinafter called the court) shall be a Court of Record for the investigation of the titles of persons to Native lands and to hereditaments of which the Native owner shall have died intestate and for the other purposes hereinafter set forth. (1865 -- ,NZ Statutes,29 Vict. 71/264)

>  The Native Land Court of New Zealand (hereinafter called "the Court) shall be a Court of Record for the investigation of the titles of persons to Native land according to Native customs and usage, and for the other purposes hereinafter set forth. (1873 -- ,NZ Statutes,37 Vict. 56/239)

>  A judge of the Native Land Court may suspend an interpreter. (1909 -- ,NZ Statutes,9 Edw. VII 15/256)

>  There shall continue to be a Court of record called as heretofore the Maori Land Court. (1931 -- ,NZ Statutes,22 Geo.V. 31/166)

>  The cumbersome machinery of the Native Land Court had to be brought to bear on the several blocks... the Natives being fleeced by the lawyers, interpreters, Pakeha-Maoris, et <i>hoc genus omne</i>. on the one hand, and by the publicans on the other. (Jan 23, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,7)

>  Any European is considered more likely to do justice in the Native Land Court than a native expert, however learned in the lore of his race. (Jun 25, 1904 -- ,NZ Observer,23)

>  We cannot believe that either the Native Land Court or the Native Minister, any Native Minister, would not be particularly careful before approving an application. (Jan 11, 1913 -- ,NZ Observer,3)

>  Maori freehold land is essentially land which the Native (or since 1945 Maori) Land Court has converted from customary Maori tenure to English freehold tenure and has not been alienated by its Maori possessors. There are a good many specialised terms in this field such as ahiwhenua trust and putea trust. (Jan 1999 -- ,NZWords,2)

>  The practice of the Native Lands Court lately is to endorse on the back of the memorial of ownership the names of all the persons having an interest in the block. (Apr 5, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,12)

>  English Law Courts are.. pantomimes in briskness compared with a Native Lands Court. (Sep 3, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,21)

>  A peculiar position was mentioned at a sitting of the Native Land Court at Greymouth. (Feb 5, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,1)

>  Saw Rolleston who is holding a Native Land Court [at Christchurch]. (Apr 22, 1868 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,220,1950 ed.)

## native lark
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [pihoihoi](../P#pihoihoi))

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Native Lark, dipping and rising. (1940 -- ,Te Waimate,235,1954 ed.)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> `Swampies' (swamp hens) and paradise and grey ducks were abundant in the waterlogged valley, while the native lark seemed ubiquitous. (1947 -- ,Pioneer Recollections,5,1956 ed.)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Pihoi (<i>Anthus N.Z </i>) is the so-called native `lark.' It is a ground pipit, and may often be seen fluttering and chirping about a bush road. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/224)

>  <b>MANNERING</b> Stilts were common on the river - blackbill gulls, black-backed gulls, shags, dotterel, native larks (pipits). (1943 -- ,Eighty Years,22) [NOTES: [In Canterbury in the 1870's]]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> The New Zealand pipit, sometimes inaccurately called ground lark or native lark, is a local race (<i>Anthus novaeseelandiae </i>) of a widely distributed species ... (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/776)

>  <i> Pipit Anthus novaeseelandiae </i> (GMELIN, 1789) ... <i> New Zealand Pipit A.n. novaeseelandiae </i> (GMELIN, 1789). <i> Auckland Islands Pipit A.n. aucklandicus </i> GRAY, 1862. <i> Antipodes Pipit A.n. steindachneri </i> REISCHEK, 1889. ... OTHER NAMES: <i> Pihoihoi, groundlark, native lark.</i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,270)

>  The eggs of the native lark.. are so marked as to be well concealed in the tussock and fern where they are laid. (Dec 11, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,41)

>  The native lark is a pipit. (Mar 18, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,70)

## Native Minister
 
 <i>n.</i> [A real doozy this one, as the Yanks say. Lots of confusion, popular use masquerading as official and confused official use; am working my way through but have to go back to Turnbull where a helpful young lady produced as I was leaving the NZ Almanacks which seem to be the ONLY source of official titles. Will chase them up further. People in Hansard use very loose terminology and there is no official listing of Ministers in Hansard. The following is my best effort from the Wellington Almanack (and others): 1822-1850 No record. 1851 Commissioner for acquiring the Cession of Lands from the Natives: - Donald McLean. 1851 Native Secretary H. Tacy Kemp GBP200 per annum [in addition to above]. 1852 Native Secretary [from Estimates]. 1853 Native Secretary Native Secretary's Department [from Estimates]. 1855 Native Secretary's Department [from Estimates]. 1857 Native Secretary - D. McLean. 1858 AJHR E-No.1/17 Donald McLean, Native Secretary, writes from Native Secretary's Office, 28 June 1858. 1860 Native Secretary's Department. 1861 Minister for Native Affairs - Honourable C.W. Richmond Native Secretary's Office. 1862 Minister for Native Affairs - F.D. Bell (Acting) Native Secretary's Department OR Native Department. 1863 Native Minister - F.D. Bell Native Land Purchase Department - Crown Commissioner .. D.McLean Native Secretary's Department - Native Secretary D. McLean NOTE; This is the first time the big three Ministers are joined by a new portfolio. 1864 Minister for Native Affairs - Honourable William Fox Native Secretary's Department. 1866 Native Minister - Honorable A.H. Russell Native Department - Minister for Native Affairs - Honorable A.H. Russell. 1867 Native Minister - Honorable J.C. Richmond Native Department - Minister for Native Affairs. 1868 Native Department - Under Secretary W. Rolleston. 1870 Ministry has now become Cabinet Donald McLean, Minister for Colonial Defence and Native Affairs. 1873 Honorable Donald McLean, Native Minister. 1878 Native Minister & Minister of Justice. 1879 Native and Defence Office Native Minister. 1880 Honorable John Boyce, Minister for Native Affairs Native Office. 1885 Native and Defence Minister Native Office. 1888 Minister of Public Works and Native Affairs. I gave up about here. Basically, you can take your pick.]

## native mistletoe
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Five native mistletoe species are among more than 400 plants species identified as being at risk of extinction. (May 2004 -- ,Footnotes,No. 21/8)

>  The [Greytown] motor camp is one of the few places you can still find native mistletoe. (Dec 17, 2003 -- ,Capital Times,6)

>  The magnificent beech forests of the South Island, now aflame with the scarlet flower of the native mistletoe. (Jan 26, 1922 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,19)

>  <b>BULLER</b> It is placed on the lateral fork of totara, supported underneath by an epiphytic growth of native mistletoe (<i>Loranthus micranthus</i>). (1888 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,1/233)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> <i>V[iscum] Lindsayi </i>[Oliver] is one of the `Native Mistletoes' of the settler: an extremely insignificant one ... when compared with the handsome Otago species of <i>Loranthus,</i> whose showy scarlet flowers alone are sometimes ... two inches long ... (1868 -- ,Contributions to NZ Botany,52)

>  One [new plant] is a native mistletoe with large red flowers.. (Feb 5, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,6)

>  On the whole the beech forest is dark and gloomy, but here and there it may be lighted up by a tuft of scarlet or yellow native mistletoe [fn Species of <i>Loranthus.</i>], or in spring by the gay garlands of white clematis; while in the more open places grows the beautiful ribbon-wood [fn <i>Gaya ribifolia</i>, slightly different from the more western <i>Gaya lyallii</i>.], with a profusion of white flowers resembling pear-blossom. (1900 -- ,Canterbury Old & New,186)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> `From this point back to Moawhango the track, for some miles, leads through a lovely piece of bush where again native mistletoe rautawhiri [=P. tenuifolium] and other lovely shrubs were in bloom.' (1959 -- ,Tales of the Mails,49) [NOTES: c1880s an anonymous tourist in 1959 L. Masters Tales of the Mails 49]

>  New Zealand has eight native species, seven of them endemic and one found also on Norfolk Island. A ninth species, also endemic, was last seen in the 1950s and is presumed extinct. (Sep 2002 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 5/80)

## native oven
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [Maori oven](../M#maori-oven),[umu](../U#umu))

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> A'ngi; Name of a certain tree; a native oven; ... (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,134) [NOTES: [|prec.= acute, '= stress]]

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> Coppre [kopa] Mourie, or the Native Ovens are described I rather think in Captain Cookes Voyages. They are done this way. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),44,1963 ed.)

>  <b>POLACK</b> The only method of cooking formerly in use [by the New Zealanders] was by the kapa [a's-acute] or angi maori, the native oven, already described. (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),1/399)

>  <b>BIDWILL</b> ... and when I visited it, I saw all the native ovens (copper mowries, according to English pronounciation) in which the cooking had been performed, and a portion of the entrails, etc., were strewed about. (1841 -- ,Rambles in NZ,30,1952 ed.)

>  <b>SELWYN</b> A small native village is here [at Ohinemutu], with the usual appurtenances of a native steam kitchen at the hot springs; namely, hot plates, made of large slabs of stone, laid over boiling water ... steam hanghis, or native ovens, always in readiness ... A native swing completes the equipment of this fashionable watering place ... (1845 -- ,New Zealand (Selwyn),Part II (Church in the Colonies VII) 32) [NOTES: 1843 Oct 30 Selwyn Journal in 1845 NZ Part II (Church in the Colonies VII)]

>  <b>ANGAS</b> On one occasion, when on a visit to one of the missionaries at Waimate, a fine gander attracted his [sc. Waka Nene's] attention, and he secretly ordered it to be seized, and prepared for his dinner in a native oven; ... <i>Ibid.</i> 2/125 ... Newman [was] busily engaged in cooking ... the pig, in a native oven in the ground between hot stones. (1847 -- ,Savage Life,1/298)

>  <b>POWER</b> The friendly natives ... were busy ... cleaning their arms, singing `hakas', and making speeches, while waiting till the old women opened the native ovens, which, well filled with pork and kumeras, sent up an appetising steam in all directions. (1849 -- ,Sketches in NZ) [NOTES: 1849 Power Sketches in NZ 29 Aug 24th [1846].-]

>  <b>SHORTLAND</b> KAURU, the root of the Ti or Whanake after it has been baked in the native oven. (1851 -- ,Southern Districts of NZ,308) [NOTES: Vocab]

>  <b>MACKIE</b> Two women were preparing a native oven as we passed and we stopped to watch the process. (Apr 5, 1853 -- ,Traveller under Concern,97,1973 ed.)

>  <b>SWAINSON</b> Two long lines of native ovens mark the spot where the bodies were cooked; and a smaller oven with a wreath around its edge ... (1853 -- ,Auckland,145)

>  <b>MOSER</b> I am regaled with some potatoes and a bit of fish, admirably cooked in a native oven; ... (1863 -- ,Mahoe Leaves,6)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Hapii, n. native oven or cooking pit; haangi. <i>Ibid.</i> 59 kopa ... 2. ... native oven. (1871 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,19,3rd ed. ed.)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Hangi, n. native oven. <i>Ibid.</i> 21 Hapi, n. native oven or cooking pit; haangi. (1892 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,20)

>  It ... is generally known among European settlers as the `native oven', though the term `steaming-pit` would be a more exact description. (1903 -- ,TrNZI,35/23)

>  <b>GORST</b> (1908 -- ,New Zealand Revisited,108)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Hangi, n. 1. Native oven, consisting of a circular hole in the ground, in which the food was cooked by heated stones. 2. Contents of the oven. (1957 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,34)

## native parrot

>  One of their pastimes was to find a nest of native parrots and to put a large cage over it, with netting so that the mother bird could feed the young through the holes. (Apr 2004 -- ,NZ Memories,47/52)

>  <b>1978</b> The first year after it had been completed the roof was entirely torn off by an invading army of kakas and native parrots, which came there in thousands just as the paraquets have done in more recent times. (1914 -- ,History of North Otago,32) [NOTES: 1894 Oamaru Mail in 1914 History of North Otago (1978) 32]

>  <b>HYDE</b> Through the forests the outlaws and the native parrots, the red-plumed kakas, had swept in varying degrees of magnificence. (1936 -- ,Passport to Hell,31)

## native pepper
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  More recently,. He launched tea made from kawakawa (native pepper) and horopito, - both labelled as 'organically grown in nuclear-free New Zealand". (Apr 1991 -- ,NZ Geographic,116)

>  Almost if not quite alone among the peoples of the world, the Maori was a water-drinker, ignorant of alcohol in any form, and without artificial beverages of any description. The Rawakawa [sic] or native pepper, grew in every forest, but he made no "kava." (Apr 7, 1908 -- ,Evening Post,6)



## native pepper tree
 
 <i>n.</i> Kawakawa

>  <b>DAWSON</b> Kawakawa is sometimes called `native pepper tree' because of its hot tasting leaves, and it is in fact related to the true pepper plant of Indonesia. ... Horopito (<i>Pseudowintera axillaris</i>) with its dark green shiny leaves has also been termed `pepper tree' for the same reason, but it is not in fact related to the kawakawa. (1988 -- ,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,103)

## Native Protector
 
 <i>n.</i> an official appointed to look after the interests of Maori; a Protector of Aborigines.

>  It may be said that they are themselves possessed of sufficient funds for this purpose, in the tenth of land reserved for them; but it is worse than useless - it is cruel to talk of such a reserve, while at present much of it is not available, and very probably may not be so for a considerable time, in consequence of the difficult and therefore protracted nature of the surveys; and also from the absence of the Native Protector, as he is officially designated, who .. might by his presence here help to facilitate arrangements on behalf of the natives. (Jul 31, 1841 -- ,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2)

>  Supposing this intended with a view of giving full effect to the system of amalgamation, there should be no exceptional law for natives within the municipalities, Native Protectors, for example, are an exceptional class of officers, whose very appointment suggests the idea of a natural hostility between the colonists and the Natives; ... letters addressed to `Squire Clarke', the chief Protector of the Aborigines under Captain Hobson ... (1949 -- ,Selected Documents,15A) [NOTES: >1846 Jan 20 E.G. Wakefield to Gladstone in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 15A]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> At Kapiti, ... [Fitzroy] told the natives to be guided by `their true friends the Missionaries, the Native Protectors, and the `Government Officers,' - thus virtually advising suspicion ... towards the settlers. <i>Ibid.</i> 80 Among other useful ameliorations, [Grey] abolished the office of [81] Native Protector; ... (1848 -- ,Handbook for New Zealand, The,76)

>  The natives who are at present at work on the diggings are fast acquiring a knowledge of gold mining , and some of them are doing exceedingly well. I [Mr. Mackay, jnr.] have been requested by several of them to act as native protector. ... as the natives become more numerous, it will be essential to appoint a person for this purpose. (Jul 4, 1857 -- ,Lyttelton Times,5)

>  After receiving a good bullying from the Native Protector.. for swearing to my own goods, the native was fined double the value of the hat. (Sep 17, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)



## Native question
 
 <i>n.</i> = Maori problem (See [Native difficulty](../N#native-difficulty),[Native problem](../N#native-problem))

>  We are all happy in a prospect of a speedy settlement of the native question. (Nov 23, 1842 -- ,Letters from New Plymouth,70) [NOTES: (1842 Nov 23) 1843 (1968) Letters from New Plymouth 70]

>  <b>WOOD</b> The Company's agent promises roads, and the settlement of the native question, ... (1843 -- ,Twelve Months in Wellington,16)

>  <b>WILLIAM MORGAN</b> The native question is still absorbing the attention of the people of this colony. (Jul 5, 1863 -- ,Journal,42,1963 ed.) [NOTES: 1863 July 5 Journal William Morgan (1963) 42]

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> What is called the Native Question, presents greater difficulties. (1946 -- ,Selected Documents,18) [NOTES: 1846 May 26 Gladstone to G. Grey in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 18]

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> 1 The tenor of this [Ministers' memorandum on Native Affairs] would lead a person unacquainted with the past history and present condition of this Colony to suppose that now, forthe first time, the difficulties of the native question have been properly appreciated and successfully grappled with; (1946 -- ,Selected Documents,105) [NOTES: Oct 13 Memorandum by Native Secretary in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 105.]

>  The Native Question is growing into a subject of anxious consideration. (Jul 4, 1857 -- ,Lyttelton Times,5)

>  A frank ... discussion of their supposed grievances ... might bring the whole affair to an end, and place the country in a much better position as regards the `native question' than it has ever hitherto obtained. (1860 -- ,Maori King Movement,72)

>  We spent Three Million Pounds, and more,/ Upon the `Native Question'./ (1868 -- ,Punch or the Wellington Charivari,16)

>  <b>MOSER</b> To attempt to solve the difficulty of the Native question has not been my object. (1863 -- ,Mahoe Leaves,69)

>  The Native question now has lost much of the importance it once possessed. (1892 -- ,NZ Official Handbook,275)

>  <b>MILLER</b> While the smugglers erased the customs along the coast, the native question was becoming progressively more complicated. <i>Ibid.</i> 149 ... a struggle for control of the land, and the land question involved the native question and the system of precarious military alliances established by Fitzroy. (1958 -- ,Early Victorian NZ,82)

>  <b>McNEISH</b> `It's the native question, you see, and this stupid new Governor.' (1970 -- ,Mackenzie,23)



## native rat
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [kiore](../K#kiore))

>  The whole lot were, in all probability native rats, though they are supposed to have become extinct in the Wairarapa upwards of twenty years ago. (Jul 17, 1885 -- ,Wairarapa Daily,2)

>  It was plain from earlier records that the native rat existed in hordes in the early days of the Maori in New Zealand. (Apr 10, 1934 -- ,Feilding Star,7)



 
 <i>n.</i> kiore (See [also Maori rat](../A#also-maori-rat))

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> The Native Rat was more like a field Mouse and was considered a Luxury in old times, but the Rat from Norway or England came and is called Rah Parkieah or Stranger rat. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),53,1963 ed.)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> ... formerly before the introduction of pigs the native rat was much prized and sought after all the rat runs were known (May 10, 1939 -- ,Journal,2/130)

>  <b>BEST</b> Dieffenbach got a Native rat. (Apr 9, 1841 -- ,Journal,286,1966 ed.)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> [The] native rat still lingers here and there [in the central North Island], although it has disappeared on the coast; the native dog has become a mongrel; ducks, teals, the red-billed porphyrio or pukeko, a large bittern, a tern, two kinds of gulls, cormorants ... are found here. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/362)

>  <b>HAAST</b> The native rat (Mus rattus) is the only known indigenous land quadruped; but ... I was unable to find any. (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,135)

>  <b>HAY</b> Pigeons, nestors, parrots, rail, kiwi, swamp-fowl, water-fowl, owls, parson-birds, all these and more were eaten; while the native dogs and rats were held to be great dainties. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/151)

>  The native rat, which the Maoris named Kiore, was very different to the Norway rat, and strictly herbivorous. (1914 -- ,History of North Otago,4) [NOTES: 1906 W.H.S.Roberts in 1914 History of North Otago (1978) 4]

>  The kiore maori, or native rat, is said in Maori tradition to have been imported ... from Polynesia ... Many persons stoutly maintain that the old Maori rat is still with us ... (1909 -- ,TrNZI,41/242)

>  <b>SHAND</b> Native Rats, called Kiore, were common to the [Chatham] island: but it is believed they were not eaten by the Morioris .. (1911 -- ,Moriori People of the Chatham Islands, The,6)

>  <b>BEST</b> The native dog, now extinct, is said to have been introduced by old-time Polynesian voyagers from the isles of Polynesia. It was sometimes slain for food. The native rat was quite an important article of food. (1925 -- ,Tuhoe: The Children of the Mist,11)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Matapo, ... 2. Native rat. (1917 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,220)

>  native rat n, NewZeal : KIORE (1961 -- ,Merriam-Webster Third International,1506)

>  Hinamoki = inamoki, n. 1. Native rat (Rattus exulans). (1971 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,51)

>  <b>MIKAERE</b> In those days the haumata (snow grass) grew over one and half metres high, and abounded with ... succulent kiore (native rats). (1988 -- ,Te Maiharoa,75)

>  For many years the 'native rat' (looking rather like a big house mouse) was thought to be harmless, particularly in comparison to its larger, more sinister European-introduced cousins - the Norway rat and the ship rat. (Nov 2002 -- ,Forest & Bird,306/11)



## Native reserve
 
 <i>n.</i> later Maori reserve. [See Native Reserves Act ?1856]

>  At the lottery for priority of choice, the native reserves proved greatly to exceed the average of fortune. (Aug 21, 1839 -- ,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,7)

>  NO SAWYER or other person will be permitted to cut timber or erect houses on any NATIVE reserve. (Oct 17, 1840 -- ,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2)

## Native settlement
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  The medical students are being distributed throughout the native settlements. (Sep 2, 1913 -- ,Evening Post,8)

>  On descending the range, we entered a valley of considerable extent, to the left of which was a native settlement. The inhabitants having, a short time previously, left for Paupo, we found no one there but an old woman, who luckily had a good supply of corn and potatoes, and in whose hut I managed to sleep very comfortably. (Aug 26, 1842 -- ,New Zealand Colonist & Port Nicholson Advertiser,4)

>  His Excellency visited every native settlement in the district; all the natives said, that if Aramakaraka was willing, peace would be made. (Apr 17, 1855 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  A pah is strictly a fortified village, but it has ceased to be applied to a fortified one only, and a collection of huts forming a native settlement is generally termed a pah now a days, and the pah I refer to as "Our pah" has no signs of fortifications about it. (Sep 11, 1862 -- ,Wellington Independent,5)

>  Further, the Maoris at the native settlement, Okatire, seven miles up the river by water, say they can no longer get fresh water, as they could until recently, out of the river, the scour caused by the harbor works enabling the tide to run up much further. (Mar 27, 1880 -- ,Evening Post,2)

>  It was at the native settlement of Ruatoki, situated within thirty miles of that historic Church at Opotiki, that one of the most inspiring conferences of Maori Church leaders was held, from Wednesday evening, May 21st, to Sunday evening, 25th. (Jul 1, 1930 -- ,Waiapu Church Gazette,13)

>  On the return of a detachment of the Militia from the front, while passing through the native settlement of Tamahin, they were strongly urged by one of the chiefs to dispose of part of their ammunition, and even their arms. (Feb 19, 1870 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,3)

>  Some years ago a typhoid epidemic broke out at Omarumutu, a small native settlement east of Opotiki, Bay of Plenty, when many natives were swept off the surface of the earth. (Jul 9, 1897 -- ,Colonist (Nelson),4)

>  Dr. Pomare, the Government Native Health Officer, carefully examined the various Native settlements during his visit and impressed on the people inhabiting them the necessity of more careful attention to sanitary matters. (May 13, 1903 -- ,Ohinemuri Gazette,2)

 <i>n.</i> Tetragonia tetragonioides (See [New Zealand spinach](../N#new-zealand-spinach))

>  <b>HAY</b> There are the native spinach or Renga-renga (<i>Tetragonia expansa </i>), the Pana-pana, or cress (<i>Cardamine hirsuta </i>), and the Reti-reti, or sorrel (<i>Oxalis [211] magellanica </i>), which do for salad and green vegetables. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/210)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Korohea (<i>Turnagra Hectori</i>) is the native thrush, and a poor imitation it is of the English throstle. It is scarce. Sometimes its song may be listened to with pleasure. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/224)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Piopio ... Turnagra tanagra, native thrush. </i> (1917 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,327)

<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>



## Native title
 
 <i>n.</i> = Maori title [NATIVE [MAORI] (CUSTOMARY) TITLE. A fascinating and still proceeding investigation. The phrase is not defined in the acts - Native is but not Native Title. There are at least two types of Native (Maori) title - customary, which I presume means communal (of the iwi), and freehold. There will undoubtedly be more colloquial references in second readings to bills. The earliest reference is quoted from "1847 NZPCC 387 n at 390" which I will need to consult with a lawyer about - the book in question doesn't have an explanation of NZPCC. (HWO note from 1990s)]

>  In such a large and important matter asdealing with the Native title, such apower should be given to the Judges. (1873 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,XIV/609)

>  To declare, as some people declare, thatthe situation of the Native-landdifficulty is to individualise everyNative-land title, and allow the Nativeto sell his land and become landless, iscounsel not only of folly but also ofweakness. (1909 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,CXLVIII/1273)

>  "Native land" was land in the Colonyowned by Natives under their customs andusages, the title whereto has not beenascertained by the Court]. (1894 -- ,NZ Statutes,58 Vict. 43/312 Sect. 2)

>  <b>HINDE, McMORLAND & SIM</b> In solemnly guaranteeing the Nativetitle, and in securing what is called theQueen's pre-emptive right, the Treaty ofWaitangi, confirmed by the Charter of theColony, does not assert either indoctrine, or in practice, any thing newand unsettled. (1986 -- ,Introduction to Land Law,16, Sect. 1.019)

>  A Proclamation by the Governor that anyland vested in His Majesty the King isfree from the Native customary titleshall in all courts and in allproceedings be accepted as conclusiveproof of the fact so proclaimed. (1909 -- ,NZ Statutes,9 Edw. VIII 15/181 Sect. 85)

>  The bed of the lake known as Lake Taupo,and the bed of the Waikato River.. arehereby declared to be the property of theCrown, freed and discharged from Nativecustomary title (if any) or any otherNative freehold title thereto. (1926 -- ,NZ Statutes,17 Geo. V 64/697, Sect. 14(1)) [NOTES: Native Land Amendment & NativeLand Claims Adjustment Act 1926]

>  <b>SALMOND</b> The customary Native title to the land ofNew Zealand has now for the most partbeen extinguished. (1932 -- ,Public Acts of NZ,VI/87) [NOTES: 1909 in 1932]

>  The Maori customary title shall be deemedto have been lawfully extinguished inrespect of all land which.. wascontinuously in the possession of theCrown. (1953 -- ,NZ Statutes,94/1143, Sect. 57(2)) [NOTES: Maori Affairs Act, 1953]

>  The transformation of Maori customarytitle into freehold titles was requiredto be carried out by the Maori Land Courtuno ictu. (May 4, 1976 -- ,NZ Law Journal,9/205)

>  <b>HINDE, McMORLAND & SIM</b> [Hdg] Maori customary title and theMaori Land Court. Upon the assumption ofsovereignty over New Zealand the Crownacquired not only title to all land inNew Zealand, but also the exclusive rightof extinguishing Maori title, either bythe free conquest of the Maori occupiersor by legislation. (1986 -- ,Introduction to Land Law,18, Sect. 1.020)

>  <b>von HAAST</b> The practice of extinguishing Nativetitles by fair purchases is certainlymore than two centuries old... It is nowpart of the law of the land, and althoughthe Courts of the United States, in suitsbetween their own subjects, will notallow a grant to be impeached underpretext that the Native title has notbeen extinguished, yet they wouldcertainly not hesitate to do so in a suitof one of the Native Indians... Insolemnly guaranteeing the Native title,and in securing what is called theQueen's pre-emptive right, the Treaty ofWaitangi, confirmed by the Charter of theColony, does not assert either indoctrine, or in practice, any thing newand unsettled... The right which residesin the Crown is.. the exclusive right ofextinguishing the Native title. (1938 -- ,NZ Privy Council Cases,390) [NOTES: 1847 in 1938. It is likely that there are earlierreferences in US law to this concept butfrom the remarks made in the abovejudgment, it seems that these were called"Indian titles", not "Native titles".]

>  Any notification.. stating that theNative title over any land being a Nativereserve therein described wasextinguished previously to a date thereinspecified, shall for all purposes beconclusive proof that the Native titleover the land described in such noticewas extinguished at some time previouslyto the date therein specified. (1873 -- ,NZ Statutes,37 Vict. 60/279, Sect. 40) [NOTES: The Native Reserves Act, 1873]

>  <b>von HAAST</b> All Native titles by Native Custom wereextinguished. (1938 -- ,NZ Privy Council Cases,402) [NOTES: 1901 in 1938]

>  The proceedings commenced by bringing ina claim for investigation of the Nativetitle. (1938 -- ,NZ Privy Council Cases,284) [NOTES: 1904-05 in 1938]

>  It embraces several important collateral[sic] points, such as the "extinction ofnative title", the distinction between"Crown lands and the demesne lands of theCrown," "reparation or remedy andpenalty". (Sep 13, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)

>  Claimants have argued the land was for food gathering and the cultivation and that native title to the land was never extinguished. (May 16, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,A6)

>  [The judgement] embraces several important collateral points, such as.. the "extinction of native title," the distinction between "Crown lands and the demesne lands of the Crown". (Sep 13, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)



## native tobacco
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Verbascum thapsus</i>;flannel leaf; velvet; golden rod; an occasional name for mullein

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> FLANNEL LEAF (<i>Verbascum thapsus</i>) is commonly called mullein in England, and is frequently so called here as well. Its commonest local name is probably tobacco plant, or native tobacco, a name for which there is no justification in either appearance or use. Velvet mullein woolly mullein, golden rod, and Aaron's rod are other appellations, but flannel leaf, one of the common sheep-station names is both descriptive and distinctive. The plant is found in dry soil and river beds ... in both islands. (1926 -- ,Weeds of New Zealand,155,1967 ed.)

>  5. VERBASCUM THAPSUS, a weed of poor land has many popular names. In New Zealand it is called native tobacco, velvet, flannel leaf and golden rod. (Nov 22, 1974 -- ,Dominion)

>  From Waikato despatches is gathered the notice that a kind of native tobacco is found growing in some of the abandoned Maori Stations. (May 3, 1864 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,3)

>  The Kawhia natives have never experienced any difficulty in the cultivation of the tobacco plant, the only difficulty being how to render the "weed" most enjoyable. (Apr 30, 1873 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  About three or four weeks since, our attention was directed to a sample of Native tobacco, grown at Wakatane, in the Bay of Plenty. At that time it was raw and uncured, twisted up on rools not unlike, in colour and appearance, to ropes of native flax. (Oct 5, 1852 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)



## Native township
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  It [the new bridge] will cross the Waipa at Whatiwhatihoe, and literally joins together the European and Maori townships, bringing Finch's hotel in a line with the native township, a facility which, judging by last night's proceedings, will be largely taken advantage of. (May 1, 1882 -- ,Hawke's Bay Herald,3)

>  Can the Government prevent Europeans from at least feuing land in these native townships? (Oct 28, 1843 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  The land at the proposed native township is high, overlooking the river, being the termination of the fern range from riverhead to the Kaipara. (Jul 22, 1864 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,4)

>  House, Garden, and 16 Allotments of Land, being sub-division of Sections nos. 200 and 201, Native Township, fronting Herbert street and Chapel street west. (Jan 17, 1873 -- ,Grey River Argus,3)

>  The only residents near are one stationholder, the inhabitants of a small Native pah and a Native township at Omaio Bay. (Nov 11, 1921 -- ,Ashburton Guardian,5)

>  1895 NZ Statutes 59 Vict. 12/29An Act to provide the Settlement andOpening-up of the Interior of the NorthIsland... The Short Title of this Act is"The Native Townships Act, 1895." (1895 -- ,NZ Statutes,59 Vict. 12/29)

>  In every Native township there shall bereserved and laid off for the use of theNative owners such allotments,hereinafter called "Native allotments,"not exceeding in the whole an area oftwenty per centum of the total area ofthe township... It shall be the duty ofthe Surveyor-General to include in suchreservations every Native burying-ground,and every building actually occupied by aNative at the date of the gazetting ofthe Proclamation. (1895 -- ,NZ Statutes,59 Vict. 12/30)

>  An Act to amend "The Native TownshipsAct, 1895." (1903 -- ,NZ Statutes,3 Edw. 33/69)

>  Subject to the provisions of this Act,all Native land situated in a Nativetownship under the Native Township Act1895.. shall.. become vested for a legalestate in fee-simple, without grant orother assurance, in the Maori Land Boardof the district in which that township issituated. (1910 -- ,NZ Statutes,1 Geo. V 18/48)

>  Any land acquired by the Crown in anyNative Township.. may be disposed of byway of sale, lease, or otherwise inaccordance with the regulations madeunder section twenty of that Act. (1919 -- ,NZ Statutes,10 Geo. V 22/67)

>  An Act to consolidate and amend the lawrelating to the Administration of thelands comprised in Maori reserves, WestCoast settlement reserves, and "Maori"townships... This Act may be cited as theMaori Reserves Act 1955. (1955 -- ,NZ Statutes,I 38/264)

>  Mr. Lang (Waikato) asked the NativeMinister, whether the Native townships inthe King-country would be opened at anearly date? (1903 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,127/854)

>  It is only by purchasing land from theNatives, and giving the lessees or theirsuccessors a freehold title, that youwill get these Native townships liftedout of the ruck they are in at thepresent moment. (1910 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,151/275)

>  For example, there are the Maoritownships at Tokaanu, Te Kuiti, andOtorohanga. (1955 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,307/2947)



## Native track
 
 <i>n.</i> = Maori track

>  <b>RIESCHEK</b> I sent Caesar after him, but because the native track was exceedingly narrow, the dog could not get ahead of the horse. (1930 -- ,Yesterdays in Maoriland,155,1933 ed.)



## native tree daisy
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The native tree daisy - Oharia [<i>sic</i>] gardnerii - is no oil painting, but Department of Conservation staff are thrilled after 15 new species were found, increasing the gene pool for propagation. Until now there weree only 80 known trees, mostly in a DOC-managed reserve near Taihape. (May 8, 2002 -- ,Evening Post,15)

>  Members of Doc's Biodiversity Wairarapa team recently discovered 15 critically endangered native tree daisies (Olearia gardnerii), a discovery likened to discovering 15 new kakapo... The tree daisy is a small tree, growing only five or six metres. It has a short lifespan of around fifty years. (May 22, 2002 -- ,Capital Times,16)



## native trout
 
 <i>n.</i> = kokopu [Freshwater <i> Galaxias</i> spp., esp. <i> G. argenteus</i> or <i> G. fasciatus<i/>, KOKOPU.]

>  <b>McDOWALL</b> The fourth species in the catch is rarely seen, and is the giant kokopu (<i>Galaxias argenteus</i>), again commonly known as Maori trout or native trout. (1984 -- ,NZ Whitebait Book, The,14)

>  <b>SPEIGHT</b> The species of <i>Galaxias</i> may broadly be divided into the small, sea-running whitebait minnows (<i>Galaxias attenuatus</i>), the larger native `trout' (<i>G. fasciatus</i> and allied species), which probably breed inland, and an aberrant species of medium size (<i>G. borrowsii</i>) which approaches the Westland mudfish (<i>Neochanna apoda</i>) in form....The larger mottled brown native trout occur in most of the streams and lakes of Canterbury. Their breeding habits are little known from actual observation, but from the lack of evidence of a seaward migration it is inferred that they breed in the streams and lakes. (1927 -- ,Natural History of Canterbury,196) [NOTES: 1927 Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury 196]

>  <i>Galaxias fasciatus</i>: [European Name] Native trout or mountain trout. [Maori Name] Kokopu. (1936 -- ,Handbook for New Zealand, The,73)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Eels, crayfish and cockabullies (native trout) were plentiful in some streams, but were very seldom eaten [in Otago in the 1860s]. (1939 -- ,First White Boy Born in Otago,122)

>  Among fishes, the `native trout' (<i>Galaxias fasciatus</i>). (1939 -- ,TrRSNZ,69/125)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Fifty years ago the Waimate Creek was full of native trout, or Kokopuru; ... (1940 -- ,Te Waimate,241,1954 ed.)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Kokopu - throughout these lists the translator [sc Beattie] has translated this as `native trout'. A good-sized one would weigh 1lb. The translator has eaten one and found the flesh sweet, but it was full of bones. (1945 -- ,Maori Place-Names of Canterbury,63)

>  <b>LYON</b> .. to wade up the clear gully stream in pursuit of the small koura, or crayfish, and any native trout or kokopu we could spear, or guddle. (1952 -- ,Faring South,79)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> MINNOW or WHITEBAIT (INANGA) <i>Austrocobitis attenuatus</i> (Jenyns) ... For a long time it has been known to the Maori and to Europeans that our New Zealand Whitebait is the young of a native freshwater fish commonly known as Minnow, or Inanga or Hiwi to the Maori, and erroneously called a Trout. ... The early settlers called it Maori Trout, Native Trout, Rock Trout and Minnow. But it is not a Trout. Nor is it a Minnow, through it resembles the English freshwater Minnow. While the name Trout is not correct, it is and will always be known as such by farmers and naturalists. In the Bay of Plenty, Whitebait is known to the Maori as Porohoe. (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,118)

>  Sometimes the banded and giant galaxias are called `native trout', but they are not related to the trout and the former names are more appropriate. (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/235)

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> There was an accommodating pool here well stocked with <i>kokopu</i>. ... These little native trout soon became tame ... (1970 -- ,Life's Vagaries,44)

>  <b>OGILVIE</b> Other pioneers in the Opihi district [of Canterbury] refer to the abundance of native pigeons, wood hens, native trout, fresh water crayfish, eels and Maori rats - which were small, grey, fluffy, vegetarian and evidently succulent. (1971 -- ,Moonshine County,131)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> MINNOW or WHITEBAIT (INANGA) <i>Austrocobitis attenuatus</i> (Jenyns) ... For a long time it has been known to the Maori and to Europeans that our New Zealand Whitebait is the young of a native freshwater fish commonly known as Minnow, or Inanga or Hiwi to the Maori, and erroneously called a Trout. ...The early settlers called it Maori Trout, Native Trout, Rock Trout and Minnow. But it is not a Trout. Nor is it a Minnow, through it resembles the English freshwater Minnow. While the name Trout is not correct, it is and will always be known as such by farmers and naturalists. In the Bay of Plenty, Whitebait is known to the Maori as Porohoe. (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,118)

>  The "bully" or "bull-head," and the kokopu (sometimes called the "native trout," a very lazy fish) are found in rivers and streams. (1909 -- ,NZ School Journal,3(10) : 330)

>  There is an old coalpit at Lovells Flat operations in connection with which have been suspended for many years. The pit is chock full of water - in fact it overflows and a lagoon has been formed above and about it. Where the water comes from nobody knows. No stream enters it, and it has no connection with any running water. It must be an accumulation of rain water. Yet it swarms with cockabulli (native trout), and the fresh water crayfish. (Sep 4, 1891 -- ,Bruce Herald,2)

>  The bully, or bull-head, and the kokopu, or native trout, live entirely in fresh water. (Nov 13, 1901 -- ,Star, The,4)

>  The <i>Lyttelton Times</i> says it would appear that the native trout, better known by many as the grayling, is being exterminated. (Aug 25, 1871 -- ,Grey River Argus,2)

>  Three fine specimens of these now rare fish, the native trout, the largest 1a lb, have been added to the many imported varieties which may be seen at the Opawa fish-breeding establishment, Canterbury. (Nov 15, 1889 -- ,Hawke's Bay Herald,2)

>  Then there was the stream with its native trout and deep bathing pools. (Jan 5, 1917 -- ,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,2)

(1874 -- ,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,225)

>  <b>McNAB</b> Up to this time [1834], ... British troops had never met Maoris, and ... the lessons which had not been learned in the `sixties', after several Native wars had taken place, [could be known] by intuition before the first British-Maori engagement. (1913 -- ,Old Whaling Days,118)

With that generation also had largely passed the pakeha who was wedded to the idea of England being `Home'. ## natural boundary
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a cliff, gully, river, gorge or thick bush separating two properties or areas [ possibly retaining stock without fencing. ]

>  <b>BARKER</b> A view of downs rolling into a narrow gully, the flax swamp of which formed a natural boundary. (1871 -- ,Christmas Cake in Four Quarters,251)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> We are practically dependent on natural boundaries on all our summer country ... (1972 -- ,Sheep Thief,26)

>  Natural Boundary.- Any b. between two stations or two parts of a station which was not made by man. It may be river, bush, or rock, and is often all three. (PAUL, p. 91) (Nov 11, 1933 -- ,Press, The,15)

>  <b>CAIRD</b> Wherever it is possible natural boundaries, such as rivers, swamps, and forests, are made use of ... (1874 -- ,Notes on Sheepfarming in NZ,18)

>  <b>MCLEOD</b> The wilder and rougher it is the more you can depend on 'natural boundaries' - deep gorges, rivers, patches of bush or the tops of high ranges. (1951 -- ,NZ High Country,12)

>  <b>MCRAE</b> .. the station relied mainly on natural boundaries: the Hurunui River to the south and the bush-clad mountains to the west. The Genrae and Mandamus rivers provided rather elastic block boundaries, depending on the flow of water. (1963 -- ,By the Braes of Balquether,31)

>  <b>STACEY</b> bull break: a fence across a gully joining natural boundaries such as cliffs. (1996 -- ,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,164)

 <i>adj.</i> (of plant and animal species)

>  Naturalized species are those exotic species that maintain themselves in the wild independent of direct human intervention. (Mar 2003 -- ,Open Space,No. 60/20)



## nau
 
 <i>n.</i> Cook's scurvy grass

>  Another significant plant recovery projectis the strong-smelling native herb, nau,or Cook's scurvy grass (Lepidiumoleraceum). (Feb 9, 1996 -- ,Dominion,11)

>  <b>FEATON</b> LEPIDIUM OLERACEUM (<i>Forst</i>.) ... This plant is confined to New Zealand ... It is a good anti-scorbutic ... The Maoris call it `Nau'; and it is stated that they formerly cultivated it as a pot herb. <i>Ibid/</i> 178 Nau [Maori name] .. Scurvy weed [Settlers' name] .. Lepidium oleraceum .. Herb (1889 -- ,Art Album NZ Flora,21)

>  <b>BROOKER et al.</b> <i>Lepidum oleraceum</i> Forst.f. Common name: <i>Cook's scurvygrass</i> Maori name: Nau (1981 -- ,NZ Medicinal Plants,48)

>  <b>BURTON</b> A voyager on Cook's third voyage in <i>Discovery</i> in 1772, Dr Sparrman, notes that `the green stuffs which were cooked with pease and broth or used as salads, consisted of wild celery (<i>Apium prostratum</i>), wild spinach (<i>Tetragonia expansa</i>) and Cook's scurvy grass (<i>Lepidum oleraceum</i>).' The last mentioned, known as <i>nau</i> by the Maori, has nowadays been virtually eaten out of existence by sheep and cattle. (1982 -- ,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,16)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Lepidum oleraceum</i> COOK'S SCURVY GRASS ... Nau ... Erect or bushy hairless herb up to a half a metre tall . (1982 -- ,Stewart Island Plants,140)

## naughty
 
 <i>v.</i> to have sex with

It looks as if they are going to join veges, ciggy,brekky, cardy - and all the rest as OK idiom. But there is one antipodean expression that charms people internationally ... ## ne/nei
 
 <i>exclam.</i> interrogative, giving emphasis to a question, request, or proposal (cf. eh) [ORIGIN: Maori, usu. in Maori-English contexts (ne, nei used in Maori like n'est-ce pas). [Ma. n&emacr. /ne:/]

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> In vain my protestations. `Taihoa, taihoa, all in good time - what's the hurry, O Pakeha!' time was made for slaves - hurry no man's cattle, as we in the classics say. `What's the odds, a day or two sooner or later? There's Motu-Korea; it won't run away - taihoa nei?' with a chuck back of the head as they ended with the interrogative. (1881 -- ,Poenamo,Bk4/4/269)

>  <b>VOGEL</b> `I will be back ...' [Maori girl answers] `And be with me all the summer, ne?' (1898 -- ,Maori Maid,32)

>  <b>VOGEL</b> `You big chief, ne?' the [Maori] woman said in a tone that suggested an assertion rather than a question - though the `ne' is a verbal note of interrogation. (1909 -- ,Tragedy of Flirtation,45)

>  <b>ASHTON-WARNER</b> nei? [complete the cit.] (1963 -- ,Spinster,70)

>  <b>MASON</b> KANI. Dug it,Pera. ... Five feet, almost roast an ox in it [sc. hangi], whole, Time for a beer, ne? [note p. 97] ne: to give emphasis to a question. (1969 -- ,Awatea,17,1978 ed.)

>  <b>MASON</b> HINGAWARU ... When Rangi chooses his wahine, someone else pays, ne? RANGI: When I choose, Dad, I'll choose a girl, not a wahine! Hate that word! Makes me see fat slobbery old tarts waddling along the beach. (1974 -- ,Hand on the Rail, The,20) [NOTES: 1981 TS]

>  <b>HULME</b> Then, he had only chuckled again and said, `Well, we got him on the way, nei?' (1983 -- ,Bone People, The,5)

>  <b>1986</b> `You hate me now, ne?' the matriarch called. `Yes!' he shouted. `I hurt you, ne?' she asked. (1986 -- ,Matriarch, The,132)

 <i>n.</i> a horse [Jim writes : NSOD and Collins tag <i>neddy</i> as <i>Austral.</i> but it is surely used internationally as an informal synonym of horse - "gee up Neddy". ## needlefish
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> LONG-SNOUTED PIPEFISH <i>Syngnathus (Novacampus) norae</i> Waite (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,143,2nd ed.)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> NEEDLEFISH ... Other names: <i>Scomberesox forsteri</i>; saury, skipper, double beak, ocean garfish, ocean piper. (1966 -- ,Sea Anglers' Guide,205)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> NEEDLEFISH [<i>Scomberesox forsteri</i>] ... Other names are ocean piper, skipper, saury, and doublebeak. (1967 -- ,Marine Fishes,16)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> Family SCOMBERESOCIDAE (skippers and needle fishes). <i>Scomberesox forsteri</i> ... is likely to have its name altered ... It looks superficially like a garfish, but has a double beak. (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,213)

>  <b>WILSON</b> NEEDLEFISH (<i>Scomberesox forsteri</i>) The sea-going version of the common Piper, or Garfish, this fellow is a common, surface-water fish that is widespread in our territory and is often found well out to sea, ... (1981 -- ,Fisherman's Bible,169)

>  <b>AYLING</b> SAURY (Needlefish) <i>Scomberesox saurus</i> ... It is similar in shape to the piper but on the saury both the upper and lower jaws are projected forward to form a sharp beak. ... [172] Sauries are often called skippers in reference to their habit of leaping clear of the water and skipping along on their tails for short distances. (1982 -- ,Collins Guide,171,1984 ed.)

>  [species name] garfish .. [scientific name] <i>Hyporhamphus ihi</i> .. [other name(s)] piper, halfbeak, needlefish... [species name] needlefish .. [scientific name] -- [other name(s)] may be GARFISH [<i>Hyporhamphus ihi</i>], or SAURY [Scomberesox saurus]; Hastings region, GARFISH. (Jul 23, 1986 -- ,Codes for Species in NZ Waters)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] needlefish 975-9, 1134 (1987 -- ,Fishes: A Bibliography)



## needlegrass
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an exotic grass Stipa variabilis which is invasive in sheep fleecewool.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> speargrass: another name for needlegrass. Both terms refer to more than one plant species, but perhaps the most common are Stipa variabilis or S. neesiana. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,76)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> needlegrass: an exotic grass (Stipa variabilis) accidentally introduced, with a tiny, hard, barbed head which penetrates in hundreds through the pelts of sheep and deep into the flesh of their briskets. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,55)

## neenish tart
 
 <i>n.</i> [A small round short-pastry tart with a custard filling but especially characterised by the icing, half of which is black and half white; in Dunedin with a peppermint flavour (check). Other variations in the filling include lemon juice, rum essence or vanilla. Known to me from Dunedin in 1952. An Australian version (see below) is on a biscuit base instead of being in a tart and includes a layer of jam but has the essential bicoloured icing. Possible origins of the name : Nimes (in France); Neen in Shropshire (Neenton, Neen Savage, Neen Sollars); have been told of a Dutch woman making them. There is an outside chance it might be related to the neem tree ("a meliaceous tree of India and Ceylon, sometimes 50 feet high... The natives chew its small twigs and use them as tooth brushes") but I think not. David Burton does not mention them in his books on the raj. Kathryn Walls knows the lemon-flavoured variety from Invercargill (thought they were bakery rather than home-made), Vincent had heard of them, Bill Manhire hadn't. Nelson Wattie does not know them from NZ but when the bicoloured icing was described said he knew them from Germany as "Americans", possibly because of the black and white racial suggestion. Tony Simpson makes, them, his mother made them, his grandmother made them, but he doesn't know anything about the origin. The name appears as a headword in the Macquarie Dictionary but none other that I can find. None of the books on the cookery shelves at National Library has them, not do any of the present stock of second-hand ones in Quilter's but then most modern cookery books are written (or compiled) by restaurant chefs who do not bake and not by bakery cooks.]

>  Neenish Tarts... 4 tablespoons Butter 4 tablespoons Sweetened Condensed Milk 4 tablespoons Icing Sugar 2 dessertspoons Lemon Juice. Soften butter, add sifted icing sugar, condensed milk and lemon juice. When set, ice half the top with white icing and half with chocolate icing. (1982 -- ,Budget Macquarie Dictionary,447)

>  Neenish Tarts... 4 tablespoons Butter 4 tablespoons Sweetened Condensed Milk 4 tablespoons Icing Sugar 2 dessertspoons Lemon Juice. Soften butter, add sifted icing sugar, condensed milk and lemon juice. When set, ice half the top with white icing and half with chocolate icing. (1966 -- ,Edmonds Cookery Book,30)

>  Neenish Biscuits... Spread half the tops of each biscuit with icing, allow to set. Add sifted cocoa to remaining icing... spread over remaining half of biscuit. (1800 -- ,Big Book of Beautiful Biscuits,The,28)

>  Nimish tarts... Ice half chocolate and half white on each. (1800 -- ,GHB Cookery Book,22)

>  NENISCHE TARTS. 1 lb. ground almonds, a? lb. icing sugar, 3 tablespoons flour, 4 egg whites, custard as below, icing in two colours. Sift ground almonds; icing sugar and flour. Beat egg white stiffly and add to dry ingredients. Make all to stiff paste. Line tins with this mixture, thinly. Prick with fork, bake in moderate oven till just crisp. Fill with custard and ice each cake with soft white icing and soft brown icing. CUSTARD.- 2 oz. butter, 2 oz. flour, a pint milk, 2oz. sugar, 2 egg yolks, essence to taste. Melt butter, add flour, stir in till smooth and cook one minute. Add milk, stir till boiling and cook 2 or 3 minutes. Add sugar and yolks, and cook without boiling. Cool and add essence. (1942 -- ,Aunt Daisy's Cookery Book,5th ed.:)

>  (1968 -- ,Edmonds Cookery Book,9th printing) [NOTES: as Neenish Tarts]

>  Neenish Tarts.[This recipe uses raspberry jam in the filling and raspberry coloured icing in place of chocolate. The only recognisable constituent is having a circular iced top in two colours - in this case, white and pink. (1994 -- ,Family Circle Tempting Treats) [NOTES: So far no further NZ recipe books have turned it up and it looks as though the 60s might be about the earliest printed occurrence, but I haven't given up hope yet. I have now been through the Turnbull's five boxes without luck.]

>  Stories paint pictures around the recipes, and there are many which will bring back memories for New Zealanders. Remember Neenish tarts, chicken Kiev, roly-poly pudding? (Dec 20, 1997 -- ,Dominion,21) [NOTES: A friend suggests this might be a Yiddish word.]

>  I was on my way to a big house, once a children's home. now filled with small ghosts. When I got there I said, "I saw a lake like a Neenish tart." (Jan 8, 1994 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),36)

>  The study of basalt columns on the Chatham Islands.. joins a host of similar images playing with repetition and variation, whether of Neenish tarts or the snows on Ruapehu. (Jun 28, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),57)

## negrohead
 
 <i>adj.</i> . [negro-head] (See [also niggerhead n1](../A#also-niggerhead-n1))

>  <b>YATE</b> ... for the mere trifle of a fig of negro-head tobacco (1835 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),127) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]

>  <b>ANGAS</b> We left Tuhua, and on taking leave of our hospitable chief, I gave him five figs of negrohead tobacco (worth about three pence) ... (1847 -- ,Savage Life,2/103)



 
 <i>n.</i>



 
 <i>n.</i> A tobacco type; NZ use common in early colonial days and as a common payment to a trade with the Maoris; also smoked by early settlers, sailors, stationhands etc. [negro head, negro-head]

>  <b>HODGSKIN</b> I purchased a pig ... for a few negro-heads of tobacco and two gun flints. (1841 -- ,Narrative of Eight Months Sojourn in NZ, A,38)

>  <b>McLEAN</b> Improving the road, so as to make it worth his [the Maori chief's] while to erect a turnpike tote, and exact a negrohead - or some other equivalent for every passenger (1845 -- ,Papers,1/12)

>  Tobacco is the currency of the Island: you could no more travel without it than in Europe with `the ready'; nothing however but American negro-head will pass; Brazil they [sc. Maoris] will scarcely accept as a gift - ... (1834 -- ,NZ Independent,31,1979 ed.)

>  <b>CHAPMAN</b> The proper way to arrange a blanket for the bush is to sew it up like a sack. As to food you carry what you like; and what the host always furnishes is tea (at every meal), damper, i.e. a sort of dough cake baked in the ashes, very good if well made; and fried pork. He can generally furnishthe traveller with a short black pipe and some `negro-head' or Cavendish. (Nov 24, 1847 -- ,Letters to His Father, 1843-1851) [NOTES: 1847 Nov 24 H.S. Chapman Letters to His Father [TS,ATB] in 1958 Miller Early Victorian NZ 140]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> tobacco, negro-head, [per] lb, 6-1/2d. to 10d.; ... (1848 -- ,Handbook for New Zealand, The,180)

>  <b>McLEAN</b> ... payment of 400 sticks of negrohead. Thomson, JT Reconnaissance Survey foetid negrohead. Reed NZ 165 (1851 -- ,Papers,IX/199) [NOTES: Halse]

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> TOBACCO. - The `Lion' brand (negrohead) is getting much liked; ... (1857 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/568)

>  <b>WILLIAMS & REEVES</b> Only think how much more graceful all the landscape will appear. / No more pigroots, whalebacks, hogbacks - these names that shall not last; / ... Negro-heads shall be re-christened. I for one will take no heed, / While to me there yet remaineth Negrohead, the fragrant weed. (1889 -- ,Colonial Couplets,3)

>  <b>HALL</b> ... foetid negrohead through a `cutty pipe' (1971 -- ,Mr Surveyor Thomson,21) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]



 
 <i>n.</i> a swamp-tussock bush (cf niggerhead, maorihead).

>  <b>WILLIAMS & REEVES</b> Only think how much more graceful all the landscape will appear. / No more pigroots, whalebacks, hogbacks - these names that shall not last; / ... ## neinei
 
 <i>n.</i> A small North Island tree with long tapering leaves. Also called pineapple tree, spider wood. [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  The surface is covered with stunted nei- nei, Bidwill's Pine, mountain flax, dwarfed forms of tawhero and tawari, and danthonia, brightened up by patches of snowberry. (Jun 15, 1922 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,15)

>  From there, the group takes a well-graded track through red and silver beech, mountai neinei (<i>Dracophyllum traversii</i>), tree daisy (<i>Olearia lacunosa</i>) and mountain cedar to Mt Arthur Hut. (Oct 17, 2000 -- ,Dominion,15)

>  <b>POLACK</b> There are many other woods of much service to Europeans, differing in quality, among others: the 30 <i>Warangai </i> - 31 <i> Pata </i> -32 - <i> Niho </i> - 33 <i> Tangio </i>, - 34 <i> Maihoi </i> - 35 <i> Nene </i> [e's-acute] - 36 <i> Taraiti </i> - 37 <i> Kohihihu </i> 38 <i> Waihupuku </i> - 39 <i> Kaikamoko </i> - 40 <i> Pukapuka </i> - 41 <i> Karanghu </i> - 42 <i> Utuhutu </i> - 43 <i> Angiani </i> - 44 <i> Akkas </i> of various kinds. 45 <i> Parekireki </i>, &c. Of all these woods there are abundant quantities. (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),2/400)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> [We found] what was to us quite a new kind of tree, called <i> nei nei </i>. It is something like a diminutive ti. (1959 -- ,Early Travellers,355) [NOTES: 1858 Jan 8 Smith Notes of a Journey in 1959 Taylor Early Travellers 355]

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Neinei, Col[enso]. <i> Dracophyllum latifolium. </i> (1867 -- ,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766)

>  <b>HAAST</b> An undescribed, superb, tree-like <i>Dracophyllum </i>, not unlike the <i>Drac. latifolium </i> of the northern island, began to appear here [<i>D. Traversii </i>]. The natives call it Nene. <i>Ibid.</i> 119 There were some very large groves of <i>nene (Dracophyllum traversii </i>), surrounded by a great number of good sized shrubs of Dracophyllum latifolium ... (1879 -- ,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,78)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Neinei (<i>Dracophyllum latifolium</i>) is but a small tree. The wood is hard, and is valued for making mallets ... (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/197)

>  <b>HECTOR</b> <i>Dracophyllum longifolium</i>, Br. Neinei.--An ornamental shrub-tree with long grassy leaves. (1883 -- ,Handbook of New Zealand,128)

>  [Native name] Neinei .. [Settlers' name] Neinei .. [Family] Epacrideae [Native name] Neinei .. [Settlers' name] Grass-tree ..[Family] Epacrideae (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,122) [NOTES: 1886 T.Kirk in 1902 Settler's Handbook of NZ 122]

>  Neinei .. Dracophyllum latifolium .. Wood of a reddish brown colour, prettily figured; ... (1890 -- ,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,12)

>  Among the strange shrubs [on the West Coast range] the nei-nei is most noticeable, having sometimes growing to thirty feet in height. (1894 -- ,NZ Alpine Journal,6/326)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Neinei, n</i>. Maori name for New Zealand shrub, <i>Dracophyllum longifolium</i>, R. Br., also <i>D. traversii</i>, N.O. <i>Epacridea. </i> (1898 -- ,Austral English,318)

>  <b>VON HAAST</b> (1865 -- ,Geology of Westland,78) [NOTES: 1865. J. Von Haast, `A Journey to the West Coast, 1865' (see `Geology of Westland,' p. 78):]

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> <i>D[racophyllum latifolium</i>] ... Neinei. (1906 -- ,Manual NZ Flora,419)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Dracophyllum latifolium (The Broad-leaved Grass-tree)</i>. A shrub or tree, from 10 ft.- 25 ft. high ... Maori name, Nei-nei. (1907 -- ,Plants of NZ,330,1940 p. 351 ed.)

>  <b>COWAN</b> We have climbed above the pines [on the West Coast]; ... and up here on the mountain side the vegetation is the flowering <i>rata</i>, the <i>akeake</i>, the native alpine broom, the white blossomed ribbonwood, ... the flax-like <i>kiekie</i>, and the semi-tropical <i>neinei</i>, half-shrub, half-palm, and whole forests of fern trees. (1926 -- ,Travel in NZ,(Vol II South Island) 95)

>  <b>BUCK</b> Portions of spear, made of nene (<i>Dracophyllum sp.</i>) .. 1 (1937 -- ,Moa-Hunters of NZ,85)

>  <i>nei nei</i> The Maori name for a New Zealand shrub with very long, narrow leaves. (Pronounce <i>nay| nay|</i>). (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),273,8th ed.)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> Reference has already been made to the PINE-APPLE TREE of the western uplands of Nelson and Westland, otherwise known as the NEI-NEI or less commonly as the SPIDER-WOOD. (1961 -- ,Flora NZ,192,1929 ed.)

>  <i>neinei, nene</i> n. New Zealand ornamental shrub [Maori]. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  <i>neinei, nene</i> ... n. N.Z. ornamental shrub; broad-leaved grass-tree [Maori]. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  <i>neinei n.</i> NZ shrub with long narrow leaves. [M] (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,503) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng.Dict.]

>  The channelled stems of the Neinei (<i>Dracophyllum latifolium</i>), and <i>the ... saplings of Toatoa, or Tanekaha (Phyllocladus trichomanoides)</i>, made valued walking-sticks. (1869 -- ,TrNZI,1/III/34) [NOTES: Colenso Botany North Isd]

>  <i>neinei</i>, a shrub (Dracophyllum latifolium), grass-tree. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> native broom [is this correct.]

## Nellie bin
 
 <i>n.</i> a fish bin [[note: this term `Nellie bin' is an error. The correct term is `Nally' bin, which is a brand name. Source: Tony Walker, Director, Fishing Industry Services, Christchurch, agent for Nally bins.]]

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> Vessels were often under catch restrictions: no more than four or five baskets (the term 'baskets' was still freely used despite wicker baskets having been replaced by plastic Nellie bins) of species such as trevally and dogfish. (2004 -- ,Hooked,305)



## nelly
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Macronectes giganteus</i>, giant petrel,=stinker, =stinkpot (See [giant petrel](../G#giant-petrel))

>  Saw a number of Nellys [petrels] and had a fine chase after several. Killed 5. (1999 -- ,Enderby Settlement Diaries,50) [NOTES: Entry for 25 October, 1850]

>  We headed up a great herd of nellies (giant petrel or breakbones). (Jan 30, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,15)

>  We got an egg which contained a full-grown Nelly chick. (Mar 6, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,11)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> <i>Ossifraga </i>... or <i> Macronectes ... giganteus </i> ... If a birder comes near the nest the old birds move away a little and the young ones squirt a horrible smelling oil out of their mouths for a distance of 8 ft. Nellies are filthy birds that range the whole of the Southern Hemisphere and deserve their name of stinkpot. Nelly, Stinkpot. TOROA-RURU. (1952 -- ,Chatham Islands,86)

>  (1955 -- ,Cook's Journals,1-60 [fn])

>  [27b. <i> Macronectes giganteus </i> (Gmelin, 1789) <i> Southern Giant Petrel (Nelly) </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,21,1980 ed.)

>  <b>GRADY</b> Many giant petrels, larger scavenger birds, known [to the Cook Strait whalers] as Nellies or stinkers, were also found. (1982 -- ,Perano Whalers,114)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> GIANT PETREL <i> Macronectes giganteus Other names </i>: Nelly, Stinker, Black Molly. (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,35)

>  <i> Northern Giant Petrel Macronectes giganteus </i>(GMELIN, 1789) ... OTHER NAMES: <i> Nelly, stinker, giant fulmar. </i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,68)



 
 <i>n.</i> northern giant petrel

>  [27. <i> Macronectes halli </i> Mathews 1912] <i> Northern Giant Petrel Nelly </i>) ...ranging widely throughout the southern hemisphere. (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,20,1980 ed.)



 
 <i>n.</i> giant petrel [[?is this a different bird or merely a synonymous name: CCRansom notes that O. gigantea `giant bonebreaker' is listed nowhere: Giant petrel = giant fulmar]]

(1872 -- ,TrNZI,4/64) [NOTES: Travers Notes on the Chatham Isds]

>  <b>HAAST</b> Of birds still belonging to the Avifauna of New Zealand, the following species were represented :- <i> Graculus punctatus </i>, spotted shag; <i> Eudyptula undina </i>, small blue penguin; <i> Anas superciliosa </i>, grey duck; <i> Graculus carbo </i>, black shag; <i> Graculus varius </i>, pied shag; <i> Graculus brevirostris </i>, white throated shag; <i> Ossifraga gigantea, nelly </i>; <i> Apteryx australis </i>, large kiwi, <i> Nestor meridionalis </i>, kaka; <i> Stringops habroptilus </i>, kakapo, and some other undetermined species. ## nelly-birding
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>GRADY</b> Nelly-birding - Quite often the Peranos would pursue a pair of whales. If the cow was harpooned first, the bull whale would hang around. The whalers, therefore, tried to get a bomb into a bull in a near vital spot. But sometimes the chaser got out of position, and one of the other chasers would fire their harpoon into it. This was called `Nelly-birding'. (1982 -- ,Perano Whalers,227)
