>  The Nelson green gecko is endemic to New Zealand, found only in the Nelson area of the South Island. (2009 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.absoluteastronomy.com/topics/Nelson_green_gecko]



## Nelson huntaway
 
 <i>n.</i> a boulder rolled downhill to move stock [musterers' local]

>  <b>ELWELL</b> In mustering times,Ernest used to set some of the huge boulders on the sides of [the cliffs] rolling, and they make quite a roaring noise ... This noise startled the sheep ... which always made upwards.] (1878 -- ,Boy Colonists,169)

>  <b>McCASKILL</b> Danger could also be caused by a musterer rolling a large rock to frighten sheep along and save running a dog. [These rocks, started on purpose, were known as `Nelson huntaways' becauseyoung men from that district used to come mustering with young, untrained dogs. When frustrated they would use any method to move a sheep along the beat. (1969 -- ,Molesworth,100) [NOTES: [? 1907-14] A.G. Mowat Diary (? or reminiscence)in 1969 McCaskill Molesworth 100]

>  <b>BAKER</b> Nelson huntaway, a stone rolled down a hillside to move stock below instead of sending a dog out. `From the fact that in the early days the Nelson musterers were noted for having few and poor dogs.' (1941 -- ,NZ Slang,59)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Brownie sent a boulder hurtling down the face - a `nelson huntaway' (1949 -- ,High Country Days,29)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> On the hill, when sheep are sticking far below you, it's sometimes possible to roll a large rock down a steep face and scare them into movement with its crashing and bounding. This is called a`Nelson huntaway,' or just a `Nelson.' (1951 -- ,High Country,42)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> A lot of young fellows from Nelson used to start off mustering there [at Hillensden Station, Marlborough] and they became known as the Nelson College. If anyone said `I'm going back to the college, you knew where he was going. Nelson men in Marlborough and North Canterbury used to be called Sandy Hookers. Stones being rolled down steep faces to frighten sheep were called Nelson Huntaways. (1968 -- ,Remembered Trails,128)

>  Nelson huntaway n. stone rolled down a hillside to move stock, used instead of sending a dog out. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

## Nelson snob
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>THOMSON</b> ...it is worthy of notice, that the colonists, soon after the formation of all the settlements, acquired distinguishing epithets; thus there was an Auckland cove, a Wellington swell, a Nelson snob, a Taranaki exquisite, an Otago cockney, and a Canterbury pilgrim. These epithets, almostalready forgotten, are too characteristic to be buried in oblivion, as each originated in some peculiarity of the leading colonists. The Otago settlers derived theirs from their ignorance of the country in which they lived; the Auckland people from their extensive Sydney connexions; and the origin of the other names are sufficiently obvious without explanation. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,2/188)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Nelson snob a person from Nelson. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,75)



## Nelson system
 
 <i>n.</i> a system of religious instruction for State schools

>  The agreement was made through the supreme courts of the Churches which give the major attention to the Nelson System. (May 3, 1955 -- ,Dominion,14)

>  <b>SALMOND</b> Lesson Outlines for Christian Education in New Zealand Schools under the Nelson System [title] (1930 -- ,Lesson Outlines for Christian Education)

>  <b>PINFOLD</b> I used to attend, week by week, the State school of the town in which I lived, and gave Bible lessons to the children whose parents indicated their desire for them to remain. This is called the `Nelson System' because it was started in that city. (1930 -- ,Fifty Years In Maoriland,133)

>  <b>McSWEENEY</b> When in 1897 the `Nelson system' of religious instruction was introduced by the Rev. J.H.McKenzie, Presbyterian minister at Nelson, Catholic priests followed this method. (1966 -- ,History of Catholic Education in N.Z.,208)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Since schools taught for more than the four hours daily required by law, it had become usual, and was legal under the system started in Nelson, for ministers of religion with the approval ofheadmasters and school committees to take classes for half-an-hour weekly, though the children did not have to attend. (1986 -- ,Home Front,1/129)

## Nelson/Nelson huntaway
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a rock sent down a hillside to disturb sheep [ to save running out a dog.] (See [Taranaki huntaway](../T#taranaki-huntaway),[Maori huntaway](../M#maori-huntaway))

>  <b>MCCASKILL</b> These rocks, started on purpose, were known as 'Nelson huntaways' because young men from that district used to come mustering with young, untrained dogs. (1969 -- ,Molesworth,100)

>  <b>BAKER</b> Also reported is Nelson huntaway, a stone rolled down a hillside to move stock below instead of sending a dog out. (1941 -- ,NZ Slang,59)

>  <b>MCLEOD</b> On the hill, when sheep are sticking far below you, it's sometimes possible to roll a large rock down a steep face and scare them into movement with its crashing and bounding. This is called a "Nelson huntaway" or just a "Nelson." (1951 -- ,NZ High Country,42)

>  You only run a 'Nelson huntaway' when the sheep have defied your dogs and they, in turn, have defied you. "If your best dog's tired and beaten early, all you've got left is heart strain, hoarse voice and a Nelson huntaway." [Nelson] (Nov 15, 1983 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,20)

## nephrite
 
 <i>n.</i> [for pounamu, greenstone, 'nephrite jade']

>  <b>SHORTLAND</b> In Phillip's Mineralogy this stone [pounamu] is described under the name of Nephrite, .. (1851 -- ,Southern Districts of NZ,38)

>  <b>HAAST</b> They did not possess implements of greenstone (Nephrite). (1879 -- ,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,430)

>  It is called pounamu or poenamu by the Maoris, and `jade,' `jadeite,' or `nephrite,' by various writers, while old books refer to the `green talc' of the Maoris. (1892 -- ,TrNZI,24/481)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> The Maori greenstone (pounamu) called jade, jadeite, nephrite, etc., by Europeans, is a mineral very restricted in its deposits. ... one variety (tangiwai) is found at Milford Sound. (1904 -- ,Maori Race, The,248)

>  Pounamu (Nephrite, or Greenstone). - Used for ornamnental purposes. (1918 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,1/36)

>  <b>ELDER</b> Ninety-nine per cent. of genuine tikis are made of nephrite,the lighter and more translucent varieties of the stone beingpreferred. (1932 -- ,Letters & Journals of Samuel Marsden,432,1932 ed.)

>  <b>BUCK</b> Doubtless many of the nephrite implements found have been concealed in former times ... (1937 -- ,Moa-Hunters of NZ,205) [NOTES: fn quoting Best Stone Implements of the Maori 172]

>  The Natives are very fond of New-Zeeland green stones [ed. fn nephrite], of mother of Pearl-Shells ... (1982 -- ,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,IV/608) [NOTES: 1982 note in 1774 Aug 17 Forster Resolution Journal (1982) IV/608]

>  (Oct 4, 1864 -- ,Lyttelton Times,2) [NOTES: <i>Nephrite</i> (Greenstone of the Maoris), in rolled pieces, on teh beach of the West coast.]

>  Besides these there is heitiki, or nephrite neck ornament, and a carved tahaia, or staff of manuka. (Aug 24, 1880 -- ,Colonist, The,2)



## Neptune's necklace
 
 <i>n.</i> = Venus' necklace

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>CROWE</b> Neptune's necklace <i>Hormosira banksii</i>... A well-known seaweed up to 50 cm high, made up of pimply olive-brown hollow beads, each usually about 1 cm across. <i>Ibid.</i> 150 When the incidence of goitre was high in New Zealand, a schoolteacher here claimed to have appreciably reduced the goitre incidence in his school by getting the children to eat one 'bead' of Neptune's necklace a day. (1997 -- ,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,149)

>  <b>HULME & MORRISON</b> Neptune's necklace (again, just 'rimu') is tangy when you nibble it, and so are the young tips of rimuroa (bladderweed). (1989 -- ,Homeplaces,61)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> Fig.1. Seaweeds of the intertidal belt, Stewart Island, here consisting mainly of Bull-kelp (<i>Durvillea</i>), Strap-weed (<i> Xiphophora</i>), Moss-weed (<i> Bostrychia</i>), Neptune's Necklace (<i> Hormosira</i>), and <i> Sea Bombs</i> [sic] (<i> Adenocystis</i>). (1961 -- ,Flora NZ,4,1929 ed.)

>  <b>DEMPSEY</b> Every shallow pool is a garden of seaweed, with long ruffles of sea lettuce, carrageen and Neptune's necklace. (1964 -- ,Little World of Stewart island,25)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> Hansard ... could see [in Otago Harbour] large chitons and delicate seaweed overhung with beads of a coral-like growth - perhaps the seaweed that children call `Neptune's necklace'. (1978 -- ,Cruise of the Acheron,109)

>  <b>BURTON</b> Other edible varieties of seaweed commomly used by the Maori include rimurapu or bull kelp (Durvillea antarctica), which was also dried and then boiled with tutu juice to form a jelly, and the beaded Neptune's (1982 -- ,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,9)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i> Hormosira banksii</i> NEPTUNE'S NECKLACE ... Venus' necklace ... Necklace seaweed ... Brown or yellow-brown strings of thick-walled bladders, flopped over rocks ... at low tide. necklace (<i> Hormosira banksii</i>), which is palatable raw. (1982 -- ,Stewart Island Plants,318)

## never-never
 
 <i>n.</i> [never-nevers, never never, never nevers]

>  A shearer comes along with a cheque for 80 or 100 [pounds], gives it to the publican, gets rapidly drunk, and then drunker, and then D.T.'s and then wakes up and goes back into the never-never to rake up another cheque. (Oct 3, 1908 -- ,NZ Truth,172/4/5)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> To have lived such an open life and then to be suddenly caged up in an enclosure such as this Army camp often tempted me to make a break for the never-never, ... (1952 -- ,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,108)

>  never-never n. the thinly populated country far from settlement, esp. in Western Queensland and the Northern Territory. [prob. fr. Abor. (Comderio) nie-vah vahs, unoccupied land] (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  Never Never n. the sparsely populated far outback, the back-blocks. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  never-never ... NZ & Austral. any remote area, esp. land beyond backblocks in central Australia. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,505) [NOTES: cf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 758; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 327]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The other four shooters were out on the job for a few more days fly-camping, so the two of us had the first night in the never-nevers to ourselves, ... (1952 -- ,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,32)

## new chum
 
 <i>n.</i> a newly arrived settler or immigrant; a novice [See 1871 citation]

>  "Aw, may I awsk what a colonial is?" said a supercillious new chum the other day to a full-blooded Aucklander in Queen-st. "Certainly, a colonial is an Englishman," was the answer, "with all the modern improvements." (Apr 2, 1887 -- ,Te Aroha News,2)

>  New chums come and go in great numbers. (Nov 14, 1855 -- ,Nelson Examiner,2)

>  They naturally think they are equally as worthy attention as new chums. (Apr 12, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,6)

>  Mr. Singleton is a recent arrival in the colony - a new chum, so to speak. (Oct 4, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,19)

>  Our driver.. was a bit of a "new chum" at the wheel. (Apr 1, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,72)

>  SIR,- Some numbers of your paper, containing an effusion of two from a new chum, y'clept "A Pilgrim of the Plains," have just reached this out-of-the-way place. (Feb 4, 1854 -- ,Lyttelton Times,7)

>  One of the party (our new chum, who would wear a coat) not being used to river wading got giddy when he had reached the middle, and decalred he should fall, and could not go a step further; when losing both his nerve and balance at the same time, he was carried off his feet and floated down the river. (Jan 20, 1863 -- ,Colonist (Nelson),3)

>  "Well I don't wonder at a new chum like you, fresh from the drawing rooms of Belgravia, being surprised at anything you see out here," replied Harold Courtney, in a tone of veiled sarcasm; "but if you knew the circumstances you would understand that his marrying a Maori wife and settling down at Stratford was the easiest and most natural thing in the world." (Dec 15, 1896 -- ,Observer (Auckland),17)

>  As he is, comparatively, a new chum, he clearly was not aware of the conditions when the bridge was built. (Jul 23, 1904 -- ,Wanganui Chronicle,2)

>  I am - a New Chum. My business is to critcise, moralise upon, compare, sketch, and, where necessary, correct or praise the peculiarities of my adopted countrymen. First, then, arises this question: what is a 'New Chum'? and it may be answered in two ways, according to the manner in which it is asked. For there is a real New Chum; and there is a New Chum that is ideal only. The New Chum proper, is, I understand, a person who has but freshly come to the Colony from some part or other of the old world. The New chum that most colonists, here, appear to have in their mind's eye, is quite a different being in most cases. He is a pattern of neatness and elegance in his dress; of cheek and superciliousness in his deportment; of ignorance, meanness, cowardice, spiritlessness, vagueness, helplessness in time of emergency, vanity, presumption, and emptiness, in his temperament; and of all these vices, combined with effeminate and pedantic precision, in his talk. His delight is to abuse colonial customs; to run down the local 'bigwigs'; to show contempt for the industries and pursuits of the colonists generally; and affect a languid indifference to the matters which most engage their interest. His purse is even emptier than his head. He is without resources and without pluck. His ambition is at once low and unreasonable. he puts his trust in letters of introduction, and looks for a Government billet. (Aug 30, 1871 -- ,Timaru Herald,6)

>  <b>CARR</b> ... the sooner a `New Chum' loses his identity as such, the better for himself. (1913 -- ,Country Work,5)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an initiate, or one inexperienced in farming.

>  To their credit .. farmers out here are ever willing to teach a 'new chum ... ' (Aug 27, 1864 -- ,Weekly News,7)

>  <b>BARKER</b> A stranger, or colonially speaking, a 'new chum' would have thought we must needs cross that barrier-range before we could penetrate any distance into the back-country ... (1873 -- ,Station Amusements in NZ,63)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> If an unfortunate new chum ventures mildly to protest against some trifling hardships, he is at once silenced by being told that it is evident that he is not 'colonised' yet and that he must get used to such things. (1874 -- ,Colonial Experiences,8)

>  There are of course, new-chums and new-chums; we speak of those who come out to the colony burning with British eagerness to own land and cultivate it. (Aug 1882 -- ,Northern New Zealand Settler,3)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> A new chum who was working at the station had a theory that a beast would not charge if one turned his back on it.. (1940 -- ,Te Waimate,137)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> In New Zealand the joke is rather on the 'new chum' who shows his ignorance of country life like the Bulletin's English visitor to the sheep station who shoots his host's prize ram in the belief that it is a wild animal. (1946 -- ,Pastoral NZ,94)

>  <b>WILSON</b> Altogether the neighbours, so critical of 'new chums' formed a high opinion of him. (1955 -- ,Land of My Children,170)

>  If he succeeds in satisfying his companion that he is fit to be trusted alone, our new chum will presently be sent off on a sweep of this own ... At this preliminary stage of a muster men often work in pairs, and the new chum will be sent with an old hand. (Apr 13, 1955 -- ,Weekly News,34)

>  <b>KERR</b> They [Merinos] roamed far in the days before fences, and mustering was difficult. The new chum found to his cost that 'they'd rush for miles at the sound of a bark, and you would have a pile up and a smother on your hands.' (1976 -- ,From the Beginning: chronicles of a county,131)

>  <b>STACEY</b> His [harvesting] fork was his own and tapu'd an inch thick. Lord help any new chum who had the impudence to touch it. [newch'm] (1996 -- ,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,41)

## new chum gold
 
 <i>n.</i> [also new chums' gold]

>  <b>COOPER</b> Many new comers are misled by the abundance of mica and mundie [sic], which is consequently called `new chums' gold', and it is difficult to convince them that they have not got theprecious metal. (Nov 12, 1867 -- ,Digger's Diary,12,1978 ed.)

>  <b>PAULIN</b> The sand [in Dusky Sound] ... glittered with scales of yellow mica. It is sometimes called New Chum Gold, as, seeing it for the first time, one can hardly believe it is not the precious metal itself. (1889 -- ,Wild West Coast,14)

>  When the battered dish flowed empty, bar a tail of new-chum gold,/ Or it gave a ring of `colour' that betokened wealth untold. (1985 -- ,Penguin Book of NZ Verse,93) [NOTES: c1890-1900 EDWIN EDWARDS `Waitekauri Every Time' in 1985 Penguin Book of NZ Verse 93]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> We found traces of gold at different places, golden mica, known as new chum gold, and two types of pyrites. (1952 -- ,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,73)

## new chumship
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BUTLER</b> ... I was anxious to become an old chum, as colonial dialect calls a settler - thereby proving my new chumship most satisfactorily. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,iv : 55)



## New Edinburgh
 
 <i>n.</i> (Dunedin) [cf. Edinburgh of the South]

## new egg
 
 <i>n.</i> a new boy [1951 14 M 14 St Bede's, Chch 25 NEW EGGS]



## New Enzedder
 
 <i>n.</i> a non-British immigrant

>  [Hdg] Truculent "New Enzedders." (Jan 2, 1951 -- ,NZ Truth,6)

>  The [Prime Minister's] visit [to a Danish immigrant girl in Adelaide] originated a new phrase, `New Enzedder', which Mr Holland said would become the equivalent of `New Australian'. Thegirl said to Mr Holland: `You can't call us new New Zealanders; it's too clumsy. Why not NewEnzedders?' `That is excellent.' said Mr Holland. `we will use it'. (Jun 18, 1952 -- ,Evening Post)

## new hand
 
 <i>n.</i> new chum; not an old hand

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> A new hand, pulling one of the oars, begins to look frightened .... a volley of oaths from the headsman accompanies a threat to `break every bone in his skin if he funks now'. (1845 -- ,Adventure in NZ,1/327)

>  Of course, Mr. T. is a new hand here and knows nothing of the particular case. I am sure no old hand could have so written. (1871 -- ,McLean Papers,37/161)



## New Holland
 
 <i>n.</i> an early name for Australia

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Mr. Marsden entered with much apparent interest, upon the idea of a New Zealand settlement in Port Jackson; that is to say, within the Colony. ... [We think] that it should not be within one hundred miles of Sydney; that the natives should not be subject to visits from the curious intruders, ... [May 5 54] The natives have had much conversation, relative to the projected settlement in New Holland; ... They wish to be distinct from the whites or blacks, to retain their own teachers... (Apr 9, 1827 -- ,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,51,1961 ed.)

>  New Holland n. (now only historical) name given to Australia by first Dutch explorers. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)



## new iniquity
 
 <i>n.</i> the newcomers (espec. miners) to Dunedin in the 1860s. (contrast OLD IDENTITY) [(obs. Otago) often pl. constr. in sg. usually with the.]

>  In our celebrations of the arrival of theOld Identities, we overlook the all-important fact that had it not been forthe advent of the digger, that "newiniquity".. our Dominion would not sospeedily have attained the forwardposition she new occupies. (Dec 30, 1909 -- ,Otago Daily Times,8)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> [Old Identity] was the name applied by the people of Victoria, who flocked hither in thousands on the discovery of gold [in 1862], to those who had been in the province before that time. Those honest folks, the tenour of whose ways had previously been very even, were greatly startled by this immense and sudden influx of population. 16,000 persons landed in three months, andeverything went up to worse than famine prices. It is alleged that one minister actually prayed that the stream of rogues and vagabonds which was flowing into the country might be stayed, but this I believe to have been a base invention of the `new iniquity', as the new-comers were nicknamed. (1874 -- ,Colonial Experiences,25)

>  <b>REEVES</b> The canny Scotch `old identity' was swallowed up in the `new iniquity', as they mutually styled each other. (1892 -- ,Homeward Bound,3)

>  `the new iniquity' was the Otago settlers' name for the unwelcome Australian immigrants who arrived in the 60's; ... (Apr 2, 1938 -- ,Press, The,18) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]

>  <b>AYSON</b> Thomas had had a taste of `the new iniquity' before leaving for the goldfields. An old couple named Reid lived in a sod hut on the outskirts of Milton. (1937 -- ,Thomas,33)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> New Iniquity. Australian immigrants: New Zealand (mostly Otago): coll.: ca. 1862-80. Opp. old identity, q.v. at identity. Morris. (1937 -- ,Dictionary of Slang,557)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> Dunedin became the largest town in the country, while, to the horror of the `Old Identity', saloons, billiard rooms, gambling dens and dance halls sprang up to attract the gold of the `New Iniquity'. (1959 -- ,History of New Zealand,104)

>  <b>MCGILL</b> new iniquity immigrants, specifically from Australia to the Otago goldrush areas, 1860s to 1880s; the opposite of `old identity'. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,75)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (non-Otago) a newcomer to a neighbourhood (as part of an influx)

>  [Wanganui] We got new doctors ... lawyers ... Jew pedlars ... et hoc genus omne, and this infusion of `new iniquities' has fairly bedeviled the place. (Jul 3, 1871 -- ,Evening Post,2)



## New Leinster
 
 <i>n.</i> Stewart('s) Island (see also MIDDLE ISLAND) see also cit. NEW ZEALAND 1859 [hist.]

>  And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said islands shall be known and designated as the colony of New Zealand, and the principal islands, heretofore known as or commonly called the `Northern Island', the `Middle Island', and `Stewart's Island', shall henceforth be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster', `New Munster', and `New Leinster'. 1840 Letters Patent isusued on 16 November: quoted from The Ordinances of New Zealand, Domett 1850 pp.4-8 in 1971 Speeches and Documents 54 And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said Islands shall be known and designated as the Colony of New Zealand, and the principal Islands,here to fore known as, or commonly called, the `Northern Island,' the `Middle Island,' and `Stewart's Island,' shall henceforward be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster,' `New Munster,' and `New Leinster.' ... (Nov 16, 1840 -- ,Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ) [NOTES: in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 8]



## New Munster
 
 <i>n.</i> the South Island (see also MIDDLE ISLAND) see also cit. NEW ZEALAND 1859 [sic - ? 1840 Letters Patent issued on 16 November: quoted from The Ordinances of New Zealand, Domett 1850

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said Islands shall be known and designated as the Colony of New Zealand, and the principal Islands, here to fore known as, or commonly called, the `Northern Island,' the `Middle Island,' and `Stewart's Island,' shall henceforward be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster,' `New Munster,' and `New Leinster.' ... (1971 -- ,Speeches & Documents,54)

>  And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said islands shall be known and designated as the colony of New Zealand, and the principal islands, heretofore known as or commonly called the `Northern Island', the `Middle Island', and `Stewart's Island', shall henceforth be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster', `New Munster', and `New Leinster'. (Nov 16, 1840 -- ,Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ,8)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> On 28th January, 1848, [Grey] appointed and swore in some of the officers of the Southern Province, which is called `New Munster'; and on the 10th of March proclaimed as the boundary between that and `New Ulster', or the Northern Province, the parallel of latitude running ... about 39[degrees] 46' S. (1848 -- ,Handbook for New Zealand, The,86)

>  New Zealand is divided into two provinces, New Ulster, and New Munster, each of which is under a Lieutenant-Governor, who enacts ordinances for the province over which he presides, with the advice of a Legislative Council, composed of the officers of Government, together with a few persons nominated by the crown from amongst the inhabitants. A Governor-in-Chief rules the whole of New Zealand and is assisted in his legislation by a Council constituted similarly to the provincial Councils. At present, the Governor-in-Chief holds the subordinate office of Lieutenant-Governor of New Ulster , in addition to his higher Commission: and Mr Eyre is the Lieutenant of New Munster, the southern province. (Feb 1, 1851 -- ,Lyttelton Times,2)

## New Ulster
 
 <i>n.</i> the North Island (cf also MIDDLE ISLAND) see also cit. NEW ZEALAND 1859 1840 Letters Patent issued on 16 November:

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said Islands shall be known and designated as the Colony of New Zealand, and the principal Islands, here to fore known as, or commonly called, the `Northern Island,' the `Middle Island,' and `Stewart's Island,' shall henceforward be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster,' `New Munster,' and `New Leinster.' ... (1971 -- ,Speeches & Documents,54) [NOTES: quoted from The Ordinances of New Zealand, Domett 1850 pp.4-8 in 1971 Speeches and Documents 54]

>  And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said islands shall be known and designated as the colony of New Zealand, and the principal islands, heretofore known as or commonly called the `Northern Island', the `Middle Island', and `Stewart's Island', shall henceforth be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster', `New Munster', and `New Leinster'. (Nov 16, 1840 -- ,Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ) [NOTES: 1840 Nov 16 Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 8]

>  <b>JAMESON</b> Governor Hobson has recently given to the three Islands of New Zealand the names. New Ulster, New Munster and New Leinster; but as no reference is made in recemt communications to these appellations, which are not very euphonious, and are certainly rather perplexing, from the similarily of their terminations, it would appear they are not calculated to please the public taste (1841 -- ,New Zealand, S. Aust. and NSW,319)

>  <b>TERRY</b> [Quoting the Charter, 31st December, 1841] `The principal islands' heretofore known as, orcommonly called the `Northern Island', the `Middle Island', and `Stewart's Island' shall henceforward be designated, and known respectively, as `New Ulster', `New Munster', and `New Leinster'. (1842 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),31)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> On 28th January, 1848, [Grey] appointed and swore in some of the officers of the Southern Province, which is called `New Munster'; and on the 10th of March proclaimed as the boundary between that and `New Ulster', or the Northern Province, the parallel of latitude running ... about 39[degrees] 46' S. ] (1848 -- ,Handbook for New Zealand, The,86)

>  New Zealand is divided into two provinces, New Ulster, and New Munster, each of which is under a Lieutenant-Governor, who enacts ordinances for the province over which he presides, with the advice of a Legislative Council, composed of the officers of Government, together with a few persons nominated by the crown from amongst the inhabitants. A Governor-in-Chief rules the whole of New Zealand and is assisted in his legislation by a Council constituted similarly to the provincial Councils. At present, the Governor-in-Chief holds the subordinate office of Lieutenant-Governor of New Ulster, in addition to his higher Commission: and Mr Eyre is the Lieutenant of New Munster, the southern province. (Feb 1, 1851 -- ,Lyttelton Times,2)

## New Zealand
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) the New Zealand language (Maori)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> We had prayers both in New Zealand and English. The Natives were very attentive and repeated the sentences in New Zealand correctly. (1823 -- ,Journals and Correspondence,252,ed. Barton ed.)

>  Since I left England I have only studied New Zealand and Hebrew, and have found no kind ofdifficulty with these. (Sep 1, 1843 -- ,Letter) [NOTES: 1843 Sep 1 letter in 1969 Macmorran Octavius Hadfield 181]



 
 <i>adj.</i> As an attributive from the Country's name, it had in very early times, a connotation NATIVE, MAORI (cf NEW ZEALANDER) often with suggestions of roughness, wildness, or barbarism. It was quickly adapted (see 1840 entry) to include the white settlers and the civilised homeland they were preparing, a sense and use which obliterated the earlier. Many uses (eg New Zealand Company (1839), New Zealand Gazette) were merely defining, and had, of themselves, no racial reference.

>  We had not yet sufficient experience of New Zealand troops to trust them too far.' (Oct 6, 1769 -- ,Cook's Journals,1/570,1955 ed.) [NOTES: 1769 Oct 6 W.B. Monkhouse Journal in Cook's Journals (1955)]

>  `New Zealand War Canoes bidd defiance to the Ship' (1955 -- ,Cook's Journals,facing 1/208) [NOTES: 1769 Sydney Parkinson Caption to a drawing in B.M. Add. MS. 23920 50 in 1955 Cook's Journals facing 1/208]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> The New-Zeeland ladies never failed to put on the same kind of rouge mixed with grease, before they come on board to offer their uncouth favours to our sailors. (1778 -- ,Observations Made During a Voyage,591)

>  <b>ELDER</b> `11th. - In the evening Terra told us that he made a fire to cook potatoes and to eat a New Zealand man. (Aug 11, 1815 -- ,Marsden's Lieutenants,107,1934 ed.) [NOTES: 1815 Aug 11 John King in Elder Marsden's Lieutenants (1934) 107]

>  <b>McCRAE</b> While the ship was under weigh Morachie and Echoie two remarkably fine looking New Zealand boys ... came on board to take leave of their friends. (1820 -- ,Journal,12,1928 ed.)

>  <b>CRUISE</b> During our visit to Benny, he entered into a long argument, to prove the superiority of the New Zealand arms over the British firelock; ... (1823 -- ,Journal,60,1957 ed.) [NOTES: 1820 Mar 20 in 1823 Cruise Journal (1957) 60]

>  <b>CRUISE</b> During our visit to Benny, he entered into a long argument, to prove the superiority of the New Zealand arms over the British firelock; ... (1823 -- ,Journal,60,1957 ed.) [NOTES: 1820 Mar 20 in 1823 Cruise Journal (1957) 60]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> The New Zealand ladies, though the name of fashion has never reached their ears, are quite as fond of showing off their charms ... as our own fair countrywomen; ... (1817 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/178)

>  <b>SHEPHERD</b> He saw a village built in the New Zealand Style and about 20 or thirty Natives, said they behave sivil to him. (Mar 22, 1826 -- ,Journal) [NOTES: 1826 Mar 22 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 363]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> [He] has likewise obligingly communicated to me another of the New Zeeland music, which will be sufficient to give an idea of the taste of the people. He ... assured me that there appeared to be some display of genius in the New Zeeland tunes ... (1777 -- ,Voyage Round the World,2/477)

[We think] that it should not be within one hundred miles of Sydney; that the natives should not be subject to visits from the curious intruders, ... [May 5 54] The natives have had much conversation, relative to the projected settlement in New Holland; ... They wish to be distinct from the whites or blacks, to retain their own teachers. <i>Ibid.</i>314 [1833 May 17]At 3 the natives were served with their repast of beef potatoes and stirabout. As our boys have had some experience in this important duty ... our visitors were ... surprised at the order and expedition with which this assemblage of New Zealand rank was supplied, as the feast consisted of bout 800 dishes constructed of a plant similar to the flag. <i>Ibid.</i> 314. As our boys have had some experience in this important duty ... our visitors were ... surprised at the order and expedition with which this assemblage of New Zealand rank was supplied, as the feast consisted of bout 800 dishes constructed of a plant similar to the flag. No. Would I like one? Certainly. Then take my daughter. Which I did in New Zealand fashion. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),39,1963 ed.)

>  FOREST BIVOUAC. ... Strengthened by the usual New Zealand food of roasted potatoes and `damper', we set off ... but soon found ourselves bewildered ... in the surveyor's lines which crossed one another in all directions in the wood. We then resorted to the usual expedient of torches made of `karehao' (supple Jack) or `katoa'; ... it was [agreed] ... that we should light a fire and bivouac for the night. (1851 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),Part V (Church in the Colonies XX) 76) [NOTES: 1848 Aug 30 Selwyn in 1851 NZ Part V (Church in the Colonies XX) 76]



 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  new zealand adj, usu cap N & Z [fr. New Zealand, Brit. dominion in the south Pacific ocean] 1 : of or from New Zealand : of the kind or style prevalent in New Zealand 2 : of, relating to, or being the biogeographic region or subregion of the Australian region that includes New Zealand and a few adjacent islands (1961 -- ,Merriam-Webster Third International,1524)

 <i>n.</i> the country (See [cf MAORILAND](../C#cf-maoriland),[ENZED](../E#enzed))

>  The name <i>New Zealand</i> is sometimes wrongly attributed to Tasman himself. (Aug 2000 -- ,NZWords,4 : 1)

The spruce of New-Zeeland is a very beautiful tree, and conspicuous on account of its pendant branches ... (1777 -- ,Voyage Round the World,1/119)

>  The consequence [of ignoring the South Island] is, that in works which profess to describe New Zealand, the largest island of the three is not described at all. ... But it seems never to have occurred to the gentlemen who have published their `New Zealands', that it was necessary to see more than a very small portion of it, in order to describe the whole. (Jul 20, 1844 -- ,Nelson Examiner,20 et seq.) [NOTES: 1844 Monro Notes of a Journey (Nelson Examiner July 20 et seq.) in 1898 Hocken Contributions 231]

>  New Zealand is divided into two provinces, New Ulster, and New Munster, each of which is under a Lieutenant-Governor, who enacts ordinances for the province over which he presides, with the advice of a Legislative Council, composed of the officers of Government, together with a few persons nominated by the crown from amongst the inhabitants. A Governor-in-Chief rules the whole of New Zealand and is assisted in his legislation by a Council constituted similarly to the provincial Councils. At present, the Governor-in-Chief holds the subordinate office of Lieutenant-Governor of New Ulster , in addition to his higher Commission: and Mr Eyre is the Lieutenant of New Munster, the southern province. (Feb 1, 1851 -- ,Lyttelton Times,2)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Of the three islands forming the Zealand group, Rakiura, or the small South Island, is the only one which possesses a native name. Since the days of Cook, the North Island has been named on old maps Eaheinomawe, and the Middle Island Tavai-poenammo. These names originated thus. When the great navigator asked the natives the name of the North Island, he was told that it was `a thing fished from the sea by Maui,' - He mea hi no Maui; and that the Middle Island was Te wahi pounamu, or `the place of the greenstone.' When New Zealand became a British colony, the first governor, who was an Irishman, proclaimed that the North, Middle, and South Island were thenceforth to be denominated respectively New Ulster, New Munster, and New Leinster; and these names he selected for the colony because New Zealand, like Irelands, had no toads. This modern method of naming places by applying the epithet `New' to countries which are as old as the originals is not only incorrect, but possesses many disadvantages. The Constitution Act of 1852 discarded this unsuitable nomenclature, and the North, Middle, and Stewart's Islands are the names now invariably given to them by the settlers, and those by which they are familiar toseamen. The South Island was named in honour of the sealer who, in 1808, discovered its insularity. Sentimental settlers designate New Zealand the Britain of the southern hemisphere, on the same principle that the Dutchman Tasman, who discovered the country, called it New Zealand. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,1/4)

>  <b>BULLER</b> ... I have accordingly commenced my history of our avifauna with an account of the New-Zealand Crow. (humorously) New Zillund, etc [discuss this pronunciation] (1888 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,1/xvi) [NOTES: usu. spg: also North-Island]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> New Zealand, ... This name was given to the colony by Abel Jansz Tasman, the Dutch navigator, who visited it in 1642. He first called it Staaten-land. It is now frequently called Maoriland(q.v.). - (1898 -- ,Austral English,320)

>  fn Since the pig was introduced by Captain Cook ... [fn] Hence modern Newzealand slang a Captain Cook or Captain Cooker, for a wild pig. (Current since the late 19th century.) Also, slang, Pig-islands, Newzealand, Pig-islander, a Newzealander. <i>Ibid.</i> 1946 55/141 ... Words that appeared to me to have become current in English, at any rate as written and spoken in Newzealand. Neither ... do we Newzealanders in our ordinary writing; (Jun 1945 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,54/225)

>  It is no surprise that a conservative organisation such as the RSA has taken exception to the use of the phonetically pleasant `Aotearoa' instead of the clumsy `New Zealand' or colloquial `Newzyullind'. (Jul 1, 1988 -- ,Dominion,10) [NOTES: (Davis Jones)]

>  `The name Aotearoa was made popular around 1898 with the publication of William Pember Reeves' book The Long White Cloud - Aotearoa,' historian ... Sir Keith Sinclair says. After 1870, most Maori referred to the country as Niu Tirani - a result of Pakeha settlers calling the three islands New Zealand. Sir Keith said that in all the written Maori he had read from last century the word Aotearoa had been used in only one newspaper. Te Wanaanga, published in Napier, used Aotearoa in the 1870s. Sir Keith said Maori often referred to New Zealand by using `te motu nei' (in this island). Sir Keith said the literal translation of Aotearoa was `land of the long twilight'. The name may have come about because the early Polynesians were not used to the long evenings in New Zealand. (Jul 17, 1988 -- ,Sunday Star Times,A6)

>  <b>MARSH</b> `That's what they reckon. Nothing like it anywhere else in N'yerzillun.' (1980 -- ,Photo-Finish,31)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> She is left with several ... children, who are dressed in the Maori costume, and know no language but that of New Zealand. (1847 -- ,Savage Life,1/293)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> As a separable qualifying prefix to, or in loose combination with, the common names of fauna and flora to denote the indigenous variety distinct from English or European varieties to which it may bear some real or imaginary resemblance; or to denote their origin or habit at in the New Zealand bio-geographic region. In colloquial N.Z. speech often omitted, and is interchangeable with NATIVE and to some extent with MAORI. In this glossary it is considered aseparable prefix when used with most names of specific plants etc. This use is obsolescent in mostcases. Current uses are underlined: -->PLANTS: Acacia, Anise, Aniseed, Arbor Vitae, Ash, -->BEECH, BIDDY-BID, BIRCH, blue-bell, Bramble, BROOM, BURR, CANE, CARROT,CEDAR Celery, Clematis, Coffee, COTTON, COTTONWOOD, CURRANT, Cypress, Daisy, EASTER ORCHID, Edelweiss, EYEBRIGHT, FLAX, FUCHSIA, GRAPEFRUIT, GRASS (seeGRASS ENTRY), HAREBELL, HEMP, Hawthorn, Holly, Honeysuckle ICE PLANT, JASMINE, LABURNUM, LAUREL, LILAC, LINSEED, MINT, MISTLETOE, MULBERRY, MYRTLE, OAK, ORANGE BLOSSOM, Palm, PASSION FLOWER, PINCUSHION, PINE,PINEAPPLE, PLUME GRASS, PRIMROSE, SCREW PINE, SPINACH, STRAWBERRY,TEAK, THORN, VINE, WIND GRASS, YELLOW PINE, NEW ZEALAND -->in general combinations see also BUSHMEN, -->as a distinguishing epithet in the names of plants see also ACACIA, [?talicise current usage: ? or obs usage] ACACIA, ACAENA, ANISE, ANISEED, ARBOR-VITAE, ASH, ATROPIS, BEDSTRAW, BEECH, BEGONIA; BIDDY-BID, BIRCH, BLUEBELL, BOX, BRAMBLE, BROOM, BURR, BURR-REED, CALCEOLARIA, CALTHA, CANE, CARROT, CEDAR, CELERY, CHICKWEED, CHRISTMAS TREE, CLEMATIS, COFFEE, CONVOLVULUS, COTON, COTTONWOOD, CRESS, CURRANT, CYPRESS, DAISY, DAMSON,DANDELION, DAPHNE, DOCK, DONATIA, EDELWEISS, EYEBRIGHT, FERN (various, see entry), FIG, FIR, FLAX, FLAX-PLANT, FUCHSIA, GRAPEFRUIT, GRASS (many, see GRASS entry), GUM-TREE, HAREBELL HAWTHORN, HEMP, HERPOLIRION, HIBISCUS, HICKORY, HOLLY, HONEY-SUCKLE, HYDROCOTYLE, ICEPLANT, JASMINE; BUTTERCUP, KAIKOURA; LABURNUM, LAUREL, LIGNUM VITAE, LILAC, LILY, LINSEED, MARSH MARIGOLD, MINT, MISTLETOE, MULBERRY, MUSK, MYRTLE, OAK, OAK ELM, OLIVE, ORACHE, ORANGE BLOSSOM, ORCHID (various), PACHYCLADON, PALM, PASSION-FLOWER; PENNYWORT, MARSH; PINCUSHION; PINE (various, see entry), PINEAPPLE, PLANTAIN, PRIMROSE, PRIVET, REED, SANDALWOOD, SCREW-PINE; SPINACH, SPURGE, STRAWBERRY,TEAK, THORN, VINE, VIOLET, WATER-PIMPERNEL, WILLOW; WINEBERRY, YEW -->BIRDS: CROW, DOTTEREL, PIPIT, ROBIN, THRUSH, TURKEY, -->animals: BAT, DOG, FROG, FUR SEAL, RAT -->fish: COD, COWRY (shell), GURNARD, HAKE, LATCHET, MINNOW, SHARK (various), SMELT, SOLE, WHITEBAIT

## New Zealand Alliance
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The New Zealand Alliance Convention opened to-day, Mr Wesley Spragg (Auckland), president, in the chair. The report presented stated that the outbreak of war injuriously affected campaign work and the vote for prohibition and no-license. Approval was expressed of the refusal of the Government to allow wet canteens at Expeditionary Force camps. (Apr 14, 1915 -- ,Ashburton Guardian,2)

>  The liquor traffic, in the "Evening Post" of October 11, and in other papers, challenges the New Zealand Alliance to produce "any evidence that the American people have ever voted or had an opportunity of voting upon the question of National Prohibition." (Oct 21, 1922 -- ,NZ Truth,10)

>  Government acted thus, because after making a count they found that the publicans had more noses in the House than the teetotallers; and the object of the New Zealand Alliance for the Reform of the liquor laws was to let Mr Sheehan and those of his sort see that the teetotallers had as many noses in the House as the publicans. (Mar 25, 1879 -- ,Bruce Herald,5)

>  At the annual public meeting, held under the auspices of the New Zealand Alliance for the supression of the sale of liquor, resolutions were adopted pledging an offer of support in the endeavour to have the principle of prohibition represented in the next Parliament, protesting against compensation to publicans whose houses were closed by popular veto, and resolving to take steps for educating the people upon the temperance question ... (Dec 3, 1890 -- ,Evening Post,2)

>  Like Father Hays and the British Alliance, our grand ideal is: local option for all, total abstinence for the individual, and no-license , or prohibition for the State. This has been the paramount aim of the New Zealand Alliance since its inception. (May 11, 1905 -- ,West Coast Times,3)

>  ".. In the long run, though not now, the New Zealand Alliance may prove too strong for the publican party." (Jul 19, 1871 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,2)

>  He moved, "That this representative meeting of the New Zealand Alliance draws the attention of the Government to the request made by the Maoris at the congress held at Wellington in 1908, when the principle of local option should be extended to the Native race, and earnestly hopes that the numerous petitions presented by the Maoris to the Prime Minister will be favorably considered." (Jun 28, 1910 -- ,Poverty Bay Herald,6)

>  Bitter complaint was made.. as to the conduct of the New Zealand Alliance in regard to the Tauranga election. (Oct 8, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,19)

>  Some startling allegations as to the drinking habits of Maoris in Taranaki are contained in a petition presented to the House by Mr Atkinson from the Executive of the New Zealand Alliance. The petitioners forwarded with the petition a sworn declaration by the Rev. R. Haddon, of Hawera, Wesleyan Maori missionary, who is a half-caste, in reference to the disastrous effects of drink upon the Maoris of Taranaki. (Oct 4, 1901 -- ,Poverty Bay Herald,4)

>  Among the Members of Parliament pledged to the New Zealand Alliance are a number who represent "Continuance" constituencies. This, in itself, is sufficient indication of the undemocratic policy of the Alliance, in attempting to force its opinions over the heads of the people. (Oct 11, 1928 -- ,NZ Truth,10)

## New Zealand army worm
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  New Zealand Army Worm. Grass Caterpillar (<i>Mamestra composita</i>). Some of its habits greatly resemble those of the American army worm, noticeably the manner of advance from old feeding-grounds to new, marching like an army, sometimes many thousands strong. (1895 -- ,Ann. Rep. Dept of Agriculture,III/155)

>  New Zealand Army Worm, Grass Caterpillar (<i>Mamestra composita</i>) . . . During the last few years great complaints have been made regarding the ravages committed in pastures and in crops by armies of caterpillars, which march across the country, eating all before them. (1896 -- ,Leaflets for Farmers,No. 33/1)

## New Zealand cowrie

The New Zealand species is very scarce. (1947 -- ,Native Animals of NZ,27)

## New Zealand Cross
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  There were Crimean decorations there and New Zealand Crosses, as well as the more plentiful Maori War medal. (Aug 10, 1912 -- ,NZ Free Lance,4)

>  Now, therefore. His Excellency the Governor.. doth by this present Order institute a Decorative Distinction to be conferred on... Members of the Militia, Volunteers and Armed Constabulary, who may particularly distinguish themselves by their bravery in action, or devotion to their duty while on service... The decoration shall consist of a Silver Cross, with the name of the Colony and the name of the recipient engraved thereon. (1869 -- ,NZ Gazette,II.Mar.)

>  Major Ropata.. wore at his side the handsome sword presented to him by the Queen, and on his breast the New Zealand Cross. (May 28, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,22)

>  The public intimation of the Government intention to decorate Mr. ** with the New Zealand Cross has caused a very great amount of astonishment, indignation, and disgust, not only among the present holders of the New Zealand Cross, but also among Volunteers who rightly consider it their bounden duty to protect the dignity of this much-prized decoration. (1898 -- ,AJHR,H-31Q/1)

>  <b>COWAN</b> Mair was the only pakeha ... in the action that won him a captaincy and the New Zealand Cross. (1940 -- ,Sir Donald Maclean,102)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> NEW ZEALAND CROSS, 1869. [heading] A silver maltese cross with a six pointed star in gold upon each limb. ... Institution, Order of the Governor in Council, dated 10 March, 1869. New Zealand Gazette 1869, p. 127. [229] This cross was instituted by the Colonial Government and awarded to members of the local Militia, Volunteers, and Constabulary for acts of bravery and distinction. (1941 -- ,Numismatic History of NZ,228)

>  A rare New Zealand Cross, awarded for bravery during the New Zealand land wars, is the centre-piece of a major auction of coins and medals in Melbourne later this month. The cross, described as a non-military version of the Victoria Cross, is one of only 23 awarded to the militia, volunteers and armed constabulary during the wars, ... (Jul 3, 1984 -- ,Dominion,6)

>  His Excellency the Governor.. doth bythis present Order institute a DecorativeDistinction, to be conferred on membersof the Militia, Volunteers or ArmedConstabulary. who may particularlydistinguish themselves by their braveryin action, or devotion to their dutywhile on service... The decoration shallconsist of a Silver Cross, with the nameof the Colony and the name of therecipient engraved thereon. (1869 -- ,NZ Gazette,127)

>  The Speaker announced that he hadreceived from His Excellency the Governora message, transmitting the New ZealandCross of Honor (sic) Bill. (1989 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates,VI/662)



## New Zealand crow
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) kokako (See [kokako](../K#kokako))

>  <b>YATE</b> <i>Kokako</i>; called, by some, the New-Zealand crow. ... It has a strong black beak ... and a small brilliant light-blue flap hanging down on each side, from the ear. (1835 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),64)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> <i>29. Callaeas cinerea. Lath.</i> ... Kokako of the natives. - New Zealand crow. <i>Yate. Dieffenbach</i>. Kakako of the natives. <i>Polack. </i> (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,2/19) [NOTES: 1842 Gray's Fauna in 1843 Dieffenbach Travels in NZ II/191]

>  <b>DRURY</b> BIRDS MET WITH IN PELORUS ...New Zealand Crow - Kokako of the natives. (1855 -- ,Sailing Directions,65)

>  <b>HAAST</b> The first is the kakako (Callaeas cinerea), the New Zealand crow; generally a pair together; ... (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,139)

>  <b>HARPER</b> It [the Otira Pass area] is also the haunt of the New Zealand crow, a shapely bird, purple, shot with black, and reddish yellow rings under the eyes, very tame, ... (Jul 20, 1868 -- ,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,130,1914 ed.)

>  Callaeas cinerea. Kokako. New Zealand Crow. (1869 -- ,TrNZI,1/160) [NOTES: (Hutton Birds Great Barrier)]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Kookako</i>, n. <i>collaeas</i>[sic] <i>cinerea</i>, or <i>New Zealand crow. </i> (1871 -- ,Dictionary of the NZ Language,57)

>  In this forest ... are found ...the rarer <i>Glaucopis cinerea</i> or Kokako, commonly called the New Zealand Crow. (1872 -- ,TrNZI,4/206) [NOTES: (Travers NZ Birds)]

>  <b>BULLER</b> ... I have accordingly commenced my history of our avifauna with an account of the New-Zealand Crow. (1888 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,I/xvi)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Although the New Zealand Crow, the New Zealand Thrush were still to find [in Nelson province], Bush Canary at any rate were plentiful. (1936 -- ,NZ Naturalist,57)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Kokako,</i> n. <i>Callaeas cinerea cinerea</i> and <i>C. Cinerea wilsoni</i>, New Zealand crow. (1957 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,129)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> ... higher up still there was the ding dong of the New Zealand crow, a bird which I have never been lucky enough to see, and have heard a few times only on Te Aroha; ... (1973 -- ,Once Is Enough,14)

>  Sir James Hector, in drawing attention.. to a very fine specimen of the New Zealand crow or jay. as it should more properly be called, made interesting references to the complete isolation of the orange wattle crow, in the South Island, and the blue wattle crow in the North Island. (Dec 21, 1897 -- ,Otago Daily Times,10)

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> Higher up still there was the ding dong of the New Zealand crow, a bird which I have never been lucky enough to see, and have heard a few times only on Te Aroha. (1986 -- ,Countless Signs,165) [NOTES: From Frank Sargeson (1973) : Once is Enough]

## New Zealand Day
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) a short-lived replacement name for Waitangi Day

>  <b>HAYWARD</b> Mr K. announced at the Waitangi Day celebrations that henceforth it would be called New Zealand Day and become a public holiday,* `though I think that this will still often be called Waitangi Day rather than New Zealand Day. ...' [fn]* On 6 February 1976 the day again became officially known as Waitangi Day. (Feb 13, 1973 -- ,Diary of the Kirk Years,112,1981 ed.)



## New Zealand death
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) death by drowning, especially when crossing rivers on horseback [1800s]. [aka national death]

>  <b>PASCOE</b> Had he drowned, it would have been said that he had died 'the New Zealand death.' (1959 -- ,Great Days in NZ Exploration,67)

>  <b>PICKERING</b> Freshes were lethal for many early colonists and drowning became so commonplace that it was called the New Zealand death or New Zealand disease. (1988 -- ,Hills, The,23)

>  <b>IRELAND</b> The colonel's name does not appear on the list of drownings - the "New Zealand death' as it was called. (1998 -- ,Under the Bridge & Over the Moon,24)

>  Drowning was once termed "the New Zealand death" because in the young colony it became the biggest killer of settlers chancing swollen rivers like the Rakaia. (Jul 2000 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 47/86)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> In the early days .. so many persons were drowned that drowning was often described as 'The New Zealand Death.' (1947 -- ,Early Runholding in Otago,24)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> 'The New Zealand death' he knew was the old name for drowning in a river. (1984 -- ,Tales of the Coast,16)

>  <b>MCGILL</b> New Zealand death, the: drowning. (1989 -- ,Dinkum Kiwi Dictionary, The,71)

>  <b>Relph</b> Isolation was a real problem in the absence of roads, and increased by the danger of crossing the larger rivers. Indeed, drowning was known as 'the New Zealand death'. (2007 -- ,From Tussocks to Tourists,77)

>  Drowning has ben termed "the New Zealand death," and of the numbers who annually in New Zealand are the victims of drowning accidents, the Wanganui River and Casltecliff beach claim a fair share. (Jan 30, 1912 -- ,Wanganui Chronicle,4)

>  A river-ford death already figures in the holiday casualties. The fords of rivers generally claim a strong, efficient type of victim, because other types usually do not take a risk at an obviously tricky ford; but it was different in the roadless, bridgeless days, when all backblockers had to choose between the ford or remaining in the wilderness, and it was then that drowning in a river became known as "the New Zealand death." (Dec 27, 1935 -- ,Evening Post,6)

>  Fatalities to persons crossing rivers in this colony have been so frequent that they have not inaptly been classed under the heading of "The New Zealand Death." (Aug 15, 1903 -- ,Hawera & Normanby Star,2)



## New Zealand disease
 
 <i>n.</i> the anti-nuclear policy (from an American viewpoint)

>  The United States still fears the spread of the "New Zealand disease" and will not reactivate military links till New Zealand changes its anti-nuclear legislation. (Jan 1, 1997 -- ,Dominion,1)

## New Zealand English
 
 <i>n.</i> the distinctive accent, vocabulary, etc. of New Zealanders

>  NEW ZEALAND ENGLISH. Mr.E.W. Andrews, of the Napier Boys' High School, is an admitted authority on English. His presidential address, recently delivered at the conference in Wellington of the Secondary Schools' Assistant Teachers, is ... the more interesting. The subject was `New Zealand English'. (Aug 10, 1910 -- ,Triad, The,37)

>  <b>TURNER</b> Language in Australia and NZ 152 Mullock is less frequent and retains a specialist sense of `rubbish from a mine'. It is not common in general New Zealand English, but mullock rocks, `waste material from quartz mines', occurs in a West Coast context. (1966 -- ,English as it is spoken in NZ)

>  There are entries [in The Macquarie Dictionary] ... for Australian English, Australianism and Strine, but none for New Zealand English, New Zealandism or New Zild. 2.That variety of English spoken and written by native-born New Zealanders; the totality of English spoken and written; the English language as it is found in the speech and writing of native-born New Zealanders. by native-born New Zealanders. (Jan 21, 1984 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30) [NOTES: Deverson]



## New Zealand ermine
 
 <i>n.</i> fur from a stoat [A weasel has a black-tipped tail and brown fur which turns white in winter. In England it is called a stoat while it has its brown coat.]

>  <b>WODZICKI</b> [The increase in number of stoat skins supplied to the market] is due to ... secondly, the establishment of a modern fur dressing and dyeing plant in Dunedin. In consequence a new article of the fur trade called the New Zealand Ermine has been produced, and such coats are valued at 180 pounds each. (1950 -- ,Introduced Mammals of NZ,74)

## New Zealand falcon
 
 <i>n.</i> an indigenous bird of prey, of which there are three forms: the bush falcon, the eastern falcon, and the southern falcon.

>  The New Zealand Falcon/Karearea is classed as a single variable species divided into three forms, as described by Fox (1977). (2007 -- ,Internet - see Notes,1) [NOTES: www.canterburynature.org/species/lincoln_essays/nzfalcon.php]

>  <i>Falco novaeseelandiae</i>, New Zealand<i> Falcon</i> [check] (1948 -- ,NZ Forest Inhabiting Birds,16)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> NEW ZEALAND FALCON Falco novaeseelandiae <i>Other names</i>: Bush Hawk, Sparrow Hawk, Quail Hawk, Karearea. (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,99)

>  120. Falco novaeseelandiae Gmelin, 1788 <i>New Zealand Falcon (Karearea) </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,39,1980 ed.)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> The spot on Sugarloaf where weird old Jimmy Gilfillan had his hut and spent all his time shooting every sparrowhawk (New Zealand falcon) he could find, declaring: `They fly down and pick your eyes out.' (1981 -- ,Exiles of Asbestos Cottage, The,122)

>  <i>New Zealand Falcon Falco novaeseelandiae</i> GMELIN, 1788 ...[155] OTHER NAMES: <i>Karearea, sparrowhawk, bush hawk, quail hawk, eastern falcon, southern falcon, bush falcon. ... Bush falcon: In the North Island confined to the main ranges, ... Eastern falcon: The main ranges of the South Island ... Southern Falcon: Fiordland, the Auckland Islands, and Stewart Island. </i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,154)

>  The Raptor Association, through contact with pigeon clubs, has been told by several racing pigeon enthusiasts that they have shot native New Zealand falcons attacking their pigeons. (Aug 21, 1999 -- ,Evening Post,4)

>  A project to reintroduce the New Zealand falcon to Marlborough's Wairau Plains has Conservation Department approval and funding. (Jul 12, 2005 -- ,Dominion Post,A8)

## New Zealand fur seal
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Arctocephalus forsteri</i> (See [Kekeno](../K#kekeno))

>  Another interesting specimen is that of the New Zealand fur seal, placed in the case containing the bears, the skin being so perfect that it was thought desirable to keep it under cover; it was killed not long ago on the Ocean Beach by Mr R. Paulin. (Sep 14, 1888 -- ,Otago Witness,16)

>  I can only speak of the latter, or New Zealand fur-seal. Formerly they were very abundant along the west coast of the South Island and on the Tasmanian coast. (1893 -- ,TrNZI,25/257) [NOTES: (Hector)]

>  Seals [ed. fn.3] The New Zealand Fur Seal or Kekeno, <i>Arctocephalus forsteri</i> (Lesson). (Mar 27, 1773 -- ,Cook's Journals,2/111,1961 ed.)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> <i>[Arctocephalus forsteri] </i>The New Zealand fur seal is peculiar to this country, although very similar to a closely related form inhabiting southern Australia and Tasmania. (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/203)

>  <i>Arctocephalus forsteri</i> called the `New Zealand fur seal' (Apr 1987 -- ,National Geographic Magazine)

>  Despite being hunted to the edge of extinction in the 19th century, New Zealand fur seals seem to be making a modest recovery. (Nov 2001 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 54/34)

>  New Zealand fur seals were almost invisible against the rocks as they lay, warming up on the ledges after a morning's fishing. (Aug 14, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,E16)

## New Zealand grapefruit
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [goldfruit](../G#goldfruit))

>  When we were living [in South Africa] in the 1960s, we heard of a citrus orchard in Rustenberg that grew hybrod oranges named Rex Union oranges, famous for making excellent marmalade. We went to this pretty little country town and found an orchard named 'Dunedin' and bought a bag of the oranges... What surprised us was that the Rex Union oranges seemed to be identical to our New Zealand grapefruit. Many years later.. I was reading about Morrison's Orchard in Warkworth and was struck by the statement that the original grapefruit tree had come from Kawau Island and that Sir George Grey couldn't remember where he had got the plant. Grey had been Governor of Cape Province, South Africa, from 1854-1861 and was known to have brought many South African plants to New Zealand. Are 'New Zealand grapefruit', 'Rex Union oranges'? (Oct 26, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),40) [NOTES: Letter from Jill Williamson]

>  One Aucklander.. was unable to obtain a New Zealand grapefruit at any of the hotels at which he stayed. (Feb 9, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,78)

>  The New Zealand grapefruit is probably the most successfully grown orange in New Zealand. (Apr 27, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,79)

## New Zealand lemon sole
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> NEW ZEALAND LEMON SOLE <i> Peltotretis flavilatus </i> Waite. The Lemon Sole, like the Common Sole, Flounder, Brill and Turbot is a bottom-dwelling fish with both eyes on the upper surface of the body, known as the right side. This fish and the others mentioned are known to fishermen, auctioneers, retailers and the public generally as Flatfish. Skate and Electric-ray are also Flatfish, the upper and lower surfaces being the back and belly. (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,210)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> LEMON SOLE ...<i> Other names: Pelotretis flavilatus </i>; New Zeland lemon sole. (1966 -- ,Sea Anglers' Guide,219)

## New Zealand Medal
 
 <i>n.</i> [The New Zealand Medal (its correct name, not Maori War Medal) was awarded to those who had actually been under fire in any engagement from 1845 through 1847 or from 1860 through 1872. It was approved for award to British troops in 1869 and six months later extended to colonial forces.]

>  <b>TINNE</b> most of them distinguished themselves ... during the war, which are but ill rewarded by the universal New Zealand medal, and a most scanty pay. (1873 -- ,Wonderland of the Antipodes,32)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> THE NEW ZEALAND (MAORI WAR) MEDAL. [heading] ... The Gazette issued at Auckland on 23 October, 1869, p. 590, notifies the extension to the Colonial Forces of the grant for the decoration conferred `for the War in New Zealand to all persons whether belonging to the local forces or loyal native tribes, who were actually under fire in any engagement with the enemy, or were otherwise conspicuous for distinguished service in the field.' (1941 -- ,Numismatic History of NZ,228)

The orthodox Maori name for these reeds is pukakaho, but the white men called them `bukaka'. (1939 -- ,First White Boy Born in Otago,10)

## New Zealand pigeon
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) the NZ wood-pigeon, also called a dove, kereru any of a family of fast-flying birds with a compact body, small head and short legs. [wood pigeon, kereru see also native, New Zealand, wild pigeon <i>Hemiphaga novaeseelandiae</i>, bush pigeonwood-pigeon; kuku, kukupa; kereru; (white-)breaster] (See [wood pigeon](../W#wood-pigeon),[kereru](../K#kereru))

>  <b>FORSTER</b> In the morning ... I went ... to the Indian Cove, & shot there two new Kingfishers [[ed. fn] Now the new Zealand Kingfisher ...], some new yellow headed Flycatchers [[ed. fn] Now the Yellowhead <i>Mohoua ochrocephala</i> (Gmelin, 1789).], a fine large green and brown Pigeon [[ed. fn] Now the New Zealand pigeon ...] ..., a small Perrokeet, green with a red forehead ... [ed. fn] Now the Red-crowned Parakeet ... (Apr 2, 1773 -- ,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/245,1982 ed.) [NOTES: 1773 May 11 see also HAWK]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> A very fine species of crayfish (<i>cancer homarus</i> Lin.) longer than the lobster, some shell-fish, and ... a cormorant, duck, pigeon, or parrot gave us an agreeable variety at our table ... (1777 -- ,Voyage Round the World,1/144)

>  <b>SAV AGE</b> ...the only [bird] species I saw that struck me as new, was a pigeon, of beautiful plumage, large size, and delicious eating. (1807 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),10)

>  <b>CRUISE</b> We saw a few small birds of very rich plumage, and shot some wild pigeons. (Mar 14, 1820 -- ,Journal,56,1957 ed.) [NOTES: 1823 (1820 March 14) Cruise Journal (1957) 56]

>  <b>BISCOE</b> [At Chathams] to my great surprise saw a large Black Cat, I likewise shot some very large white & blue pigeon. (Dec 2, 1831 -- ,Journal) [NOTES: 1831 Dec 2 J.Biscoe Journal in 1913 McNab Old Whaling Days 420]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> All were very kind; they had prepared a feast of Pigeons, <i>Pipis</i> and <i>Kumara.</i> (May 21, 1833 -- ,Early History of the Dovedale Valley,315,1961 ed.)

>  <b>MAY</b> The Pigeon or Wood Quest (Kukupa) is a very beautiful bird. (1840 -- ,Journal of Ensign Best,222,1966 ed.)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> The pigeon is larger than the English, and far handsomer. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,9/138)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> In the lower regions the forest was much enlivened by birds. I shot some parrots [fn Nestor meridionalis. Kaka of the natives.], pigeons [fn Columba spadicea, Lath. Kukupa.], and wattle-birds [fn Glaucopis cinerea, Gmel. Kokako.]. Among the branches of the trees hopped the neat fantail flycatcher [fn Muscicapa flabellifera, Gml. Piwakawaka.], and a social bird of a yellow colour, which has been depicted by Forster [fn Orthornyx heteroclytus, Lef. Popokatea.], and which had much the habits of the finch. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/29)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Several kinds of birds are indigenous to the woods and waters; among them the oyster-catcher, bittern, kingfisher, cormorant, quail, wild-duck, mocking or [16] parson bird, parrots, parroquets, woodhen, pigeon, etc. (the [sic] gigantic Moa bird is believed to be now quite extinct). (1867 -- ,Twelve Years in Canterbury,15)

>  I noticed the following:- Pigeon, tui, korimako, kaka, ruru, piwakawaka, toutouwai, kingfisher, duck and teal in the lakes, and pukeko in the swamp, and various sea birds. (1884 -- ,TrNZI,17/419) [NOTES: Gold-Smith) [pl]]

>  54. <i>Carpophaga novae-zealandiae</i>. Pigeon, Kuku. (Jul 1885 -- ,NZ Journal of Science,483) [NOTES: (T.H. Potts)]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Kuku,</i> n. <i>pigeon; </i> (1892 -- ,Dictionary of the NZ Language,78)

>  <i>pigeon</i> ... (also <i>New Zealand pigeon</i>) green and bronze NZ bird. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,568) [NOTES: [cf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 857]]

>  The stillness is unbroken save by the soft note of the pigeon. (Oct 11, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,7)

>  A wild pigeon had just been feeding on berries that seemed to have been steeped in turpentine. (Dec 17, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> Pigeons live on berries of different Trees, ...<i>Ibid.</i> 56 When in the Woods she looked out for the (Coo Coupers) [<i>kukupa</i>] or Pigeons, and had a sharp eye. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),34,1963 ed.)

>  <b>McDONNELL</b> The <i>Pigeon</i> and <i>Zooe</i> are perhaps the handsomest - the former is really a magnificent bird - almost as large as a barn-door fowl, and equally invites the attention of the Ornothologist and the epicurean - the latter has two feathery [35] wattles pendent from its ear, and some few of its notes are not unlike those of the wattle bird of Van Diemen's Land. (1834 -- ,Extracts From a Journal,34)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) kereru [ORIGIN: Maori] (See [kereru](../K#kereru))

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> ... the berries of which form the favourite food of the beautiful New Zealand pigeon, the plumage of which displays all the colours of the rainbow. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/27)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> ... one of the beautiful New Zealand pigeons (<i>carpophaga Novae Selandicae</i>), ... would flutter away into the darker recesses of the forest. (1847 -- ,Savage Life,2/30)

>  <b>ELDER</b> The New Zealand pigeons are as small as half-grown fowls. We had them dressed and had a plentiful dinner and something to spare. (Aug 3, 1819 -- ,Marsden's Lieutenants) [NOTES: 1819 Aug 3 William Hall in Elder Marsden's Lieutenants (1934) 223]

>  <b>McDONNELL</b> The New Zealand pigeon is as splendidly beautiful in plumage as it is exquisitely delicious to the taste. (1834 -- ,Extracts From a Journal,16,1979 ed.)

>  <b>BULLER</b> CARPOPHAGA NOVAE ZEALANDIAE. (NEW-ZEALAND PIGEON.) ... <i>Native names</i>. Kuku, Kukupa, and Kereru. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,157,1888 2nd edn 1/229 ed.)

>  <b>DURRELL</b> [NZ Pigeon]. (1966 -- ,Two in the Bush,45)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> NEW ZEALAND PIGEON <i>Hemiphaga novaeseelandiae Maori names</i>: Kereru, kuku,kukupa (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,168)

>  <i>211.a. Hemiphaga novaeseelandiae novaeseelandiae</i> (Gmelin, 1789) <i>New Zealand Pigeon (Kereru) </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,57,1980 ed.)

>  An interesting feature is the number of New Zealand birds - the kukupa, or New Zealand pigeon, the tui, kaka, and others. (Jan 11, 1897 -- ,Evening Post,5)

## New Zealand pine
 
 <i>n.</i> an old word for kauri(-pine).

>  From the great increase of population at Melbourne, and the consequent demand for house room, very large supplies of timber are required; and the great superiority of New Zealand pine over the hard woods of Australia, from the facility with which it is worked, is so great a consideration where carpenter's wages are 20s a day, as to cause it to be in universal request. (May 4, 1853 -- ,New Zealand Spectator & Cook's Strait Guardian,2)

>  .. New Zealand pine, assorted, 9s 3d to 9s 9d. (May 3, 1870 -- ,Wellington Independent,3)

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> ... the Sawn Plank is very cheap in the Colonies and lately there is some prejudice against the New Zealand Pine. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),48) [NOTES: see also p. 33]

>  <b>McDONNELL</b> Her spars, one and all, were of New [19] Zealand pine (cawdie); they were faultless. (1834 -- ,Extracts From a Journal,18,1979 ed.)

>  <b>MATHEW</b> ... here I first saw the famed Koudi tree which is now so extensively used for masts and spars under the name of New Zealand pine. It is a magnificent tree certainly, but not at all like our ideas of Pine, as it has a fine branching head quite different to any of the fir species. The Coudie or Courie [<i>kauri</i>], as it is called, the New Zealand Pine, growing 80 or 90 feet without a branch .... (Apr 25, 1840 -- ,Journal) [NOTES: 1840 Apr 25 Sarah Mathew Journal in 1940 Founding of NZ 126]

>  In the new duties on timber New Zealand pine is exempt, and 1s 6d per hundred lineal feet is imposed on boardings. (Aug 1, 1892 -- ,Hawke's Bay Herald,3)

>  It is estimated that the tariff will affect butter manufacturers using New Zealand pine to the extent of aGBP10,000 per annum. (Aug 15, 1907 -- ,Evening Post,7)

>  Her spars, one and all, were of New Zealand Pine (cowdie) [sic]; they were faultless. (Jul 18, 1840 -- ,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,3)

>  We are glad to hear that 25,000 feet of New Zealand Pine will be shipped from here in the <i>Mary Anne</i> to Sydney. (Jul 5, 1845 -- ,New Zealand Spectator & Cook's Strait Guardian,2)



## New Zealand pipit
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BULLER</b> ANTHUS NOVAE ZEALANDIAE. (NEW -ZEALAND PIPIT.) ... Native names. Pihoihoi and Whioi; `Ground-Lark' of the colonists. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,132,1888 2nd edn 1/63 ed.)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> <i>The Ground Lark</i> There are several species ... that may be called homestead birds, such as the New Zealand Pipit, or Ground Lark, the Waxeye, the Warbler and the Fantail. (1927 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,133)

>  <b>POWELL</b> NEW ZEALAND PIPIT (<i>Anthus novaeseelandiae</i>), also called the ground lark, is a common bird throughout the country. (1947 -- ,Native Animals of NZ,86)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> NEW ZEALAND PIPIT <i>Anthus novaeseelandiae Other names</i>: Pihoihoi, Sandlark (Chathams). ... The Skylark, with which it is commonly confused, lives in pasture land where the Pipit is generally absent, but the ranges of the two species overlap in rough open country, etc. (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,216)

>  <i>246.a. Anthus novaeseelandiae novaeseelandiae</i> (Gmelin, 1789) <i>New Zealand Pipit (Pihoihoi) </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,65,1980 ed.)

>  <i>Pipit Anthus novaeseelandiae</i> (GMELIN, 1789) ...<i> New Zealand Pipit A.n. novaeseelandiae</i> (GMELIN, 1789). <i>Auckland Islands Pipit A.n. aucklandicus</i> GRAY, 1862. <i>Antipodes Pipit A.n. steindachneri</i> REISCHEK, 1889. ... OTHER NAMES: <i>Pihoihoi, groundlark, native lark. </i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,270)

>  <b>Moon</b> The New Zealand pipit is widely distributed throughout the country. It inhabits rough pastures, riverbeds, sandy beaches and forest margins. (2002 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,111)

## New Zealand quail
 
 <i>n.</i> koreke (extinct)

>  The rapid disappearance of the New Zealand quail from this island is a subject of great regret to our sportsmen. (Jul 7, 1866 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,5)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> ... they [dogs] are perhaps the cause that the New Zealand quail (Coturnix Novae Zelandiae, Quoy et Gaim.) is so scarce in the northern island, - and also the guana. (1843 -- ,Travel in NZ,i/417)

>  <b>BULLER</b> COTURNIX NOVAE ZEALANDIAE. (NEW-ZEALAND QUAIL.) ... <i>Native names</i>.- Koreke {and Kokoreke}. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,1/161, 1/225)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Koitareke n. New Zealand Quail; coturnix Novae Zelandiae. </i> (1871 -- ,Dictionary of the NZ Language,57) [NOTES: 3rd ed.]

>  <b>WHITE</b> The New Zealand Quail, which was very plentiful, is nearly extinct now: Bush-fires have burnt it, and dogs, cats, and rats have eaten it, but the Australian and Californian varieties have filled its place. (1905 -- ,My NZ Garden,69)

>  Related very closely to the still surviving Australian stubble quail, the New Zealand quail was once common in parts of the North and South Islands. Being a species living mainly in grasslands, it was more abundant in the South Island on its eastern side than in the North where its distribution was far more localised. By the mid-nineteenth century the koreke, as it was known to the Maori, was already uncommon in the North Island, though at about the same time it was still abundant in parts of Nelson and Otago. By 1870 it was regarded as having been completely exterminated in the North, and five years later the South Island populations had vanished too. (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/7)

>  124 <i>Coturnix novaezelandiae novaezelandiae</i> Quoy and Gaimard, 1830 New Zealand Quail ... extinct since about 1870.(Koreke) (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,40) [NOTES: 1980 ed.]

>  The New Zealand quail (Coturnix novae-zealandiae) is often cited as showing how rapidly a species will become extinct. (1889 -- ,TrNZI,21/207)

## New Zealand robin
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment)

>  The New Zealand robin (curiously the South Island and not the North Island variety) hopped round the traveller freely. (Oct 20, 1906 -- ,Star, The,4)

>  Across the path ... comes the bush, full of birds singing all day, tuis and bell-birds and New Zealand robins ... (Sep 8, 1851 -- ,Letters from Canterbury, NZ) [NOTES: 1851 Sep 8 C. Godley ?Letters from Canterbury in 1947 Reed Story of Canterbury 100]

>  <b>DRURY</b> BIRDS MET WITH IN PELORUS ... New Zealand Robin - common in the bush (1855 -- ,Sailing Directions,66)

>  <b>HAAST</b> Amongst the small inhabitants ... is the Totoara, the New Zealand robin, which is the first to welcome the explorer in the wilderness, and which remains steadily near the camp. (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,140)

>  <b>BARKER</b> Many of [the native birds] have English names, which I found very tantalising, especially when the New Zealand Robin was announced, and I could only see a fat little ball of a bird, with a [94] yellowish-white breast. (1866 -- ,Station Life in NZ,93)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Toutou (<i>Miro longipes</i>) is a small grey and white bird, which some people have said is called the New Zealand robin. It is to be seen in the bush now and then, and seems tame, but <i>we</i> prefer to call another species <i>our</i> robin. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/224)

>  PETROICA. - New Zealand Robin. ... The song is very melodious, especially that of <i>Petroica longipes</i> (North Island Wood Robin). (1886 -- ,TrNZI,18/102) [NOTES: (Reischek)]

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> NEW ZEALAND ROBINS (<i>Petroica australis</i>, ... ) are divided into various subspecies: the North Island <i>longipes</i> is a bit smaller and duller, and rather more mottled, than the South Island <i>australis</i>, the Stewart Island one is<i> rakiura</i>. The Chatham Island robin is <i>P.traversi. </i> (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,279)
