## New Zealand snail
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) ['predication; in cit.? (predation?)]

>  Life is tough for the New Zealand snail. <i>Powelliphanta marchanti</i> is one of many large endemic carnivorous snails whose numbers are in decline due to habitat loss and predication from introduced pests. (Jul 2005 -- ,Conservation Action,23)



## New Zealand spinach
 
 <i>n.</i> a spinach-like edible native plant, Tetragonia tetragonoides (formerly expansa) or T. trigyna

>  For example, so-called New Zealand spinach (<i>Tetragonia tetragonoides</i>), whose seeds are still sold by that name as a native vegetable, is a strand plant found naturally from Japan south across the Pacific to Australia and New Zealand. (Mar 2001 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 50/24)

>  New Zealand spinach has more bulk than other varieties. (Sep 27, 1999 -- ,Dominion,9)

>  GARDEN SEEDS. PEAS, broad beans, dwarf French beans ... and New Zealand spinach. (Jul 27, 1859 -- ,Lyttelton Times,8)

>  New Zealand spinach, which is supposed to be hardier than ordinary spinach and is used as a substitute for it, is in every way inferior. (Oct 13, 1928 -- ,Evening Post,20)

>  The subject of soil management for intensive cropping with vegetables, fruit, herbaceous borders, and lawns, was then dealt with more fully, and vegetables specially suitable for a sandy coastal soil were mentioned, with a special reference to the New Zealand spinach. (Jul 17, 1935 -- ,Evening Post,20)

>  New Zealand spinach does well now if the seeds are soaked first in hot water. (Dec 29, 1941 -- ,Evening Post,8)

>  ..the sea celery.. for example, tastes similar to our own, while Australia's warrigal greens are one and the same as our New Zealand spinach. (Oct 25, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,E6)

>  What we call kokihi or rengamutu you probably know better as New Zealand spinach or Warrigal cabbage (<i>Tetragonia tetragonoides</i>). (Oct 2000 -- ,Growing Today,37)

>  New Zealand spinach may be sown now, or in some localities plants may be obtained from the sea beach. (Sep 1, 1870 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,3)

>  For the first time in the Colony we have seen New Zealand spinach, a very fine parcel of which was exhibited by Mr. Hendry. (Jan 30, 1863 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,3)

>  New Zealand spinach, Tetragonia trigyna, a succulent plant native to the Chatham Islands. was boiled and eaten by whalers for a month to rid them of scurvy. (Aug 1991 -- ,NZ Gardener,29)

>  Among the species being grown in the new reserves are <i>Muehlenbackia astonii</i> (shrubby tororaro), <i>Euphorbia glauca</i> (shore spurge, thought to have been extinct in the Wellington region for 20 years, then rediscovered in 1996/96 on a rocky stack off Kapiti Island), <i>Discaria tomentosa</i> (a species of anchor plant, one of Wellington's - and the North Island's - rarest plants), <i>Hebe speciosa</i> (showy hebe) and <i>Tetragonia tetragonioides</i> (New Zealand spinach). (Jan 2004 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 67/11)

>  <b>CROWE</b> (New Zealand spinach is found on coastal sands, dunes and stony beaches; it does not have a red, fleshy fruit, but a dry seed capsule)... While beach spinach is native only to this country, New Zealand spinach is , in spite of its name, native also to Tasmania, Australia, the Pacific Islands, Japan and southern South America, having been used as a vegetable in south Brazil, Mauritius, Tongatapu and Kanaky (New Caledonia). It is eaten also as a vegetable side dish in Indonesia (especially on Java), where it is sometimes grown for sale. (1995 -- ,Which Coastal Plant?,30)

>  New Zealand spinach is a native plant that Captain Cook used to treat his crew for scurvy. It is a coastal plant that scrambles on sand dunes, and will thrive in a dry sunny corner of your garden. (May 1, 2008 -- ,NZ Herald,D2)

## New Zealand storm petrel
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The eminent ornithologist W.R.B. Oliver was one of the few dissenters, opting for full specific status for the New Zealand storm petrel in <i>The Birds of New Zealand</i>, where he wrote that as Wilson's storm petrel is "only a straggler to New Zealand and is not known, where it is found in abundance, to produce colour varieties, for the present I treat <i>maorianus</i>, of which three very similar specimens have been taken, as a separate species". (Jul 2004 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 68/11)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  [He] suggested that it looked like a New Zealand storm petrel which was thought to have become extinct 150 years ago. (Jan 28, 2004 -- ,Capital Times,7)

>  Scientific confirmation of the return of the New Zealand storm petrel, or <i>Oceanites maorianus</i>, is still awaited. (Dec 27, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,A3)

>  The eminent ornithologist W.R.B. Oliver was one of the few dissenters, opting for full specific status for the New Zealand storm petrel in <i>The Birds of New Zealand</i>, where he wrote that as Wilson's storm petrel is "only a straggler to New Zealand and is not known, where it is found in abundance, to produce colour varieties, for the present I treat <i>maorianus,</i> of which three very similar specimens have been taken, as a separate species". (Jul 2004 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 68/11)

## New Zealand War(s)
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> the series of armed clashes between Maori and Pakeha in the 19th century. [New Zealand War (singular) in 19th-c. citations below] (See [Land Wars](../L#land-wars),[Maori War(s)](../M#maori-war(s)))

>  ...there have been, he suggests, a number of worthwhile books about the New Zealand Wars. (Jul 29, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),9)

>  <b>BARBER</b> But it was not until the Second New Zealand War, the so-called land war, that the Queen's Maori soldiers came into their own. (1984 -- ,Red Coat to Jungle Green,38)

>  Their simple act of wading ashore onto the western banks of the independent Te Rohe Potae effectively ended the complex New Zealand Wars. (Oct 4, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> I know there are people in England who say that the New Zealand War was purely agrarian, arising out of disputed lordship and ownership of the soil. (1871 -- ,Autobiography,59)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> I know there are people in England who say that the New Zealand War was purely agrarian, arising out of disputed lordship and ownership of the soil. (1871 -- ,Autobiography,59)

>  <b>DOMETT</b> Having a little discussion about the New Zealand war, and to whose fault, that of the colonists or to the Maories, it was attributable; ...<i>Ibid.</i> 107 Thanking me in his letter [the Governor, Sir George Bowen] went on to urge me to `write a novel on the Maories and the New Zealand war' saying that he had pressed the same thing on Anthony Trollope. (Aug 28, 1873 -- ,Diary,102,1953 ed.)

>  <b>GRACE</b> Preface: This sketch of the Maori War ... A Sketch of the New Zealand War -<i>Ibid.</i> 169The New Zealand War lasted about ten years. (1899 -- ,Sketch of the NZ War,title page)

>  <b>COWAN</b> There is at least one sense, however, in which Cowan can be said to be ahead of his time. He steadfastly clung to the term `New Zealand Wars'. He rejected the more popular but misleadingexpression `Maori Wars', which implied that Maoris alone were responsible for the outbreaks of conflict. From the 1960s historians have explored more neutral and more accurate terms to characterise the period. Some opted for `Land Wars', but this is not entirely satisfactory because issues were involved in addition to that of land. Others suggested `Anglo-Maori Wars' but this too is less than adequate; the English were not the only Europeans involved and. as already noted, Maoris fought on both sides. For this latter reason `Maori-Pakeha Wars' is also unsuitable. ... If they are referred to as the New Zealand Wars they cannot be confused with any others, particularly if earlier actions involving Maoris alone are designated `tribal wars'. (1922 -- ,NZ Wars, The,xi,1983 ed.) [NOTES: 1983 Michael King in 1922 Cowan NZ Wars (1983) xi]

>  <b>BARBER</b> Maori, Colonial and Imperial participation in the New Zealand wars in the 1840s and 1860s, ... marked the first 50 years of the army's life. (1984 -- ,Red Coat to Jungle Green,8)

>  But the most notable of the grievances was the raupatu - the confiscation of Maori land during the New Zealand wars of the 1860s in Taranaki, Waikato, Bay of Plenty, and the East Coast. (1986 -- ,Na To Hoa Aroha,1/33) [NOTES: Sorrenson]



## New Zealand Wars

## New Zealand whisper
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>PEGLER</b> They had had a most enjoyable tea, and he thanked those ladies who had kindly prepared it. They had been favoured with beautiful weather, and by their visit to the Castle they had learned something more about their history. He thanked them again most heartily, and said he wished their two Maori chiefs were present to give them their native song, but they had left the country. But they would endeavour to sing in the Maori tongue `For he's a jolly good fellow.' Led by Mr. Bray, the New Zealanders then sang `For he's a jolly good fellow ' with much effect. They then gave the `New Zealand Whisper' for the ladies, after which `God Save the King' was sung by all present, Mr. Naylor (of St. George's Chapel Choir ) leading. Three cheers were next given for the visitors, finishing with an `English whisper' for the New Zealand ladies. A most enjoyable time then terminated with all joining in `Auld Lang Syne'. (1926 -- ,Dominion Bowlers' Book,48 (?18))

## New Zealand willow
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Hebe salicifolia </i> ? [New Zealand willow; obsolete term for koromiko but see alt usage eg Papers Past Evening Post, Feb 15, 1930 - re trees with diameter 28".]

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> <i>V[eronica] Salicifolia</i>, Forst. ... In allusion to its frequent habit and habitat - on the banks of streams - it is locally known as the New Zealand `Willow', (Buchanan.) The Otago settlers also call it `Korimuka', (Martin,) - `Korumeek,' (Hector,) - properly spelled `Koromiko' - a term also applied to <i>V. parviflora</i>, Vahl. ... and perhaps in general, to all <i>shrubby</i> species of the genus. (1868 -- ,Contributions to NZ Botany,68)

>  Mr Curlett brought under the notice of the Committee a cricket bat, manufactured by Mr J. Hoole, out of New Zealand willow. (Nov 26, 1892 -- ,Star, The,3)

>  It somewhat resembles a New Zealand willow, but its leaves are thicker and of a yellowish green colour. (Feb 17, 1909 -- ,Otago Witness,83)

>  Four tons of new Zealand willow, from the Masterton district, were shipped to Messrs. a Dye and Sons,Sydney, by the Maunganui yesterday afternooon. It is understood that the willow, which has been cut into lengths of about 2ft 6in, is in the nature of a trial shipment, and will be used in the making of cricket bats. (Feb 15, 1930 -- ,Evening Post,10)

## New Zealander
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) a Maori

>  <b>MACKIE</b> A customhouse boat came off to us manned by N. Zealanders or as they are called here Maoris (pronounced Mouries). (1973 -- ,Traveller under Concern,72) [NOTES: Quote from 30/1/1853]

>  Prior to the arrival of European navigators such as Abel Tasman, Captain Cook and De Surville, the indigenous people of New Zealand had no one word for themselves as an ethnic group. Instead, they were known by their tribal names of Tuhoe, Ngapuhi, Ngati Porou, etc. For recording purposes this was too cumbersome, so in their writings the navigators referred to the inhabitants of the country as New Zealanders. The closest concept the people had for such an all-encompassing label was tangata whenua, people of the land. But this did not come into general use until later as it had a more specific meaning in tribal relations between hosts and guests. (May 14, 1990 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),100)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Chris Pugsley suggested one reason for Gallipoli's importance to us was that it was the first time large numbers of us had heard ourselves called New Zealanders and that simple fact changed out view of ourselves. (Apr 27, 2005 -- ,Capital Times,Vol 30 / No 29 / p19)

>  I observed that the term `New Zealander' is already employed in this district; only, in lieu of `Noui-Tireni", as the natives of the Bay of Islands pronounce it, they say `Noui-Tirangui' [NuiTirangi] [sic], which gives the word more of an indigenous sound. The word pakeha serves themto signify all whites, whom they also call `Iouropi' (Europeans). I did not observe that they had a special name for the English. (1909 -- ,TrNZI,41/139) [NOTES: Smith transl. D'Urville's Visit to Tolaga Bay in 1827]

## New Zealandisation
 
 <i>n.</i> the process of phasing out JOINT VENTURES between New Zealand and foreign fishing companies that followed the declaration of the 200-mile exclusive economic zone, and entailing the acquisition and operation of deep-sea fishing vessels by New Zealand companies.

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> In 1982 the government took its legislative pen and struck foreign vessels and joint ventures from the tax-credit list ... It was also part of the New Zealandisation process, applying additional pressure on joint ventures to make a commitment to the industry.<i>Ibid.</i> 351 By 1981 the major fishing companies ... were building up substantial fleets. Initially, much of their catching capacity had been through joint ventures, but increasingly they used their own vessels ... While this investment set the foundation for the New Zealandisation of the deepwater fishery, it increased pressure on the inshore fishery. (2004 -- ,Hooked,278)

>  Talley is known as a suporter of what the industry calls "New Zealandisation" replacing foreign vessels and crew with domestic capacity. (Sep 19, 2004 -- ,Sunday Star Times,D1)

>  He said it was an error to assume "New Zealandisation equates with the natural interest" and wrong to argue that "New Zealand ownership of all quota, no matter what the circumstances, will automatically be in the national interest." (Feb 7, 2002 -- ,Evening Post,2)

>  He has long been an advocate of what the industry calls "New Zealandisation" - building up New Zealand's fishing capability to the point where foreign capacity is required only at the margins. (Sep 5, 2004 -- ,Sunday Star Times,D1)

>  We have to be careful in this area of New Zealandisation of fisheries ... New Zealandisation is at the heart of this Bill. Hon Colin Moyle: This question of New Zealandisation [rather than foreign ownership] of fisheries ... (Jul 15, 1986 -- ,NZ Parliamentary Debates) [NOTES: H. of R. debate on Bill concerning overseas fishery interests (11.25 pm) [check Hansard: on privatisation]

>  <b>FRAME</b> They now say streams. And fields. And the Minister of Agriculture has been talking of the New Zealandisation of Fisheries! (1988 -- ,Carpathians, The,30)

(1835 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),229)

>  Had Mr Mills possessed a very nice sense of honour he would have curbed the eagerness of his young New Zealandism and perhaps of something else in this particular instance. (Jun 2, 1888 -- ,Southland Times,2)

>  With the press and public men preaching assiduously the creed of "New Zealandism," the manufacturers bestirred themselves. (Jun 4, 1908 -- ,Evening Post,3)



## New Zealandness
 
 <i>n.</i> the essential New Zealand character of a person or thing.

>  This book, beautifully written and perceptive, only confirms the place that his work has already achieved as a representation of New Zealandness in a tradition of New Zealand literature. (May 8, 1999 -- ,Dominion,22)

>  Publishers around the world had turned it [the novel <i> Coal Flat </i>] down because of its great length and "New Zealandness". (Apr 2010 -- ,North & South,96)

>  An immigrant heritage means we're not "common" Kiwis. Immigrantness is invisible from New Zealandness. (Nov 30, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),17)

>  Amid the turmoil of the WRC disaster I realised how much this 'New Zealandness' means to me and how I'm the product of a Kiwi upbringing rooted in human values. (Sep 8, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,A5)

>  .. a situation of Kiwiana as marginal differentiation in a postmodern late capitalist international culture ('New Zealandness' as the egg and beetroot in the McDonald's burger) - that is at least one way to describe the path of New Zealand writing as it has dealt with New Zealand cultural identity, at least as I have been experiencing it. (Sep 2000 -- ,English in Aotearoa,12)

>  <b>FRAME</b> I was much influenced by the West Indian writers and, feeling inadequate in my New Zealand-ness (for did I not come from a land then described as `more English than England'?) (1985 -- ,Envoy from Mirror City,19)

>  The interviewer puts his set questions on New Zealandness in poetry and on the literary influence of James K. Baxter and does not steer himself into prominence as much as he doeswhen he gets under way. (Aug 1986 -- ,Islands,37/80)

>  Tired notions of nationalism are paraded, so the building is ... not just a work of art but the first building to be true to this place, to capture the `real' New Zealandness of New Zealand. (Jun 4, 1985 -- ,Dominion,11)

>  The way he treated his patients, the way he put all the different ranks together, his straight-forward approach - he never lost his New Zealandness. (Jul 21, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),51)

>  We have a lot of brands in the market now which are New Zealand geographical indicators in their own right, or have a particular New Zealandness about them, such as Kikorangi or Aorangi [cheeses]. (Sep 30, 2002 -- ,Dominion Post,C1)

>  I worked then, as now, through literature, and nothing I wrote gives a clue to my native "New Zealandness" because New Zealand was not part of literature of my compulsions. (May 22, 1982 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),86)

>  It's partly the fact that we don't dilute our New Zealandness overseas that makes us attractive to those audiences. (Feb 4, 1991 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26)

## New Zealish
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  Some determinedly "New Zealish" writers thought London effete and Bloomsbury downright decadent; it seemed unlikely that the London Library was high on their list of desirable places to be near. (Jan 7, 1991 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),6)



## New Zild
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand; New Zealand English [two senses mixed in citations below]

>  A different kind of abbreviation of the primary name is found in <i>New Zild</i> (also <i>Newzild, Noo Zild</i>), a conventional representation of the broad, maximally elided Kiwi pronunciation, which is traced in <i>DNZE</i> to the book <i>New Zild and How to Speak It</i> 1966, Arch Acker's answer to <i>Strine</i>, where <i>New Zild</i> is used both for the accent and for the country itself (although <i>DNZE</i> cites it as the name of the country only from the 1990s). Other (less clipped) renderings of the broad New Zild pronunciation recorded in <i>DNZE</i> include <i>New Zillun(d)</i> and <i>Noo Zilland</i> and, more idosyncratically, <i>NyaZilnd, N'yerzillun</i>, and <i>Newzyullind</i>. All such forms normally have either a jocular or a judgmental implication. (Aug 2000 -- ,NZWords,4 : 2)

>  But Australia is Oz and New Zealand is Newzild and I don't think we should confuse the two - particularly the accents. (Aug 2, 1998 -- ,Sunday Star Times,A9)

>  The other old favourites (Cooch, Cheeky, Hunk, Spit, Irish Murphy ...) are joined by some nasties (Vernon the Vermin, King of the Rats, and the deliciously vicious croco-pigs) to add flavour to tiny-town Newzild. (Jan 31, 1987 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),24)

>  In buying Newzild the council was setting an example to other quangos and to private enterprise. (Oct 10, 1986 -- ,National Business Review,10)

>  Witi Ihimaera, the author of Whale Rider, says 'I'm amazed at the transformational possibilities that Pacific creativity has offered little old New Zild. ..." (Feb 2006 -- ,Metro,Issue 296: 52-57)

>  However, I was unaware that the definition of Newzild had changed to "accent of the neurotically thin-skinned who are above criticism"... I will, however, brush up on my politically correct Maori pronunciation - Taipoo, Fangaray, O'armaroow, Fakatarnay; and at least beogin prectising Newzild - Unvucargoow, Heemoowtun, Weelungtun, Kroistcheerch. But what will Hyacinth Bucket think? (Jun 9, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10)

>  [Hdg] NewZild rules... The Kiwi accent is a unique, developing and ever-changing one. (Dec 19, 1998 -- ,Dominion,18)

>  There's no mistaking novelist Ronald Hugh Morrieson territory - small-town, postwar (1949) Newzild, where the outside world arrives as parody, flashy and phoney, with feet of clay already cracking. (Aug 31, 1985 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),40)

>  It aims to be a musical satire of corporationist attitudes ruining Seacliffe, a previously idyllic rural town in the backblocks of Noo Zild. (Jun 8, 1993 -- ,Salient,22)

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> One mustn't be over-romantic, but I can barely overemphasise the significance of First Return in the literature of New Zild. (1980 -- ,All My Lives,120) [NOTES: 1980 Imogen De La Bere in Thompson All My Lives]

>  <b>ARCH ACKER</b> Newzilders speak Newzild. (1966 -- ,New Zild,7)

>  There are entries [in The Macquarie Dictionary] ... for Australian English, Australianism and Strine, but none for New Zealand English, New Zealandism or New Zild. (Jan 21, 1984 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30) [NOTES: Deverson]

>  <b>OVENDEN</b> `That's French slang for a cigarette. I learned it today. I,m going to use it in Godzone and make it part of Newzild.' (1986 -- ,O.E.,144)

>  `For example, Chris McVeigh ... couldn't tell jokes unless he put on a cockney accent ... [sic] he couldn't time New Zild.' (meaning the accent.) (Sep 1986 -- ,Metro,35)

>  <b>McGILL</b> New Zild New Zealand the way others hear us say it, allegedly through gritted teeth. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,75)

>  If you want to know how it feels to have made that journey from homophobic suburban Newzild to a tolerant new century, read <i>Long Loop Home</i>. (Jun 30, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),62)

>  [Hdg] Forget the Europop, this is New Zild (Aug 24, 2001 -- ,Evening Post,4)

>  If, however, the name of the language we speak in this country was officially changed from "English" to "Noo Zild", I'd shrug my shoulders and shut up! After all, the Australians speak "Strine" rather well, don't they? (Oct 31, 1998 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),9)

>  They work with familiar material, slyly inverting cliches as well as pure New Zild like white Valiants, but they look at it from a fresh angle. (Aug 23, 1992 -- ,Dominion Sunday Times,21)

>  <b>MITCHELL</b> Now you must understand New Zild as sheis spoke. Elocution teachers sometimessay that New Zild is only lazy speech...In fact New Zild is a substitute forspeech, a verbal shorthand. New Zilders speak slowly as if they were boring themselves into sleep. (1972 -- ,Half-Gallon Quarter-Acre Pavlova Paradise, The,181)



 
 <i>adj.</i> (informal) of New Zealand [also Noo Zild below]

>  Jennifer Ludlam donned slippers, trackies and a broad New Zild accent to play the white-trash Connie, in <i>Potiki's Memory of Stone</i>. (Jan 3, 2004 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),63)

>  Her Lyn of Tawa character made us acutely aware of the New Zild accent and the Kiwi inflection. (Nov 19, 2003 -- ,Capital Times,11)

>  Her only comment on the writing, <i>per se</i>, is that it uses that New Zild bete noire "fancy words" - long words where good four-by-twos would do. (Feb 24, 1992 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),11)

>  Somehow their New Zealand accent is always different from your Newzild accent. (Dec 23, 1991 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28)

>  A sigh of relief from the columnists and the letter writers who continue to police the spoken word for anything too ... "Newzild." (Apr 7, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28)

>  In the meantime, she's interested to see how a New Zild <i>Neighbours</i> will work. (Jan 15, 1990 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33) [NOTES: Listener & TV Times]

>  His flat, laconic New Zild tone is rivertalk fed by 200 years of historical tributaries: the talkback sound of the nation babbling in its sleep. (Jan 16, 1999 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42)

>  Mel Dodge's Noelene is a diva of the New Zild accent; hanging onto her personal chunder-bucket, she leaves her voice ringing in your ears. (Dec 2, 2000 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),53)

>  A few years ago, his band Dark Tower got our attention with a radical reworking of the Country Calendar theme, set against slow beats and lyrics that name-checked Vegemite, Ches and Dale, the whole package redolent of long childhood summers on the sweltering plain, as Newzild as that big bottle in Paeroa, as sandflies and sunburn. (Jan 22, 2000 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),63)

>  middle New Zild English must be delivered with surgical precision (Oct 1992 -- ,Political Review,34)

>  Sharon Crosbie may be happy with the "Noo Zild" type pronunciation in general use on National Radio.. But what of the grammar".. Noo Zild pronunciation may be acceptable to some, but why is it that so many of the female presenters on TV are quite unable to pronounce the most common letter "e" and substitute an "a" in its place? >  [Hdg] Talking Noo Zild blues. The standard of English that Boris Moiseiwitsch complains of.. issimply the matey kind of spoken "Zilnd" that one hears in the pubs or work-places. (Nov 28, 1998 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),8)

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> `And what languages have you, Mr Thompson?' ... `English?' (I offered this in that apologetic interrogative tone that has since become the hallmark of New Zild speech.) (1980 -- ,All My Lives,60) [NOTES: [c.early 1950s]]

## new-chum's pigeon
 
 <i>n.</i> a moss

>  ... the long grey moss which hangs in festoons and beards from some of the trees in the heavy bush [especially in mountain country in the North Island]. One kind of the moss ... is almost white and very conspicuous in the gloom of the bush. The settlers' name for it in my part of the country is `new-chum's pigeon'. It is so called because of the imagined resemblance of the mossy bunches to the white breast of the wild pigeon; ... I'm hanged if it would deceive any New Zealander country-born or bush-bred. (May 10, 1920 -- ,Quick March,39)

## next
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The best-known primer for this kind of activity, and indeed an integral part of the hoon-ethos itself, is the drinking game. Designed to take the monotony out of rapid, heavy drinking, the drinking game perhaps found its purest expression in the version entitiled Next. Down a drink as fast as possible (commonly known as `sculling'), turn to the bloke on your left and say `Next', whereupon he does the same ... very efficient. (Dec 10, 1986 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),13)

## Nga Tamatoa
 
 <i>n.</i> a radical Young Maori group of the 1970s

>  [Poata] was with the Maori movement Nga Tamatoa in the 1960s, and founder of the Maori Organisation on Human Rights. The MOOHR republished two important books, <i>Aueretanga - the groans of the Maori</i> by George Rusden and <i>Te Karanga a Te Kotuku,/i> by Saana Murray. (Dec 1, 2005 -- ,Dominion Post,B7)

>  ...she was caught up there with the formation of Nga Tamatoa (the young watriors), a pioneering protest movement that highlighted Treaty issues in the wider public consciousness for the first time, if only for being regarded so disparagingly by politicians and the media. (Feb 4, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,B5)

>  Radicalism had begun to rumble within the Maori world, and in the early 1970s, she joined Nga Tamatoa, a new Auckland-based activist group of young, educated, urban Maori. (Apr 2000 -- ,North & South,67)

>  Another [development] was the increasingly vociferous protest groups, like Nga Tamatoa, who marched, demonstrated, picketed, petitioned Parliament and harried the media - all in a bid to have Maori concerns attended to. (Dec 1996 -- ,Mana,No. 14 : 20)

>  According to founding member, Syd Jackson, Nga Tamatoa "demanded the right to be Maori ... without fear"... For Paul Smith, a New Zealand <i>Herald</i> reporter at the time, Nga Tamatoa were "warrior intellectuals" who declared war on a state of mind, namely Pakeha apathy for the Treaty. (Apr 28, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),64)

## ngaeo
 
 <i>n.</i> Williams 227 <i>Nga[a]eo ... Hyridella</i> spp., fresh-water molluscs; also <i>Lepsia haustrum</i> and <i>Cookia sulcata,</i> mollucs. ... =<i>ka[a]eo</i>.: spg <i>kaeo </i>

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Kaeo - this may be meant for koeo, a small form of the paua or pawa with a delicately-tinted irridescent shell and an edible `sea-slug' inhabiting it, or for kaio or ngaio, the sea-nut; probably the latter. (1945 -- ,Maori Place-Names of Canterbury,62)

 <i>n.</i>

I am a totara and pointing to a young man, he is a ngaio. (Jun 16, 1849 -- ,Journal,6/113)

(e.g.) the Houhere, <i>Hoheria populnea</i> .. the Horopito <i>Drimys axillaris</i>; the Manuka-rau-riki, <i>Leptospermum ericoides</i>; the Kohuhu, <i>Pittosporum tenuifolium</i>; the Kowhai, especially the small-leaved mountain variety, <i>Sophora tetraptera</i> var.: <i>grandiflora</i>; the Koromiko-taranga, <i>Veronica</i>, several species; the Mairehau, <i>Phebalium nudum</i>; the Toro, <i>Persoonia Toro</i>; the Pukapuka, <i>Brachyglottis repanda</i>; the Northern Maire, <i>Santalum Cunninghamii</i>; the Tawari, <i>Ixerba brexioides</i>; the Tipau, <i>Myrsine Urvillei</i> and <i>M. salicina</i>; the Tangeao, <i>Tetranthera calicaris</i>; the Ramarama, <i>Myrtus bullata</i>; the Ti, <i>Cordyline australis</i>; the Kahikomako, <i>Pennantia corymbosa</i>; the Pate, <i>Schefflera digitata</i>; the Horoeka, <i>Panax crassifolia</i>: and, on the sea-coast .. the Karo, <i>Pittosporum crassifolium</i>; and the truly evergreen Ngaio, <i>Myoporum laetum</i> ... (1869 -- ,TrNZI,1/III/5) [NOTES: Colenso Botany North Isd]

>  The Titoki (<i>Alectryon excelsum</i>), the Akeake (<i>Dodonaea viscosa</i>), the Ngaio (<i>Myoporum laetum</i>), and above all the Rewa Rewa (<i>Knightia excelsa</i>), yield woods, out of which some very beautiful pieces of furniture have been made. <i>Ibid.</i> 1/III/47 (Buchanan Botany Otago) Ngaio. <i>Myoporum laetum</i>, Forst. >  Myoporum Latum (-->Ngaio). This is generally called kio by colonists. It is very rapid-growing tree for the first five or six years after it has been planted. They are very hardy, and like the sea air. I saw these trees growing at St. Kilda, near Melbourne, thirty years ago.' (Nov 3, 1892 -- ,Otago Witness) [NOTES: 'Native Trees':]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Myoporum laetum (The Ngaio)</i>. Small tree, 10ft-40ft in height: Both islands: usually near the sea-shore. (1907 -- ,Plants of NZ,362,1940 ed.)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Kaio. A `popular corruption in the South Island of New Zealand of <i>Ngaio</i>,' the Maori name for <i>myoporum loetum</i>, a tree whose wood is used for gun-stocks: from ca, 1870. Morris. (1937 -- ,Dictionary of Slang,449)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> <i>ngaio</i>, a tree (Myoporum laetum). Rona caught hold of a young ngaio when the deity of the moon seized her when she called him a `cooked-head,' and the tree was taken up with her,and there she is in the moon with the tree and the calabash in which she was to bring water one night of drifting cloud. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154)

>  ngaio A handsome small New Zealand tree, growing chiefly near the sea. (Pronounce <i>ni|-o|</i>). (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),274,8th ed.)

>  <i>ngaio n -s</i> [Maori] : a small tree (<i>Myoporum laetum</i>) of the New Zealand coast with edible fruit and light tough wood. (1961 -- ,Merriam-Webster Third International,1524)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Ngaio .. ngaio .. <i>Myoporum laetum.</i> (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712)

>  <i>ngaio n.</i> a small evergreen New Zealand tree. [Maori] (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,759) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 506; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 327]]

>  <b>SANSOM</b> Further round from Kelly's Beach you can find the site of the ancient Maori kaik and the cemetery, its landmark a huge ngaio tree. (1982 -- ,In the Grip of an Island,140)

>  <b>HYDE</b> Under a ngaio, Mr. Bellister opened Wednesday's letter. (1937 -- ,Wednesday's Children,272,1989 ed.)



## ngaioing
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WILSON</b> ngaioing - the taking of birds in the first half of the season by wire and hooks . Ibid. 87The first half of the season is spent getting the birds from the burrows. (This is called Ngaioing). (1979 -- ,Titi Heritage,[16])

>  <b>WILSON</b> This part of the season in which the young birds are pulled from the burrows during the daytime is called the ngaioing period. We called it the dirty period! To insert the hand right inside the burrow it is necessary to get right down on to the ground, so within a few moments of commencing work one's clothes were in a filthy condition, especially if there had been rain during the night. (1979 -- ,Titi Heritage,120)



## ngaione
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BROKKER et al.</b> The leaves [of the ngaio] contain mannitol and ngaione. Ngaione is an optical isomer of ipomeamarone derived from the sweet potato (cf. pomoea). Ngaione is related to furfural which has fungicidal and bactericidal powers and which has been used as a cure for athlete's foot and swimmer's itch. ... Mannitol is used medicinally. (1981 -- ,NZ Medicinal Plants,69)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [Ma.] an essential oil of the ngaio tree that causes poisoning in cattle [ and possibly other stock.]

>  <b>NZ VET ASSN</b> .. an essential oil, ngaione, mainly in the leaves. (1971 -- ,Diseases of Domestic Animals in New Zealand,285)



## ngaiore
 
 <i>n.</i> Williams 227 <i>Ngaiore </i> ... A fish. Young of smelt, <i>Retropinna retropinna. = paraki, ngore.: </i>

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> The larval smelts [<i>Retropinna</i> sp.] which run upstream at the close of the whitebait season in November are termed Ngaiore on the Whanganui ... <i>Ibid.</i> 56 Maoris at Hiruharama, Whanganui River, showed me stores of larval examples caught in the river and dried in the sun. These were salted and eaten during winter months, being there universally termed `ngaiore'. (1940 -- ,Fishes NZ,53)

## ngarara
 
 <i>n.</i> money-spider

>  <b>LOCKLEY</b> They're only little ngarara [insects], `money-spiders' you Pakeha call them. I'll find a real katipo for you. (1980 -- ,House Above the Sea,15)



 
 <i>n.</i> a lizard; (in Maori myth) a reptilian monster.

>  Why he should call it a nagara he did not know, for that was merely the Maori name for a lizard. (Mar 15, 1890 -- ,NZ Observer,9)

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> Nga r|ara; General name for reptiles. (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,229)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> 6. <i>Hatteria punctata</i>. Gray, ... Gigantic Lizard, <i>Cook's </i>Voy., 3, I. 153., or Guana. - <i>Polack</i>, N.Z. i. 317. ... `I have been apprized of the existence of a large lizard, which the natives called <i>Tuatera</i>, or <i>Narara</i>, with a general name, and of which they were much afraid. ...' <i>Dieffenbach. </i> (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,2/204) [NOTES: 1842 Gray's Fauna in 1843 Dieffenbach Travels in NZ 2/204-205]

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> ... they [the natives] tried much to dissuade me from the attempt [to ascend Mount Egmont], by saying that the mountain was `tapu', and that there were ngarara (crocodiles) on it, which would undoubtedly eat me; the mysterious bird `moa,' ... was also said to exist there. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/140)

>  Lizard (Ngarara). (1842 -- ,Journal of Ensign Best,353,1966 Taylor ed.)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Was it not right to drive away the [231] ngararas, or reptiles, from them (so they called their ancient gods) (Sep 10, 1851 -- ,Journal,7/230)

>  ... the presiding deity has been offended: - his vengeance assuming the form of a Ngarara (lizard) enters the body of man, consumes his vitals, and thereby causes death. (Aug 15, 1857 -- ,Lyttelton Times,3)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> There are six small harmless lizards in the colony, which are held in terror by the natives, from a superstition that bodies within the spirits of their deified ancestors revisit the earth ... In such terror do the New Zealanders hold all the above reptiles, that the very pronunciation of the word Ngarara, a general term for the whole race, makes the bravest warrior tremble. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,1/29)

>  <b>HAAST</b> On the 11th of June we started from the cave, called by the natives Te-ana-o Matuku, respectingwhich they tell a very interesting tale of a large ngarara, a monster in the form of an enormous lizard, which formerly lived here, and devoured all who attempted to pass. Since they have become Christians, however, they have called it by another name, Te whare Taipo (the Devil's house), probably referring the formation of the cave, of which I shall speak more fully in the geological part of my report, to supernatural agency rather than to the hand of nature. (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,50)

>  Unu ngarara or ngarara burrows were frequently met with on the plains. ... The ngarara was darker in colour than the ruatara. They varied in size from two or three feet in length, and ten to twenty inches in girth ... (1875 -- ,TrNZI,7/296) [NOTES: Stack]

>  <b>HAY</b> ... the only reptiles are pretty little ngarara, or lizards (<i>Mocoa Zelandica</i> and <i>M. ornata), ... </i> (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/214)

>  The ridge-boards are carved to represent a number of <i>ngarara</i>, or lizards, running along the roof, and the<i> maihi</i>, or gable-boards, have carvings of the mythological animal known as the <i>manaia</i> - probably a kind of taniwha. (1895 -- ,TrNZI,27/674)

>  The ridge-boards are carved to represent a number of <i>ngarara</i>, or lizards, running along the roof, and the<i> maihi</i>, or gable-boards, have carvings of the mythological animal known as the <i>manaia</i> - probably a kind of taniwha. (1953 -- ,Short Stories (various authors),13) [NOTES: 1901 Grace Tales of a Dying Race in 1953 Short Stories 13]

>  The use of a lizard-form as a personal ornament amongst a Maori people must have been rare, as in most cases all kinds of <i>ngarara</i> were regarded with horror and aversion. (1903 -- ,TrNZI,35/112)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> [The Maori's] existence was burdened with the knowledge of huge reptilian monsters, ngarara on land, and taniwha in the water, who could speak, and roar, ... [39] and decoy with satanic duplicity! (1905 -- ,Where the White Man Treads,38)

>  <i>ngarara:</i> fabulous monster reptile. (1947 -- ,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.,175)

>  <b>BUCK</b> (1949 -- ,Coming of the Maori, The,61)

## Ngati
 
 <i>n.</i> short for Ngati Porou [DNZE p.536 attrib. here] (See [DNZE p.536](../D#dnze-p.536))

>  According to Ngati tradition this tree dates from time of the ancestorRerekohu who lived thirteen generations ago. (Mar 29, 1987 -- ,Ephemera) [NOTES: sighted on a notice on the pohutukawa Te Wahao Rerekohu (The Mouth ofRerekohu) at Te Araroa.]



 
 <i>pref.</i> first element in Maori tribal names: = people of

>  Warfare in the Rotorua lakes district in the early 1700's between Te Arawa iwi Tuhourangi, Ngati Whakaue and Ngati Pikiao climaxed with Tuhourangi being driven south to Tarawera. (Nov 13, 2004 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),38)

>  [Caption] <i>Standing in the Sunshine</i>: a young woman from the Hippolite family of Ngati Koata poses with her bicycle for a Tyree Studio photographer in 1901. (Nov 27, 1992 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),67)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Names of tribes (iwi) were generally denoted by the prefix Ngati, meaning `descendants of', as Ngati-Raukawa the descendants of Raukawa. Sometimes sub-tribes (hapu) or [162] or small remnants of tribes used Ngati before their names. The prefix varied into Ati and Ngai as Te Ati-awa, Ngai-tahu. (1904 -- ,Maori Race, The,161)



 
 <i>n.</i> (i) occasionally used for the local people or tangata whenua: 'the Ngati have placed a temporary prohibition on the use of this road.' (ii) (transferred use) a brumby, wild Maori horse (chiefly of the central North Island plateau)

>  <b>SHORTLAND</b> Ngati is the word most commonly prefixed to the name of an ancestor to designate his descendents. It is the compound word Nga-ati, signifying the offspring. (1865 -- ,Traditions & Superstitions,225)

>  <b>CRUMP</b> Here's how we're going to catch ourselves some of those long-haired, tussock-eating ngatis ... The brumbies are used to us coming and going along there now. (1961 -- ,Hang on a Minute, Mate,54)

>  ngati n. N.Z. a tribe or clan. [< Maori] (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,759) [NOTES: cf. 1986 NZPOD 506; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 327]



 
 <i>pref.</i> indicating humorously or ironically a quasi-Maori tribe or entity; see cits.

>  Welcome to the world of the ngati cappucino. At number 15 on National's list, unveiled last week, Parata is the cream of a new breed of upwardly mobile Maori who enjoy many of the trappings of Western urban life, but are equally confident in the Maori tribal world... <i>Ibid.</i> C14 The ngati cappucino made its first show of force in National a year ago when Parata's husband.. rolled incumbent Maori vice-president of the party, Dale Stephens. (Jun 23, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,C1) [NOTES: As opposed to previous use, this citation applies to Maori, not pakeha.]

>  [Hdg] Ngati chardonnay... New Zealand's first Maori-owned and branded wine company, Tohu is a partnership formed in 1998 by three Maori businesses... (Dec 14, 2003 -- ,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),15)

## ngawa
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BULLER</b> Hochstetter divides the steam jets into three kinds: 1. Puias, which are geysers, continuously orintermittently active. 2. Ngawas, which are inactive Puias - emitting steam, but not throwing up columns of water. 3. Waiarikis, which mean any sort of cistern of hot water suitable for bathing. (1878 -- ,Forty Years in NZ,11)

## ngawha
 
 <i>n.</i> Wiiliams 232 <i>Nga[a]wha[a] ... 2. Typha angustifolia ... =raupo[o].:</i>

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Ngawha, Lindsay. Typha angustifolia. (1867 -- ,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  People opposed to the building of the jail at Ngawha, near Kaikohe, moved on to the site on Saturday and refused access to workers trying to finish earthworks. (Jun 5, 2002 -- ,Evening Post,4)



 
 <i>n.</i> Williams 232 Nga[a]wha[a] ... 1. Boiling spring, or other volcanic activity.: early spg ngawa used in the North Island as a borowing from Maori passing into semi-technical geological or naturalists' use, ? passing out of use in the early 20th century)

>  The chief ngawha from which the greatest supply of water is derived is called "The Sodawater Spring." (Mar 19, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,8)

>  At intervals of considerable length, sometimes extending to many months, all these "ngawhas" begin to play together and form a scene which must be wonderful and beautiful. (Jul 8, 1859 -- ,Auckland Provincial Gazette,VIII(14) : 98)

>  Dr Hochstetter.. predicted that this very spot, in the vicinity of which are numerous ngawhas, fumeroles and hotwater streams, would some time be the area of a great volcanic eruption. (Jan 15, 1887 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,15)

>  <b>SELWYN</b> Here [at Ohinemutu] are to be seen all the varieties of Ngawha (hot-springs). There are mud cauldrons, black, blue, grey, green, yellow, and red, the very emblem [31] of laziness; ... (1845 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),30) [NOTES: 1843 Oct 30 Selwyn Journal in 1845 NZ Part II (Church in the Colonies VII) 30]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> This is a most singular place [Ohinemutu] it is literally filled with ngawas and many are very[182] dangerous and many lives have been lost the natives cook all their provisions in them ... some are paved over on which they dry their hinanga and shell fish, ... (Nov 26, 1845 -- ,Journal,3/181)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> We ... found the pa enveloped in vapour, which was rising from the numerous ngawha. <i>Ibid.</i> 169 We were received by a venerable-looking old man, ... who informed us that we must proceed to the ngawha by land, as the river was under tapu so that no canoe could enter it, . (1959 -- ,Early Travellers,155) [NOTES: 1847 Johnson Notes from Jrnl in 1959 Taylor Early Travs 155]

>  The word Puia is especially used in the Taupo country, to designate the intermittent, geyser-like fountains of Tokanu ... and of Whakarewarewa on Lake Rotorua. Puia has ... the more general meaning of crater or volcano, and is applied to active as well as extinct volcanoes. ... Ngawhas [is] a term specially used for non-intermittent springs, for the solfataras and sulphurous hot-springs on the Rotomahana, Rotorua, and Rotoiti. Finally, the springs suited to bathing purposes, the water of which never reaches the boiling point, and all naturally warm baths are called `Waiariki,' corresponding to the Laugar of Iceland. -->= ? ngawha (1870 -- ,TrNZI,2/171)

>  <b>MEADE</b> [We] soon found ourselves at the foot of the great ngawha, or hot spring, Te Tarata - a natural wonder ... [ibid. 449] There are about twenty-five large ngawha, as the natives term the hot springs of the Te Tarata kind, scattered round the lake, and many hundred smaller ones. (Dec 30, 1870 -- ,Ride Through Disturbed Districts) [NOTES: 1870 Dec 30 Meade Ride Through Disturbed Districtsin 1959 Taylor Early Travellers 447]

>  These characters ... distinguish the thermal waters of White Island from the Puias and Ngawhas of the Rotorua and Taupo Districts ... (1871 -- ,TrNZI,3/282) [NOTES: Davis]

>  ... innumerable steam jets, ngawhas, fumeroles, and geysers .... (1873 -- ,TrNZI,5/337) [NOTES: Kirk]

>  <b>TINNE</b> The `Babies' Cauldron' ... never rose into the air with a surging column of water, like the more turbulent ngahaus around. (1873 -- ,Wonderland of the Antipodes,14)

>  Immediately across the ferry is a nearly circular Ngawha, or pond of boiling water, some twenty feet in diameter ... ... The water of this Ngawha is of a rich mazarine blue ... (1877 -- ,TrNZI,9/8) [NOTES: Travers `on the Lake Districts ...']

>  Hochstetter's map of Rotomahana, published in 1889 ..... shows the location of most of the ngawhas, puias, fumeroles, and solfataras that surrounded Rotomahana. (1905 -- ,TrNZI,37/452) [NOTES: Hill]

>  ... all aspects of volcanic phenomena occur, from the active volcano, as Ngaurahoe and Tongariro, to the slowly dying ngawha and puiaa as seen at places like Tokaanu ... [281] ... abounding with terraces, puias, ngawhas, and geysers ... (1911 -- ,TrNZI,43/278, 281) [NOTES: Hill]

## ngehe

## ngere-ngere

## ngirungiru
 
 <i>n.</i> the South Island tomtit. Compare MIROMIRO. [Maori] Williams 233 <i>Ngirungiru ... Petroica toitoi, white-breasted tit</i> (North Island), and <i>P. macrocephala macrocephala, yellow-breasted tit</i> (South Island). = <i>miromiro</i> ...: spg also <i>ngidu ngidu, ngiru-ngiru </i>erron. <i>ngoru-ngoru</i> cf also MIROMIRO, PIRANGIRANGI : ? white breasted ? or yellow-breasted SI tit

>  <b>LEE& KENDALL</b> Ngi|du ng|idu; A bird so called. (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,230)

>  <b>YATE</b> <i>Ngirungiru</i> - This bird lays its eggs in the holes of trees; ... It is a very small bird, not larger than the tom-tit; ... (1835 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),56)

>  <b>POLACK</b> The <i>nirungiru</i> [check] (Parus macrocephalus), or great-headed titmouse, is very common in the country. (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),1/298)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> 28. <i>Miro toitoi</i>, ... Nirungiru [sic] of the natives. <i>Polack</i>. Ngirungiru of the natives. <i>Yate, Dieffenbach</i>. To-i-toe of the natives. <i>Less. </i> (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,2/191) [NOTES: 1842 Gray's Fauna in 1843 Dieffenbach Travels in NZ 2/191]

>  PETROICA MACROCEPHALA, Gml. Ngirungiru. Piro piro. Tomtit. (1870 -- ,TrNZI,2/59) [NOTES: Potts Birds]

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 29. PETROICA DIEFFENBACHII. Gray. Yellow-breasted Tit. Ngirungiru. (1871 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,12)

>  <b>BULLER</b> MYIOMOIRA TOITOI. (PIED TIT {NORTH ISLAND TOMTIT}.) ... <i>Native names.</i> Miromiro, Komiromiro, {Pimiromiro,} Ngirungiru{, Pingirungiru, and Pipitori}. ... [40] Its note in the early morning is like the Maori syllables <i>ngi-i-ru, ngiru-ngiru</i>, from which it derives its name, the first syllable being rather prolonged. <i>Ibid.</i> MYIOMOIRA MACROCEPHALA. (YELLOW-BREASTED TIT {SOUTH-ISLAND TOMTIT}.) <i>Native names</i>. Miromiro, Komiromiro, {Pimiromiro,} Ngirungiru{, Pingirungiru, and Pipitori}... (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,124,1888 2nd edn 1/39 ed.)

>  28. <i>Petroeca macrocephala</i>, Gml. Yellow-breasted Tit or Robin, Miro-miro, Ngoru-ngoru [sic]. (Nov 1884 -- ,NZ Journal of Science,286) [NOTES: Potts]

>  <i>miromiro</i>, a bird (Petroeca toitoi), North Island tomtit; white-breasted tit it differs from the South Island form (P. macrocephala) yellow-breasted tit in the colouration of the breast. Whilst miromiro was the name of the North Island bird, both of them bore the name ngirungiru, so that has been adopted for the South Island form, though the South Island Maori would probably give the `ng' sound scurvy treatment and call the bird kirukiru, as he calls ngaio kaio. .. <i>ngirungru</i>, a bird (Petroeca macrocephala), South Island tomtit, <i>see</i> miromiro. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> TOMTIT <i>Petroica macrocephala Other names</i>: Miromiro (North Island), Ngirungiru (South Island), Wheedler, Butcher-bird (Taranaki). (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,200)

>  257.<i> b. Petroica macrocephala macrocephala<i> (Gmelin, 1789) <i>Yellow-breasted Tit (Ngiru-ngiru)</i> ... South Island. (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,68,1980 ed.)

>  <i>Tit Petroica macrocephala</i> (GMELIN, 1789) ... <i>Pied Tit P.m.</i> toitoi (LESSON, 1828). <i>Yellow-breasted Tit P.m. macrocephala</i> (GMELIN, 1789). <i>Chatham Island Tit P.m. chathamensis </i> FLEMING, 1950. <i>Black Tit P.m.dannefaerdi </i> ROTHSCHILD, 1894). <i>Auckland Island Tit P.m. marrineri </i>(MATHEWS & IREDALE, 1913). ... OTHER NAMES: <i>Ngiru-ngiru, piropiro, South Island tit, Maui-potiki, North Island tit or tomtit, white-breasted tit or tomtit, Snares tit, Snares Island robin. </i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,284)

## ngoiro
 
 <i>n.</i> Williams 234 <i>Ngo[o]iro</i> ... 3. Young gulls, karoro.: (See [karoro; southern black-backed gull](../K#karoro;-southern-black-backed-gull))

>  The dissimilarity in appearance of immature gulls and their elders prompted Maori to give fully grown birds one name - karoro - and juveniles another - ngoiro. (May 2005 -- ,NZ Geographic,73 / 47)

>  <b>BULLER</b> LARUS DOMINICANUS. (SOUTHERN BLACK-BACKED GULL.) ... <i>Native names.</i> Karoro; the young bird distinguished as Ngoiro, {Koiro, and Punua}. [271] This fine Gull, which ranges over the whole Southern Hemisphere, is extremely plentiful. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,270,1888 2nd edn 2/47 ed.)

>  <b>SHAND</b> The young of many sea birds ... were used as food [on the Chathams], such as <i>Kuaka</i>(plover), young gulls (<i>Ngoiro</i>), shags (<i>Kuau</i>) ... <i>Ho[macron]po</i> (the albatross), H <i>Hakoakoa</i> (mutton bird), <i>Taiko</i>[macron] (a smaller-sized mutton bird of slatey blue colour), <i>Titi</i>[i's macron] (a still smaller size) ... (1911 -- ,Moriori People of the Chatham Islands, The,6)

>  <b>FALLA et al</b> SOUTHERN BLACK-BACKED GULL <i>Larus dominicanus Maori name</i>: Karoro, Ngoiro (imm). (1966 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,154)



 
 <i>n.</i> the conger eel. [Maori ngoiro[o1-macron]] Williams 234 <i>ngo[oiro ... 1. Leptocephalus conger, conger eel</i>. see also<i> ngoringori</i> (Williams 235 1. a small black eel.) =CONGER EEL (See [also ngoringori](../A#also-ngoringori))

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> Ng|oiro, s. A fish so called; (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,230)

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> CONGER EEL (<i>Conger vulgaris</i>). ... [18] Known to the Natives as ngoi[r]o, and attains a very large size, with a length of 6ft. ... Congers are captured by hooks and lines, either used by the hand, or long lines with hooks at regular intervals... <i>ibid.</i> 70 NGOIRO (see CONGER EEL) (1886 -- ,Handbook Fishes of NZ,17)

>  <b>BEST</b> The following fish were caught with hook and line: araara, maomao, moki, mango, ngoiro, pakirikiri, kahawai, tamure, tarakihi, and warehou. The kanae, kehe, kopipiro, moho, nanu and other species were taken by net. (1929 -- ,Fishing Methods,43)

>  8. <i>Leptocephalus conger</i> Linnaeus. Conger Eel. Maori: Ko-iro, Ngoiro. (1929 -- ,TrNZI,61/140) [NOTES: Young]

>  <b>POWELL</b> CONGER EEL (<i>Leptocephalus verreauxii</i>), Ngoiro of the Maoris, is the common one of a number of marine eels. (1947 -- ,Native Animals of NZ,64)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> <i>Other names: Conger verreauxi</i>; ngoiro, koiro (Maori). (1966 -- ,Sea Anglers' Guide,198)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> CONGER EEL [<i>Conger verreauxi</i>] ... The Maori names koiro and ngoiro are sometimes used. (1967 -- ,Marine Fishes,30)

## nibbler
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a high country kea that rips wool from sheep's backs but does not kill them.

>  <b>MCKENZIE</b> If they are within easy approach and not killers but nibblers, simply mimic the call-kea and they will come right up close to be shot. Nibblers do not actually do harm, just rip the wool on the back of a sheep, and then fly off to another. Apart from that aspect one never knows when a nibbler wil become a killer. (1951 -- ,Wakatipu Pioneers,99)

>  <b>MCKENZIE</b> They [keas] automatically took up their positions, some holders, some nibblers, as though they were highly trained. (1951 -- ,Wakatipu Pioneers,106)



## nibby
 
 <i>n.</i> a mustering stick [station]

>  <b>NEWTON</b> By dint of much labour, not to mention the mining of several nibbies (mustering sticks) they finally chipped a hole in ice. (1947 -- ,Wayleggo,86)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a straight mustering stick with no hook. (See [hillpole](../H#hillpole),[hillstick; mustering stick; mustering pole; rakau](../H#hillstick;-mustering-stick;-mustering-pole;-rakau))

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The high-country musterer covers a lot of dangerous country, day after day, and with nothing to aid him but his nibby or walking stick. (1964 -- ,Straggle Muster,94)

>  <b>STACEY</b> .. with the aid of my trusty nibbie I was able to get about slowly. (1996 -- ,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,162)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> By dint of much labour, not to mention the ruining of several nibbies (mustering sticks) they finally chipped a hole in the ice. (1947 -- ,Wayleggo,86)

>  <b>HALL</b> The stick he's carrying is a 'nibby' or hill stick, made from a seasoned piece of straight manuka stripped of its bark. (1987 -- ,Shepherd's Year,  A,22)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> The mustering stick, known as a 'hillstick' or 'nibbie', was usually made from manuka and had a metal ferrule. (1990 -- ,Forgotten Worker, The,63)

>  <b>STACEY</b> nibbie: a musterer's staff, usually made of straight manuka. When killing a mutton, the nibbie is put through the hind legs so two men can support it while the third man finishes the dressing. [nibbie] (1996 -- ,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,164)



## nibling / niebling
 
 <i>n.</i> collective noun for nieces and nephews coined by Trina Stevens

>  Trina Stevens says she has invented the word "niblings", on the analogy of "siblings", to define nieces and nephews. She says it arose from frustration in describing family groupings with multiple nieces and nephews. It also owes something to "anklebiter". (Jan 28, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,B 5)

>  <i>nieblings:</i> A collective term for your nieces and nephews, after siblings. try it it's handy. (Dec 18, 2005 -- ,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),10)

## nick
 
 <i>n.</i> IN THE NICK, in the nude (cf also NICKETTY BOO)



 
 <i>v.</i> with away, off, out, to depart (?quickly and often surreptitiously) -->to nip, pop, hop etc off (away) etc (Prob. a variant of nip off) [ca1951 Wellington X's nicked away to have a beer; ca1953 Wellington (H.S. Gajadhar) I'll just nick off and get some lunch.]

>  <b>McGILL</b> nick away to leave, usually surreptitiously or quickly; eg `Look, I'm going to nick away before the speeches start. Let me know if they say anything important, will you?' (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)



 
 <i>v.</i>

>  <b>CRUMP</b> Flynn, sensing my decision to nick out for a bit of hunting, kept dashing out. (1960 -- ,Good Keen Man, A,121)



 
 <i>v.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> nick over to visit, usually without warning; eg `Marge, I'm just going to nick over and see Harry before din-dins, okay?' (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)



## nick in
 
 <i>v.</i> slot in, mesh (with)

>  <b>PERRY</b> It is a good idea to introduce a fresh strain from time to time to experiment with, and though it may not nick in well enough for the half-[62]strain rams to be used in the flock, the ewes will certainly be of use. (1922 -- ,Sheep Farming in NZ,61)



## nicked
 
 <i>adj.</i> [farming]

>  After the deposit [of fat] had begun, every other part simultaneously receives its share, the back and kidneys receiving the most, the former becoming `nicked', i.e. the fat is felt through the skin to be divided into two portions, from the tail head along the backbone to the top of the shoulder... 39 when you find the rump `nicked' on handling you expect to find fat on the back. (1902 -- ,NZ Loan and Merc. Annual Review,38)

## nigger
 
 <i>n.</i> an abusive and contemptuous term for MAORI relatively rare (at least in written record) since the Land Wars of the 1860's, when it was especially frequent among Taranaki settlers, regimental soldiers (?fresh from India and other colonial venues) In theRichmond-Atkinson Papers (1960), up to 1860, except for one use by Jane Maria (1860 July 171/618) and one by H. Ronalds in his Diary of the Taranaki War (1860 Mar. 9, 1/535), it seems the personal use of A.S. Atkinson all in his private journal and not in his `public' letters. His first use is Aug 18, 1855 (see quote), thence 1856 June 10, with two more `playful' uses of negroes, the first curious (1858 Sep 19, I-429 `Consulted with Maria whether it would be well to go among the Negroes for six months to learn Maori'), the second 1859 Mar 8 I-452 `Attended conference between Negroes and Governor'. [(obsolesc offensive)] (See [also NEGRO; HORI; BLACK JOB](../A#also-negro;-hori;-black-job),[BROWN JOB](../B#brown-job))

>  <b>BLACKLEY</b> It was only when a number of new settlersin the district, mostly low class Irish,began to exhibit their inhospitabilitytowards the 'niggers', as they calledthem, that those 'niggers' began to do soalso to the whiteman. (1992 -- ,Art of Alfred Sharpe, The,29) [NOTES: From 1888 SHARPE in Newcastle Morning Herald]

>  We heard that Bishop Selwyn (who has been here two or three days) thinksthere is a fair chance of collision with the `niggers'. (Aug 18, 1858 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/175,1960 ed.) [NOTES: A.S. Atkinson Jrnl]

>  The 40th Band was sent to Waitara ... they meant to charm the niggers <i>Ibid.</i> 7/2 Suppose we were sure that the niggers wouldn't come in day light (1860 -- ,Taranaki Punch,V/6)

>  Will speaks of settling at Otago and us all going, to which Arthur elegantly remarks `he'll be blowed if he does' and James agrees with him that migration to an entirely `nigger' settlement would be preferable to living amongst those Scotchmen. (Aug 31, 1860 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/631)

>  The bump of antiniggerism is most consolingly prominent) (1861 -- ,Taranaki Punch,2/3)

>  <b>FITZGERALD</b> But you will find in these papers complaints by chiefs that some of the settlers are in the habit of speaking of them as `black niggers' and `bloody Maoris'. But this is only as much to say, that amongst the English settlers there are some ruffians and blackguards. ... (1862 -- ,Native Policy of NZ, The,12)

>  <b>CARBERRY</b> It is said that the niggers intend fighting. (Nov 18, 1863 -- ,Journal) [NOTES: 1863 18 Nov CARBERRY Journal (MS ATB) (MS ATB)]

>  <b>NICHOLL</b> ...the natives were firing at them but they did not return it as they were about 1000 yards from each other. With my glasses I could see one of the niggers walking up and down on the parapet waving his hands about. (1864 -- ,Journal,199)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> There can be little doubt that the same cause which rendered our countrymen the objects of native dislike in India also operated here; the Maori is constantly being called a nigger and black fellow to his face, and viewed as an inferior being. 113 ibid. Before [the present Taranaki War] began some of the Europeans felt so confident of their own power to maltreat, that they were neither ashamed nor afraid to express their wish to `polish off the black niggers'. (1868 -- ,Past and Present of NZ,111)

>  <b>MONEY</b> ... at least not to be such easy pots for the concealed niggers. From the place where we cavalrymen] halted, ... we saw the niggers come outside, and some mounted men riding to and fro. (1871 -- ,Knocking About in NZ,130)

>  <b>HOOD</b> The Vulgar Europeans call their tawny skinned neighbours [about the time of the Taranaki Wars] `Niggers', `Savages' and other opprobrious epithets more applicable to themselves. (1875 -- ,Chowbokiana,51)

>  <b>WISNIOWSKI</b> Their boys thought they were too good for her [a Maori]. They called her a nigger girl. (1877 -- ,Tikera,178,(trans. Podstolski 1972 ed.)

>  <b>SENIOR</b> I met colonists who could not use objurgations too strong against the Maoris - `niggers' as, John Bull fashion, they term them. (1880 -- ,Travel & Trout,198)

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> ... those of later days, who arrive in the colony and have on their tongues the talk of the `nigger element', and who have no knowledge and no ideas of Maori character, save as gathered fromthese later days, ... (1881 -- ,Poenamo,Bk3/9/216)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> The boss of the new men call me `nigger' [fn] Maoris dislike the term `nigger' (1895 -- ,Ngamiki,140)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Nigger, n. an Australian black or aboriginal. [Of course an incorrect use. He is not a negro, any more than the Hindoo is.] 1874 (1898 -- ,Austral English,320)

>  <b>118</b> [Potatau's, the Maori King's] last words to his people were: `Hold fast to love, to law, and to faith;' and from his deathbed he sent a message to his friend Sir William Martin, the former Chief Justice of New Zealand: `Be kind to the niggers.' [? date of death] (1908 -- ,New Zealand Revisited)

>  Heavens above, we filch the nigger's land and then we make an alien of him. Why the day will come when the Dago won't be at home here. (Jan 7, 1911 -- ,NZ Truth,289/1/5)

>  <b>LAMBERT</b> It was a very common thing [c1865] to refer to them as `niggers,' and it is difficult to discover any more opprobrious epithet to apply, for the Maori is not a `nigger' in any sense of the term ... (1936 -- ,Pioneering Reminiscences of Old Wairoa,142)

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> But the only girl Roddy liked was that Eriti Fleete. A nigger, thought Brian, who had already absorbed Auckland's notion of Maoris. (1938 -- ,Promenade,166)

>  <b>BEAGLEHOLE</b> Skin colour and the mixture of blood which is responsible for it [sc. high visibility in a white world] are often commented on by one Maori to another. The terms `mongrel', `black mongrel', `dago', or `black nigger', for instance, are often used by Maoris either in referring to themselves or to other Maoris in the neighbourhood. Thus one informant, in introducing herself, remarked: `I'm only a mongrel' - meaning only a mixed-blood Maori. (1946 -- ,Some Modern Maoris,299)

>  Later a girl by the campfire said `why don't you niggers do a haka and ....[sic] off.' (Mar 28, 1984 -- ,Dominion,7)

>  <b>HUNT</b> (Marama, Kaikoura) Or when we were playing around having a fight down the back, someone would say, `Go away, nigger.' ... Then there'd be a fight. Same thing when the towneyites - the kids from up Kaikoura - came down here. (1985 -- ,I'm Ninety-five,98)

>  <b>EDWARDS</b> Pakeha people will start calling me"black nigger" again. (1992 -- ,Mihipeka,115)



 
 <i>n.</i> [(prison slang, Paremoremo) ca1984 Paremoremo Prison (C.B.Orsman p.c.)]

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> In those days racism was more blatant than it is now. ... I had become accustomed to being addressed as `snow', `darky', and even `Hori', often by adults who would have been surprised had they known they were giving offence. And at high school Stuart was known as Sam (short for Sambo). But `nigger' was the ultimate, the unforgivable epithet. (1984 -- ,Island to Island,93)



 
 <i>n.</i> [equipment]

>  The "nigger", as it is called, disappears again into the floor. (1944 -- ,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),2(9) : 26)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> an offensive term of abuse for a Polynesian (See [cf coconut](../C#cf-coconut))

 <i>n.</i> (See [niggerhead](../N#niggerhead))

## niggerhead
 
 <i>n.</i> sticks (? or figs) of coarse, dark `plug' or `twist'. spg also nigger head [? from brand name`negrohead', and/or having a negro's head as the trademark] [(obs)]

>  6 lb N Hd 6lb Cavendish 1 doz Pipes (Apr 1839 -- ,Piraki Log,80) [NOTES: Station Stocklist]

>  <b>E. J. WAKEFIELD</b> niggerhead (<i>passim</i>) (Mar 30, 1840 -- ,Diary)

>  <b>MONEY</b> Luckily I had two or three pieces of `nigger-head' tobacco (Barrett's twist) in my pocket, and we chewed this as we sat crouching back to back ... (1871 -- ,Knocking About in NZ,76)

>  <i>Niggerhead.</i>- (1) ... (2)The name of the tobacco usually kept in station stores for the men in the eighties. (C.) (Nov 11, 1933 -- ,Press, The,15) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>PASCOE</b> In the middle nineties [Douglas] smoked `niggerhead', a tobacco of two dozen thin sticks to the pound. (1957 -- ,Mr Explorer Douglas,70)



 
 <i>n.</i> (?rare) <i>Mariscus ustulatus </i> [ca1957 Nov. (R. Mason) `heard for the first time']



 
 <i>n.</i> a hard black-stone

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Nigger-head, n.</i> (1) Name given in New Zealand to hard black-stones found at the Blue Spur and other mining districts. They are prized for their effectiveness in aiding cement-washing. The name is applied in America to a round piece of basic igneous rock. -->(2) Name used in Queensland for blocks of coral above water. -->OED 1876 (New Guinea) black points of coral rock. 1877, 1886 a) black concretionary nodules formed in granite b) any hard dark-coloured rock weathering into ... [rounded] boulders; 1898 Morris (1898 -- ,Austral English,321)



 
 <i>n.</i> a swamp tuft-grass whose raised clumps turn black when dry. spg also nigger(-)head 1. the blackened tussocks of swamp-sedges of Carex spp., esp. Carex secta and C. virgata [are these synonyms?], (cf MAORIHEAD, NEGROHEAD) 1882 Poo 1900 DCO (C. virgata) 10 entries (C. secta) 1906 LB 1924 CCN. (Andersen) (See [Maorihead](../M#maorihead))

>  On the way they had to cross a gully five or six feet deep and about fifteen or twenty feet wide which was filled with nigger-heads. (1988 -- ,Tales of Pioneer Women,150) [NOTES: Writing of 1940]

>  We had a bacon house, where mum used to cure her bacon and dad used to smoke it, by burning niggerheads. (1959 -- ,Women of Westland,32) [NOTES: 2nd edition]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> The edges of streams on the tussock-clad plains are often fringed with flax and bulrush, whilst, in the water itself, stand numbers of blackened stumps about two feet high, bearing on their summits drooping brushes of long, coarse, green, or tawny threads. These are termed by the colonists, nigger-heads. (1940 -- ,Plants of NZ,5) [NOTES: [check 1907]]

>  <b>BAKER</b> Maorihead for <i>Carex virgata, Niggerhead</i> for <i>Carex secta</i>. [check] (1941 -- ,NZ Slang,45)

>  <i>nigger-head:</i> sedge grass. (1947 -- ,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.,175)

>  <b>SHAW & FARRANT</b> When the first settlers made their way over from Dunedin to take up the rural sections marked out by Mr Kettle's survey they were confronted with a land of swamps and winding canals, with giant `nigger-heads' showing above the water. (1949 -- ,Taieri Plain,2)

>  <b>LEVY</b> Nigger head (1951 -- ,Grasslands of NZ,88 [Table 1],1970 ed.)

>  <b>EVANS</b> The numerous wet places [at Waikaka] were crowded with interesting plants ... sedge, or cuttygrass, niggerheads, wiwi or rush, and epilobium or willow herb with its beautiful white flowers. (1962 -- ,Waikaka Saga,239)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> These massive sedges best known as NIGGER-HEADS (<i>Carex secta, C. virgata</i>, and <i>C.appressa</i>) are very similar in general appearance. (1929 -- ,Flora NZ,133,1929 ed.)

>  <i>nigger-head</i>: The name used in New Zealand for a large kind of swamp grass growing in wet places; in Queensland for blocks of coral above water. (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),275,8th ed.)

>  <i>nigger-head</i> n. New Zealand name for certain hard black stones; above water blocks of Barrier Reef coral. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  <i>nigger-head</i> n. in Australia, stick of strong popular bush tobacco; in N.Z., clumps of coarse native grass or sedge in swamps, often on burnt tree stumps. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> The land of the magpie and the myna, the pukeko scuttling into raupo and niggerhead, the plantations regimented across the landscape. (1968 -- ,Pagan Game, The,20)

>  <i>niggerhead [=] cyperus ustulatus [s] </i>... <i>niggerhead [s] Carex secta</i> Boott (1969 -- ,Standard Common Names for Weeds,53)

>  <b>McNEISH</b> He crossed swamps, jumping from nigger-head to nigger-head. (1970 -- ,Mackenzie,67)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Mata [a12-macrons] ... 2. <i>Carex diandra</i> and <i>C. secta</i>, niggerhead; swamp plants. <i>Ibid.</i> 308 <i>Pu[u]kio</i>, n. Carex diandra, niggerhead; a swamp plant which grows in raised tufts. (1971 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,185)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Pukio</i>, n.<i> Carex diandra, niggerhead</i>; a swamp plant that grows in raised tufts. (1975 -- ,Dictionary of the Maori Language,308,1971 ed.)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Carex secta</i> NIGGERHEAD (1982 -- ,Stewart Island Plants,366)

>  <i>niggerhead n. NZ </i>tree-fern; matted clump of swamp tussock-grass (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,506) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  <b>WARR</b> Flax, niggerheads, toitoi and rushes grew in abundance in all swampy areas ... (1988 -- ,From Bush-Burn to Butter,49)
