## night
 
 <i>n.</i> winter in the Antarctic

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> "Who are the chaps who stay over through the winter - your winter population here?" "Well, there are usually about fourteen staying put during the night." (1962 -- ,One Foot at the Pole,149)



## night barker
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a sheep dog that persistently requires attention or incites other dogs at night by barking.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> night barker: Pretty obvious term for a dog with that bad habit. It invariably disturbs other dogs which also start up, and in next to no time a whole district is reverberating to a distressing chorus. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,ii 2)



## night camp
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a place to where sheep rise during the evening.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> camp: sheep will settle down for the night in groups and always in the same spot. This night camp is easily noticed by day as the ground is covered only by plants such as the horehound which will tolerate very rich soil. (1981 -- ,Loughnan Glossary,15)

>  <b>KERR</b> This avoids the extreme heat and catches sheep just up from their night camp, when they travel better because their bellies are empty. (2000 -- ,High Times in the High Country,44)



## night demon
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Majaqueus parkinsoni</i> Bray, BLACK PETREL (See [hakuwai](../H#hakuwai))

>  <i>Majaqueus parkinsoni</i> Gray. (Black petrel.) ... I am quite satisfied that this bird is the `Night Demon' of our diggers. I had a live one ... from Collingwood ... On windy nights the bird would become very excited, and then it would give vent to the hysterical laugh or scream from which it takes its name. (1895 -- ,TrNZI,27/122) [NOTES: Buller]



## Night Limited
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The introduction of the Limited train between Auckland and Wellington made an immediate appeal to the popular imagination... The introduction of a summer running train, which took advantage of the daylight, was also 'limited in its accommodation'... It brought about a desire for more specific titles, so "Daylight Limited" and "Night Limited" became the terms commonly used in referring to them. (Jul 18, 1928 -- ,NZ Free Lance,35)



## night paddock
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) [stud farming] a paddock where stud mares, ewes or cows are kept before and immediately after parturition.

>  <b>TAIT</b> From 1930, the practice of using night paddocks for the herd was discontinued. (1958 -- ,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol II,495)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) an accommodation paddock for stock in droves, [usually overnight or for short periods.]

>  <b>PULLEN</b> We were given this great night paddock full of grass. (1996 -- ,On the Road Again,31)

>  <b>HOLDEN</b> It was bright enough to have picked up a bridle and gone looking for a horse in the night paddock on a station that still relied on them. (1993 -- ,Station Country I,54)

>  <b>PULLEN</b> They [the cattle] must have been getting enough [feed] to keep them contented though, as they would camp as soon as they got to their night paddocks each evening. (1996 -- ,On the Road Again,17)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [dairying] a paddock where cows are kept prior to morning milking.

>  <b>DARE</b> The night paddock was land surrounding the house and easily accessible ... (1956 -- ,Rouseabout Jane,122)

>  The paddock is now used as a night paddock for 18 cows.. (Oct 30, 1926 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,56)

>  <b>STAPLEDON</b> On an appreciable number of farms the system of the 'night paddock' is largely adapted. This consists of always bringing the dairy cows to a a particular paddock at night. The 'night paddock' will be rotated round the farm just as the dairy beasts themselves. (1928 -- ,Tour in Australia and NZ,55)

>  To avoid trouble of this kind cows should be prevented from wading in stagnant water and kept out of badly fouled night paddocks. (May 20, 1933 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,330)

>  The cows have three or four days in each paddock, with a separate night paddock. The night paddocks are also grazed in rotation. (Aug 1, 1936 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,44)

>  In the case of night paddocks, it is useful to remember that cows do not eat much after midnight. (Jun 1, 1946 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,13)

>  To save time and work getting in the cows in the morning, a farmer will frequently use his paddocks handy to the cowshed as night paddocks .. . (Oct 1, 1949 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,75)

>  It may be difficult to avoid day and night paddocks on a farm broken by deep gullies or awkward terrain. (Jun 1982 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,2)

>  <b>STEWART</b> We were genuinely able to be of some usein the cowshed - helping Dick to bring upthe herd.. from the night paddock,hustling them into the shed, leg-ropingthem.. attaching the machine's strangecups to their fleshy teats. (1983 -- ,Springtime in Taranaki,116)



## night parrot
 
 <i>n.</i> indigenous parrot, the kakapo [aka kakapo]

>  The kakapo, a night parrot (<i>Strigops habroptilus</i>), is a kind of yellowish green owl, also making its nest in a hole in the ground, and coming out at night. (Apr 23, 1870 -- ,Otago Witness,9)

>  Another writer added: "in Otago during the construction of a certain road, the Night Parrot was slaughtered in great numbers, one bird making 'a plenteous meal for two men'." (Mar 2002 -- ,NZ Geographic,56/24)

>  ka[a]ka[a]po[o], a bird (Strigops habroptilus), ground-parrot; green parrot; night-parrot; once common in both islands, now confined to rugged south-west mountains of South Island (po, night, whence night-parrot). (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/143) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English In O.E.D., but no macrons.]

>  The destruction of the bush, the introduction of stoats, weasels and ferrets, the increase of wild cats, the importation of European birds and the consequent greater demand made on the supply of bird-food, are all blamed, and no doubt those remarkable birds, the kakapo (or night parrot), the kiwi and the wood hen (weka) will fall an easy prey to stoats and weasels, more especially the two former, by reason of their sleeping in holes and hollow logs in the day time. (Mar 27, 1897 -- ,Star, The,6)

>  There is a small lake named kakapo (the night parrot, stringops) on the river, which runs into Lake Poteretere from the north. (Oct 13, 1909 -- ,Otago Witness,63)

>  [The Maori] ... said that formerly he had often been there to catch kakapos (green night parrots) in a black birch wood above the Awatere pass. (Feb 21, 1851 -- ,NZ Government Gazette,31) [NOTES: 1851 Jan Weld Letter in 1851 Feb 21 NZGG 31]

>  <i>ka[a]ka[a]po[o]</i>, a bird (Strigops habroptilus), ground-parrot; green parrot; night-parrot; once common in both islands, now confined to rugged south-west mountains of South Island (po, night, whence night-parrot). (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/143) [NOTES: In O.E.D., but no macrons.]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Night parrots are almost extinct. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,1/28)

>  <b>HAAST</b> We were separated from the whole world, our only neighbour the abnormal night parrot, whose shrill cry enlivened the dismal nights ... (1948 -- ,Julius Von Haast,282)

>  <b>HAAST</b> During the night we heard for the first time the cry of the kakapo, and of the large kiwi, ... I was much interested here on observing the tracks of numerous kakapos, or night parrots, ... (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,57)

>  <b>COWAN</b> ... the range ... had in old times had been a well-known hunting-ground for the kakapo (night-parrot or ground-parrot) ... (1922 -- ,NZ Wars, The,2/459)

>  <i>night parrot n 1</i> : KAKAPO (1961 -- ,Merriam-Webster Third International,1527)

>  46. <i>Stringops habroptilus,</i> Gray. Ground parrot, Night par8rot, Kakapo. - It is still abundant in many parts of Westland; notwithstanding the persecution that constantly pursues it for the sake of supplying the requirements of museums and collectors. (Jul 1885 -- ,NZ Journal of Science,479) [NOTES: (T.H. Potts)]

>  <i>Kakapo Strigops habroptilus</i> GRAY, <i>1845 ... OTHER NAMES</i>: Tarapo, tarepo, owl <i>parrot, ground parrot, night parrot.</i> (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,242)



## night pen
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) [joc.] a hotel room or bar facilities for use when wet weather postpones shearing or mustering.

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> Country hotels often keep a night pen for wet day shearers, so do they keep some wild sorts of sheep for wide running dogs ... [night-pen; nightpen] (1973 -- ,Boot in the Stirrup,61)



 
 <i>n.</i> a sheltered pen (often in shearing shed) where sheep can be kept the night before shearing [(station)]

>  From each side of a central building.. are the night pens and shearing floors. (Oct 12, 1885 -- ,Wairarapa Daily,2)

>  <b>KOEBEL</b> [The fleeces] was all here on top of the nightpen rail. (1907 -- ,Return of Joe,305)

>  <b>REES</b> [A nightpen] is inside the [shearing] shed. The sheep are kept in there ready for the morning's shearing. [check this quotation] (1928 -- ,Wild, Wild Heart,72)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> ... they [the tally pens] can be used as a night pen. No actual night pen is lost by this, ... . Except in dire circumstances it is not good practice to fill the catching pens for use as night pens, ... (1955 -- ,Wool Away!,112)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> The woolshed was ... enormous with its four-stand shearing plant, large wool room and [73] storage space for about 200 bales, and a huge night pen to cover over 1,500 sheep. <i>Ibid.</i> 130 The drafting yards and dagging pens were concreted and all under cover, so they could also be used as night pens. (1986 -- ,Off the Sheep's Back,72)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a covered yard for retaining of dry sheep prior to shearing or a small area in woolshed or under cover to house stud stock during lambing or calving.

>  <b>TAIT</b> The old slab-built shed was therefore dismantled, the iron being used to enlarge the night pen of the new shed. (1958 -- ,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol II,347)

>  There are two shearing boards, one running down each side of the night pens; ten men shear on each side. (Oct 1891 -- ,NZ Farmer,418)

>  The night pen should be yarded warily in the afternoon. (Oct 1918 -- ,NZ Farmer,1243)

 <i>n.</i> = black nightshade

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> BLACK NIGHTSHADE (<i>Solanum nigrum</i>), is called also white nightshade, mother-of-thousands, black tomato, black potato, wonder berry, and by a serious confusion deadly nightshade and bittersweet. (1926 -- ,Weeds of New Zealand,152,1967 ed.)



 
 <i>n.</i> any of a large group of plants and shrubs related to the potato and poroporo, the berries of some of which, especially the potato, belladonna or deadly nightshade, are poisonous. See also BLACK NIGHTSHADE.

>  <b>BANKS</b> (1770 -- ,Banks Journal) [NOTES: see sowthistle]

## nikau
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> One half of the long native house had been prepared for the reception of the females ... it was neatly filled with the <i>nikau</i> and made very comfortable. (Nov 8, 1827 -- ,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,83,1961 ed.)



 
 <i>n.</i> a native palm tree with a tall unbranched trunk and a crown of fan-like leaves with very broad leaf-bases. [Maori nikau[i-macron]] 1.<i> Rhopalostylis</i> (formerly <i>Areca) Sapida</i>, the NEW ZEALAND PALM, NIKAU-PALM, the edible flesh of its shoots, or its leaf or leaves [>1843. `An Ordinance for imposing a tax on Raupo Houses, Session II. No. xvii. of the former legislative Council of New Zealand': -->[From A. Domett's collection of Ordinances 1850.] `Section 2,... there shall be levied in respect of every building constructed wholly or in part of raupo-->, nikau-->, toitoi-->, wiwi-->, kakaho-->, straw or thach of any description 20 [pounds].']

>  <b>HUNT</b> You could also make great pickles out of nikau. (1981 -- ,Speaking a Silence,130)

>  In <i>The Natural World of the Maori</i>, ethnologist Margaret Orbell states that the word "nikau" is derived from the Tahitian word for the leaf of the coconut palm which the nikau closely resembles - sans nuts. (Sep 2003 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 65/79)

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> N|Ikau, s. A shrub, so called from its numerous leaves proceeding from the same stalk. (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,185)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> One half of a long native house ... was neatly filled up with <i>nikau</i> and made very comfortable [sc nikau leaf] (Nov 8, 1827 -- ,Journal,83,1961 ed.)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> we rested at ten in a little shed constructed of palm leaves (the nigau) where the Waimate boys had passed the preceding night <i>Ibid.</i> 2/179 ... nigau ... (Nov 13, 1839 -- ,Journal,2/167)

>  <b>BURTON</b> Another tree called the Nikau is said to be very good; I have had a little bit and found it very nice, but was told that what I had was of a very inferior quality. (1982 -- ,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,20) [NOTES: 1844 Chapman in 1982 Burton Two Hundred Years of NZ Food 20]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> [The house was] covered with a thick coating of the nikau (a kind of palm) (1845 -- ,Adventure in NZ,1/270)

>  <b>MACKIE</b> I cut a beautiful leaf of `nikau', areca sapida, from 9 to 12 ft. long ... (Jul 23, 1853 -- ,Traveller under Concern,124,1973 ed.)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> <i>Areca sapida</i>, Soland. ... Nat. name, `Nikau', <i>Colenso. </i> (1853 -- ,Flora NZ,I Flowering Plants 262)

>  <b>LUSH</b> [He] said he would soon get me some luncheon ... We speedily spied a young <i>Nikau</i>, the native palm tree, and cutting off the upper end he cut the inside ... It tasted like sweet turnip. (Feb 4, 1854 -- ,Auckland Journals,150,1971 ed.)

>  <b>GOLDER</b> note The <i>necko</i> or <i>neko</i> is a large tree-like plant known elsewhere as the mountain cabbage. (1854 -- ,Pigeon's Parliament,75)

>  You have to make yourself a small hut of poles and thatch it with nikau (the N.Z. palm) ... (Nov 10, 1854 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/157) [NOTES: H.A. Atkinson]

>  There she lived for six weeks on Nicau and now and then a few potatoes, got at the risk of her life. (1864 -- ,Von Tempsky Memoranda,25)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Nikau, <i>Col[enso]. Areca sapida. </i> (1867 -- ,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766)

>  The young inner blanched leaves and heart of the Ti, or `Cabbage-tree' (<i>Cordyline australis</i>), and of the Nikau, or New Zealand Palm, (<i>Areca sapida</i>), were eaten both raw and cooked. (1869 -- ,TrNZI,1/III/32) [NOTES: Colenso Botany North Isd]

>  <b>MONEY</b> We found a good quantity of bush plant called `nikou,' the pith of which is much like hazel nut, and is very good eating when roasted or boiled. (1871 -- ,Knocking About in NZ,61)

>  <b>BAINES</b> The ground was covered with ... young seedling palms, `Nikou'. [sic] [149] ... Perhaps Nikau palms are the most beautiful specimens ... in the forests of New Zealand. (1874 -- ,Edward Crewe,148)

>  <b>DOMETT</b> Savoury Palm-tree (Nikau) ..... O. Palmeae; G.Areca; S. A. sapida. (1883 -- ,Ranolf and Amohia,2/336)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Nikau</i>... Maori name for a New Zealand palm-tree, <i>Areca sapida</i>, N.O. Palmeae. <i>Spelt also</i> Necho <i>and</i> Neko. (1898 -- ,Austral English,321)

>  Even the semi-tropical true pepper [fn <i>Piper excelsum</i>.] disputes with the false native pepper [fn <i>Drimys colorata</i>.] the right to its name, and yet another semi-tropical plant, the nikau [fn <i>Areca sapida</i>.] - New Zealand's only palm - extends as far south as Banks Peninsula, though not found on the Port Hills. (1900 -- ,Canterbury Old & New,184)

>  The nikau, or southern palm, is plentiful all through the district, and is everywhere accompanied by noble tree-ferns and palm-lilies. (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,116)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> For in their season he could supplement his dry fare with berries and nuts, with the tender bulbous shoots of the tii (cabbage tree), at heart white and delicate, baked in a haangi; the tender leaves of the nikau - that part between the last ring and where the leaf stems separate, below which there is a huge bulge, clasped firmly, clasp on clasp, with the grip of steel bands. (1905 -- ,Where the White Man Treads,16)

>  <i>nikau</i>, a palm (Rhopalostylis sapida), the one true palm of New Zealand. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  nikau The Maori name of a handsome New Zealand palm, the `nikau palm'. (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),275,8th ed. ed.)

>  ~ n. New Zealand palm-tree [Maori]. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  <i>nikau</i> ... n. N.Z. palm-tree [Maori]. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> nikau: a palm, whose leaves can be used to thatch roofs or decorate dance-halls. (1973 -- ,Brown Man's Burden,141) [NOTES: Pearson's Glossary]

>  <i>nikau n.</i> native NZ palm. [M] (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,507) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 328]



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  He.. took up his residence in a nikau whare with a clay floor. (Sep 21, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,12)

>  It was a wing of the nikau "homestead" of Alf. Woodham (Sep 21, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,12)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> as an attrib or in combin. NIKAU LEAF, HUT, TREE

>  <b>POLACK</b> Some remains of sheds, made of <i>nikau</i>, or palm leaves, indicated that some travellers had put up at this place previously to our arrival. (1838 -- ,New Zealand  (Polack),1/69)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> In the evening it came on very raining, we have encamped by a river in a nikau hut. (Jan 8, 1840 -- ,Journal,2/178)

>  <b>BODELL</b> We made a Miamia (a small house) composed of sticks tied together and the leaves of the NikauTree. (1890 -- ,Soldier's View of Empire, A,171,1982 ed.)

>  <b>CARBERRY</b> Curious customs in Maori Warfare ... she fled into the Bush when she heard our soldiers ransacking the whares and lived there for six weeks her only food being the pith of the Nikau Tree ... (1865 -- ,Journal)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  ...we set to work and built a nikau shelter and got a fire going in the bed of the creek... (Jan 2002 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 55/24) [NOTES: 1932 Journal of W M Hamilton in NZ Geographic]

>  Nikau fronds make an ideal impromptu shelter.. but the palm was important to Maori not just for thatching but also for weaving and as a food source: the young flower stalks and heart ("millionaire's salad") were the parts most commonly eaten. Even so, the first Polynesian arrivals to Aotearoa were no doubt nonplussed that the local palm did not bear edible fruit like the cocnut. Indeed, one explanation for the origin of the name "nikau" is that it means "no nuts." (Sep 2003 -- ,NZ Geographic,No. 65/85)

>  <b>HAWES</b> He'd learnt from his mother who'd learnt from her mother, back in the days when nikau jam was legal. (2000 -- ,Dream of Nikau Jam, The,18)



 
<b>3</b> <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Rhopalostylis Cheesemanii </i> (1919 -- ,NZ Plants & Their Story)



## nikau heart
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BURTON</b> Succulent and sweet to the taste, nikau heart later became known as `millionaire's salad' because the tree dies when the heart is removed. (1982 -- ,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,7)

>  Hungry children went to the bush and got the heart of nikau, a good food. (Sep 21, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,12)



## nikau hut
 
 <i>n.</i> a rough or makeshift hut made of (or thatched with) nikau leaves.

>  <b>TINNE</b> Our nearest neighbours are a colony of gum-diggers, at the Kaiwaka swamp ...; they have built quite a little village of these `nekau' huts ... (1873 -- ,Wonderland of the Antipodes,53)

>  He lived with his maternal grandparents, Pera Te Kaongahau and Hitia Te Rangihaeata in a nikau hut in the bush until he was seven. (Jan 2, 1982 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),35)



## nikau palm

## nikau whare
 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> Nikau whares are extremely pretty and picturesque, but are now rarely seen, owing to the cheapness of corrugated iron. Bushmen, however, still make them occasionally for temporaryresidences. (1907 -- ,Plants of NZ,84,1940 p. 87 ed.)

>  <b>MANDER</b> [The gum-digger] made a hut of sacks and mud and ti-tree, or he built a nikau whare, an art learned from the Maoris, out of the broad-leaved native palm. (1920 -- ,Story of a NZ River, The,230,1974 ed.)

>  <b>FEATON</b> And where the nikau whare stood,/ ... Now sheltering stands in calm repose,/ The Saxon cot, encircled with the rose. (1879 -- ,Waikato War,28)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> It was not long before the Rongo family instructed the brothers in building a ponga and nikau whare (bush hut) alongside their tent, in which they could cook and eat their meals. (1986 -- ,Off the Sheep's Back,14)

## ninety nine
 
 <i>exclam.</i>

>  <b>OGONOWSKA-COATES</b> Ninety-nine. A more recent addition to shearers' slang. When a woman enters the usually all-male shed, the words `ninety-nine' are passed down the board, presumably to give the shearers a chance to smarten up their language. (1987 -- ,Boards, Blades & Barebellies,97)

>  ninety nine: This means there is a woman on the board or at least within hearing distance, so watch your language. It originated from the Maxwell Smart television programme in the 1960s where the female agent was called `ninety nine'. (1989 -- ,NZ English Newsletter,3/26) [NOTES: Wallace Shearing Glossary]

 <i>n.</i> [also Ninety Miles Beach (South Island)]

This day, we decide to make use of the Ninety-Mile beach "road" and its the only place I've ever seen an official road sign on a beach. (Dec 18, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,15)

## nip
 
 <i>n.</i> a standard measure of spirits; a small glass of spirits (occas the approx? 3 fluid ounce glass a `nip' is served in) (cf NOBBLER)

>  Ordinary nips were being charged up to eighteenpence in the lounge and bar. (Nov 29, 1943 -- ,NZEF Times,III/127 : 8)

>  There would be from 21 to 22 quarter-gill "nips" in the bottle. (May 18, 1922 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,16)

>  Poor Haupokia.. will never again receive a "nip" out of the royal bottle. (May 3, 1873 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,5)

>  I had started ... with ... a bottle of grog fo [sic] the men on the other side of the river. ... I gave them a nip and then started back. (Jan 29, 1863 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,75,1950 ed.)

>  Going into pubs is worse. ... If I did get a skinful many a `nip' I drank and stood. (1869 -- ,Punch or the Auckland Charivari,83)

>  <b>MONEY</b> A man who was taking a `nip' five minutes before in a shanty that I had entered for the rum, ... (1871 -- ,Knocking About in NZ,122)

>  <b>LADY BARKER</b> The Landlord ... was amazed at the orders for tea and coffee instead of the usual `nips' of spirits. (1873 -- ,Station Amusements in NZ,103)

>  <b>CAIRD</b> He probably gets a `nip' as a glass of grog is called. (1874 -- ,NZ Sheepfarming,25)

>  <b>ELWELL</b> About every two hours he went round with a bottle of rum, giving each hand a small `nip', as it was called. (1878 -- ,Boy Colonists,31)

>  <b>BRADSHAW</b> [At the hotel] a halt was made, during which the ladies filled their pockets with `collies', and the gentlemen made an opportunity for a quiet `nip' (1883 -- ,New Zealand As It Is,85)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> Either he takes to `nips' and degenerates into a mere loafer, scrambling ... for odd shillings which he spends in `shouting' (standing drinks to his friends) - a life which ... never lasts long. (1888 -- ,Antipodean Notes,222)

>  <b>DAVIDSON</b> His friend Kelly could always do a stiff nip when off duty ... (1889 -- ,Stories of NZ Life,3)

>  [Hdg] Price Fixed for Standard Nips (Jul 16, 1945 -- ,NZEF Times,IV/212 : 2)



 
 <i>n.</i> pinch esp. Hard nip, hard pinch a steep part of a (coach or waggon) road

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> Look at mother and dad; pulled the old waggon uphill together, now they can spell-ho. Stands to reason; they took a good grip; hard nips here and there; wheels wanting grease and such-like! (1905 -- ,Where the White Man Treads,305)



 
 <i>v.</i> to take a (companionable) nip

>  <b>PHILLIPS</b> Giddy and mate called and nipped (Sep 22, 1867 -- ,Journal Kept at Rockwood and the Point,116)



 
 <i>v.</i> to snatch, steal [in ODE, US informal]

>  `Oh an old fellow came in while you were out and nipped your `paper'.' I said: `oh, did he?' `Yes,' said the other fellow, `he came in, looked over all the seats, and then put his peeper on your sheet and poled it.' (Jan 21, 1911 -- ,NZ Truth,291/6/5)

## Nippon clipon
 
 <i>n.</i> either of the Auckland Harbour Bridge extensions [often plural]

>  Auckland's Harbour Bridge had two lanes added to each side in the early seventies - "the Nippon Clip-ons" - and the contractors towed huge barges in from.. Japan to use as construction platforms. (Mar 1998 -- ,Cuisine,88)

>  <b>armstrong</b> Trouble is.. New Zealand First voters are corried about the big Nippon Clip-on you've attached to the bridge to let large numbers of Asian migrants in. (1997 -- ,True Colours,53)

>  The Auckland Harbour Bridge, built too small from the beginning, and forced to live its life forever with the indignity of the "Nippon clip-ons" hanging off its flanks is another [reminder of letting opportunity slip]. (Jan 31, 2001 -- ,Capital Times,19)

>  In Japan he will be involved in negotiations with ...... I.H.I., known in this country for its "Nippon clip-on" extension to the Auckland harbour bridge. (Aug 5, 1970 -- ,Otago Daily Times,2)

>  When we reach the western "Nippon Clip-on", one of the two massive pre-fab structures literally clipped on to the original structure in 1969 to add four lanes, you can see what he means. The clip-ons move visibly up up and down, up to half a metre at a time. (Oct 29, 2002 -- ,Dominion Post,D2)

>  As far as the public was concerned, what became known as the Nippon Clip-ons couldn't be attached soon enough. (Oct 5, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,5)

>  Built in 1959 as a four-lane span, its [sc. Auckland Harbour Bridge's capacity was doubled 10 years later when Japanese engineers built two more lanes ('The Nippon Clip-ons') on each side. (1987 -- ,Insight Guides (NZ),112)

>  <b>TEMPLE & APSE</b> The centre four lanes [of the Auckland Harbour Bridge] were opened in 1959 but traffic flows trebled in less than a decade forcing the addition of four more outer lanes. These are affectionately known as 'Nippon clip-ons' (1986 -- ,New Zealand From the Air,45)

>  <b>LELAND</b> Nippon-clipon: The AUCKLAND HARBOR BRIDGE was once a two-lane bridge ... Thanks to... Japanese engineering, it is now four lanes. The extra two were built in japan, towed to Auckland and attached to the sides of the existing bridge. (1980 -- ,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,70)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Nippon clipon the extra lanes on the outside of the Auckland harbour bridge, Japanese-made and attached without piles to existing structure. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)

>  The motion left more than a 10cm gap between the "Nippon clip-on" and the main bridge yesterday. (Apr 28, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,p1)

>  <b>POPE & POPE</b> Inevitably its [sc. Auckland Harbour Bridge's] design earns the nickname, 'the coat hanger', while the Japanese-built extensions adding additional lanes on either side are known as the 'Nippon clip-ons'. (1982 -- ,Mobil Illust. Guide NZ,45)

## niu
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) a pole erected for the religious ceremonial purposes of the 19th century Pai Marire cult. [Maori niu, divination] -->esp. in collocation with POLE, MAST [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  <b>IHIMAERA & LONG (ed.)</b> The shadow of the niu which fell upon the land has been taken from the land. (1982 -- ,Into the World of Light,32)

>  It was Hauhau who had raised the niu pole Rongo Nui at Maraekowhai. (Oct 1998 -- ,Mana,No. 24: 56)

>  Perhaps we could do well to remember another monument, away up the other end of the river, the niu pole, Riri kore ("No more war"), erected by the King movement at the cessation of hostilities. (Dec 3, 1994 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7)

>  A pole of a certain height, to be called a Niu, should be erected, around which all true believers were to worship, standing in a circle; those found worthy would receive the gift of tongues. (May 16, 1879 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,14)

>  In the flat open space behind the pa there was a pole (or niu) standing used in their new Pai Marire religion. It was about 10 feet high, square for 2 or 3 feet at top & bottom & rounded in the middle. <i>Ibid.</i> 2/155 (A.S.Atkinson) I was curious to know where the first niu, or worshipping post, was set up & when he said at Kaitake during the present war I said I did not see it there. `To you,' he said, `it was a color (flagstaff) to us it was a niu. . <i>Ibid.</i>2/228 (A.S. Atkinson) There is a flagstaff here [Waimate Plains], fenced in and surmounted by the figure of a bird, probably a kawekawea as it was a great niu or Pai Marire pole at one time. (Oct 11, 1864 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/125,1960 ed.) [NOTES: A.S. Atkinson]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Their [Hauhaus'] worship consisted in uttering some singular compositions containing a strange mixture of heathenism with popery and gibberish, which they chanted whilst dancing round a pole, called their Niu; (1868 -- ,Past and Present of NZ,147)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> [The priest] cast the niu rods, or interpreted the `jerkings' (takiri) of the limbs or body of asleeping man ... (1904 -- ,Maori Race, The,336)

>  <b>COWAN</b> This niu was the central symbol of worship under Te Ua's dispensation. The term was the olden Maori word for the short sticks used by the tohunga in his mystic arts of divination, particularly before a battle. Te Ua's niu was a tall pole or flag-mast, round which the faithful were to march in procession chanting their hymns. <i>Ibid..</i> 157 A tall niu, the pole of worship, stood in the principal part of the settlement; ... There were similar niu masts at other villages ... (1922 -- ,NZ Wars, The,2/6)

>  <b>COWAN</b> At the principal villages in that rugged region ... the Hauhau poles, the sacred niu, were set up and round them marched the half-crazed people ... chanting their newly learned hymns ... the prayers of the `Kororia,' Maorified English phrases and scraps of English and Roman Catholic church services. (1940 -- ,Sir Donald Maclean,85)

>  <b>BABBAGE</b> The central object of worship [of the new religion of Tella Haumene] was the `Niu.' The origin of this innovation was described in the following terms:- .in 2;`The Angel Gabriel said: `Go back to your house and erect a niu.' Horopapera enquired what a niu was. The Angel replied: `A post.' Horopapera enquired for what pur- pose? The Angel replied: `To work for you for the acquirement of the languages of all the races upon the earth.'' The term `niu' was the old Maori term for the short sticks used by the tohunga priest in his mystic arts of divination, particularly before a battle. (1937 -- ,Hauhauism,29)

>  niu, divination by means of small rods. Niu is a Polynesian name of the coconut, and in the Pacific the nut was spun in a divination rite: the Maori lost the name for the tree when he came to Newzealand, but retained it for the divinatory rite. The Hauhau fanatics gave the name niu to the pole they set up for their ceremonial dancing procession i[n] what was a horrid kind of maypole dance. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The services of the new Maori evangelism were held at a niu, a long pole, perhaps fifty feet high, with yard-arms from which hung ropes. The congregation revolved roundthis mast as round a maypole while the priests conducted a service of prayer. . <i>Ibid.</i> 302 [Maori glossary] niu Small sticks used in divining;a pole (1959 -- ,History of New Zealand,137)

>  <b>BRATHWAITE</b> NIU Mast erected for purposes of religous rites and observances in Pai-marire faith. (1967 -- ,Evil Day,380) [NOTES: Gloss.]

>  But that is Pakeha history. More significant are the Nui poles, erected in the early 1860s and well-known to canoeists who traverse the river today. The remaining two poles, at Marae-kowhai, are distinguished by their cross-arms reaching to the compass points, oneof which (Rongo Niu) has carved hands on it. From this flew the war pennants of the Pai Marire or Hauhau, around which the warriors danced prior to battle. (Mar 31, 1983 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> A few [colonial] expressions were not familiar to me. When we should say in England, `Certainly not', it is here `No fear', or `Don't you believe it'. When they want to answer in the affirmative, they say, `It is so', `It does so'. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,ch.iii 31) [NOTES: 1860 Jan 27 in 1863 Butler First Year]

>  <b>BUTLER</b> A few [colonial] expressions were not familiar to me. When we should say in England, `Certainly not', it is here `No fear', or `Don't you believe it'. When they want to answer in the affirmative, they say, `It is so', `It does so'. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,ch.iii 31) [NOTES: 1860 Jan 27 in 1863 Butler First Year]

## Niu Tireni

>  New-Zealanders have long spoken of `Peowhairangi,' Bay of Islands; `Akarana,' Auckland; `Niu Tirani,' New Zealnd, &c. <i> Ibid.</i> 25/411 In some cases our English names of places have been adopted by the Maori, with such alteration as is necessary to make them easily pronounced by Maori organs of speech. >  I observed that the term `New Zealander' is already employed in this district; only, in lieu of `Noui-Tireni", as the natives of the Bay of Islands pronounce it, they say `Noui-Tirangui' [Nui Tirangi] [sic], which gives the word more of an indigenous sound. The word pakeha serves them to signify all whites, whom they also call `Iouropi' (Europeans). I did not observe that they had a special name for the English. (1909 -- ,TrNZI,41/139) [NOTES: Smith transl. D'Urville's Visit to Tolaga Bay in 1827]

## No 2
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> [quoting Thatcher] Take this glass of nice P.B.,/ Which I now hold in my hand,/ 'Tis of the brand of Hennissy [sic],/ Taste this most superior brand,/ ... Pale brandy or P.B. was the favourite spirit [in Dunedin, 1860s], and a sparkling moselle, which was dubbed champagne, was popularunder the name of No. 2. There was even a bar on board the harbour steamer ... and I have heard No. 2 called for there on the way to Port Chalmers. ... I was told that owing to the large sales of No. 2 the producers sent a consignment of No. 1, which was the better liquor, but no one would buy it. (1925 -- ,Random Recollections,17)

## No 8/Number 8 (wire/fencing wire)
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) standard Number eight gauge fencing wire

>  <b>MARRIOTT</b> The sag on 65 metres of no 8 carrying the weight of a man is considerable, and for three-quarters of the way it was downhill. (1981 -- ,Life in the Gorge,30)

>  Ordinary No. 8 fencing-wire is stretched along the top of each row of poles each way ... (Feb 20, 1937 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,108)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> Along came also the hunter chains concept of fencing and the introduction of high tensile 125 gauge wire which was slowly to replace man's best friend, the number eight wire. (1994 -- ,I Was So Lucky,186)

 <i>n.</i> a Number 8 gauge (4mm) fencing wire, esp. when used inventively and practically for other than fencing purposes; a smooth wire, 8 gauge; a standard wire, of many and almost legendary uses around the farm

>  No! - let me tell you about No 8 fencing wire... >  (2.) Batten and wire fence, at least 3 ft. 9 in. high, of substantial material, firmly erected, with one or more wires, not lighter than No. 8 in steel, or black or galvanised iron; battens either driven securely into the ground and securely stapled, or intertwined in wires in an upright position, and not more than 3 in. apart. <i>Ibid.</i> 19 ... and a good paling fence on ordinary bush land with double No. 8 wires top and bottom, with 1/2 in. palings, and 7 in. posts sunk 2 ft. in the ground, can be erected at about 12s. per chain. >  <b>LELAND</b> number 8 fencing wire: The traditional 5 wire fence on New Zealand farms is made with this stuff. In consequence it is one of the things that is always available round a farm when you need to build or repair something. It is said that some cockies can put together a landrover, a tractorand half a milk shed with nothing but a roll of number 8. Wire comes in sizes ranging from 1 to approx. 64, with 1 being the thickest. Number 8 is 5/32 of an inch or about half the diameter of a pencil. (1980 -- ,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A)

>  <b>GLEN</b> This time I took a piece of No.8 wire and carried the trap to the creek. (1980 -- ,Bush in Our Yard,68)

>  Wire ... No. 8 wire: Smooth wire, 8 gauge (4mm). (1982 -- ,MAF Agricultural Glossary,30)

>  <b>CHRISTIE</b> Christoffer looked at what he supposed was a gate. It was made of barbed wire and battens strung between two strainer posts to which it was tied with No 8 fencing wire. Being the first Taranaki gate he had encountered, he studied the primitive contraption with a puzzled frown, then realised it was simplicity itself. `Just lift off this wire loop.' The gate sprang from his fingers, coiled into the air and collapsed in a tangled heap. Christoffer cursed the man who had invented it. However he had to admit that it was an efficient, cheap and easily-made thing - a sort of `instant gate'. <i>Ibid.</i> 150 The swing bridge, made of No 8 fencing wire, made a good support for the pipelines. (1987 -- ,Candles and Canvas,86)

>  <b>MILLER97</b> No. 8 wire was to him as string is to the average man. He could tie it ..., and with a strainer he could tighten a wire until it literally sang when it was flicked. (1946 -- ,There Was Gold)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> The only tools we used to build the camp were an axe, spade, slasher, a short length of No. 8 wire, ... (1986 -- ,Off the Sheep's Back,56)

>  No 8 wire has always been the great standby of rural handypersons. (Jul 23, 1988 -- ,Dominion,8)

>  New Zealanders are proud of their `do it yourself' flair, whipping up anything to order with a few lengths of four-by-two and some No. 8 fencing wire. (Feb 14, 1989 -- ,Press, The,6)

>  One by one [the vehicles] ground to a halt, despite some amazing feats of No-8-wire-mechanical engineering and cannibalism. (Sep 12, 2001 -- ,Capital Times,2)

>  ...nimble-fingered Hawke's Bay ladies.. started tossing scone dough round tins with 5 cent piece holes, and dousing the dough with local boy-made-good James Wattie's canned spaghetti in tomato sauce that it hit me... Now we're an enlightened species, we all know it was a No 8 fencing wire attempt that spawned parlours and pizzerias more resembling the real thing. (Feb 22, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,F8)

>  He is an eloquent chap, wild-haired and more than a little wild-haired and more than a little wild-eyed as he reels off the traumatic list of disasters and desperate remedies that made up the "No-8-wire treasure hunt". (Jan 31, 2004 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23)

>  Innovation exponents have criticised New Zealand's "No 8 wire" philosophy as outdated and question its relevance to the country's constantly evolving national identity... Historian Jock Phillips said No 8 wire was an ingrained part of the national identity, but did not exclusively belong to New Zealand. "I think there's been a very powerful mythology about it, which you will find also in other countries ... Australia, the United Kingdom, and the United States," he said. (Jun 28, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,C3)



## No 8/number 8 (wire/fencing wire)
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) [fig.] a symbol for Kiwi ingenuity.

>  No 8 wire can't fix it: New Zealand needs to tap into the international corporate culture. The No 8 mentality is about making do, about having no ambition to be or have the best. (Mar 3, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,E2)

>  Number Eight the best, strongest, most likely to succeed; from the thick gauge No. 8 fencing wire used on farms; eg Joy Shepard remembers a doctor saying to a patient on whom he had done surgery that had come a bit unstuck: `I'll use No.8 this time and make a real job of it.' (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,77)

>  With a No 8 fencing wire approach, he has turned the logistical labyrinth into a workable reality. (Jan 30, 2006 -- ,Dominion Post,A11)

>  Whack 'em on the backs of the trucks with No 8 wire and drive as fast as we can? (Mar 3, 2001 -- ,Dominion,22)

>  This, together with the sheer competence of the New Zealand farmer, is what has made the varied uses of number eight wire - the metaphor for many New Zealanders' basic approach to life," said Dame Silvia. (Jun 14, 2001 -- ,Waikato Times,4)

>  'Beyond No 8 wire' is the theme of Massey University Richmond Southern North Island Sheep and Beef cattle farmers conference ... (May 2, 2002 -- ,Internet - see Notes,News) [NOTES: www.agriculture.massey.ac.nz]



## No 8/Number eight (wire)
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of people or ventures] ingenious.

>  <b>DENSEM</b> From these layers outwards "Men," in the number-eight-wire sense, dominated. (1995 -- ,Letter,90) [NOTES: in Dominy (2001) Calling the Station Home]

>  Despite this failure Richard Pearse was a genuine Kiwi original - a No 8-wire man of the first rank. (Nov 1994 -- ,Quote Unquote,30)

>  <b>DOMINY</b> As the back country that "number eight Men" populated gets retired and covenanted for conservation purposes ... (2001 -- ,Calling the Station Home,91)

>  If you have a production story, a community event or comments for the programme, the No 8 team would love to know. (May 22, 2002 -- ,RD1.com,On the Farm 39)

>  Burt Munro was the ultimate eccentric - a real 'number eight' wire inventor. (May 2007 -- ,Tearaway,22)

 <i>phr.</i> (See [beg-pardon](../B#beg-pardon))



## no biggie
 
 <i>phr.</i> no big deal

>  "I'm not really too fussed about it," he shrugged. "If it happens it happens. But it's no biggie." (Jul 23, 2000 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B1)

>  I don't think I've got to churn out a play every year too. If it happens for me too it's great but if it doesn't that's no biggie. (Mar 3, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,F7)



## no fear
 
 <i>exclam.</i> I don't believe you; not under any circumstances; no way, an exclamation intensifying a negative (`certainly not') answer to a question or request is very common in NZ, and often thought to be local idiom.

>  <b>BUTLER</b> A few [colonial] expressions were not familiar to me. When we should say in England, `Certainly not', it is here `No fear', or `Don't you believe it'. When they want to answer in the affirmative, they say, `It is so', `It does so'. (1863 -- ,First Year in Canterbury Settlement) [NOTES: 1860 Jan 27 in 1863 Butler First Year]

>  <b>MUTER</b> A `mob of cattle', to use the colonial term, after a few expressions of `My word' and `No fear' would be sold at some 10 pounds per head. (1864 -- ,Travels in NZ,2/273)

>  <b>BURGESS</b> He replied: `Did you think I was going to let a bloody wretch like that best me? No fear, the - shall sweat there till Monday ...' (1866 -- ,Confessions,93,1983 ed.)

>  <b>COOPER</b> One energetically declared that the new ground to the south was the best, while another ... vociferated, `No fear! no fear!'. (Dec 14, 1867 -- ,Digger's Diary,22,1978 ed.)

>  <b>BAINES</b> `Why, Janson, I thought she was going to turn turtle.' `No fear', he replied ... (1874 -- ,Edward Crewe,75)

>  As expletives `My word' and `No fear' are favourites, both drawn out as long as possible. (1885 -- ,TrNZI,18/54) [NOTES: J.C.Crawford]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> `Are you not going to make a pile at the diggings ... ' `No fear,' laughingly replied Leslie; ... (1881 -- ,Waitaruna,145)

>  `Can you produce them?' `No fear'. (1895 -- ,Diary of Jonathan Roberts,5)

>  It is the result of conviction or the dictates of conscience? No fear. Nothing of the kind. (Jun 24, 1905 -- ,NZ Truth,1-2)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The settlers, he learnt, said `no fear' instead of `certainly not'. [He = Samuel Butler]. (1959 -- ,History of New Zealand,101)

>  <b>MUIR</b> `She didn't commit no bloody suicide, mate. No bloody fear. The flatties thought of that one. Mind you, she was in pod, you know.' (1960 -- ,Word for Word,254)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> ... I'd like to see the bloody Hun who's going to make me start now. No fear, me old cobber General Freyberg, VC,'d never forgive me. (1975 -- ,Breathing Spaces,97)

>  <b>HUNT</b> And there was no hot water laid on, no fear. (1981 -- ,Speaking a Silence,20)

>  <b>LADY CAMPBELL</b> [Tobin warns the speaker that it is T's father he is to tell bad news of] `No fear; I am not likely to forget.' (1864 -- ,Martin Tobin,2/68)



## no good to gundy
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  This [interference] was no good to Gundy, so cobber the wood-chopper got busy swinging his axe ... (Sep 2, 1918 -- ,Quick March,1)

>  (phrase) elaboration of the simple phrase `no good to me' [orig. doubtful, possibly American. [cf NZ nickname]. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. (Aust.-NZ Suppl.),616)

>  gundy, no good to (Phr.) elaboration of phrase not good enough for me [Scot. coll. gundie greedy]. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),10)



## No Maoris, No Tour
 
 <i>phr.</i> slogan of the campaign to prevent the All Blacks touring South Africa without Maori representation

>  The subsequent success of the No Maoris - No Tour movement affirmed the belief that New Zealand rugby would be played the New Zealand way and not according to the repugnant rules applying in South Africa. (Jun 28, 1986 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),5)

>  The radical <i>Notes from the Second Year</i>.. focused me very firmly in the muddled turmoil of No Maori No Tour, hippiedom and the Vietnam war. (Sep 17, 1990 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),108)



## no milk in one's coconut
 
 <i>phr.</i> no brains in one's head

>  <b>BEARDSLEY</b> no milk in her coconut no brains in her head (1984 -- ,Blackball 08,244)



## no sense sticking your nose in butter...
 
 <i>phr.</i> in full: no sense sticking your nose in butter if you're going to have to eat dripping the rest of your life

>  <b>McGILL</b> no sense sticking your nose in butter if you're going to have to eat dripping the rest of your life a discouragement to put on airs if you cannot afford them, usually parental negativity towards offspring leaving the nest in Depression era, as quoted in Stevan Eldred-Grigg's Oracles and Miracles. (Longest c/p known to compiler.) (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)



## no show/not a show
 
 <i>phr.</i> no chance (also as exclamation - see notes) [As exclamation meaning something like Not Likely, or No Chance, or Over my Dead Body, as in: "Are you going to the Smith's Cocktail Party?" "No show!" This is quite different from no-show as used in restaurants - "They're a no-show" meaning they didn't turn up to claim their table reservation.]

>  <b>BARLOW</b> Engineers, to use a colonialism, have not a `show' at all [of making money] - particularly now that the borrowing policy has been partially given up. (1889 -- ,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,205)

>  <b>ASHTON-WARNER</b> `She's dying ... [sic] or should be; there's not a show.' `What does that mean ... [sic] `there's not a show'?' `Oh ... [sic]', he laughs. `It means `She hasn't got a chance'. New Zealandese.' (1960 -- ,Incense to Idols,162)



## no worries
 
 <i>phr.</i> not a problem (US), she'll be right; cf. no sweat [ca1961 Wellington No worries. I'll get a dozen from home.]

>  <b>McGill</b> 'Forget the damned dog,' Jack growled. 'Ring that priest, will you? See if he's coming.' 'No worries,' Ron said, wondering what was eating Jack. (1995 -- ,Gold in the Creek,29)

>  <b>McGILL</b> no worries expression of confidence; eg `Even without Martin Crowe we're a match for the Aussies, no worries.' NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)

## no-go area
 
 <i>n.</i> area dubbed "low employment location" (See [dead zone](../D#dead-zone))

(Mar 6, 2005 -- ,Sunday Times (NZ),A15)



## no-go zone
 
 <i>n.</i> areas in which benefits will not be paid to unemployed able-bodied workers

>  The no-go zones policy perpetrates the idea the unemployed people are dole bludgers, willing to move to Nightcaps or Ruatahuna in order to render themselves unemployable beyond all risk. (Mar 6, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,B4)

>  Footrot Flats bas been declared a no-go zone. Cooch hasn't had his dole cut off yet, but is being regularly hassled by Work and Income to move to the city. (Apr 2004 -- ,Green Times,4)



## no-hoper
 
 <i>n.</i> totally incompetent or inadequate person socially or at sport [1960 17C F B10 Napier Girls H.S. 10 NO HOPER -->1960 17C M B6 St Bede's, Chch 1 NO-HOPER ->1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) no-hoper]

>  <b>TURNER</b> Marginally colloquial are the words from phrases no-hoper `useless person' and offsider `horse harnessed at the right of a main horse in a team', `helper', `companion' generally. (1966 -- ,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,46)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Her husband, whom she openly scorned as a no-hoper, sold paint in the hardware store ... (1968 -- ,Pagan Game, The,166)

>  no-hoper n. NZ & Austral. sl. incompetent person, failure. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,509) [NOTES: nf 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

>  <b>McGILL</b> no-hoper totally incompetent or inadequate person socially or at sport; used in horse racing for a useless racehorse; eg `Cripes, that horse is a no-hoper: six starts, and last in every one.' c. 1925. NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)



 
 <i>n.</i> a. A horse with no prospect of winning, an outsider. b. A useless or incompetent person, one from whom no good can be expected. [In OED as Austral. from 1943]

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> Thew working class in state housing districts were also the worst working class. No-hopers. (1995 -- ,Mum,62)

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> The working class in state housing districts were also the worst working class. No-hopers. (1995 -- ,Mum,62)

>  We've seen some amazing talent come out of people who would otherwise have been called no-hopers. (Oct 18, 2001 -- ,Evening Post,37)

>  <b>McGEE</b> I've got a wife and child to pull through this world, and that kid is not going to be sitting in a class of no-hopers getting the sort of shit thrown at him that I got. (1981 -- ,Foreskin's Lament,39)



## no-license

 <i>n.</i> [attrib. use; see no-license noun]

>  From Oamaru - which is a "no-license" town. (Dec 30, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,1)

 <i>n.</i> the standard, number eight gauge fencing wire

>  The notions that Mitchell whipped up out of surfboards, Tom Wolfe and No8 wire still resonate. (Aug 27, 2005 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33)



## noa
 
 <i>adj.</i> free from tapu or any other restriction

>  <b>IHIMAERA & LONG</b> The taniwha in im curled up and went to sleep with a contented smile on its face. Henry at last was noa. (1982 -- ,Into the World of Light,218)

>  Metcalf explains the Maori concept of noa, where food is used 'to make things normal, to make things okay, after a cermony". (Jul 5, 2003 -- ,Nelson Mail,13)

>  After a burial everyone comes back for the hakari or feast that brings back noa. (Jul 5, 2003 -- ,Nelson Mail,13)

>  The solution was to draw an aukati line around the floor, dividing tapu from noa. (Nov 23, 2005 -- ,NZ Herald,B5)

>  In Tauranga there has been a plethora of disturbing unexplained phenomena, which has resulted in calls for tohunga intervention to make the area "noa" - or free from tapu. (Jul 24, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,TV Week : 15)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Common Noa (1817 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/336) [NOTES: English-NZ Vocabulary]

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> N|oa, a. Common, free, disengaged, &c. (1820 -- ,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,185)

>  <b>WILEY & MAUNSELL</b> He replied that his grandfather Kukutai had sent word that he would not consent to his (Ngataru's) becoming Noa (common) while he retained those garments. (Nov 2, 1839 -- ,Robert Maunsell,61,1938 ed.)

>  <b>BIDWILL</b> The natives always require an additional consideration for taking off the `taboo', or making `noa' any places which may be included in a purchase. (1841 -- ,Rambles in NZ,118,1952 ed.)

>  <b>SHORTLAND</b> [The] ceremony of making `noa' the `taua', that is, of restoring the men who composed it to their ordinary condition, by severing the intimate connexion existing between them and the `Atua' so long as they remained `tapu'. <i>Ibid/</i> 103 Every thing not included under the class of tapu is called noa, meaning free or common. (1851 -- ,Southern Districts of NZ,69)

>  ... the spirit is held fast whilst some food is being cooked; and ultimately the tapu is pronounced `no' `[sic] or void'. (Aug 15, 1857 -- ,Lyttelton Times,3)

>  Up to the time when the planting commenced everything was noa, or `common', but once the seed began to be handled ... the whole thing became tapu, or consecrated... (1903 -- ,TrNZI,35/17)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> The new-born babe and its mother were both very sacred at this time and not to be touched by outsiders till they had been made `common' (noa). (1904 -- ,Maori Race, The,43)

>  noa, common; free from tapu. (1946 -- ,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> The dining-house was noa, and slaves or any person could enter it. (1948 -- ,Taina,29)

>  <b>HOWE</b> Noa - the narrowest sense of this word is `freeing from restriction' or `cleansing', but like itscomplementary quality `tapu' (q.v.) it pervades all aspects of Maori life and custom. Generally it is associated with cooked food, water, women, the low-born, and the young, good, and ordinary life events. (see `tapu') Only women or special males have the power to remove `tapu'. (1985 -- ,Towards Taha Maori,21)

## nobble
 
 <i>v.</i> of money, to spend, to use up (possibly reformed from nobbler; or from nobble to secure a person (or racehorse) to one's side by bribery OED 1865; NZ 1890 (Parliament))

>  <b>WICKHAM</b> The bridge, a noble structure - ... half a hundred thousand was `nobbled' to build it - still spans the stream. (1888 -- ,Ramblings,60)



## nobbler
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Sure enough there were two full whisky nobblers on my dresser. (1969 -- ,Landfall,23(1) No. 89?29)

>  The hotel-keepers and publicans ofWestport.. reduced the price of theindispensable "nobbler" to the smallcharge of one sixpence!... Moreover, thereduction was made not alone for thatday, but for all time; "stout, spiders,and hot grog excepted." (Mar 27, 1869 -- ,Westport Times,2)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> ... a box of `nobbler glasses', as small thick tumblers of limited capacity were called. (1913 -- ,Sodger Sandy's Bairn,79)

>  nobbler n. a drink of spirits. - nobb'lerise v.i. to drink heavily. (1929 -- ,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,8)

>  <b>BRERETON</b> Mr Ross [`true Milesian type of Irishman'] had lived in America and sailed on Yankee ships and he affected a few Yankee expressions. He often spoke of having a `nobbler of whiskey'. (1947 -- ,No Roll of Drums,77) [NOTES: [Refce.1880's]]

>  <b>DAVIN</b> Putting the nobbler down empty (1949 -- ,Roads from Home,45)

>  <b>MUNDY</b> ... from a bottle of whiskey poured him out a good nobbler, (1953 -- ,Days That Are No More,30)

>  ~ n. a glass of spirits. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  nobbler n. glass of spirits. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  (1969 -- ,Landfall,23/29)

>  <b>WILSON</b> The steward came over with our drinks, and by the time we'd paid for our nobblers Don had ... disappeared again. (1973 -- ,NZ Jack,91)

>  nobbler n. NZ & Austral. sl. small alcoholic drink. [orig. unkn.] (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,508) [NOTES: nf 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  <b>McGILL</b> nobbler mid-C19 dram of any alcoholic spirit, usually gin, brandy, whisky, rum or brandy. NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,76)

>  Meals, beds, and horse-feeds were from half a crown to 5s each, and nobblers 1s. (Mar 11, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,60)

>  <b>DAVIES</b> On one occasion, an inventive genius tooka "nobbler" of "pain killer", pronouncedit good, and the whole stock was sold ata shilling a nip. (1884 -- ,Poems & Other Literary Remains,273)

>  When the hateful `nobbler' shall be forgot,/ (Nov 1856 -- ,Canterbury Rhymes,41)

>  1/306 (Sep 12, 1857 -- ,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/306,1960 ed.) [NOTES: (C.W. Richmond)]

>  <b>HIGGINS</b> In the Colonies glasses of anything are called noblers so that if youwant anything you ask for a nobler of what you want... (Dec 27, 1858 -- ,Letters,(3)) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]

>  <b>SHAW</b> You are accustomed to the lazy and lounging and nobblerizing community too often to be found in the towns of New Zealand and in Sydney where people prefer ...nobblers to occupation. [F.N.] A `nobbler' is, I believe, half a glass of grog or spirit. (1858 -- ,Gallop to the Antipodes, A,58)

>  <b>UNCLE JOHN</b> What harm in the young wife serving them over the counter with nobblers. (1859 -- ,Hints to Colonists,16)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> In vain I nobbers drank. (1862 -- ,Canterbury Songster,9)

>  I subjoin the retail prices of a few articles sold at Tuapeka:- Brandy, 15s. per bottle, or 1s.6d. per nobbler; ... common lace-up boots, from [pounds]2 to [pounds]2 10s. per pair; American pegged boots, [pounds]3 10s.; ... (Oct 1, 1861 -- ,NZ Goldfields,31,1961 ed.)

>  .. business, drinking `nobblers' (1864 -- ,Chambers Edinburgh Journal,349) [NOTES: A glance at Dunedin]

>  When [spirits] shall be as common as ginger beer .... men will not think of treating each ither to the deleterious nobbler. (1864 -- ,Saturday Review,1/3)

>  ... sending up a nobbler or a fig of tobacco. (Aug 12, 1864 -- ,Dunedin Punch,113)

>  <b>MUELLER</b> From them I heard that a report has been spread in Invercargill of Price selling nobblers on board the steamer Bruce. ... Price refused to serve them, saying he would sell spirits only by the bottle, and I know for certain that never did sell one single nobbler while here. (Oct 12, 1865 -- ,My Dear Bannie,59,1958 ed.)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> And the [bar] counters all are full, and folks for nobblers loudly call,/ (1865 -- ,Otago Songster,10)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> a nobbler from his private cellar stood for good sauce for our meal. (1867 -- ,Rambles with a Philosopher,49)

>  <b>FRERE</b> Even at that early hour in the morning, `nobblers' were considered desirable. (1870 -- ,Antipodes,102)

>  <b>MONEY</b> ... and there behind the bar ... was Reinecker ... serving a `nobbler' to a sashed and booted digger. <i>Ibid.</i> 102 Turning into the nearest store, I called for a nobbler, and asked the storeman, while he bittered the decoction, what he would give a man for bringing the various articles he required. (1871 -- ,Knocking About in NZ,13)

>  <b>TINNE</b> ... a `nobbler' or drink ... <i> Ibid.</i> 5 ... when I requested them to give me a `nobbler' ordrink, his wife informed me that not having a spirit license [sic], she must refuse to serve me; ... (1873 -- ,Wonderland of the Antipodes,3)

>  <b>WISNIOWSKI</b> .. I went to fetch the brandy - a quarter of a litre f it, the quantity served in the colonies, in smalltumblers of very thick glass. This modest drink is known as a `nobbler'. (1877 -- ,Tikera,250)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> There's nothing like a good stiff nobbler first thing in the morning for picking you up. (1881 -- ,Waitaruna,220)

>  <b>NESFIELD</b> Their object is the `nobbler'. (1881 -- ,Chequered Career,29)

>  <b>HOPEFUL</b> If spirits are called for, instead of the quartern or half-quartern, it is here, and inAustralia, called a `nobler.'[sic] (1887 -- ,Taken In,135)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Nobbler, n. a glass of spirits; lit. that which nobbles or gets hold of you. Nobble is the frequentative form of nab. No doubt there is an allusion to the bad spirits frequently sold at bush public-houses, but if a teetotaler had invented the word he could not have invented one involving stronger condemnation.[1852] (1898 -- ,Austral English,321)

>  <b>SATCHELL</b> I'll take a nobbler of rum. (1902 -- ,Land of the Lost,25)

>  ... dispensing the sixpenny nobbler to the multitude. (Sep 30, 1905 -- ,NZ Truth,15/1/6)

## nobody home

## nodder
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a nodding thistle.

>  <b>CANDY</b> Gather ye nodders while ye may, They'll only be worse another day. Recently a prize was awarded for the biggest nodder, and it went to a grazier with a choice specimen nearly 3m high. (1988 -- ,Every Living thing,13)



## noddie
 
 <i>n.</i> A four-wheeled barrow or small wagon on which passenger luggage was formerly (to the mid-1970s) placed for carriage on the inter-island ferries. The noddie, or a rake of them, are drawn by hand or by tractor onto the ferry, and again onto the terminal at disembarkation.

>  A slowly moving rake of waggons had caught a luggage `noddie' ... and the noddie in turn had caught the deceased ... I instructed him to take off the centre rake of railway wagons first, and then pull off one passenger luggage barrow or `noddie' by hand. A tractor was not needed or necessary. ... I then discovered a smashed luggage noddie on the port side of the linkspan [of the Cook Strait rail-ferry] and could see feet protruding from beneath it. (May 26, 1972 -- ,Evening Post,18)



## nodding thistle
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [thistle](../T#thistle))

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> NODDING THISTLE (<i>Carduus nutans</i>), also called bastard Scotch thistle, is found in Auckland, South [established in] Canterbury and Otago. [and is increasing in other parts of both islands.] ... It is biennial with deeply-cut prickly edged leaves, ... (1926 -- ,Weeds of New Zealand,192,1967 ed.)

>  <i>nodding thistle</i> [s]<i> Carduus nutans</i> L. <i>Ibid.</i> 78 <i>thistle, nodding </i>[s] <i>Carduus nutans</i> L. (1969 -- ,Standard Common Names for Weeds,53)

## nogging
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  NOG: (Bld) A block of wood the size of a brick, built into a wall to provide a means of nailing, skirting, etc. Hence "nogging", the filling in of the spaces between timber in walls and partitions.NOGGING PIECES : Stiffening pieces between studs. (Dec 1957 -- ,NZ Timber Journal,59)

>  The building is what they call brick nogging. (Feb 3, 1853 -- ,Journal of Henry Sewell,1/123,1980 ed.)

>  <b>PAUL</b> ... church of brick noggin ... [fn] The design ... was for a long, high structure of timber with brick in-filling and tower, but the length was reduced for economy reasons and the unseasoned timber shrank rendering the building unsafe. (1857 -- ,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,64)

>  <b>BACON</b> The house was a mere shell, all its constructional details plainly visible. Rafters, studs and noggings, in all their naked glory, flaunted themselves unashamedly before my eyes. <i>Ibid.</i> 181 (Glossary) noggings-In building, horizontal spacers securely fastened between the uprights (studs) of a house frame. Usually covered by exterior walls and interior lining material. (1963 -- ,In the Sticks,7)

## noho marae

>  You are required to attend four five-day intensive noho marae (live-ins) which take place at the beginning of each school holiday. (Dec 2, 2005 -- ,Whakatane Beacon,10)

>  These noho marae are held in the mataatua rohe (Whakatane student) and in Wellington (Wellington students). (Nov 17, 2006 -- ,Whakatane Beacon,13)

>  Most of your study is done at home. There are four noho marae held throughout the year, online tutorials and chat rooms, and you'll receive plenty of support from your lecturers who will visit your home area weekly. (Dec 2, 2005 -- ,Whakatane Beacon,10)

>  In addition to the evening classes and tutorials, you use DVD resources, a structured workbook and have two noho marae (live-ins). (Dec 2, 2005 -- ,Whakatane Beacon,10)



## nohoanga
 
 <i>n.</i> a campsite. [also kainga nohoanga, a temporary campsite.]

>  It was the announcement of the creation of nohoanga (exclusive riverside campsites facilitating access to customary fishing sites) and topuni reserves (the granting of additional status to areas of special significance) within the Ngai Tahu settlement that resulted in Mr Graham's recent comments that there were some different laws for Maori and pakeha. (Jun 15, 1997 -- ,Sunday Star Times,C1)

>  A total of 72 nohoanga (literally places of temporary occupation) entitlements will provide Ngai Tahu with temporary campsites near rivers and lakes throughout the tribal area. (Sep 28, 1997 -- ,Sunday Mirror Magazine (London),A6)

>  Misunderstandings around possible nohoanga (camping and food-gathering sites) had also arisen, Mr Logan said. (Aug 31, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,2)

>  That 1868 judgement [of the Native Land Court] also did not make redress regarding mahika kai or kainga nohoanga. (May 28, 2012 -- ,Otago Daily Times,A11)

>  More than 300 nohoanga have been found on the Waitaki River valley - a reflection, says Davis, of both its significance as a food source and as a convenient pathway through a landscape largely covered with scrub and bush to moa-hunting grounds in central Otago and pounamu rivers in Westland. (May 2001 -- ,NZ Geographic,51/44)

>  Nohoanga were established in the South Island under the Ngai Tahu Treaty settlement in 1996 but former Treaty settlements minister Doug Graham said no national park land would be used. (Aug 22, 2000 -- ,Dominion,1)

## non-New Zealander
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Suppose a friend says to me, `You know that drongo who was on the chain with me at the works last summer? Just a little joker he was, remember? A real serious ankle-biter. Hailed from somewhere down the Wire Wrapper. A right useless bastard. Couldn't see the road to the dunny if it had red flags on it. Well, it seems that he's shacked up with some pluty Remmers sheila and she's about to drop her bundle.' ... A non-New Zealander will probably require a translation, ... My friend is communicating to me the information that a slow-witted, incompetent person of diminutive stature, who was reared in the Wairarapa district and worked for a time in the killing gang of an abattoir, is now cohabiting with a wealthy woman from the affluent Auckland suburb of Remuera. This woman is soon to give birth. (Jan 1989 -- ,Pacific Way,10) [NOTES: Sharp New Zildish]

## non-strip

>  This is a non-strip shed. (Apr 11, 1960 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,13)



## non-stripping
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of farms, herds and cowsheds] where cows are not stripped before and after milking by machine.

>  This is a non-stripping shed. (Mar 1, 1947 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,7)

>  The adoption of a non-stripping technique and the elimination of leg-ropes from the shed will have other effects besides those mentioned. (Aug 1, 1949 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,15)

>  Expecting satisfactory production under a non-stripping milking technique, without due consideration to all other aspects of farm and shed management ... (Dec 1, 1952 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,54)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) the milking of cows without stripping before and after the process.

>  I had to axe one or two hard milkers when I first started but non-stripping has certainly speeded up the whole job. (Mar 1, 1947 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,7)

>  Where the milker handled 1.5 sets of cups or less, the percentage of herds practising non-stripping or machine-stripping was 16 per cent. (Aug 1, 1949 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,15)

>  The Dairy Board intends to obtain more detailed information on these latter farms and to examine the effects, if any, on production since non-stripping was introduced. (Mar 1, 1955 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,42)

>  Being one of the Nondies, or in otherwords, one of the small fry, I have beenasked to make a few remarks on theirbehalf. (1919 -- ,Blue and White,114)



## nong
 
 <i>n.</i> a fool; a silly or stupid person (cf NING-NONG) [1984 16 F E36 Pakuranga Coll. 1 NONG -->1960 16C F B1 Nelson Girls C. 1 NONG -->1960 16C F B3 Marton D.H.S. 1 NONG -->1960 16C F B4 Wanganui Girls C. 1 NONG -->1960 16C F B5 Wellington Girls C. 1 NONG -->1960 17C M B7 Hamilton H.S. 1 NONG 1961 June Stage I University Student's Essay She's a nong]

>  Some don't want urban nongs straying over their land, leaving gates open, biffing their rubbish; others just let them take their chances with the bulls. (Nov 16, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),58)

>  There may be the odd enrolled nong who thinks, "Well, I was going to vote for the one that starts with L, and has lots of red on it, but it looks as if I was mistaken and it actually starts with A and has a lot of green, silly me, oh well, tick tick." (Jul 6, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),5)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `I told you not to come, Dick. Don't be such a flaming galah.' ... `I can't see you. Don't be such a nong.' (1976 -- ,Pacific Star)

>  Mortimer looked a real nong. `This here is Mortimer,' said Fred. `He's a pongo, but he can't help it.' (1977 -- ,Listener Short Stories,147) [NOTES: 1973 Grant `NZ Short Story' in 1977 Listener Short Stories 147]

>  <b>BERRY</b> Like assuming that all [prison] psychologists are nongs ... (1980 -- ,First Offender,69)

>  <b>DUCKWORTH</b> Like assuming that all [prison] psychologists are nongs ... (1984 -- ,Disorderly Conduct,12)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `Don't be a nong. Why should we! You'll still have plenty of fun.' - (1984 -- ,South Pacific Street,57)

>  The poor little nong thinks deer velvet makes you sexy - he'll get himself killed. (Oct 1, 1987 -- ,Evening Post,34) [NOTES: (Footrot Flats)]

>  <b>McGILL</b> nong idiot; short for `ningnong'; eg `I tell you, Pasmore is a complete and utter nong. You only have to look at the stupid way he walks.' NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,77)

>  <b>JANET HOLMES/L. BAUER</b> In course of a lecture at x[?Lancaster] University on apologetic language referred to one interlocutor using `I felt a bit of a nong.' British audience did not know this word in sense of `fool, wet' etc. (1989 -- ,Personal Communication)



## nonghead
 
 <i>n.</i> [(c1950s)]

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> In other words I was a lad of some pretension - a dreamer, who soon gathered to himself a series of unlovely nicknames. ## noni
 
 (a fruit grown in the Cook Islands) <i>n.</i>

>  The fruit of the noni is believed to alleviate arthritis. "Everyone's into noni," says Dennis. "Noni noni everywhere." (Nov 24, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,C14)

>  On Saturday mornings at the lively Frankton market in Hamilton, a Fijian man sells large bottles containing the distilled juice of the nonu fruit, also known as noni... Nonu/noni, botanical name <i>Morinda citrifolia</i>, has been part of any Pacific healer's treatment kit for many hundreds of years, whereas kava (from the plant <i>Piper methysticum</i>) is the base of a drink consumed on both ritual and social occasions. (Sep 21, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28)



## noni juice
 
 <i>n.</i> a juice made from the fruit of the noni tree, a Polynesian remedy for various ills

>  A friend rang up and by way of extolling the virues of noni juice began: "You know I can be a cruel, ruthless bastard ..." Well, he continued, he'd been taking noni juice for a month now. He pissed like a fire hydrant, he felt boundless energy, his blood pressure count had dropped; he dropped his voice as he came to the relevant part, the bit he thought would interest me "And my hair's grown back." I thought, "You cruel, ruthless bastard." (Jan 6, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),29)

>  Noni juice is a pungent, dark-brown concotion, but the fruit from which it is made has been identified as a key ingredient in transforming Niue from a struggling nation dependent on New Zealand aid to a blossoming market economy. (Oct 29, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,B5)

>  Among the various wines, beer and spirits left over was a soft-drink bottle about a quarter filled with noni-juice, the all-purpose and powerful-smelling elixir of the islands... (Nov 2, 2002 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7)

## nor'west arch
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a delineation between cloud and clear sky that appears with a nor'west wind in Canterbury region.

>  <b>Relph</b> High country people are only too aware that the nor'west arch is the precursor of strong winds roaring down the valleys of the Waimakariri, Rakaia, and Rangitata Rivers, with heavy rain falling in the headwaters. (2007 -- ,From Tussocks to Tourists,34)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The sun had set through a low nor'west arch .. the dusk was taking on a velvety, indistinct aspect ... (1952 -- ,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,13)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> The sky is clouded but with some blue far to the west where the nor'west arch sweeps across. (1959 -- ,Gun in My Hand, A,19)

>  <b>VANCE</b> Its distinctive cloud formation, the lenticular alto-cumulus 'hogback', has become the warning signal to mountaineers as well as musterers, and the 'nor'west arch' is one of the features of Canterbury. (1965 -- ,High Endeavour,41)

>  <b>VON HAAST</b> The Canterbury plains are swept by two winds. On a Nor'-West day before the Nor'-Wester comes from the north-west across the mountains drained of its moisture, the clouds in the sky form a `Nor'-Wester' arch, ... (1948 -- ,Julius Von Haast,128)

>  ... the sky is clouded but with some blue far to the west where the nor'-west arch sweeps across. (1959 -- ,Gun in My Hand, A,19)



## nor'west days

## nor'wester

## Norfolk pine
 
 <i>n.</i> The Norfolk Island pine tree (<i>Araucaria heterophylla</i>) [Jim Cameron writes: this common and long-standing variation of the international term <i>Norfolk Island pine</i> is given only Aust and NZ citations in OED - the first is 1940 (Sargeson). it goes back to at least the 1930s - is not recorded in any dictionary I have seen.]

>  [Hdg] Lights out for norfolk pines at Parliament. Parliament's Speaker Jonathan Hunt.. won't be putting Christmas lights on the norfolk pines at Parliament. (Dec 12, 2000 -- ,Evening Post,3)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> Contrast clumsy macrocarpas with delicate Norfolk pines along a pleasant white beach. (1959 -- ,New Zealanders, The,203)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> In those days it was a quiet place up in the North there; a very old settled place with a row of Norfolk pines planted along the beach. (1937 -- ,Collected Stories,88)

>  <b>STOKES</b> Quote needed (1980 -- ,History of Tauranga County,288)

>  The wind rattles noisily in the towering Norfolk pines, icons of Napier. (Jun 1999 -- ,North & South,8)

>  "I look at all the Norfolk pines and pohutukawa around Wellington and I think - they're very pretty, but do they say anything to me about Wellington at all," she says, leaping astride a favourite hobbyhorse. (Sep 2004 -- ,Branch Out,No. 14/2)

>  The Chatham Islands astelia (Astelia chathamica) or kakaha is a plant used frequently by both home gardeners and landscapers. (Nov 13, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,E13)

>  A large enough seaside garden is immediately given atmosphere by the addition of a Norfolk pine (<i>Araucaria heterophylla</i>), but your neighbours may not be so pleased, nor may your successor, for the eventual size is immense. (Feb 2001 -- ,Growing Today,57)

>  The Norfolk Pine (<i>Araucaria araucana</i>) and the monkey puzzle (<i>A. heterophylla</i>) are related evergreen conifers. The former has branches with a stiff upward tilt, the latter, from Chile, "fingers that hang off the branches", and, according to the <i>Reader's Digest: Your Gardening Questions Answered</i>, its spiral foliage pattern is :"supposed to be difficult for a monkey to descend". (Oct 15, 1990 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),113)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> There is a Norfolk Pine which her mother called a monkey puzzle when Una was little but never explained why. (1990 -- ,Girls High)

>  The shape of the political process is approximately that of a Norfolk pine. (Jan 14, 2000 -- ,Dominion,00) [NOTES: check page]

>  ... the Norfolk pine in the front yard, the grapefruit tree and the woolshed and the sleep-out in the backyard. (Oct 6, 2001 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94)

>  Floodlighting along the beach foreshore was by courtesy of the Keys wallet and it is said that the fine stand of Norfolk pines fringing the northern border of Churchill Park were personally dug in by Len himself. (Aug 2001 -- ,NZ Memories,No. 31/43)

## North
 
 <i>n.</i> [see also Far, roadless, winterless North]

>  <b>MANDER</b> But the north was not respectable. It was the land of lost men. It was peopled with nomads and wasters. And it was `the roadless north', the `barbaric north', where the Maoris still might war upon you in the night. It had no railway beyond Helensville. The Southerner curled his lip at it. (1925 -- ,Allen Adair,6,1971 ed.)

>  <b>HULL</b> Between Waimea and the North Cape lies a tract of country which ... is vaguely referred to as `the North'; at election times the alliterative title of `the Neglected North' is bestowed upon it. (1907 -- ,College Songs,46)



 
 <i>n.</i> usually UP NORTH, occas. GO NORTH (cf DOWN SOUTH) check UP Broadly, anywhere north of the speaker's home district, but in different districts the idiom has a specific meaning. In the South Island, `North' is usually the North Island (even in Dunedin) and Wellington the objective. In Marlborough (where one speaks of `going over to Wellington', `up north' means further `up the line' q.v. In more Southern parts of North Island `up North' would usually be taken to mean Auckland or thereabouts.

>  <b>SCOTT</b> Business called me up north for a few weeks [to North Island]. (1900 -- ,Colonial Turf,216)

>  <b>MIDDLETON</b> `The family was brought up on a farm up north', said the Professor. (1959 -- ,Stone, The,70) [NOTES: Corporal's Story]

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> He went away up north [from Christchurch]. (1987 -- ,Oracles and Miracles,24)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> NOTHOFAGUS TRUNCATA. Hard beech, the only Nothofagus native to north Auckland, is absent from the east and south of the South Island. (1978 -- ,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,80)

(1997 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.govt.nz/upload/documents/conservation/land-and-freshwater/land/te-paki-ecological-district/te-paki-shrublands-n02-003.pdf]

>  This is one of the last large remaining contiguous habitats in Northland for a distinct form of the endemic North Cape green gecko ... ## North Island
 
 <i>n.</i> [It is collocated with the definite article in NZ usage. (as is `the South Island', `the Middle Island', but not `Stewart[s'] Island'). -->The absolute use without the article is not used by New Zealanders except (so R. Mason) Dr L. Cockayne, Dr H.H. Allan (see A Handbook of the Naturalized Flora of N.Z., D.S.I.R. Bulletin 83, e.g. 211 `... and the northern half of South Island') and G Simpson, all botanists who may have picked up the usage from Cockayne]

 <i>adj.</i> of or from the northern of NZ's two main islands [in early journals, often NORTHERN ISLAND, but this usage soon gives way to the standard form; as a distinguishing epithet in the names of plants and animals see EDELWEISS, EYEBRIGHT, LILY, RATA, crow, kiwi, oystercatcher, robin, saddleback]
