## North Island Main Trunk
 
 <i>n.</i> railway

>  The King Movement and its supporters retreated to the hills, where they were able to hold up construction of the North Island Main Trunk railway for years until the government finally made peace, but not amends, with them. (Nov 1999 -- ,North & South,60)

>  The first section of the North Island Main Trunk railway runs through the whole length of the block, and trains run twice a week to a point called Rangatira, three miles beyond Hunterville. (Jan 23, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,7)

>  <b>LEITCH</b> In contrast to the North Island Main Trunk which opened up large areas for settlement, the South Island's Main North Line would cross barren hill country suitable only for extensive sheep grazing. (1972 -- ,Railways of NZ,129)



## North Island rata
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>M. robusta </i> [aka northern rata]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Metrosideros robusta </i> (The North Island Rata). A tee, 50 ft.-100 ft. in height. ... North Island and northern extremity of South Island. ... Maori name <i> Rata</i>. ... [297] This is the North Island rata, but it is also found on the west coast of the South Island ... It is often described as twining round some forest tree, ensheathing it and finally killing it by a close embrace; this account .... misrepresents, ... <i> M. robusta</i>. It does not begin life as a climber, though there are species which do so, e.g., <i> M. scandens</i>; but it very often germinates as an epiphyte high up in the forks of a tree ... As the young plant develops, it sends down roots to the ground. These roots inosculate, and slowly enclose the stem of the supporting tree. (1940 -- ,Plants of NZ,296)



## North Island robin
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment)

>  [Caption] The first North Island robin chick to be hatched at the Society's forest reserve at Bushy Park near Wanganui. (May 2002 -- ,Forest & Bird,304/42)

>  It's an unlikely breakfast call, but within minutes a North Island Robin flutters around us, then pounces on the worms Luton has shaken onto the ground. (Sep 2002 -- ,AA Directions,16)

>  Four kilometres off Whangaparaoa peninsula, the predator-free reserve is now home to saddleback, little spotted kiwi, whitehead, parakeet, kokako, North island robin and takahe. (Apr 2000 -- ,North & South,85)

>  They are now applying to bring back another bird, the North Island robin, and hope in future to have whitehead, kakariki parakeets and other rare birds gracing the peninsula again. (Nov 2002 -- ,Forest & Bird,305/42)

>  The North Island robin is a small sooty-black forest bird with grey streaks on head and throat, and a white belly and under-tail .. It has very long thin legs and stands upright. It is smaller than a starling but bigger than a sparrow. (Jun 2001 -- ,Absolutely Positively Wellington,4)

>  The arrival of the first "resident" North Island robin chicks on Matiu / Somes Island coincided with celebrations of a 25-year volunteer effort ... (Nov 2006 -- ,Footnotes,26 / 1)

>  On the lower slopes we found that rare bird, the North Island robin. (Apr 27, 1938 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,38)

>  <i>Robin Petroica australis</i> (SPARRMAN, 1788) ... <i>North Island Robin P.a. longpipes</i> (LESSON, 1828). <i>South Island Robin P.a. australis</i> (SPARRMAN, 1788). <i>Stewart Island Robin P.a. rakiura</i> FLEMING, 1950. ## North Island thrush
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  North Island Thrush. The native thrush, the piopio, appears to be a fast expiring species. (May 19, 1928 -- ,Evening Post,17)

>  <b>BULLER</b> TURNAGRA HECTORI. (NORTH ISLAND THRUSH.) ...<i>Native names</i>. Piopio, {Koropio,} Korohea, and Tiutiukata. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,135,1888 2nd edn 1/26 ed.)

>  <b>MYERS</b> <i>Turnagra tanagra</i> Schlegel. North Island Thrush. This is practically the only species of which absolutely no records are forthcoming. (1923 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/91)

>  <i>281.a. Yurnagra capensis tanagra</i> (Schlegel, 1865) <i>North Island Thrush (Piopio)</i> ... now rare and local, perhaps extinct ... (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,73,1980 ed.)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> Family TURNAGRIDAE (thrushes). Any thrushes most of us see today are likely (let's face it) to be imported ones; however, Oliver's description of the native South Island thrush (<i>Turnagra capensis</i>) optimistically puts its distribution (south-west of South Island, west Nelson) in the present tense. The last North Island one definitely recorded was encountered in 1887. (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,286)

>  Next we come to the cases of New Zealand birds, and here the species seem also to be well represented, including the extinct quail, the stitch bird, North Island thrush, kiwi, and many others that are nearly gone. (Apr 4, 1894 -- ,Wanganui Herald,2)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

## North Island tomtit
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) [also (see each) white breasted tit, North Island tit]

>  [Caption] North Island tomtit. Tomtit have begun to return to Matuku. Tomtit, a close relative of the New Zealand robin, are rare beteen Whangarei and the southern Waikato. (Nov 2002 -- ,Forest & Bird,306/37)

>  <b>BULLER</b> MYIOMOIRA TOITOI. (PIED TIT {NORTH ISLAND TOMTIT}.) ... <i>Native names.</i> Miromiro, Komiromiro, Pimiromiro, Ngirungiru, Pingirungiru, and Pipitori}. ... [40] Its note in the early morning is like the Maori syllables <i>ngi-i-ru, ngiru-ngiru</i>, from which it derives its name, the first syllable being rather prolonged. (1888 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,1/39)

>  <i>Myiomoira toitoi Lesson. North Island Tomtit. </i>As early as 1886 Reischek (p.101) included this species in a list of birds extinct or extremely rare on the mainland. (1923 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/88) [NOTES: Myers Endemic Birds]

>  The North Island tomtit (<i>Myiomoira toitoi</i>) was seen on every trip we made ... (1923 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/88) [NOTES: -->1925-26 NZJST 8/17 (Hamilton Poor Knights)]

>  North Island tomtits, males particularly, were much in evidence. (Jun 8, 1922 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,15)



## North Island weka
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Forest and Bird was delighted to be asked to supply North Island weka for release. This bird is a threatened species... (Nov 2002 -- ,Forest & Bird,306/42)

>  <i> 134.a. Gallirallus australis greyi</i> (Buller, 1888) <i>th Island Weka </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,42,1960 ed.)

>  The North Island weka was scarcer and more valuable than a kiwi, and he thought the Society should give a vote of thanks to Mr. Boulter. (Aug 4, 1868 -- ,Daily Southern Cross,4)

>  In matters of nomenclature the changes which will be most apparent are the substitution of the name Apteryx bulleri (North Island kiwi) for A. mantelli, and of Ocydromus greyi (North Island weka) for O. earli. It appears that the old and well-known names were in both cases applied to South Island birds, and that the North Island forms have hitherto been strictly speaking nondescripts. (Jun 29, 1889 -- ,Otago Daily Times,1)

>  Mr McCormick, one North Island weka (albino). (Jul 27, 1908 -- ,Wanganui Herald,7)

>  The hybridisation in the offspring is shown by the birds having the red legs of the Stewart Island breed and the body markings characteristic of the North Island weka. (Mar 18, 1925 -- ,Evening Post,7)

>  Such species is the blue wattled crow, brown kiwi, and North Island weka are found in the district. (Aug 16, 1930 -- ,Evening Post,17)

>  Eventually one female North island weka, a different species of bird, was found, and the two birds were put on Kapiti Island. (Oct 18, 1941 -- ,Evening Post,8)

>  North island weka were once so common in the Gisborne area they were considered a pest. (Jul 2004 -- ,Conservation Action,25)

## North Island zebra moth
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  A closely related moth, the North Island Zebra Moth, which is confined to the North Island, has the forewings ornamented with broken black lines, giving the wings a more or less speckled or spotted appearance. (Sep 16, 1972 -- ,Press, The,11/8)



## North Islander
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The New Plymouth boatmen are some of them great scamps. ... These North Islanders are a bad lot. (Jul 11, 1877 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,259,1950 ed.)



 
 <i>n.</i> a resident of, or person from, the North Island of New Zealand. [ca1955 R. Mason notes that `North Islander' is a South Island term (cf MAINLAND (ER)). People in the North Island do not usually look upon themselves as North Islanders, but as, say, Aucklanders or Taranaki-ites, or merely as New Zealanders. In the South Island one is often told:' Oh you're a North Islander are you?' Perhaps this is a reflexion of the parochial or `cinderella' spirit of the South Island which sees itself as being overcome in importance by the more populous and industrial North Island. ?a S.I. usage mainly]

>  Well, who runs the South Island... North Islanders! (Jul 12, 1943 -- ,NZEF Times,III/107 : 6)

>  <b>BROOME</b> The creed of the North Islander will depend on his province...... The political belief of every province is moulded and modified, as is only natural, by its own particular circumstances. (1869 -- ,Crisis in NZ,417)

>  <b>BULLER</b> NOTORNIS MANTELLI. (MANTELL'S NOTORNIS.) ... Native names. Moho, Takahe, and Tokohea. [191] Mr. Walter Mantell states that ... It was known to the North-Islanders by the name of `Moho', and to the South-Islanders by that of `Takahe'. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,189, 191,1888 2nd edn 2/85 ed.)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> Unscathed in four campaigns, two North Islanders had been invalided home. (1945 -- ,Gunner Inglorious,122)

>  <b>DEMPSEY</b> And we are apt to say, when speaking of a person who is away, that he is `off the Island'. If we are told that someone has gone over to the Other Side ... we understand he has gone to Invercargill or to Bluff. Where the North Islander talks of his beach cottage, or bach, we call these little places cribs, a much more euphonious name. (1964 -- ,Little World of Stewart island,6)

>  And he told me he had one broadside he wanted to deliver while he was still a North Islander.] (Oct 31, 1972 -- ,Dominion,1/4)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> Although living in the back-blocks, (North Islanders say `in the sticks', presumably referring to bush or scrub) station people seem to encounter more than their fair share of incident. (1973 -- ,Historic Sheep Stations NI,100)

>  <b>FRY</b> Song of a North Islander [title] (1979 -- ,Brushed Words,1)

>  <b>SANSOM</b> I'm not a North Islander either. (1982 -- ,In the Grip of an Island,146)

>  North Islander native or inhabitant of the North Island. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,511) [NOTES: nf 1982 Collins Concise Eng.Dict]

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> Friendly, chatty ... Australians, Americans, English, North Islanders. (1987 -- ,Oracles and Miracles,208)

 <i>n.</i>

## Northern Island

>  ... there were seven large Cannoes of these New-comers, and.. They came from somewhere towards, if not actually from the Northern Isalnd [check Wales's position: Ship Cove?] (1773 -- ,Cook's Journals,2/790,1961 ed.) [NOTES: 1773 Wales Journal in Cook's Journals (1961) 2/790]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> ...they have nothing in which to keep liquids except a minute kind of calabash or gourd, which grows only in the northern island, and was extremely scarce among the people of Queen Charlotte's Sound. <i>Ibid.</i> 1/230 [Teiratu] seemed to know [potatoes] very well, evidently because a similar root, the Virginian or sweet potatoe (convolvulus batatas,) is planted in some parts of the Northern Island, from whence he came. (1777 -- ,Voyage Round the World,1/220)

>  <b>McNAB</b> A proposal was some time since made to me ... of forming a settlement at their own expence on the northern island of New Zealand, for the purpose of collecting the flax plant of that country, and manufacturing it .. (1908 -- ,Historical Records,1/297 5.) [NOTES: 1810 Mar 12 (Sydney) Governor M acquarie to Viscount Castlereagh in 1908 McNab Historical Records 1/297 5.]

>  <b>McNAB</b> ... there is every prospect of a rapid increase of trade ... under the ... protection of the British Government, which could not be given so effectually from any station as from the Northen Island of New Zealand. (1908 -- ,Historical Records,1/664) [NOTES: 1826 Apr 24 Memorial to Earl of Bathurst in 1908 McNab Hist Records 1/664]

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> New Zealand or the Northern Island, as there are three, is known to the Natives by the name of Eaheinomawe. (1834 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),29,1963 ed.)

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said Islands shall be known and designated as the Colony of New Zealand, and the principal Islands, here to fore known as, or commonly called, the `Northern Island,' the `Middle Island,' and `Stewart's Island,' shall henceforward be designated and known respectively as `New Ulster,' `New Munster,' and `New Leinster.' ... (1971 -- ,Speeches & Documents,54) [NOTES: 1840 Letters Patent issued on 16 November: quoted from The Ordinances of New Zealand, Domett 1850 pp.4-8 in 1971 Speeches and Documents 54]

>  And we do hereby declare, that from henceforth the said islands shall be known and designated as the colony of New Zealand, and the principal islands, heretofore known as or commonly called the `Northern Island', the `Middle Island', and `Stewart's Island', shall henceforth bedesignated and known respectively as `New Ulster', `New Munster', and `New Leinster'. (Nov 16, 1840 -- ,Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ,8) [NOTES: 1840 Nov 16 Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 8]

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> We now saw more of the middle island, which seemed to consist of a chain of steep snow-capped hills, running through the middle of the island, and rising in a succession of ridges from the sea-shore. On the northern island we saw the mountains of Tararua in the neighbourhood of Port Nicholson; these also were covered with snow. [187] ... I have reason to believe that the Middle Island has a configuration similar to the Northern; ... (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/22, 187)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> Early in the spring of 1844, I set out on a journey ... to explore [2] various portions of the interior of the Northern Island; ... (1847 -- ,Savage Life,2/1-2)

>  <b>EARP</b> The Middle Island is separated from the Northern Island by Cook's Strait, and from Stewart's Island by Foveaux's Strait. <i>Ibid.</i> 95 [l.c. init.] The Wellington settlers ... were ardent followers of the whale fishery, carried on chiefly on the coasts of the northern and middle island by means of boats - a method technically called `bay-whaling', ... (1853 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),34)

>  <b>SEWELL</b> ... they have never succeeded in attaching to their cause, more than limited support, confined mainly to the central parts of the Northern Island. (1864 -- ,NZ Native Rebellion,4)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> The natives in the Middle Island had always been inferior in numbers ... to the Northern Islanders, who from time to time made cruel and butcherly raids upon them. (1866 -- ,Polynesia,166)

## northern kingfish
 
 <i>n.</i> = yellowtail kingfish

>  <b>MORELAND</b> YELLOWTAIL or NORTHERN KINGFISH [<i>Seriola grandis</i>] ... The Maori name haku is still in use. (1967 -- ,Marine Fishes,50)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> YELLOWTAIL or NORTHERN KINGFISH ... <i>Other names: Seriola grandis</i>; yellowtail (Australia); haku (Maori). The general adoption of the term yellowtail for this species would be more appropriate than the sometimes confusing term of kingfish. (1966 -- ,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,234)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> <i>KINGFISH, NORTHERN </i> (a) Scientific name: <i>Seriola grandis</i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: Kingfish; Yellowtail. (c) Other common names: None known. (d) Principal localities where taken: Auckland, Tauranga, Gisborne, Napier. ...Under the general heading of Kingfish ... is generally lumped with the Silver or Southern Kingfish. <i>Rexea solandri</i> to which it is not related. This fish is a well known gamefish of northen waters. (1970 -- ,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,31)

>  [species name] kingfish .. [scientific name] <i>Seriola lalandi </i>.. [other name(s)] yellowtail, yellowtail kingfish, northern kingfish, northern yellowtail, kingi (Jul 23, 1986 -- ,Codes for Species in NZ Waters)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] kingfish, northern 1365-76 (1987 -- ,Fishes: A Bibliography)

## northern rata
 
 <i>n.</i> Meterosideros robusta [aka North Island rata. 1875 RDT 1919 CNZ (Andersen, 7 entries)]

>  The northern rata, Metro sideros robusta, is really a parasite in the way I have mentioned, and in any forests at this end specimens can be seen killing the trees upon which they grow. (Feb 15, 1894 -- ,Otago Witness,5)

>  Northern rata can also establish on the ground, especially where forest has been destroyed by fire. (Mar 13, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,E10)

>  Northern rata (Metrosideros robusta) does grow naturally in Wellington (in the South Island from Hokitika northwards). Both are known as the New Zealand Christmas tree... Pohutukawa doesn't grown naturally here; it is a weed in parts of the region; it occupies sites where the northern rata should be; being closely related to the northern rata, pohutukawa has the potential to hybridise; pohutukawa inhibits the growth of other plants; northern rata's bloom is superior to pohutukawa's. (Feb 19, 2004 -- ,Wellington Contact,31)

>  Rata, northern .. <i> Metrosideros robusta </i> .. Timber dense, heavy, and tough; used principally for wheelwrights' work and firewood (1890 -- ,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,13)

>  The kahikatea stands next in abundance, and is closely approached by the kamai, while the miro, matai, totara, Hall's totara, are generally distributed; but the northern rata and pukatea scarcely occur south of Greymouth. <i>Ibid.</i> 335 18. The northern rata (<i> Metrosideros robusta)</i>, A. Cunningham 19. The southern rata (<i> Metrosideros lucida </i>), Menzies... 22. Manuka rauriki (<i> Leptospermum ericoide </i> s), A. Richard. (1894 -- ,NZ Official Yearbook,330) [NOTES: (T. Kirk)]

>  Amongst the most conspicuous trees of this area there are two worthy of special mention, the northern rata and the black-maire. (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,118)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> The northern rata (<i> Metrosideros robusta</i> ) ... frequently behave in this most ungrateful manner. (1910 -- ,NZ Plants,37)

>  <b>KIRK</b> The Northern rata [sic] Metrosideros robusta), when growing in moist places, produces timber of inferior quality ... (1875 -- ,Durability NZ Timbers,3)

>  <b>KIRK</b> (And) <i> Metrosideros robusta</i> northern rata (1889 -- ,Forest Flora)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> (And) <i> Metrosideros robusta</i> northern rata (1905 -- ,Tree Culture in NZ)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> And) <i> Metrosideros robusta</i> northern rata (1908 -- ,AJHR,C-14) [NOTES: Report on a Botanical Survey of Waipoua Kauri Forest]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> (And) <i> Metrosideros robusta</i> northern rata (1910 -- ,NZ Plants & Their Story) [NOTES: Also 1919 edition]

>  <b>NAIRN & SON</b> (And) <i> Metrosideros robusta</i> northern rata (1912 -- ,Catalogue (Nairn & Son))

>  (And) <i> Metrosideros lucida</i> northern rata (1917 -- ,Duncan & Davies' Catalogue)

>  The chief indigenous trees of commercial value are: Kauri (<i> Agathis Australis</i> ), Kahikatea or white pine (<i> Podocarpus dacrydioides</i> ), rimu or red pine (<i> Dacrydium cupressinum</i> ), totara (<i> Podocarpus totara</i> and <i> Podocarpus Hallii</i> ), Miro (<i> Podocarpus ferrugineus</i> ), Kaikawaka or mountain cedar (Libocedrus Bidwilli), yellow pine or silver pine (<i> Dacrydium Colensoi</i> and <i> D. intermedium</i> ), red and hard beech (<i> Nothofagus fasca</i> and <i> Nothofagus truncata</i> ), silver beech (<i> Nothofagus Menziesii</i> ), black beech (<i> Nothofagus Solandri</i> ), black maire (<i> Olea Cunninghamii</i> ), northern and southern rata (<i> Metrosideros robusta</i> and <i> Metrosideros lucida</i> ). (1936 -- ,Agricultural Organization in NZ,578)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> Northern Rata (<i>M. robusta </i>). This is the North Island rata, but it is also found on the west coast of the South Island as far south at least as Karamea. (1940 -- ,Plants of NZ,297)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Rata, northern .. .. rata .. ..<i> Metrosideros robusta </i> (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712)

>  <i>Northern Rata, Metrosideros robusta.</i> ... The tree is large and sturdy and grows in lowland and subalpine forests from Three Kings to Nelson. (1971 -- ,New Zealand's Heritage,1/267)

>  Metrosideros robusta,/ the northern rata. Usually / commencing life as an epiphyte/ becomes a tall, massive tree / 60 to 100 feet high. (1972 -- ,Islands,1/5) [NOTES: Curnow `Names are News']

>  <b>DAWSON</b> Northern rata (<i>Metrosideros robusta </i>) is the most notable and common example [of strangling epiphytes] here. (1988 -- ,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,83)

>  Like the northern rata, <i>M. robusta</i>, Bartlett's rata usually starts life as an epiphyte perched in the branches of another tree or ponga. (Jul 2000 -- ,NZ Geographic,47/12)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> Tall northern rata (<i>Metrosideros robusta</i>) ... and kahikatea ... are scattered emergents ... (1981 -- ,Paparoas Guide,157)

>  Easier to spot, especially when they're laden with scarlet flowers, are the tall northern rata. (Jan 5, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,C3)

>  The biggest northern rata in the region can be found along the Butterfly Creek and Mount Hawtrey trail in Eastbourne, while one that began its life as an epiphyte can be seen entwined with a 400-year-old rimu in the Otari-Wilton's Bush trail. (May 2003 -- ,Footnotes,No. 19/6)

## Northerner

>  Friday night saw the Northerner train's last journey on the main trunk line.... But for the 125 passengers who travelled on Friday night's final Northerner out of the capital, the last trip was the only topic of conversation. (Nov 15, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,A5)

>  The whistle has been blown on a Kiwi tradition - the night trains that have carried passengers between Wellington and Auckland for almost a century are going. The Northerner service is being cut for economic reasons, Tranz Scenic says. Its final run will pull out of Wellington and Auckland railway stations on Friday, November 12. (Oct 30, 2004 -- ,Dominion Post,A1)



## Northland
 
 <i>n.</i> the North Auckland peninsula, often, by non-residents, North Auckland. The usage has become more frequent outside the region since WW2.

>  <b>RUSSELL</b> This country, now [?date] officially called `Northland'. (1919 -- ,New Zealand Today,27)

>  <b>REED</b> I board the service car at Northland's capital; ... (1946 -- ,Farthest North,13)

>  `The province in the North called Northland; they don't like to be called North Auckland. (Oct 5, 1950 -- ,2ZB) [NOTES: Anne Stewart's Session. S. Toogood:]

>  <b>GOLDBLATT</b> ... we reach the capital of Northland, the busy, growing town of Whangarei. (1957 -- ,Democracy at Ease,11)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> The region is usually referred to as North Auckland, but the use of the shortened Northland is gaining wider currency. (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/693)

>  <b>THOMAS</b> [Kauri] grows naturally within the restricted area of the northern part of the Auckland Province and almost wholly in Northland. (1970 -- ,Way Up North,29)

>  <b>CLEARY</b> Every plant ... in Northland ... is crying / out for rain. (1975 -- ,Pocketful of Years,1-2)

>  <b>MARSHALL</b> When I was seven, my father was appointed the first District Public Trustee for North Auckland, ... Northland, as the area north of Auckland is now called, was in those days an isolated community. (1983 -- ,Memoirs,24)

>  Banded Rail Rallus philippensis GRAY, 1843 ... OTHER NAME: Moho-pereru. ... Mainly in northen mangrove swamps from Northland to Bay of Plenty. (1985 -- ,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,165)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a suburb of Wellington

>  Sir, -I see that the Viceregal party has had good fishing in northern Southland streams. This brings up the subject of Northland in the north. Often your writers refer to Northland, the suburb, in a headline, and I have to read the text to see if they are referring to Northland up north or Northland down south here in Wellington.Couldn't we plan a key to this by referring to North Northland or Northland North (as in Palmerston North? I feel there must be some easy way out. - I am., etc. (Feb 27, 1953 -- ,Evening Post,8)



 
<b>3</b> <i>adj.</i>

>  First it was a caterer ..., then a Northland kiwifruit farmer and last week a Wellington tailoring company. (May 26, 1986 -- ,Manawatu Evening Standard,1)

>  Yet two Northland cockies got the shock of their rural lives when they walked into Auckland International Airport and saw what tourists were being charged for the meat New Zealand farmers produced. (Jan 13, 1989 -- ,Dominion,3)



## Northland green gecko
 
 <i>n.</i> restricted to upper Northland, a large, pugnacious and brightly coloured gecko. [<i>Naultinus grayii</i> aka Gray's gecko]

>  Ornate skink (Gradual Decline), North Cape Pacific gecko (Gradual Decline), Matapia gecko (Gradual Decline), North Cape green gecko (Sparse), Cyclodina levidensa (regionally significant), shore skink, and common gecko. (1997 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.govt.nz/upload/documents/conservation/land-and-freshwater/land/te-paki-ecological-district/te-paki-shrublands-n02-003.pdf]

>  The Northland green gecko (<i>Naultinus grayii</i>) is bright green on top with gold or grey markings and a paler underbelly. It is most commonly seen on Manuka and Kanuka trees. (2006 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.puketi.org.nz/kids/riverwalk.html]

>  Widlife experts feared the two Northland Green Geckos and one Rough Gecko were destined for the international black market. (Apr 12, 2006 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.nzherald.co.nz/nz-customs-service/news/article.cfm?o_id=360&objectid=10377174]

## nos
 
 <i>n.</i> nitrous oxide

>  The drug in question is N2O, aka nitrous oxide, laughing gas or nos, a commonly used fuel additive that has also been an industry-standardmedical anaesthetic for over a century... [27] The police intervened and discovered a cylinder of nos on the passenger's seat that was discharging its contents into the chased car. (Feb 1, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26)

>  A walk through Christchurch on a friday night shows that the trade in nos is still alive and laughing. (Jun 11, 2005 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32)

>  What does the law say about NOS?Nitrous Oxide is classified as a prescription medicine under the Medicines Act 1981 and therefore this means that the sale of NOS without prescription is illegal.Want more information?For more comprehensive information about NOS contact FADE to order our new NOS: What you need to know pamphlet. (2006 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.fade.org.nz/alcohol-and-drug-info/nos/Accessed 26/03/06.]



 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) short for nitrous oxide

>  The mockery that this unfortunate incident brought upon poor Tim was cruel and far-reaching, and caused him far more harm [sic] his nos habit ever did. (May 2005 -- ,Lucid,14)

>  Anderton's "no ifs, no buts" comments came after suggestions that retailers could once again evade the Medicines Act by selling their nos as "catering-grade nos" instead of "get-high-on-this-shit-grade nos". ## nos bar
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [nos](../N#nos))

>  Anderton has declared war on the people he calls "parasites" who run the nos bars. (2006 -- ,NZ Herald) [NOTES: http://tvnz.co.nz/view/news_health_story_skin/537940?format+html*Accessed 26/03/06]

>  Nos barA nos bar is a phenomomen seen especially in Christchurch in the beginning of the 21st century up til halfway thru 2005. They are bars that allow you to purchase nitrous oxide in balloons, and sit on a couch, or around a table outside, and inhale the gas to get high. Similar to places like Cosmic Corner in what they sell, the sit down environment provides a safe place to use a safe drug. Better than doing it on the street. (2006 -- ,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.girlza.com/wiki/Nos_bar*Accessed 26/03/06.]

(1964 -- ,South : Man and Nature in Antarctica,70)



## nose-greeting
 
 <i>n.</i> hongi [-->1905 Baucke White Man Treads 278 Our college graduate arrives; the home-coming tangi and nose-greeting is over; ... `Whenever I'm out here in the wop-wops with youse jokers, the hori comes out in me. Kapai and hongi..' [check that these two parts belong together]]



## nose-rubbing
 
 <i>n.</i> hongi (cf. rub noses)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> Observing his embarrassment, we withdrew to some distance, leaving him to indulge his natural feeling in hongi and tangi, or nose-rubbing and crying. (1843 -- ,Travels in NZ,1/61)

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> The principal chiefs then retired a little to the back, and squatted down, according to their manner and custom, to receive the visitors, who are expected to walk up to where the chiefs are sitting and commence nose-rubbing. (1881 -- ,Poenamo,Bk3/2/140)

>  <b>WHITE</b> One does not now see much nose-rubbing for `How do you do?' - at least, in the towns - but a very mechanical shake of the hands instead. (1905 -- ,My NZ Garden,107)

>  ... the ardent pakeha finds no satisfaction in nose-rubbing. (Mar 17, 1906 -- ,NZ Truth,39/4/4)



## nose-throw
 
 <i>v.</i> to vomit through the nose [Crisp's two sources in 1989 said it was the going expression then. <i>Upchuck</i>, still common in NZ for vomit (vb.) is listed in OED Supp - not NZ origin.]



## nose-warmer
 
 <i>n.</i> a very short (clay) pipe [?or merely descriptive]

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> The ends of the stems were for ever breaking off, and the [clay] pipes soon became `nose warmers' [in the 1890s]. -- (1940 -- ,Te Waimate,67)



## noser
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a cattle dog that works cattle from the head or nose [ as opposed to a heeler.]

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> Country hotels often keep a night pen for wet day shearers, so do they keep some wild sorts of sheep for wide running dogs, a sticky mob of woollies for the heavy huntaways and bellowing cattle for the nosers and heelers. (1973 -- ,Boot in the Stirrup,61)



## nosey
 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a look

>  [A Central Otago farmer, ? Mr Mee, speaking of one of the advantages of holding discussion groups on other people's farms] They're also an excuse to have a nosey round other people's farms. (Jun 21, 1986 -- ,Radio NZ / National Radio) [NOTES: Landline Farm Programme]

>  The day after the first shoot, we did get to have a nosey at part of the finished product. (Aug 13, 1990 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),36)



## nosy
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (informal) a nosy parker; a prying person. [cf. Jack Nohi]

<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (informal) an inquisitive look around. [also as nosey]

>  <b>Owen Marshall</b> I wouldn't mind getting out of the office and up there myself for a nosey in the fresh air. (2009 -- ,Living As A Moon,22)

## not come within a bull's roar
 
 <i>phr.</i> come nowhere near, not to come within cooee of.

>  <b>1967 May 8 J.SL. Cleveland</b> They create an atmosphere that the happiness boys cannot come within a bull's roar of equating. (May 8, 1967 -- ,Personal Communication) [NOTES: 1967 May 8 J.S. Lynch in memo to NZBC Wellington Programme Advisory Committee (communicated by L. Cleveland)]

>  Chatfield was not within a bull's roar of that [ball]. (Mar 3, 1987 -- ,TV1) [NOTES: Cricket Test Commentary NZ and West Indies]

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>bull's roar, you wouldn't come within </i>you are not even close; eg `I'd flag away the sprints if I was you, Norty. You're not within a bull's roar of qualifying, let alone winning.' NZA. (1989 -- ,Dinkum Kiwi Dictionary, The,17)



## not give a butcher's
 
 <i>phr.</i> not care a toss [(?confusion with tinker's curse: or butcher's damn).]

## not give a stuff
 
 <i>phr.</i> (informal) not care the slightest bit

## not go much on
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> go much on like or care for, usually in negative phr. `not go much on'; eg `I don't go much on the new Deputy Head. Do you?' Probably NZA first. Recorded Jean Devanny in Lenore Divine, 1926. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,51)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> go much on, not. Not to care much for; to dislike: New Zealand coll.: C. 20. (Jean Devanney Lenore Divine, 1926.) P8: at go much on adds ... perhaps orig. Aus. and NZ coll but soon also British. (?a variant of OED 61e US coll. in negative contexts, to care for (1882)) (1961 -- ,Dictionary of Slang,1111)

 <i>phr.</i> have no chance, no show [ca1945 Students, Victoria College (c1951)]

## not have a foggy

## Not Just Gumboots and Scones.co.nz
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a rural website administered by rural women.

>  Kate Wilson, a Middlemarch farmer and one of the women behind rural website Not Just Gumboots and Scones, says the Internet is a serious tool for women involved in rural businesses. Not Just Gumboots and Scones was one of New Zealand's first rural information portals, built well before the launch of large commercial sites like www.fencepost.com. Ms Wilson, who farms sheep and hazelnuts with her husband, says Not Just Gumboots and Scones is being rethought to make it even more relevant and easy to use. (Dec 2002 -- ,E-farming,04)

>  The 'sterotypical perception of a rural woman' is what prompted a group of country ladies to set up an organisation in the late 1990s called Not Just Gumboots & Scones. Their website (www.notjust.org.nz) allows rural women to learn about and share views on all sorts of issues, but, in fine country style, it was launched in a woolshed with gumboots and scones much in evidence. (May 2007 -- ,NZ Geographic,69)



## not know from a bar of soap
 
 <i>phr.</i> not to know (someone, something) from a bar of soap: not to recognise, to know nothing of.

>  "I didn't know him from a bar of soap when he turned up... (Oct 13, 2002 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B2)

>  Didn't know the game [of golf] from a bar of soap, and now that I know something about it, would certainly give the cake to the soap. (Oct 24, 1903 -- ,NZ Bulletin,15)

>  This paper does not know Langlands from a bar of soap. (Aug 4, 1906 -- ,NZ Truth,59/4/5)

>  The second day after his arrival on the job, the workers made a presentation to their cook, a man called Frank Faulkner, whom Savage as he later recollected, didn't know `from a bar of soap'. [note 7] (Jun 16, 1938 -- ,Standard, The) [NOTES: 1938 June 16 Standard M.J.Savage in 1986 Gustafson Cradle to Grave 71-72]

>  <b>SLATTER</b> And even if they didn't know me from a bar of soap they do know I was in the Battalion. (1959 -- ,Gun in My Hand, A,146)

## not know shit from clay
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>wouldn't know shit from clay</i> naive or stupid persons; eg `Monty's so dense he wouldn't know shit from clay.' (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,125)



 
 <i>phr.</i> to be very stupid or ignorant

>  ... poor young android Americans from the new-clear warship <i>Truxton</i> who wouldn't know shit from clay if it was hurled in their faces by every little cunty ship-girl between the Bay of Islands and the Bluff ... (1984 -- ,Islands,33/3) [NOTES: (Wedde `Fast History)]



## not know the Brighton tram was up them...
 
 <i>phr.</i> ... till it rang its bell and people started getting off

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>wouldn't know the Brighton tram was up them till it rang its bell and people started getting off</i>, preceded by `so tight', meaning very mean with money, but could as well be used to mean slow-witted; former meaning quoted in Stevan Eldred-Grigg's <i>Oracles and Miracles. </i> (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,125)



## not know whether one is on one's arse or elbow
 
 <i>phr.</i> [(i) [Only DARE mainly N. Atlan urban 1965-70.] one's arse from one's elbow, to be ignorant or stupid. + a var. of ... from one's elbow: P: lower classes: late19-20c.] not to know one's arse from a hole in the ground (occas. a hot rock), to be very ignorant or innocent. Cf. Steinbeck Grapes of Wrath 58 (Penguin). (ii) [nf P8, DARE] whether one's on one's arse or one's elbow, to be confused; not to know where one is or what one's about.]

>  <b>DAVIN</b> They're all to hell out there, sir. Don't know whether they're on their arse or their elbow. Tanks all over the place. (1947 -- ,For the Rest of Our Lives,329)

>  <b>MIDDLETON</b> That fleeco won't know whether he's on his arse or his elbow in the morning. (1959 -- ,Stone, The,43) [NOTES: The Stone]



## not much chop
 
 <i>phr.</i> not up to much, not much cop [earlier not much chops, no chops]

>  It wasn't much chop being let down bysomeone who had crept into your heart andthoughts (Nov 1, 1945 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,67)

>  There are not many American kids with much chop. (May 10, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,f3)

>  <b>LYNCH</b> ..not much chop at [marching].. (1997 -- ,Stuff It!,15) [NOTES: get full quote]

>  Harry is no `chops' as regards elegance, but he gets runs, and that is the whole kernel of the run-making game. (Nov 20, 1909 -- ,NZ Truth,230/3/2)

>  <b>VOGEL</b> He ain't much chops, that doctor, I reckon. (1909 -- ,Tragedy of Flirtation,219)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> It's not been much chop so far. (1947 -- ,Gorse Blooms Pale, The,207)

>  <i>chop n. NZ & Austral. colloq. not much chop</i> not up to much. (1986 -- ,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,126) [NOTES: [cf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 195, sense 14]]

>  <b>McLauchlan ed.</b> 'Takes all sorts,' she says, opening the big fridge, 'but she doesn't look much chop to me.' (2000 -- ,Morrieson's Motel,25)

## not the full quid
 
 <i>phr.</i> -->ca1930 -->1951 Wellington. X SHILLINGS IN THE QUID not all there, mentally deficient (see also POUND) [1951 14 M 14 St Bede's, Chch 2 QUID, NOT THE FULL]

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Henry snorted that I was mad - only about `two bob in the quid'. (1957 -- ,Wayleggo,140)

>  `That cove looks about seven and six in the quid to me,' remarks a lance-jack. (1941 -- ,Troopship Tattoo H.M.T. 32,4)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> ... be two bob in the quid (1947 -- ,Wayleggo,140)

>  <b>LELAND</b> not the full quid: NOT TOO BRIGHT, our Charlie. (1980 -- ,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,71)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> The Mad Hatter's Teaparty; and who could have been The Mad Hatter, 10s 6d, not the full quid? (1983 -- ,Down from Marble Mountain,111)

>  <b>McGILL</b> not the full quid not sound in the head; eg `There's no doubt in my mind, he's not the full quid. I mean, who else laughs every time there's a quiet moment in the service?' NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,77)

>  <b>VIRTUE</b> `I heard Mum saying that I'm not the full quid ... I didn't want to leave Waiwhetu Road. It isn'tmy fault that ... we're having to go and live in the sticks.' (1987 -- ,Redemption of Elsdon Bird, The,50)



## not too clever
 
 <i>phr.</i> not too well in health

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Clever. </i>`At first a colloquial and local word', S.O.D.; it still is coll. if = `cunning' or `skilful' and applied to an animal or if = `well', `in good health or spirits' (mid-C. 19-20). Esp. <i>not too clever,</i> indisposed in health; the health sense is common in Australia and New Zealand. (1937 -- ,Dictionary of Slang,158) [NOTES: [P8 adds Britain.]]

>  [The traveller to New Zealand] might be ... told . . . that his acquaintance, somewhat indisposed, did not `feel too clever,' ... (Apr 4, 1938 -- ,Press, The) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]



## not too flash
 
 <i>phr.</i> unwell

>  I wasn't feeling too flash when I got back but that was due to jet lag and a big night. (Apr 4, 2003 -- ,Sunday Star Times,B9)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> There were all sorts of diverting new experiences to enjoy and interesting new words and usages to master. I found, for instance, that you didn't have to be even mildly dishonest to be `crook' - only unwell. Also that while recuperating you usually passed through an intermediate state of being `not too flash' before you won back to being `near enough' again. (1956 -- ,Green Kiwi,40,1960 ed.)



## not touch with a red-hot poker
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>wouldn't touch it with a red-hot poker</i> extreme aversion; eg `If I was you I wouldn't touch those shares with a red-hot poker.' NZA. (1988 -- ,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,125)



## not within cooee
 
 <i>phr.</i> in the constr. not come/be within cooee (of): to miss by a wide margin, to come nowhere near (success etc.)

>  <b>ANZAC</b> The niggers ... wouldn't come within coo-ee of our mob when engaged in bomb-throwing. (1916 -- ,On the Anzac Trail,39)

>  <b>COWAN</b> That was twenty-four years ago, but the railway is not yet within cooey of the Tangarakau (1916 -- ,Bush Explorers,24)

>  <b>PFAFF</b> Arrived there too late, and could not get within coo-ey of the golden claims. (1914 -- ,Diggers' Story, The)

>  <i>coo-ee n.</i> call borrowed from aborigines, used in the bush by one wishing to find or be found by another: whence r.tr. to cooee, and figuratively within cooee of, and not within coo-ee of within or not within possibility of success. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. (Aust.-NZ Suppl.),613)

>  <b>MANTELL</b> `Kept telling him how good you were, how well-liked. Sniping at him because he wasn't within cooey [of you]. It got on his nerves.' (1978 -- ,Murder in Fancydress,153)

>  <b>BEARDSLEY</b> [not within cooee] cooee (not within c-) not close (1984 -- ,Blackball 08,243)

>  Melbourne, in all its dishonesty and mad-brained speculation, was not within 'cooey" of the present idiotic boom in Auckland. (1896 -- ,AJHR,I-4A/2)

>  There is no crisis, there is no emergency, they haven't come within coo-ee of losing a vote, and the're blaming everybody but themselves. (Jun 12, 2002 -- ,Dominion,1)



## not worth a cracker
 
 <i>phr.</i> worth nothing, worthless (See [cracker](../C#cracker))

>  <b>CASEY</b> `She's a dead-end show round about four o'clock of a week-day, isn't she? ... Nothing doing worth a cracker, eh.' (1963 -- ,As Short a Spring,89)

 <i>phr.</i> worthless (See [see shit](../S#see-shit))

## not worth a tin of fish
 
 <i>phr.</i> worthless [tin of fish [added to aid search]]

>  <b>SLATTER</b> ... he tried to skedaddle the very first night in the line. Cryin like a kid he was. He wasn't worth a tinna fish over there. And now he skites about when he was at the war. (1959 -- ,Gun in My Hand, A,44)

>  Two fences further on a well-backed runner in Whitestone turned over, and Winchester was then left to win as he liked from Dorando and Maori Rifle, both of whom are not worth a tin of fish. (Dec 31, 1910 -- ,NZ Truth,2)

>  It is always the same - you're not worth a tin of fish when you are fighting, but knock off and you are a world beater. (Nov 29, 1924 -- ,NZ Truth,8)

>  This Budget is not worth a tin of fish from the point of view of putting the country right. (Aug 12, 1931 -- ,Evening Post,9)

>  .. yet already the support of the Italian people, if such exists at all, is not worth a tin of fish to Hitler, and the world knows - if Germany does not - that the popular support of Germany, ascribed to the Hungarians, has about the same value. (Oct 17, 1944 -- ,Evening Post,4)

 <i>n.</i>

## notched tower shell
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>POWELL</b> NOTCHED TOWER SHELL (<i>Phenatoma novaezelandiae</i>). This is characteristic of 77 New Zealand species, most of which have a pronounced cleft or sinus in the outer lip. None of the species are really common and most of them are of deep water occurrence. (1947 -- ,Native Animals of NZ,29)



## note
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  He asked me to lend him a note so as to make it [pounds] 2-10 (1868 -- ,Punch or the Auckland Charivari,63)

>  <b>PYKE</b> ... I'll send her back her fifty pounds ... And as to the fifty notes - well, I told you I hadn't been muchly at school; but if I can't cipher that up, I'm a Dutchman. <i>Ibid.</i> 3/vii/97 The medico looked at the querist. `Would you like to know my opinion?' he asked. `Yes to be sure.' `Then hand me over a note. I never give an opinion without a fee.' (1873 -- ,Wild Will Enderby,2/x/73,1889,1974 ed.)

>  <b>WOOD</b> When you see me working at the rate/ Of fifty notes a week!/ .../ And even at half fifty notes a-week,/ You ought to have made a pile./ (1875 -- ,Waiting for the Mail,39)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> ... but I'm none harder than other folk, except may be some falsh cad like you, Jack, who likes to fly round a bit when they've got a few notes. (1881 -- ,Waitaruna,136)

>  <b>HAY</b> ... everyone rushes at the clerk's table, and ... dumps down his note ([pound]1) for the `Miner's Right', which is his license and authority to dig for gold within the limits of the field. (1882 -- ,Brighter Britain,2/289)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> [Colonialisms] A `note' means one pound, although sovereigns are as plentiful in Australia as bank-notes, and not scarce in New Zealand. Most of the local banks issue notes for one pound. (1888 -- ,Antipodean Notes,170)

>  <b>WAKEWFIELD</b> The ordinary currency consists of one pound notes of all the six banks, which circulate indiscriminately through the colony. Hence the word `note is commonly used for `pound'. For instance a man may say, `I gave 100 note for the cattle', meaning he gave 100 pounds for them,though he may given a check for the money or ten pound notes. (1889 -- ,New Zealand After 50 Years,191)

>  <b>COTTLE</b> He had me for a few notes I was fool enough to lend him. 94 He has been giving me a note a week. (1891 -- ,Frank Melton's Luck,93)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> He had me for a few notes I was fool enough to lend him. 94 He has been giving me a note a week. Note, n. short for Bank-note, and always used for a one-pound note, the common currency. A note = [pound]1. [1864] (1898 -- ,Austral English,322)

>  <b>GRACE</b> I gave 5 notes for the horse. (1901 -- ,Tales of a Dying Race,132)

>  She wouldn't return his letters as they might be worth a few hundred notes. (Sep 30, 1905 -- ,NZ Truth,15/3/4)

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> I nefer could take der notes from der till lest I'd hurt der barman's pride!/ (1909 -- ,Ballads about Business,27)

>  <b>LAWSON</b> Twenty notes won't break us. (1951 -- ,Gold in their Hearts,102)

>  <b>HAMILTON</b> They refused to be compelled to deliver their honey to the government and when they were fined fifty notes, they refused to pay. (1953 -- ,Till Human Voices Wake Us,178)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  In the hall, two doors with lead-glasspanels bearing the College crest wereerected to make the hall more sacrosanctthan discipline-notes could make it. (1935 -- ,St Patrick's College 1883-1935,81)

>  Who will ever forget the silence, moreeloquent than words, as the Rector sat onthe stage, with the book of Weekly Notesopen on the table before him, and slowlybit his under lip while pointing hispencil at some unhappy offender, prior tomaking his slow and scathing comments. (1960 -- ,St Patrick's College 1883-1960,22)

>  Always "right dress" and "eyes front"..and "sinker", notes for this and notesfor that. (1956 -- ,St Patrick's College, Silverstream 1931-1956,16)

>  Silverstream is a place of many memoriesfor us all;.. our notes for the weekshowing our rise or decline, thefriendships we made. (1981 -- ,St Patrick's College, Silverstream 1931-1980,32)

>  We missed, too, the pomp and circumstanceof Weekly Notes. (1981 -- ,St Patrick's College, Silverstream 1931-1980,55)

## nothings

## notornis
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [takahe](../T#takahe))

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The apteryx, notornis, kakapo, and weka, are only found in New Zealand's solitudes. (1859 -- ,Story of New Zealand, The,1/35,1974 ed.)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> The <i>Notornis</i>. [picture caption] The <i>notornis</i>, owing to the feeble nature of its wings, is incapable of flight, but runs with great swiftness amongst the fern. (1866 -- ,Polynesia,53, 61)

>  <b>BULLER</b> Although no examples of the <i>Notornis</i> have since ben obtained, it does not necessarily follow that the species is absolutely extinct. (1873 -- ,Birds of New Zealand,192)

>  Mr. Connor, who was intelligent enough to suspect that he had found a <i>Notornis</i>, at once accepted the offer ... (1882 -- ,TrNZI,14/240)

>  I was informed ... that the skeleton of a Takahe had been found ... near lake Te Anau ... As is well known, the only recent remains of <i>Notornis</i> hitherto obtained are the two stuffed specimens in the British Museum ... (1886 -- ,TrNZI,18/78)

>  By a strange ... coincidence, the first two specimens of the <i>Notornis</i> Notornis, or <i>takahe</i> as it was called in the South island, were secured ... (1889 -- ,TrNZI,21/226)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Notornis, n.</i> a bird of New Zealand allied to the <i>Porphyrio</i> (q.v.), first described from a fossil skull by Professor Owen (1848), and then thought to be extinct, like the Moa. Professor Owen called the bird <i>Notornis mantelli</i>, and, curiously enough, Mr Walter Mantell, in whose honour the bird was named, two years afterwards captured a live specimen; a third specimen was captured in 1879.... The Maori names were <i>Moho</i> and <i>Takahe</i>(q.v.). (1898 -- ,Austral English,323)

>  The <i>Notornis</i>, longest of our living birds, has not been met with in Canterbury, and is indeed on the verge of extinction - (1900 -- ,Canterbury Old & New,195)

>  <b>REISCHEK</b> The fever of discovery fell upon me, for to what infinite pains had I not gone, and through whatuntrodden wildernesses, without having seen or heard sign of <i>Notornis</i>! At last one seemed to be within my reach! (1930 -- ,Yesterdays in Maoriland,264,1933 ed.)

>  <b>STEVENSON</b> Notornis (named Notornis mantelli after Mantell) resembled the pukaki, but was a much heavier bird. (1947 -- ,Maori and Pakeha in North Otago,78)

>  notornis The scientic name of the takahe, a large fightless bird of New Zealand, with a heavy bill and thick legs. (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),276,8th ed.)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> For instance, in the absence of competition from mammals, several species of flightless birds have developed. One is the <i>Notornis</i> which, as the moa once did, grazes on the grasslands; (1959 -- ,History of New Zealand,31)

>  <b>TURNER</b> For instance, in the absence of competition from mammals, several species of flightless birds have developed. One is the <i>Notornis</i> which, as the moa once did, grazes on the grasslands; (1966 -- ,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,168)

>  <i>notornis n. </i>rarest of New Zealand birds, believed extinct till 1859 when a living specimen was discovered [Maori names moho and takahe]. (1966 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  <i>notornis ... n.</i> rarest of N.Z. birds, believed extinct until 1950 when a living specimen was discovered; Maori names <i>moho</i> and <i>takahe</i> (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)

>  <i>138. Notornis mantelli</i> Owen, 1848 <i>Notornis (Takahe) </i> (1970 -- ,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,43,1980 ed.)

>  <i>notornis n.</i> a rare flightless rail of New Zealand. [C19: NL, < Gk <i>notos</i> south + <i>ornis</i> bird] (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,769) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 513]

>  [Hdg.] Home-Made Notornis. (Feb 8, 1956 -- ,Weekly News,5)

>  The first living Notornis was caught in 1849. (May 18, 1922 -- ,Auckland Weekly News,15)

>  The notornis is heavier built, and more olive-green along the back than the swamp hen. (Apr 1, 1924 -- ,Otago Witness,71)

>  This proves conclusively that the Notornis still survives in the secret recesses of these dense West Coast forests. (Apr 3, 1890 -- ,Otago Witness,18)

>  [Hdg] Is Your Car a Notornis? Thename of a New Zealand bird which untilrecently was thought to be extinct, isused in an additional sense - to describea car which is so old-fashioned that ithas become almost extinct. (Aug 23, 1949 -- ,Southern Cross (Wgton),2)

(1873 -- ,Acts of Wellington Legislature,175.4) [NOTES: 1856 Thistle Amendment Act in 1873 Acts of Wellington Legislature 175 4.]

## noxious weed
 
 <i>n.</i> a legal category of harmful weeds which must (under pain of fine) be eradicated from land by the owner (cf Noxious Weeds Act 1900)

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> For preventing the growth and spread of thistles and other noxious weeds. (1949 -- ,Selected Documents,112b) [NOTES: 1861 Nov 2 Sir George Grey Memorandum in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 112b]

>  On the Presence of some Noxious Weeds in Nelson District. [title (1895 -- ,TrNZI,27/407)

>  Noxious Weeds. ... Californian or Canadian thistle ... Bathurst Burr ... (1902 -- ,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,203)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> The gorse, noxious weed though it may be proclaimed, is a glory when its sheets of gold dazzle the eye. (1910 -- ,NZ Plants,135)

>  <b>ANTHONY</b> He cleared his throat nervously a couple of times and ... passed a few remarks on noxious weeds.`Was it true the blackberry was getting a hold of this district?' (1927 -- ,Me and Gus,1977 Gus Tomlins ed.Sturm 85 ed.)

>  <b>PERRY</b> This will include matters such as character of pastures, presence of noxious weeds, bush, etc. (1922 -- ,NZ Sheepfarming,12)

>  <b>COAD</b> Most of his stock had died, his fences were in disrepair, and noxious weeds were spreading their poisonous tentacles all over the place. (1931 -- ,Such Is Life,58)

>  <b>BRUCE</b> ... the yellow broom ... to be classed among the noxious weeds today. (1932 -- ,Early Days of Canterbury,51)

>  <b>CHERRILL</b> Well, look. Gorse is what they call a noxious weed way out home - anyhow it's the farmers' worst enemy. (1950 -- ,Straight Furrow,26)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> The rest was a broken clay bank covered with scrub, gorse and ragwort; ... The local council accused him of growing noxious weeds there, and now and then reluctantly took him to court. (1965 -- ,Among the Cinders,99)

>  <b>TURNER</b> Inspectors call to make sure ... noxious weeds, weeds officially proscribed, arecontrolled. (1966 -- ,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,142)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> As used in relation to weeds in New Zealand, the term `noxious' is a confusing one, as it has two distinct meanings. In a general, non-specific sense, it denotes any weed regarded as significant, for one reason or another, by the use of the term. It has, however, a precise legal meaning denoting weeds whose control and eradication are required under the provisions of the Noxious Weeds Act of 1950 and its amendments. These are the so called `noxious weeds'. (1966 -- ,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/597-598)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> <i>CASSINIA LEPTOPHYLLA. Cassinia</i> is a genus of Australasian and South African shrubs. The five New Zealand species, known as `cottonwoods'or `tauhinu', ... The grey-leaved <i>C. leptophylla</i> so invaded neglected hill pastures (as about Wellington) that it, along with the golden tauhinu <i>C. fulvida</i>, was designated a`scheduled noxious weed'. (1978 -- ,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,138)

>  An Act to prevent the spread of Noxious Weeds and to enforce the Trimming of Hedges. (1900 -- ,NZ Statutes,64 Vict. 10/20) [NOTES: There were also Noxious Weeds Acts in 1908, 1928, and 1950]

## nub
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  ... it is no doubt also responsible for the dark coloured varnishes produced from the chip grades. [fn] The terms dust, seeds, chips, nubs, &c., are the names given to certain of the lower kauri gum grades according to the relative sizes of the pieces. From `dust' the size of the fragments increases in the order given, as does also the purity of the grade. Chalk, or `chalky gum,' is a particular grade recovered from swampy country and is characterized by its white or chalky appearance and large water content. (1936 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,17/371)

>  In order of decreasing size, the pieces of gum were known as nuggets, nubs, peas, chips, and dust. (1991 -- ,NZ Geographic,41)

## nugget
 
 <i>n.</i> (of gold)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> ~ a lump of gold. The noun nugget is not Australian, though often so supposed. Skeat (`Etymological Dictionary,' s.v.) gives a quotation from North's `Plutarch' with the word in a slightly different shape, viz., niggot. `The word nugget was in use in Australia many years before the goldfields were heard of. A thick-set young beast was called `a good nugget.' A bit of a fig of tobacco was called a nugget of tobacco .'' (G. W. Rusden.) [1852] (1898 -- ,Austral English,323)

>  <b>LUSH</b> One of the boys took out ... a small nugget of gold (Nov 23, 1852 -- ,Auckland Journals,126,1971 ed.)

>  <b>EARP</b> One man brought in a small piece [of gold], which might be termed a nugget, as it was the firstassuming that form which had been taken out. [See also 1853 Earp NZ in 1974 Heinz Bright Fine Gold 110] (1853 -- ,New Zealand (various authors/titles),259) [NOTES: 1852 Oct 23 Report of the Sub-Committee onRecent Gold Discovery in the Coromandel District in 1853 Earp NZ 259]

>  The gold is in dust and nuggets. ... The implement most effectual in economising labour is the `Long Tom', or trough strainer, cradles are but of little comparative service. (Dec 10, 1852 -- ,NZ Government Gazette,V/30/183) [NOTES: Heaphy's Report]

>  Stray notes on Australia and the Diggings (Griffiths Collection) Origin of he word `Nugget'. - This word, now so common amongst our population, is a strange sounding word, and destitute of any apparent entymological meaning. It originated at the Ophir diggings in New South Wales, and is probably a corruption of `ingot', which is itself a corruption of `lingot', a little tongue. The wedge of gold which Achan purloined is called, in the original Hebrew, a `tongue' of gold, as a reference to a Bible with marginal readings will show. In California, the word `lump' is generally used, and sometimes the Spanish word `pepite.' (Sep 30, 1852 -- ,Leisure Hour, The,640)

>  <b>A S THOMSON</b> In November [1852] I visited the diggings [at Thames], and procured the frog which is herewith sent. [221] It was got in this way. The gold-diggers were washing the soil of a mountain-stream in the machine called `long-tom'. In excavating the banks they displaced several large boulders of quartzy rock, underneath which was discovered the living frog. The gold-diggers, who voluntarily submit to the evils and miseries of such a gambling trade, and can rarely be excited by anything unless a great nugget, were so much astonished at the sight of a frog that one of them desisted fom the seductive occupation he was at, and took the frog, and put it into a bottle of water. (1853 -- ,Edinburgh New Philosophical Journal,3/220-221) [NOTES: 1853 A.S. Thomson Edinburgh New Philosophical Journal 55/66-69 in 1920 Nov NZJST]

>  <b>SWAINSON</b> Gold in minute quantities was found by every [prospecting] party almost on the surface of the soil; ... Early in November, however, the prospect began to be of a more promising character : one party being reported to have washed out two or three ounces of gold, including several nuggets, and to have averaged fair daily wages; ... (1853 -- ,Auckland,94)

>  I've circulated rumours .../ Of people finding nuggets by the score,/ To cause a rush, ... (1866 -- ,Canterbury Rhymes,92) [NOTES: 1862 Oct in 1866 Canterbury Rhymes 92]

>  <b>HAAST</b> ... I almost thought that he had discovered a golden nugget on the river bank ... (1861 -- ,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,13)

>  ... the general character of the gold being scaly and fine, the reverse of shotty, but some of the nuggets are quite waterworn and solid. (1861 -- ,NZ Goldfields,29,1976 ed.) [NOTES: 1861 Sep 27 Tuapeka in NZ Goldfields 1861 (1976) 29]

>  I've circulated rumours, not a few,/ Of people finding nuggets by the score,/ To cause a rush,... (Oct 22, 1862 -- ,Lyttelton Times) [NOTES: 1862 Oct 22 Lyttelton Times in 1948 Von Haast Julius Bon Haast 259]

>  <b>SMALL</b> And a fifty-pound nugget, bedad, wouldn't draw me/ Away from this city and my Judy again./ (1866 -- ,NZ & Australian Songster,25,1970 ed.)

>  <b>2/121</b> [fn.] ... nugget ... from niggot of gold: West Country nig, nug `block'] (1876 -- ,NZ Magazine)

>  How is gold formed into specks and nuggets? and [I will] also dispute the theory that alluvial gold was not derived from reefs. (1890 -- ,TrNZI,22/400)

>  <b>32/311</b> The tin here is found ... in small, worn, brown lumps or pebbles, with occasionally a few larger pieces. I have seen such pieces (or nuggets) of almost pure tin weighing from 12 oz. to 20 oz. (1900 -- ,TrNZI) [NOTES: Colenso Cornish Tin Mines]

>  <b>BUTLER</b> WHEN my father reached the colony for which he had left England some twenty-two years previously, he bought a horse, and started up-country on the evening of the day after his arrival, which was, as I have said, on one of the last days of November 1890. He had taken an English saddle with him, and a couple of roomy and strongly made saddle-bags. In these he packed his money, his nuggets, some tea, sugar, tobacco, salt, ... (1901 -- ,Erewhon Revisited,chapter II/207,Everyman 1932 (1960) ed.)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Nugget: Alluvial gold in a solid lump. (1935 -- ,Gold Mining Year Book,11) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  In order of decreasing size, the pieces of gum were known as nuggets, nubs, peas, chips, and dust. (Apr 1991 -- ,NZ Geographic,41)



 
 <i>n.</i> (?1892) a dumpy island or rock outcrop in the sea; (?1967) rocks set in water, having some vegetation (Stewart Island) a projecting rock or rocky islet or outcrop in the sea, or in a bay. [?orig. whalers', now Stewart Island and s. SI)]

>  <b>HOCKEN & FENWICK</b> A remarkably beautiful reflection of the breakers and island `nuggets' off the coast. (1892 -- ,Holiday Trip to the Catlins District, A,2)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> There is a little nugget between Te Waitaua and The Neck and this is the nugget that the whaler, `C. A. Larsen,' struck. Its name is Whero (red) ... (1944 -- ,Maori Place-Names of Otago,85)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The whalers [early 19th c] called small islets formed of jutting rock `nuggets', and hence we get the names The Nuggets and Nugget Point. (1948 -- ,Otago Place Names,11)

>  <b>DEMPSEY</b> It is an almost enclosed, tranquil harbour, very small, with a little nugget in the bay. <i>Ibid.</i> 30 They were along the beach near the nugget, where the great bull-kelp writhed and twisted ... (1964 -- ,Little World of Stewart island,19)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> [The tern] is seen in all New Zealand bays and [The tern] nests on islets and nuggets' - which are little more than rocks, with a tuft of bush, set in the water- ... (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,267)



 
 <i>n.</i> a thick-set, chunky animal or person (cf NUGGETTY) [ca1935 applied to a thickset child]

>  <b>MEADE</b> The chief is noted for his preference for a very little steed. ... It was mounted on some such little `nugget' that he distinguished himself. (1864 -- ,Ride Through Disturbed Districts,3,1870 ed.)

>  <b>BELL</b> And this is your little sister, I suppose? Well, she is a nice little nugget, anyhow; and here's sixpence to buy lollies for her and you. (1899 -- ,In the Shadow of the Bush,86)

>  nugget n. small, solidly built house. (1996 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

>  nugget ... n. small, solidly built house[sic: =? horse]. (1968 -- ,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14)



 
 <i>n.</i> a chunk of kauri gum [(gumfields)]

>  <b>SATCHELL</b> He lifted out a nugget about 10 inches long (1902 -- ,Land of the Lost,41)

>  <b>MANDER</b> ... men ...had the chance to pick up in quantities ... the nuggets of amber gum which only the Kauri tree produces. (1920 -- ,Story of a NZ River, The,229,1974 ed.)

>  <b>REED</b> Lumps of gum weighing half a hundredweight or more have on rare occasions been found, but in general, ... the size varied from an occasional nugget weighing a few pounds down to `nuts'the size of an acorn or smaller. <i>Ibid.</i> 50 The hook was used for drawing up nuggets of gum from the depths of swamps, after location by the spear. (1972 -- ,Gumdiggers, The,24)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> Mechanical outfits for collecting the smaller nuggets and chips by means of washing were invented ... (1956 -- ,Green Kiwi,70,1960 ed.)

>  nugget A large lump of valuable metal, such as gold. (1958 -- ,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),8th ed.)



 
 <i>n.</i> a lump or piece of anything

 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>KNOX</b> It was a carved wooden gun, nuggeted black and filthy to hold. (1987 -- ,After Z-Hour,72)



 
 <i>n.</i> a boot polish (from trademark), usually thought of as black; now any bootpolish; [1984 Orsman letter to an enquirer Nugget `boot polish' is a New Zealand and Australian word used, I think, from before the First World War. Eric Partridge in his A Dictionary of Slang gives it as Australian colloquial from about 1910 - the date he thought he first remembered it from. I have it in my collections, but have no early date for it as I have not yet checked it up in advertisements of old newspapers, nor written to the company that produces it to ask them when they adopted Nugget as a brand-name, and why. I assume it comes from nugget `a small lump', as in nugget of gold. Perhaps you or the class might like to write to the Nugget company and ask them about this. It's also now used as a verb - `to nugget shoes ', for example. It's like many other brand-names that have passed into common speech: e.g. hoover for `vacuum cleaner': to hoover a room, etc.]

>  <b>HISLOP</b> Off came my boots and sox, and on went the nugget polish. ... with feet as black as the Ace of Spades. ... If the nugget could be made to stick on leather as it does on your skin, the company would have a monopoly in the boot polish line ... (1943 -- ,Pure Gold,37)

>  <b>McKAY</b> Early that morning he turned up at Baigent's place and said: `Lawrence, you haven't any nugget, have you? I'm getting baptized today and look at my shoes.' (Nov 4, 1948 -- ,James K. Baxter,116) [NOTES: 1948 Nov 4 in 1990 McKay James K. Baxter 116]

>  <b>HARVEY</b> I plonked my [shoeshine] box on the footpath, and started looking through all the tins of nugget and brushes to make sure everything was there. (1964 -- ,Any Old Dollars Mister?,27)

>  <b>BREAM</b> (1974 -- ,I'm Sorry Amanda,31,I added `Nugget and brushes' to my shopping list. ed.)

>  <b>RICHARDSON</b> I take the shoes outside and smear them all over with nugget. Elly has shiny black shoes .. (1986 -- ,Choices,56)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> Before the party broke up, the boys got some nugget and a brush and blackened the sleeping cook's face and bald head. (1986 -- ,Off the Sheep's Back,95)

>  <b>KEINZLY</b> The cocoa was like melted shoe nugget and the toast had stuck to the cloth, soldered in its own grease. (1960 -- ,Tangahano,152)

>  <b>GIBBONS</b> The boots had to be oiled for a week or two... We could then revert to using Nugget. (1989 -- ,Recollections (various authors/titles),14)

## nuggetty
 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (of gold)

>  <b>McLEAN</b> The gold is becoming more nuggetty ranging from a pin's head to a bean. (1857 -- ,Miscellaneous Papers,1V/83)

>  Where the diggers are now working, the gold is almost all nuggety, some running as large as horse beans. (Mar 23, 1861 -- ,Otago Witness,10)

>  The men are satisfied with the amount of gold found [in Otago] though it is not of a very good [40] quality, too fine, not nuggety enough to use a sluice well so they adopt the slower and surer way of cradling. (May 23, 1862 -- ,Chudleigh Diary,39,1950 ed.)

>  In September, 1862, ... Mr. James lamb ... brought to Dunedin about 28oz. of coarse, nuggettygold, obtained at Moa Creek ... (1863 -- ,Handbook NZ Mines,8) [NOTES: 1863 V. Pyke's Report in 1887 Handbook NZ Mines 8]

>  . 28 ozs of coarse, nuggetty gold. [Also 1863 Pyke in 1887 Handbook NZ Mines 8] (1863 -- ,AJHR,D/6/5) [NOTES: 1863 Appendix to Journals D/6/5 Pyke Goldfields Reports]

>  <b>HARPER</b> The place is prosperous, most of the gold found here differing from that near Hokitika; much larger, nuggety and rich. (Oct 1866 -- ,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,105,1914 ed.)

>  <b>HOGG</b> 'Twas when the Rocky River rush began/ ... `and stories told/ Of ounces gotten in a single pan,/ Of nuggety, and round, and shotty gold./ The news like a bush fire through the diggings ran,/ (1875 -- ,Lays and Rhymes,176)

>  <b>PYKE</b> The gold is described by the Superintendent's correspondent as being of a very `fine description' - `not much waterworn,' and `half-pea nuggety.' (1887 -- ,History of the Early Gold Discoveries in Otago,60,1962 ed.)

>  There is a nuggety or mammillated appearance about drift-gold which reef-gold does not present.' (1890 -- ,TrNZI,22/403)

>  The effect of such matters ... was rather to disperse any gold it reduces than to concentrate such gold in a nuggetty form. (1898 -- ,TrNZI,30/500)

>  <b>HOWARD</b> They ... obtained the heaviest gold yet seen in rough nuggety pieces up to a dwt. in size. (1940 -- ,Rakiura,232)

>  <b>HISLOP</b> Skippers gold is noted for its nuggety form; ... (1943 -- ,Pure Gold,21)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The gold on Coal Island was nuggety, ... (1947 -- ,Pioneer Recollections,32,1956 ed.)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> The gold from Digger's was more nuggety and from Patriarch it was nearly black. (1968 -- ,Remembered Trails,125)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> ... the gold soongained a reputation for being coarse and nuggety. (1981 -- ,Paparoas Guide,68)

<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> thickset and compactly built

>  `Nuggety is used in the same sence as Bullocky (q.v.), but with a slight difference of meaning, what we should say `compact.' Bullocky had rather a sense of over-strength inducing an awkwardness of movement. Nuggety does not include the last suggestion.' (Mar 2, 1896 -- ,Letter)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Nuggety, adj. applied to a horse or a man. Short, thick-set and strong. See G. W. Rusden's note under Nugget. (1898 -- ,Austral English,323)

## nui
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a New Zealand-bred cultivar of perennial ryegrass <i>(Lolium perenne)</i>. [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  He began his renovation programme by undersowing 25% of pastures with a mix of nui and moata ryegrass, wana cocksfoot and pitau white clover. (Jan 1989 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,11)

>  It appears that aggressive cultivars, such as Nui and Tama ryegrasses, were best suited to establishment by these techniques. [direct-drilling] Establishment data from the experiment on direct-drilling Ruanui or Nui into existing swards indicate that Ruanui was not as suited to this method of introduction as the more vigorous Nui. (Mar 1979 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,57)

>  Northland dairy farmer Doug Taylor includes tama with nui ryegrass when oversowing paddocks. (Jul 1983 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,5)

>  He has a variety of special purpose pastures. We saw one of his latest which had been direct drilled with Nui 28 kg, Manawa 6 kg .. (Sep 1985 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,16)

>  A legume crop, Huia white clover, which was undersown in the 1985 pea, grain and Nui ryegrass crops, should prove an attractive proposition. (1987 -- ,NZ Journal of Agriculture,Jan/Feb 14)

>  Lighter green colour and a stronger, denser sward than Nui. (1995 -- ,Grasslands Range of Forage and Conservation Plants,17)

>  .. Nui, a longstanding Hawkes Bay perennial, had the highest spring growth rates. (Aug 4, 2005 -- ,Dominion Post,C6)

## numbfish
 
 <i>n.</i> cramp fish, (blind) torpedo ray, electric ray

>  <i>Narcobatus fairchildi </i>(Hutton). SOUTHERN NUMBFISH, OR WHAI REPO. ... The species is also called `torpedo' or `electric ray.' Hutton states that the Natives (presumably of Napier) call it whai-ngenge. <i>Ibid.</i>101. Fig. 3. - Numbfish, <i>Typhlonarke tarakea. </i> (1929 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/98) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> [The ray] <i>T[orpedo] aysoni</i>, the numbfish or cramp fish, is round, with short tail and pelvic fins, and grows up to 3 ft. [sic] (1967 -- ,Animals of NZ, The,202)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> ELECTRIC RAY [<i>Torpedo fairchildi</i>] ... Other names are torpedo and sometimes numbfish. (1967 -- ,Marine Fishes,14)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> BLIND TORPEDO RAY OR NUMBFISH <i>Typhlonarke aysoni</i> (Hamilton) [ibid]. 92]. This uncommon fish has several names, all of which relate to its manner of producing electricshocks, some of which are Numb-fish, Crampfish and Electric Skate. These vernacular names are given by fishermen on account of the fish benumbing or paralysing the person who unwittingly grasps one with his hands. The last name is from the somewhat similar shape to that of a skate or ray, ray being another common term for skates and also the scientific family name. (1956 -- ,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,90,2nd ed.)

>  <b>WILSON</b> NUMBFISH See Electric Ray; also another name for the Blind Torpedo Ray. <i>Ibid.</i> 198 BLIND TORPEDO RAY A rare ray, <i>Typhlonarke aysoni</i>, as he is called in Latin, has theability to emit sparks and make a needle magnetic - yet not to see. Variously named Numbfish, Crampfish and Electric Skate. (1981 -- ,Fisherman's Bible,169)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] numbfish 311-20, 325-7 (1987 -- ,Fishes: A Bibliography)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  Typhlonarke aysoni (Hamilton). BLIND NUMBFISH. This numbfish was first described by Hamilton. (1930 -- ,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/100) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  <i>Typhonarke aysoni </i>(Hamilton). Blind numbfish. (1938 -- ,TrRSNZ,68/401) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

## nurse cow
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a cow that rears the calf of another cow.

>  <b>STEVENS</b> Beef, consisting of forty to forty-five fat two-year-old steers and heifers bred by the dairy herd and nurse cows, and sold on the local fat-stock market. (1958 -- ,Sheep.  Part 1, Sheep husbandry,195)

>  Nurse cows are a profitable enterprise on Kontiki farm. (Oct 22, 1998 -- ,NZ Farmer,21)

>  The use of a herd of nurse cows is each to rear two or more crossbred calves for good quality veal .. Most of the nurse cows are dairy culls, some of them good producers but hard milkers. (Dec 16, 1954 -- ,NZ Farmer,3)

>  I do not believe that a farmer should expect improved production from nurse cows through the act of having reared calves. As the weather and morale improve, the nurse cows return to the herd. (Jul 1971 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,14)

>  <b>BARTON</b> .. the beef-bred steers were single-suckled on their dams while the dairy-bred steers were raised artificially on a variety of diets or were multiple-suckled on a nurse cow. (1972 -- ,Sheepfarming Annual 1972,63)

>  Some [calves] are raised on nurse cows, but all are off the home farm from weaning. A 4ha paddock of oats provided feed for nurse cows in the difficult 1986 spring. (Mar 1987 -- ,NZ Dairy Exporter,15)

>  For sale: three nurse cows, due 1st Aug, plus 1 calved prematurely. (Jun 21, 2001 -- ,Taranaki Daily News,19)

>  Nurse cows calving early made $680 to $840 ... (Apr 12, 2001 -- ,NZ Farmer,44)



## nurse crop
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a crop used to support or protect a more refined one.

>  The advisability of sowing a nurse crop with lucerne has often been discussed, but this practice seems to have arisen in the instances where there has been some doubt of obtaining a good stand. Many still continue to grow nurse crops, but the majority of farmers have dicontinued to do so. A vigorous nurse crop is bound to leave spindly lucerne plants. (Nov 19, 1927 -- ,Hawera Star,14)



## nurse herd
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a breeding flock or herd.

>  One farmed emu can produce 15-25 chicks or more per year..Cattle produce only one offspring per year, sheep perhaps one and a half. This is a significant difference, generating higher farming efficiency because no large "nurse herd" is required. (1996 -- ,NZ Emu Farming Association Journal,Promo 10)

## nutriceuticals
 
 <i>n.</i> products containing special extracts with a health function added to the nutritional base.

>  Nutriceuticals are purified proteins which have some sort of useful biological activity in addition to their food value. Best known is lactoferrin, an iron-binding protein with antibacterial properties which has been approved for use on meat as a means of reducing bacterial spoilage. (Mar 2003 -- ,NZ Geographic,62/54)

>  Beyond that - 20, maybe 30 years away - he dreams of a brave nutriceutical world; fractionated oat products with built-in medicinal properties. (Jul 12, 2003 -- ,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32)

>  Nutriceuticals will contain special extracts with a health function added to the nutritional base. These include products aimed at reducing blood pressure, or stimulating the immune system. (Jan 13, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,C5)



## nutritional
 
 <i>n.</i> products formulated for specific customer requirements

>  Nutritionals are protein powders derived from milk: caseinates (soluble salts of casein), whey-protein concentrates (WPCs) and hydrolysates of the two. (Mar 2003 -- ,NZ Geographic,62/54)

>  Nutritionals are [health] products formulated for specific customer requirements - for the elderly, for example, and children, and for Asian markets. (Jan 13, 2003 -- ,Dominion Post,C5)

Employer's Federation". (May 10, 1918 -- ,Chronicles NZEF,IV/43 : 153)



 
<b>2</b> <i>abbr.</i>

>  NZEF (in New Zealand) abbrev. for New Zealand Expeditionary Force, the New Zealand army that served in 1914-18. 2NZEF is used to refer to the Second New Zealand Expeditionary Force, in World War II. (1982 -- ,Collins Concise English Dictionary,774) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 515; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 333]
